THE DAWN AGAIN\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE MYSTERY OF EDWIN DROOD\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Rochester castle]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\r\nTHE DAWN\r\n\r\n\r\nAn ancient English Cathedral Tower? How can the ancient English\r\nCathedral tower be here! The well-known massive gray square tower of\r\nits old Cathedral? How can that be here! There is no spike of rusty\r\niron in the air, between the eye and it, from any point of the real\r\nprospect. What is the spike that intervenes, and who has set it up?\r\nMaybe it is set up by the Sultan\xe2\x80\x99s orders for the impaling of a horde\r\nof Turkish robbers, one by one. It is so, for cymbals clash, and the\r\nSultan goes by to his palace in long procession. Ten thousand scimitars\r\nflash in the sunlight, and thrice ten thousand dancing-girls strew\r\nflowers. Then, follow white elephants caparisoned in countless gorgeous\r\ncolours, and infinite in number and attendants. Still the Cathedral\r\nTower rises in the background, where it cannot be, and still no\r\nwrithing figure is on the grim spike. Stay! Is the spike so low a thing\r\nas the rusty spike on the top of a post of an old bedstead that has\r\ntumbled all awry? Some vague period of drowsy laughter must be devoted\r\nto the consideration of this possibility.\r\n\r\nShaking from head to foot, the man whose scattered consciousness has\r\nthus fantastically pieced itself together, at length rises, supports\r\nhis trembling frame upon his arms, and looks around. He is in the\r\nmeanest and closest of small rooms. Through the ragged window-curtain,\r\nthe light of early day steals in from a miserable court. He lies,\r\ndressed, across a large unseemly bed, upon a bedstead that has indeed\r\ngiven way under the weight upon it. Lying, also dressed and also across\r\nthe bed, not longwise, are a Chinaman, a Lascar, and a haggard woman.\r\nThe two first are in a sleep or stupor; the last is blowing at a kind\r\nof pipe, to kindle it. And as she blows, and shading it with her lean\r\nhand, concentrates its red spark of light, it serves in the dim morning\r\nas a lamp to show him what he sees of her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother?\xe2\x80\x9d says this woman, in a querulous, rattling whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cHave\r\nanother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looks about him, with his hand to his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe\xe2\x80\x99ve smoked as many as five since ye come in at midnight,\xe2\x80\x9d the woman\r\ngoes on, as she chronically complains. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor me, poor me, my head is so\r\nbad. Them two come in after ye. Ah, poor me, the business is slack, is\r\nslack! Few Chinamen about the Docks, and fewer Lascars, and no ships\r\ncoming in, these say! Here\xe2\x80\x99s another ready for ye, deary. Ye\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nremember like a good soul, won\xe2\x80\x99t ye, that the market price is dreffle\r\nhigh just now? More nor three shillings and sixpence for a thimbleful!\r\nAnd ye\xe2\x80\x99ll remember that nobody but me (and Jack Chinaman t\xe2\x80\x99other side\r\nthe court; but he can\xe2\x80\x99t do it as well as me) has the true secret of\r\nmixing it? Ye\xe2\x80\x99ll pay up accordingly, deary, won\xe2\x80\x99t ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe blows at the pipe as she speaks, and, occasionally bubbling at it,\r\ninhales much of its contents.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO me, O me, my lungs is weak, my lungs is bad! It\xe2\x80\x99s nearly ready for\r\nye, deary. Ah, poor me, poor me, my poor hand shakes like to drop off!\r\nI see ye coming-to, and I ses to my poor self, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll have another ready\r\nfor him, and he\xe2\x80\x99ll bear in mind the market price of opium, and pay\r\naccording.\xe2\x80\x99 O my poor head! I makes my pipes of old penny ink-bottles,\r\nye see, deary\xe2\x80\x94this is one\xe2\x80\x94and I fits-in a mouthpiece, this way, and I\r\ntakes my mixter out of this thimble with this little horn spoon; and so\r\nI fills, deary. Ah, my poor nerves! I got Heavens-hard drunk for\r\nsixteen year afore I took to this; but this don\xe2\x80\x99t hurt me, not to speak\r\nof. And it takes away the hunger as well as wittles, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hands him the nearly-emptied pipe, and sinks back, turning over on\r\nher face.\r\n\r\nHe rises unsteadily from the bed, lays the pipe upon the hearth-stone,\r\ndraws back the ragged curtain, and looks with repugnance at his three\r\ncompanions. He notices that the woman has opium-smoked herself into a\r\nstrange likeness of the Chinaman. His form of cheek, eye, and temple,\r\nand his colour, are repeated in her. Said Chinaman convulsively\r\nwrestles with one of his many Gods or Devils, perhaps, and snarls\r\nhorribly. The Lascar laughs and dribbles at the mouth. The hostess is\r\nstill.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: In the Court]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat visions can _she_ have?\xe2\x80\x9d the waking man muses, as he turns her\r\nface towards him, and stands looking down at it. \xe2\x80\x9cVisions of many\r\nbutchers\xe2\x80\x99 shops, and public-houses, and much credit? Of an increase of\r\nhideous customers, and this horrible bedstead set upright again, and\r\nthis horrible court swept clean? What can she rise to, under any\r\nquantity of opium, higher than that!\xe2\x80\x94Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bends down his ear, to listen to her mutterings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnintelligible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he watches the spasmodic shoots and darts that break out of her face\r\nand limbs, like fitful lightning out of a dark sky, some contagion in\r\nthem seizes upon him: insomuch that he has to withdraw himself to a\r\nlean arm-chair by the hearth\xe2\x80\x94placed there, perhaps, for such\r\nemergencies\xe2\x80\x94and to sit in it, holding tight, until he has got the\r\nbetter of this unclean spirit of imitation.\r\n\r\nThen he comes back, pounces on the Chinaman, and seizing him with both\r\nhands by the throat, turns him violently on the bed. The Chinaman\r\nclutches the aggressive hands, resists, gasps, and protests.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA watchful pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnintelligible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSlowly loosening his grasp as he listens to the incoherent jargon with\r\nan attentive frown, he turns to the Lascar and fairly drags him forth\r\nupon the floor. As he falls, the Lascar starts into a half-risen\r\nattitude, glares with his eyes, lashes about him fiercely with his\r\narms, and draws a phantom knife. It then becomes apparent that the\r\nwoman has taken possession of this knife, for safety\xe2\x80\x99s sake; for, she\r\ntoo starting up, and restraining and expostulating with him, the knife\r\nis visible in her dress, not in his, when they drowsily drop back, side\r\nby side.\r\n\r\nThere has been chattering and clattering enough between them, but to no\r\npurpose. When any distinct word has been flung into the air, it has had\r\nno sense or sequence. Wherefore \xe2\x80\x9cunintelligible!\xe2\x80\x9d is again the comment\r\nof the watcher, made with some reassured nodding of his head, and a\r\ngloomy smile. He then lays certain silver money on the table, finds his\r\nhat, gropes his way down the broken stairs, gives a good morning to\r\nsome rat-ridden doorkeeper, in bed in a black hutch beneath the stairs,\r\nand passes out.\r\n\r\nThat same afternoon, the massive gray square tower of an old Cathedral\r\nrises before the sight of a jaded traveller. The bells are going for\r\ndaily vesper service, and he must needs attend it, one would say, from\r\nhis haste to reach the open Cathedral door. The choir are getting on\r\ntheir sullied white robes, in a hurry, when he arrives among them, gets\r\non his own robe, and falls into the procession filing in to service.\r\nThen, the Sacristan locks the iron-barred gates that divide the\r\nsanctuary from the chancel, and all of the procession having scuttled\r\ninto their places, hide their faces; and then the intoned words, \xe2\x80\x9cWHEN\r\nTHE WICKED MAN\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d rise among groins of arches and beams of roof,\r\nawakening muttered thunder.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\nA DEAN, AND A CHAPTER ALSO\r\n\r\n\r\nWhosoever has observed that sedate and clerical bird, the rook, may\r\nperhaps have noticed that when he wings his way homeward towards\r\nnightfall, in a sedate and clerical company, two rooks will suddenly\r\ndetach themselves from the rest, will retrace their flight for some\r\ndistance, and will there poise and linger; conveying to mere men the\r\nfancy that it is of some occult importance to the body politic, that\r\nthis artful couple should pretend to have renounced connection with it.\r\n\r\nSimilarly, service being over in the old Cathedral with the square\r\ntower, and the choir scuffling out again, and divers venerable persons\r\nof rook-like aspect dispersing, two of these latter retrace their\r\nsteps, and walk together in the echoing Close.\r\n\r\nNot only is the day waning, but the year. The low sun is fiery and yet\r\ncold behind the monastery ruin, and the Virginia creeper on the\r\nCathedral wall has showered half its deep-red leaves down on the\r\npavement. There has been rain this afternoon, and a wintry shudder goes\r\namong the little pools on the cracked, uneven flag-stones, and through\r\nthe giant elm-trees as they shed a gust of tears. Their fallen leaves\r\nlie strewn thickly about. Some of these leaves, in a timid rush, seek\r\nsanctuary within the low arched Cathedral door; but two men coming out\r\nresist them, and cast them forth again with their feet; this done, one\r\nof the two locks the door with a goodly key, and the other flits away\r\nwith a folio music-book.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Jasper was that, Tope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mr. Dean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has stayed late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mr. Dean. I have stayed for him, your Reverence. He has been took\r\na little poorly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay \xe2\x80\x98taken,\xe2\x80\x99 Tope\xe2\x80\x94to the Dean,\xe2\x80\x9d the younger rook interposes in a low\r\ntone with this touch of correction, as who should say: \xe2\x80\x9cYou may offer\r\nbad grammar to the laity, or the humbler clergy, not to the Dean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Tope, Chief Verger and Showman, and accustomed to be high with\r\nexcursion parties, declines with a silent loftiness to perceive that\r\nany suggestion has been tendered to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when and how has Mr. Jasper been taken\xe2\x80\x94for, as Mr. Crisparkle has\r\nremarked, it is better to say taken\xe2\x80\x94taken\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d repeats the Dean; \xe2\x80\x9cwhen and\r\nhow has Mr. Jasper been Taken\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTaken, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Tope deferentially murmurs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94Poorly, Tope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, sir, Mr. Jasper was that breathed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98That breathed,\xe2\x80\x99 Tope,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle interposes with\r\nthe same touch as before. \xe2\x80\x9cNot English\xe2\x80\x94to the Dean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreathed to that extent,\xe2\x80\x9d the Dean (not unflattered by this indirect\r\nhomage) condescendingly remarks, \xe2\x80\x9cwould be preferable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s breathing was so remarkably short\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94thus discreetly does\r\nMr. Tope work his way round the sunken rock\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhen he came in, that it\r\ndistressed him mightily to get his notes out: which was perhaps the\r\ncause of his having a kind of fit on him after a little. His memory\r\ngrew DAZED.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Tope, with his eyes on the Reverend Mr. Crisparkle,\r\nshoots this word out, as defying him to improve upon it: \xe2\x80\x9cand a dimness\r\nand giddiness crept over him as strange as ever I saw: though he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseem to mind it particularly, himself. However, a little time and a\r\nlittle water brought him out of his DAZE.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Tope repeats the word\r\nand its emphasis, with the air of saying: \xe2\x80\x9cAs I _have_ made a success,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll make it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mr. Jasper has gone home quite himself, has he?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Dean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Reverence, he has gone home quite himself. And I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to see\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s having his fire kindled up, for it\xe2\x80\x99s chilly after the wet, and the\r\nCathedral had both a damp feel and a damp touch this afternoon, and he\r\nwas very shivery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all three look towards an old stone gatehouse crossing the Close,\r\nwith an arched thoroughfare passing beneath it. Through its latticed\r\nwindow, a fire shines out upon the fast-darkening scene, involving in\r\nshadow the pendent masses of ivy and creeper covering the building\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfront. As the deep Cathedral-bell strikes the hour, a ripple of wind\r\ngoes through these at their distance, like a ripple of the solemn sound\r\nthat hums through tomb and tower, broken niche and defaced statue, in\r\nthe pile close at hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s nephew with him?\xe2\x80\x9d the Dean asks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Verger, \xe2\x80\x9cbut expected. There\xe2\x80\x99s his own solitary\r\nshadow betwixt his two windows\xe2\x80\x94the one looking this way, and the one\r\nlooking down into the High Street\xe2\x80\x94drawing his own curtains now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well,\xe2\x80\x9d says the Dean, with a sprightly air of breaking up the\r\nlittle conference, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s heart may not be too much set\r\nupon his nephew. Our affections, however laudable, in this transitory\r\nworld, should never master us; we should guide them, guide them. I find\r\nI am not disagreeably reminded of my dinner, by hearing my dinner-bell.\r\nPerhaps, Mr. Crisparkle, you will, before going home, look in on\r\nJasper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, Mr. Dean. And tell him that you had the kindness to desire\r\nto know how he was?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy; do so, do so. Certainly. Wished to know how he was. By all means.\r\nWished to know how he was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a pleasant air of patronage, the Dean as nearly cocks his quaint\r\nhat as a Dean in good spirits may, and directs his comely gaiters\r\ntowards the ruddy dining-room of the snug old red-brick house where he\r\nis at present, \xe2\x80\x9cin residence\xe2\x80\x9d with Mrs. Dean and Miss Dean.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, Minor Canon, fair and rosy, and perpetually pitching\r\nhimself head-foremost into all the deep running water in the\r\nsurrounding country; Mr. Crisparkle, Minor Canon, early riser, musical,\r\nclassical, cheerful, kind, good-natured, social, contented, and\r\nboy-like; Mr. Crisparkle, Minor Canon and good man, lately \xe2\x80\x9cCoach\xe2\x80\x9d upon\r\nthe chief Pagan high roads, but since promoted by a patron (grateful\r\nfor a well-taught son) to his present Christian beat; betakes himself\r\nto the gatehouse, on his way home to his early tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry to hear from Tope that you have not been well, Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it was nothing, nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look a little worn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I? O, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. What is better, I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel so. Tope has\r\nmade too much of it, I suspect. It\xe2\x80\x99s his trade to make the most of\r\neverything appertaining to the Cathedral, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may tell the Dean\xe2\x80\x94I call expressly from the Dean\xe2\x80\x94that you are all\r\nright again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reply, with a slight smile, is: \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; with my respects and\r\nthanks to the Dean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad to hear that you expect young Drood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect the dear fellow every moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! He will do you more good than a doctor, Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore good than a dozen doctors. For I love him dearly, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlove doctors, or doctors\xe2\x80\x99 stuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper is a dark man of some six-and-twenty, with thick, lustrous,\r\nwell-arranged black hair and whiskers. He looks older than he is, as\r\ndark men often do. His voice is deep and good, his face and figure are\r\ngood, his manner is a little sombre. His room is a little sombre, and\r\nmay have had its influence in forming his manner. It is mostly in\r\nshadow. Even when the sun shines brilliantly, it seldom touches the\r\ngrand piano in the recess, or the folio music-books on the stand, or\r\nthe book-shelves on the wall, or the unfinished picture of a blooming\r\nschoolgirl hanging over the chimneypiece; her flowing brown hair tied\r\nwith a blue riband, and her beauty remarkable for a quite childish,\r\nalmost babyish, touch of saucy discontent, comically conscious of\r\nitself. (There is not the least artistic merit in this picture, which\r\nis a mere daub; but it is clear that the painter has made it\r\nhumorously\xe2\x80\x94one might almost say, revengefully\xe2\x80\x94like the original.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall miss you, Jasper, at the \xe2\x80\x98Alternate Musical Wednesdays\xe2\x80\x99\r\nto-night; but no doubt you are best at home. Good-night. God bless you!\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me, shep-herds, te-e-ell me; tell me-e-e, have you seen (have you\r\nseen, have you seen, have you seen) my-y-y Flo-o-ora-a pass this way!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMelodiously good Minor Canon the Reverend Septimus Crisparkle thus\r\ndelivers himself, in musical rhythm, as he withdraws his amiable face\r\nfrom the doorway and conveys it down-stairs.\r\n\r\nSounds of recognition and greeting pass between the Reverend Septimus\r\nand somebody else, at the stair-foot. Mr. Jasper listens, starts from\r\nhis chair, and catches a young fellow in his arms, exclaiming:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Edwin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Jack! So glad to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet off your greatcoat, bright boy, and sit down here in your own\r\ncorner. Your feet are not wet? Pull your boots off. Do pull your boots\r\noff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Jack, I am as dry as a bone. Don\xe2\x80\x99t moddley-coddley, there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngood fellow. I like anything better than being moddley-coddleyed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith the check upon him of being unsympathetically restrained in a\r\ngenial outburst of enthusiasm, Mr. Jasper stands still, and looks on\r\nintently at the young fellow, divesting himself of his outward coat,\r\nhat, gloves, and so forth. Once for all, a look of intentness and\r\nintensity\xe2\x80\x94a look of hungry, exacting, watchful, and yet devoted\r\naffection\xe2\x80\x94is always, now and ever afterwards, on the Jasper face\r\nwhenever the Jasper face is addressed in this direction. And whenever\r\nit is so addressed, it is never, on this occasion or on any other,\r\ndividedly addressed; it is always concentrated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I am right, and now I\xe2\x80\x99ll take my corner, Jack. Any dinner, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper opens a door at the upper end of the room, and discloses a\r\nsmall inner room pleasantly lighted and prepared, wherein a comely dame\r\nis in the act of setting dishes on table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a jolly old Jack it is!\xe2\x80\x9d cries the young fellow, with a clap of\r\nhis hands. \xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Jack; tell me; whose birthday is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yours, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper answers, pausing to consider.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot mine, you know? No; not mine, _I_ know! Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFixed as the look the young fellow meets, is, there is yet in it some\r\nstrange power of suddenly including the sketch over the chimneypiece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPussy\xe2\x80\x99s, Jack! We must drink Many happy returns to her. Come, uncle;\r\ntake your dutiful and sharp-set nephew in to dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the boy (for he is little more) lays a hand on Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder,\r\nJasper cordially and gaily lays a hand on _his_ shoulder, and so\r\nMarseillaise-wise they go in to dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, Lord! here\xe2\x80\x99s Mrs. Tope!\xe2\x80\x9d cries the boy. \xe2\x80\x9cLovelier than ever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever you mind me, Master Edwin,\xe2\x80\x9d retorts the Verger\xe2\x80\x99s wife; \xe2\x80\x9cI can\r\ntake care of myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t. You\xe2\x80\x99re much too handsome. Give me a kiss because it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nPussy\xe2\x80\x99s birthday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d Pussy you, young man, if I was Pussy, as you call her,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Tope\r\nblushingly retorts, after being saluted. \xe2\x80\x9cYour uncle\xe2\x80\x99s too much wrapt\r\nup in you, that\xe2\x80\x99s where it is. He makes so much of you, that it\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nopinion you think you\xe2\x80\x99ve only to call your Pussys by the dozen, to make\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forget, Mrs. Tope,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper interposes, taking his place at the\r\ntable with a genial smile, \xe2\x80\x9cand so do you, Ned, that Uncle and Nephew\r\nare words prohibited here by common consent and express agreement. For\r\nwhat we are going to receive His holy name be praised!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDone like the Dean! Witness, Edwin Drood! Please to carve, Jack, for I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis sally ushers in the dinner. Little to the present purpose, or to\r\nany purpose, is said, while it is in course of being disposed of. At\r\nlength the cloth is drawn, and a dish of walnuts and a decanter of\r\nrich-coloured sherry are placed upon the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say! Tell me, Jack,\xe2\x80\x9d the young fellow then flows on: \xe2\x80\x9cdo you really\r\nand truly feel as if the mention of our relationship divided us at all?\r\n_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncles as a rule, Ned, are so much older than their nephews,\xe2\x80\x9d is the\r\nreply, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have that feeling instinctively.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a rule! Ah, may-be! But what is a difference in age of half-a-dozen\r\nyears or so? And some uncles, in large families, are even younger than\r\ntheir nephews. By George, I wish it was the case with us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause if it was, I\xe2\x80\x99d take the lead with you, Jack, and be as wise as\r\nBegone, dull Care! that turned a young man gray, and Begone, dull Care!\r\nthat turned an old man to clay.\xe2\x80\x94Halloa, Jack! Don\xe2\x80\x99t drink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsks why not, on Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s birthday, and no Happy returns proposed!\r\nPussy, Jack, and many of \xe2\x80\x99em! Happy returns, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLaying an affectionate and laughing touch on the boy\xe2\x80\x99s extended hand,\r\nas if it were at once his giddy head and his light heart, Mr. Jasper\r\ndrinks the toast in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHip, hip, hip, and nine times nine, and one to finish with, and all\r\nthat, understood. Hooray, hooray, hooray!\xe2\x80\x94And now, Jack, let\xe2\x80\x99s have a\r\nlittle talk about Pussy. Two pairs of nut-crackers? Pass me one, and\r\ntake the other.\xe2\x80\x9d Crack. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s Pussy getting on Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith her music? Fairly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a dreadfully conscientious fellow you are, Jack! But _I_ know,\r\nLord bless you! Inattentive, isn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe can learn anything, if she will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_If_ she will! Egad, that\xe2\x80\x99s it. But if she won\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCrack!\xe2\x80\x94on Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s she looking, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s concentrated face again includes the portrait as he\r\nreturns: \xe2\x80\x9cVery like your sketch indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _am_ a little proud of it,\xe2\x80\x9d says the young fellow, glancing up at\r\nthe sketch with complacency, and then shutting one eye, and taking a\r\ncorrected prospect of it over a level bridge of nut-crackers in the\r\nair: \xe2\x80\x9cNot badly hit off from memory. But I ought to have caught that\r\nexpression pretty well, for I have seen it often enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCrack!\xe2\x80\x94on Edwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\nCrack!\xe2\x80\x94on Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn point of fact,\xe2\x80\x9d the former resumes, after some silent dipping among\r\nhis fragments of walnut with an air of pique, \xe2\x80\x9cI see it whenever I go\r\nto see Pussy. If I don\xe2\x80\x99t find it on her face, I leave it there.\xe2\x80\x94You\r\nknow I do, Miss Scornful Pert. Booh!\xe2\x80\x9d With a twirl of the nut-crackers\r\nat the portrait.\r\n\r\nCrack! crack! crack. Slowly, on Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s part.\r\n\r\nCrack. Sharply on the part of Edwin Drood.\r\n\r\nSilence on both sides.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you lost your tongue, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you found yours, Ned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but really;\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t it, you know, after all\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper lifts his dark eyebrows inquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it unsatisfactory to be cut off from choice in such a matter?\r\nThere, Jack! I tell you! If I could choose, I would choose Pussy from\r\nall the pretty girls in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have not got to choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I complain of. My dead and gone father and Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s dead\r\nand gone father must needs marry us together by anticipation. Why\r\nthe\xe2\x80\x94Devil, I was going to say, if it had been respectful to their\r\nmemory\xe2\x80\x94couldn\xe2\x80\x99t they leave us alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut, tut, dear boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper remonstrates, in a tone of gentle\r\ndeprecation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut, tut? Yes, Jack, it\xe2\x80\x99s all very well for _you_. _You_ can take it\r\neasily. _Your_ life is not laid down to scale, and lined and dotted out\r\nfor you, like a surveyor\xe2\x80\x99s plan. _You_ have no uncomfortable suspicion\r\nthat you are forced upon anybody, nor has anybody an uncomfortable\r\nsuspicion that she is forced upon you, or that you are forced upon her.\r\n_You_ can choose for yourself. Life, for _you_, is a plum with the\r\nnatural bloom on; it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been over-carefully wiped off for _you_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t stop, dear fellow. Go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I anyhow have hurt your feelings, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you have hurt my feelings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Heaven, Jack, you look frightfully ill! There\xe2\x80\x99s a strange film\r\ncome over your eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, with a forced smile, stretches out his right hand, as if at\r\nonce to disarm apprehension and gain time to get better. After a while\r\nhe says faintly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been taking opium for a pain\xe2\x80\x94an agony\xe2\x80\x94that sometimes overcomes\r\nme. The effects of the medicine steal over me like a blight or a cloud,\r\nand pass. You see them in the act of passing; they will be gone\r\ndirectly. Look away from me. They will go all the sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a scared face the younger man complies by casting his eyes\r\ndownward at the ashes on the hearth. Not relaxing his own gaze on the\r\nfire, but rather strengthening it with a fierce, firm grip upon his\r\nelbow-chair, the elder sits for a few moments rigid, and then, with\r\nthick drops standing on his forehead, and a sharp catch of his breath,\r\nbecomes as he was before. On his so subsiding in his chair, his nephew\r\ngently and assiduously tends him while he quite recovers. When Jasper\r\nis restored, he lays a tender hand upon his nephew\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and, in\r\na tone of voice less troubled than the purport of his words\xe2\x80\x94indeed with\r\nsomething of raillery or banter in it\xe2\x80\x94thus addresses him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is said to be a hidden skeleton in every house; but you thought\r\nthere was none in mine, dear Ned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my life, Jack, I did think so. However, when I come to consider\r\nthat even in Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s house\xe2\x80\x94if she had one\xe2\x80\x94and in mine\xe2\x80\x94if I had one\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were going to say (but that I interrupted you in spite of myself)\r\nwhat a quiet life mine is. No whirl and uproar around me, no\r\ndistracting commerce or calculation, no risk, no change of place,\r\nmyself devoted to the art I pursue, my business my pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really was going to say something of the kind, Jack; but you see,\r\nyou, speaking of yourself, almost necessarily leave out much that I\r\nshould have put in. For instance: I should have put in the foreground\r\nyour being so much respected as Lay Precentor, or Lay Clerk, or\r\nwhatever you call it, of this Cathedral; your enjoying the reputation\r\nof having done such wonders with the choir; your choosing your society,\r\nand holding such an independent position in this queer old place; your\r\ngift of teaching (why, even Pussy, who don\xe2\x80\x99t like being taught, says\r\nthere never was such a Master as you are!), and your connexion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I saw what you were tending to. I hate it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHate it, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d (Much bewildered.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate it. The cramped monotony of my existence grinds me away by the\r\ngrain. How does our service sound to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautiful! Quite celestial!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt often sounds to me quite devilish. I am so weary of it. The echoes\r\nof my own voice among the arches seem to mock me with my daily drudging\r\nround. No wretched monk who droned his life away in that gloomy place,\r\nbefore me, can have been more tired of it than I am. He could take for\r\nrelief (and did take) to carving demons out of the stalls and seats and\r\ndesks. What shall I do? Must I take to carving them out of my heart?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had so exactly found your niche in life, Jack,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin\r\nDrood returns, astonished, bending forward in his chair to lay a\r\nsympathetic hand on Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s knee, and looking at him with an anxious\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you thought so. They all think so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose they do,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, meditating aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cPussy thinks\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did she tell you that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe last time I was here. You remember when. Three months ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did she phrase it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, she only said that she had become your pupil, and that you were\r\nmade for your vocation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe younger man glances at the portrait. The elder sees it in him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow, my dear Ned,\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper resumes, as he shakes his head with a\r\ngrave cheerfulness, \xe2\x80\x9cI must subdue myself to my vocation: which is much\r\nthe same thing outwardly. It\xe2\x80\x99s too late to find another now. This is a\r\nconfidence between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt shall be sacredly preserved, Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have reposed it in you, because\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel it, I assure you. Because we are fast friends, and because you\r\nlove and trust me, as I love and trust you. Both hands, Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs each stands looking into the other\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and as the uncle holds\r\nthe nephew\xe2\x80\x99s hands, the uncle thus proceeds:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know now, don\xe2\x80\x99t you, that even a poor monotonous chorister and\r\ngrinder of music\xe2\x80\x94in his niche\xe2\x80\x94may be troubled with some stray sort of\r\nambition, aspiration, restlessness, dissatisfaction, what shall we call\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Jack, I only ask you, am I likely to forget what you have said\r\nwith so much feeling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it as a warning, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the act of having his hands released, and of moving a step back,\r\nEdwin pauses for an instant to consider the application of these last\r\nwords. The instant over, he says, sensibly touched:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I am but a shallow, surface kind of fellow, Jack, and that\r\nmy headpiece is none of the best. But I needn\xe2\x80\x99t say I am young; and\r\nperhaps I shall not grow worse as I grow older. At all events, I hope I\r\nhave something impressible within me, which feels\xe2\x80\x94deeply feels\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndisinterestedness of your painfully laying your inner self bare, as a\r\nwarning to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s steadiness of face and figure becomes so marvellous that\r\nhis breathing seems to have stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t fail to notice, Jack, that it cost you a great effort, and\r\nthat you were very much moved, and very unlike your usual self. Of\r\ncourse I knew that you were extremely fond of me, but I really was not\r\nprepared for your, as I may say, sacrificing yourself to me in that\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, becoming a breathing man again without the smallest stage\r\nof transition between the two extreme states, lifts his shoulders,\r\nlaughs, and waves his right arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; don\xe2\x80\x99t put the sentiment away, Jack; please don\xe2\x80\x99t; for I am very\r\nmuch in earnest. I have no doubt that that unhealthy state of mind\r\nwhich you have so powerfully described is attended with some real\r\nsuffering, and is hard to bear. But let me reassure you, Jack, as to\r\nthe chances of its overcoming me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am in the way of it.\r\nIn some few months less than another year, you know, I shall carry\r\nPussy off from school as Mrs. Edwin Drood. I shall then go engineering\r\ninto the East, and Pussy with me. And although we have our little tiffs\r\nnow, arising out of a certain unavoidable flatness that attends our\r\nlove-making, owing to its end being all settled beforehand, still I\r\nhave no doubt of our getting on capitally then, when it\xe2\x80\x99s done and\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t be helped. In short, Jack, to go back to the old song I was\r\nfreely quoting at dinner (and who knows old songs better than you?), my\r\nwife shall dance, and I will sing, so merrily pass the day. Of Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeing beautiful there cannot be a doubt;\xe2\x80\x94and when you are good besides,\r\nLittle Miss Impudence,\xe2\x80\x9d once more apostrophising the portrait, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nburn your comic likeness, and paint your music-master another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, with his hand to his chin, and with an expression of musing\r\nbenevolence on his face, has attentively watched every animated look\r\nand gesture attending the delivery of these words. He remains in that\r\nattitude after they are spoken, as if in a kind of fascination\r\nattendant on his strong interest in the youthful spirit that he loves\r\nso well. Then he says with a quiet smile:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t be warned, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t be warned, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Jack, not by you. Besides that I don\xe2\x80\x99t really consider myself in\r\ndanger, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like your putting yourself in that position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we go and walk in the churchyard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means. You won\xe2\x80\x99t mind my slipping out of it for half a moment\r\nto the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and leaving a parcel there? Only gloves for Pussy;\r\nas many pairs of gloves as she is years old to-day. Rather poetical,\r\nJack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, still in the same attitude, murmurs: \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Nothing half so\r\nsweet in life,\xe2\x80\x99 Ned!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the parcel in my greatcoat-pocket. They must be presented\r\nto-night, or the poetry is gone. It\xe2\x80\x99s against regulations for me to\r\ncall at night, but not to leave a packet. I am ready, Jack!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper dissolves his attitude, and they go out together.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\nTHE NUNS\xe2\x80\x99 HOUSE\r\n\r\n\r\nFor sufficient reasons, which this narrative will itself unfold as it\r\nadvances, a fictitious name must be bestowed upon the old Cathedral\r\ntown. Let it stand in these pages as Cloisterham. It was once possibly\r\nknown to the Druids by another name, and certainly to the Romans by\r\nanother, and to the Saxons by another, and to the Normans by another;\r\nand a name more or less in the course of many centuries can be of\r\nlittle moment to its dusty chronicles.\r\n\r\nAn ancient city, Cloisterham, and no meet dwelling-place for any one\r\nwith hankerings after the noisy world. A monotonous, silent city,\r\nderiving an earthy flavour throughout from its Cathedral crypt, and so\r\nabounding in vestiges of monastic graves, that the Cloisterham children\r\ngrow small salad in the dust of abbots and abbesses, and make dirt-pies\r\nof nuns and friars; while every ploughman in its outlying fields\r\nrenders to once puissant Lord Treasurers, Archbishops, Bishops, and\r\nsuch-like, the attention which the Ogre in the story-book desired to\r\nrender to his unbidden visitor, and grinds their bones to make his\r\nbread.\r\n\r\nA drowsy city, Cloisterham, whose inhabitants seem to suppose, with an\r\ninconsistency more strange than rare, that all its changes lie behind\r\nit, and that there are no more to come. A queer moral to derive from\r\nantiquity, yet older than any traceable antiquity. So silent are the\r\nstreets of Cloisterham (though prone to echo on the smallest\r\nprovocation), that of a summer-day the sunblinds of its shops scarce\r\ndare to flap in the south wind; while the sun-browned tramps, who pass\r\nalong and stare, quicken their limp a little, that they may the sooner\r\nget beyond the confines of its oppressive respectability. This is a\r\nfeat not difficult of achievement, seeing that the streets of\r\nCloisterham city are little more than one narrow street by which you\r\nget into it and get out of it: the rest being mostly disappointing\r\nyards with pumps in them and no thoroughfare\xe2\x80\x94exception made of the\r\nCathedral-close, and a paved Quaker settlement, in colour and general\r\nconfirmation very like a Quakeress\xe2\x80\x99s bonnet, up in a shady corner.\r\n\r\nIn a word, a city of another and a bygone time is Cloisterham, with its\r\nhoarse Cathedral-bell, its hoarse rooks hovering about the Cathedral\r\ntower, its hoarser and less distinct rooks in the stalls far beneath.\r\nFragments of old wall, saint\xe2\x80\x99s chapel, chapter-house, convent and\r\nmonastery, have got incongruously or obstructively built into many of\r\nits houses and gardens, much as kindred jumbled notions have become\r\nincorporated into many of its citizens\xe2\x80\x99 minds. All things in it are of\r\nthe past. Even its single pawnbroker takes in no pledges, nor has he\r\nfor a long time, but offers vainly an unredeemed stock for sale, of\r\nwhich the costlier articles are dim and pale old watches apparently in\r\na slow perspiration, tarnished sugar-tongs with ineffectual legs, and\r\nodd volumes of dismal books. The most abundant and the most agreeable\r\nevidences of progressing life in Cloisterham are the evidences of\r\nvegetable life in many gardens; even its drooping and despondent little\r\ntheatre has its poor strip of garden, receiving the foul fiend, when he\r\nducks from its stage into the infernal regions, among scarlet-beans or\r\noyster-shells, according to the season of the year.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of Cloisterham stands the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House: a venerable brick\r\nedifice, whose present appellation is doubtless derived from the legend\r\nof its conventual uses. On the trim gate enclosing its old courtyard is\r\na resplendent brass plate flashing forth the legend: \xe2\x80\x9cSeminary for\r\nYoung Ladies. Miss Twinkleton.\xe2\x80\x9d The house-front is so old and worn, and\r\nthe brass plate is so shining and staring, that the general result has\r\nreminded imaginative strangers of a battered old beau with a large\r\nmodern eye-glass stuck in his blind eye.\r\n\r\nWhether the nuns of yore, being of a submissive rather than a\r\nstiff-necked generation, habitually bent their contemplative heads to\r\navoid collision with the beams in the low ceilings of the many chambers\r\nof their House; whether they sat in its long low windows telling their\r\nbeads for their mortification, instead of making necklaces of them for\r\ntheir adornment; whether they were ever walled up alive in odd angles\r\nand jutting gables of the building for having some ineradicable leaven\r\nof busy mother Nature in them which has kept the fermenting world alive\r\never since; these may be matters of interest to its haunting ghosts (if\r\nany), but constitute no item in Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s half-yearly accounts.\r\nThey are neither of Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s inclusive regulars, nor of her\r\nextras. The lady who undertakes the poetical department of the\r\nestablishment at so much (or so little) a quarter has no pieces in her\r\nlist of recitals bearing on such unprofitable questions.\r\n\r\nAs, in some cases of drunkenness, and in others of animal magnetism,\r\nthere are two states of consciousness which never clash, but each of\r\nwhich pursues its separate course as though it were continuous instead\r\nof broken (thus, if I hide my watch when I am drunk, I must be drunk\r\nagain before I can remember where), so Miss Twinkleton has two distinct\r\nand separate phases of being. Every night, the moment the young ladies\r\nhave retired to rest, does Miss Twinkleton smarten up her curls a\r\nlittle, brighten up her eyes a little, and become a sprightlier Miss\r\nTwinkleton than the young ladies have ever seen. Every night, at the\r\nsame hour, does Miss Twinkleton resume the topics of the previous\r\nnight, comprehending the tenderer scandal of Cloisterham, of which she\r\nhas no knowledge whatever by day, and references to a certain season at\r\nTunbridge Wells (airily called by Miss Twinkleton in this state of her\r\nexistence \xe2\x80\x9cThe Wells\xe2\x80\x9d), notably the season wherein a certain finished\r\ngentleman (compassionately called by Miss Twinkleton, in this stage of\r\nher existence, \xe2\x80\x9cFoolish Mr. Porters\xe2\x80\x9d) revealed a homage of the heart,\r\nwhereof Miss Twinkleton, in her scholastic state of existence, is as\r\nignorant as a granite pillar. Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s companion in both\r\nstates of existence, and equally adaptable to either, is one Mrs.\r\nTisher: a deferential widow with a weak back, a chronic sigh, and a\r\nsuppressed voice, who looks after the young ladies\xe2\x80\x99 wardrobes, and\r\nleads them to infer that she has seen better days. Perhaps this is the\r\nreason why it is an article of faith with the servants, handed down\r\nfrom race to race, that the departed Tisher was a hairdresser.\r\n\r\nThe pet pupil of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House is Miss Rosa Bud, of course called\r\nRosebud; wonderfully pretty, wonderfully childish, wonderfully\r\nwhimsical. An awkward interest (awkward because romantic) attaches to\r\nMiss Bud in the minds of the young ladies, on account of its being\r\nknown to them that a husband has been chosen for her by will and\r\nbequest, and that her guardian is bound down to bestow her on that\r\nhusband when he comes of age. Miss Twinkleton, in her seminarial state\r\nof existence, has combated the romantic aspect of this destiny by\r\naffecting to shake her head over it behind Miss Bud\xe2\x80\x99s dimpled\r\nshoulders, and to brood on the unhappy lot of that doomed little\r\nvictim. But with no better effect\xe2\x80\x94possibly some unfelt touch of foolish\r\nMr. Porters has undermined the endeavour\xe2\x80\x94than to evoke from the young\r\nladies an unanimous bedchamber cry of \xe2\x80\x9cO, what a pretending old thing\r\nMiss Twinkleton is, my dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House is never in such a state of flutter as when this\r\nallotted husband calls to see little Rosebud. (It is unanimously\r\nunderstood by the young ladies that he is lawfully entitled to this\r\nprivilege, and that if Miss Twinkleton disputed it, she would be\r\ninstantly taken up and transported.) When his ring at the gate-bell is\r\nexpected, or takes place, every young lady who can, under any pretence,\r\nlook out of window, looks out of window; while every young lady who is\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpractising,\xe2\x80\x9d practises out of time; and the French class becomes so\r\ndemoralised that the mark goes round as briskly as the bottle at a\r\nconvivial party in the last century.\r\n\r\nOn the afternoon of the day next after the dinner of two at the\r\ngatehouse, the bell is rung with the usual fluttering results.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Edwin Drood to see Miss Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis is the announcement of the parlour-maid in chief. Miss Twinkleton,\r\nwith an exemplary air of melancholy on her, turns to the sacrifice, and\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x9cYou may go down, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Bud goes down, followed by all\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nMr. Edwin Drood is waiting in Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s own parlour: a dainty\r\nroom, with nothing more directly scholastic in it than a terrestrial\r\nand a celestial globe. These expressive machines imply (to parents and\r\nguardians) that even when Miss Twinkleton retires into the bosom of\r\nprivacy, duty may at any moment compel her to become a sort of\r\nWandering Jewess, scouring the earth and soaring through the skies in\r\nsearch of knowledge for her pupils.\r\n\r\nThe last new maid, who has never seen the young gentleman Miss Rosa is\r\nengaged to, and who is making his acquaintance between the hinges of\r\nthe open door, left open for the purpose, stumbles guiltily down the\r\nkitchen stairs, as a charming little apparition, with its face\r\nconcealed by a little silk apron thrown over its head, glides into the\r\nparlour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! _it is_ so ridiculous!\xe2\x80\x9d says the apparition, stopping and\r\nshrinking. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, Eddy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t what, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t come any nearer, please. It _is_ so absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is absurd, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe whole thing is. It _is_ so absurd to be an engaged orphan and it\r\n_is_ so absurd to have the girls and the servants scuttling about after\r\none, like mice in the wainscot; and it _is_ so absurd to be called\r\nupon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe apparition appears to have a thumb in the corner of its mouth while\r\nmaking this complaint.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou give me an affectionate reception, Pussy, I must say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I will in a minute, Eddy, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t just yet. How are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(very shortly.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am unable to reply that I am much the better for seeing you, Pussy,\r\ninasmuch as I see nothing of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis second remonstrance brings a dark, bright, pouting eye out from a\r\ncorner of the apron; but it swiftly becomes invisible again, as the\r\napparition exclaims: \xe2\x80\x9cO good gracious! you have had half your hair cut\r\noff!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have done better to have had my head cut off, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d says\r\nEdwin, rumpling the hair in question, with a fierce glance at the\r\nlooking-glass, and giving an impatient stamp. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; you needn\xe2\x80\x99t go just yet, Eddy. The girls would all be asking\r\nquestions why you went.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce for all, Rosa, will you uncover that ridiculous little head of\r\nyours and give me a welcome?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe apron is pulled off the childish head, as its wearer replies:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re very welcome, Eddy. There! I\xe2\x80\x99m sure that\xe2\x80\x99s nice. Shake hands.\r\nNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t kiss you, because I\xe2\x80\x99ve got an acidulated drop in my mouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you at all glad to see me, Pussy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, yes, I\xe2\x80\x99m dreadfully glad.\xe2\x80\x94Go and sit down.\xe2\x80\x94Miss Twinkleton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is the custom of that excellent lady when these visits occur, to\r\nappear every three minutes, either in her own person or in that of Mrs.\r\nTisher, and lay an offering on the shrine of Propriety by affecting to\r\nlook for some desiderated article. On the present occasion Miss\r\nTwinkleton, gracefully gliding in and out, says in passing: \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you\r\ndo, Mr. Drood? Very glad indeed to have the pleasure. Pray excuse me.\r\nTweezers. Thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI got the gloves last evening, Eddy, and I like them very much. They\r\nare beauties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s something,\xe2\x80\x9d the affianced replies, half grumbling. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nsmallest encouragement thankfully received. And how did you pass your\r\nbirthday, Pussy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelightfully! Everybody gave me a present. And we had a feast. And we\r\nhad a ball at night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA feast and a ball, eh? These occasions seem to go off tolerably well\r\nwithout me, Pussy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe-lightfully!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, in a quite spontaneous manner, and without\r\nthe least pretence of reserve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah! And what was the feast?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTarts, oranges, jellies, and shrimps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny partners at the ball?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe danced with one another, of course, sir. But some of the girls made\r\ngame to be their brothers. It _was_ so droll!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anybody make game to be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be you? O dear yes!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, laughing with great enjoyment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the first thing done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she did it pretty well,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin rather doubtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it was excellent!\xe2\x80\x94I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t dance with you, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin scarcely seems to see the force of this; begs to know if he may\r\ntake the liberty to ask why?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I was so tired of you,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Rosa. But she quickly adds,\r\nand pleadingly too, seeing displeasure in his face: \xe2\x80\x9cDear Eddy, you\r\nwere just as tired of me, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I say so, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay so! Do you ever say so? No, you only showed it. O, she did it so\r\nwell!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, in a sudden ecstasy with her counterfeit betrothed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt strikes me that she must be a devilish impudent girl,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin\r\nDrood. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so, Pussy, you have passed your last birthday in this old\r\nhouse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa clasps her hands, looks down with a sigh, and shakes\r\nher head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to be sorry, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for the poor old place. Somehow, I feel as if it would miss\r\nme, when I am gone so far away, so young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we had better stop short, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looks up at him with a swift bright look; next moment shakes her\r\nhead, sighs, and looks down again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is to say, is it, Pussy, that we are both resigned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nods her head again, and after a short silence, quaintly bursts out\r\nwith: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know we must be married, and married from here, Eddy, or the\r\npoor girls will be so dreadfully disappointed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the moment there is more of compassion, both for her and for\r\nhimself, in her affianced husband\xe2\x80\x99s face, than there is of love. He\r\nchecks the look, and asks: \xe2\x80\x9cShall I take you out for a walk, Rosa\r\ndear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa dear does not seem at all clear on this point, until her face,\r\nwhich has been comically reflective, brightens. \xe2\x80\x9cO, yes, Eddy; let us\r\ngo for a walk! And I tell you what we\xe2\x80\x99ll do. You shall pretend that you\r\nare engaged to somebody else, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll pretend that I am not engaged to\r\nanybody, and then we shan\xe2\x80\x99t quarrel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think that will prevent our falling out, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it will. Hush! Pretend to look out of window\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Tisher!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThrough a fortuitous concourse of accidents, the matronly Tisher heaves\r\nin sight, says, in rustling through the room like the legendary ghost\r\nof a dowager in silken skirts: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope I see Mr. Drood well; though I\r\nneedn\xe2\x80\x99t ask, if I may judge from his complexion. I trust I disturb no\r\none; but there _was_ a paper-knife\xe2\x80\x94O, thank you, I am sure!\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\ndisappears with her prize.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne other thing you must do, Eddy, to oblige me,\xe2\x80\x9d says Rosebud. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nmoment we get into the street, you must put me outside, and keep close\r\nto the house yourself\xe2\x80\x94squeeze and graze yourself against it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means, Rosa, if you wish it. Might I ask why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! because I don\xe2\x80\x99t want the girls to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a fine day; but would you like me to carry an umbrella up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be foolish, sir. You haven\xe2\x80\x99t got polished leather boots on,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\npouting, with one shoulder raised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps that might escape the notice of the girls, even if they did\r\nsee me,\xe2\x80\x9d remarks Edwin, looking down at his boots with a sudden\r\ndistaste for them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing escapes their notice, sir. And then I know what would happen.\r\nSome of them would begin reflecting on me by saying (for _they_ are\r\nfree) that they never will on any account engage themselves to lovers\r\nwithout polished leather boots. Hark! Miss Twinkleton. I\xe2\x80\x99ll ask for\r\nleave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat discreet lady being indeed heard without, inquiring of nobody in a\r\nblandly conversational tone as she advances: \xe2\x80\x9cEh? Indeed! Are you quite\r\nsure you saw my mother-of-pearl button-holder on the work-table in my\r\nroom?\xe2\x80\x9d is at once solicited for walking leave, and graciously accords\r\nit. And soon the young couple go out of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, taking all\r\nprecautions against the discovery of the so vitally defective boots of\r\nMr. Edwin Drood: precautions, let us hope, effective for the peace of\r\nMrs. Edwin Drood that is to be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich way shall we take, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa replies: \xe2\x80\x9cI want to go to the Lumps-of-Delight shop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Turkish sweetmeat, sir. My gracious me, don\xe2\x80\x99t you understand\r\nanything? Call yourself an Engineer, and not know _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, how should I know it, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I am very fond of them. But O! I forgot what we are to\r\npretend. No, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about them; never mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he is gloomily borne off to the Lumps-of-Delight shop, where Rosa\r\nmakes her purchase, and, after offering some to him (which he rather\r\nindignantly declines), begins to partake of it with great zest:\r\npreviously taking off and rolling up a pair of little pink gloves, like\r\nrose-leaves, and occasionally putting her little pink fingers to her\r\nrosy lips, to cleanse them from the Dust of Delight that comes off the\r\nLumps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, be a good-tempered Eddy, and pretend. And so you are engaged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I am engaged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImmensely tall!\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa being short.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust be gawky, I should think,\xe2\x80\x9d is Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s quiet commentary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon; not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d contradiction rising in him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is termed a fine woman; a splendid woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBig nose, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d is the quiet commentary again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a little one, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d is the quick reply, (Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s being a\r\nlittle one.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong pale nose, with a red knob in the middle. I know the sort of\r\nnose,\xe2\x80\x9d says Rosa, with a satisfied nod, and tranquilly enjoying the\r\nLumps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ know the sort of nose, Rosa,\xe2\x80\x9d with some warmth; \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s nothing of the kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a pale nose, Eddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d Determined not to assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA red nose? O! I don\xe2\x80\x99t like red noses. However; to be sure she can\r\nalways powder it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would scorn to powder it,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, becoming heated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould she? What a stupid thing she must be! Is she stupid in\r\neverything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; in nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a pause, in which the whimsically wicked face has not been\r\nunobservant of him, Rosa says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this most sensible of creatures likes the idea of being carried\r\noff to Egypt; does she, Eddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. She takes a sensible interest in triumphs of engineering skill:\r\nespecially when they are to change the whole condition of an\r\nundeveloped country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLor!\xe2\x80\x9d says Rosa, shrugging her shoulders, with a little laugh of\r\nwonder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you object,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin inquires, with a majestic turn of his eyes\r\ndownward upon the fairy figure: \xe2\x80\x9cdo you object, Rosa, to her feeling\r\nthat interest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObject? my dear Eddy! But really, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she hate boilers and\r\nthings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can answer for her not being so idiotic as to hate Boilers,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nreturns with angry emphasis; \xe2\x80\x9cthough I cannot answer for her views\r\nabout Things; really not understanding what Things are meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t she hate Arabs, and Turks, and Fellahs, and people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d Very firmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least she _must_ hate the Pyramids? Come, Eddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should she be such a little\xe2\x80\x94tall, I mean\xe2\x80\x94goose, as to hate the\r\nPyramids, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you should hear Miss Twinkleton,\xe2\x80\x9d often nodding her head, and much\r\nenjoying the Lumps, \xe2\x80\x9cbore about them, and then you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask.\r\nTiresome old burying-grounds! Isises, and Ibises, and Cheopses, and\r\nPharaohses; who cares about them? And then there was Belzoni, or\r\nsomebody, dragged out by the legs, half-choked with bats and dust. All\r\nthe girls say: Serve him right, and hope it hurt him, and wish he had\r\nbeen quite choked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two youthful figures, side by side, but not now arm-in-arm, wander\r\ndiscontentedly about the old Close; and each sometimes stops and slowly\r\nimprints a deeper footstep in the fallen leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, after a lengthy silence. \xe2\x80\x9cAccording to custom. We\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t get on, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa tosses her head, and says she don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty sentiment, Rosa, considering.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsidering what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I say what, you\xe2\x80\x99ll go wrong again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You\xe2\x80\x99ll_ go wrong, you mean, Eddy. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be ungenerous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUngenerous! I like that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ like that, and so I tell you plainly,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa pouts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Rosa, I put it to you. Who disparaged my profession, my\r\ndestination\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not going to be buried in the Pyramids, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ninterrupts, arching her delicate eyebrows. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never said you were. If\r\nyou are, why haven\xe2\x80\x99t you mentioned it to me? I can\xe2\x80\x99t find out your\r\nplans by instinct.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Rosa, you know very well what I mean, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, why did you begin with your detestable red-nosed\r\ngiantesses? And she would, she would, she would, she would, she WOULD\r\npowder it!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, in a little burst of comical contradictory\r\nspleen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomehow or other, I never can come right in these discussions,\xe2\x80\x9d says\r\nEdwin, sighing and becoming resigned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is it possible, sir, that you ever can come right when you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nalways wrong? And as to Belzoni, I suppose he\xe2\x80\x99s dead;\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I hope\r\nhe is\xe2\x80\x94and how can his legs or his chokes concern you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nearly time for your return, Rosa. We have not had a very happy\r\nwalk, have we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA happy walk? A detestably unhappy walk, sir. If I go up-stairs the\r\nmoment I get in and cry till I can\xe2\x80\x99t take my dancing lesson, you are\r\nresponsible, mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us be friends, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, shaking her head and bursting into real tears, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwish we _could_ be friends! It\xe2\x80\x99s because we can\xe2\x80\x99t be friends, that we\r\ntry one another so. I am a young little thing, Eddy, to have an old\r\nheartache; but I really, really have, sometimes. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be angry. I know\r\nyou have one yourself too often. We should both of us have done better,\r\nif What is to be had been left What might have been. I am quite a\r\nlittle serious thing now, and not teasing you. Let each of us forbear,\r\nthis one time, on our own account, and on the other\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDisarmed by this glimpse of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s nature in the spoilt child,\r\nthough for an instant disposed to resent it as seeming to involve the\r\nenforced infliction of himself upon her, Edwin Drood stands watching\r\nher as she childishly cries and sobs, with both hands to the\r\nhandkerchief at her eyes, and then\xe2\x80\x94she becoming more composed, and\r\nindeed beginning in her young inconstancy to laugh at herself for\r\nhaving been so moved\xe2\x80\x94leads her to a seat hard by, under the elm-trees.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Under the trees]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne clear word of understanding, Pussy dear. I am not clever out of my\r\nown line\xe2\x80\x94now I come to think of it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I am particularly\r\nclever in it\xe2\x80\x94but I want to do right. There is not\xe2\x80\x94there may be\xe2\x80\x94I really\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see my way to what I want to say, but I must say it before we\r\npart\xe2\x80\x94there is not any other young\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, Eddy! It\xe2\x80\x99s generous of you to ask me; but no, no, no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey have come very near to the Cathedral windows, and at this moment\r\nthe organ and the choir sound out sublimely. As they sit listening to\r\nthe solemn swell, the confidence of last night rises in young Edwin\r\nDrood\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and he thinks how unlike this music is to that\r\ndiscordance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy I can distinguish Jack\xe2\x80\x99s voice,\xe2\x80\x9d is his remark in a low tone\r\nin connection with the train of thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake me back at once, please,\xe2\x80\x9d urges his Affianced, quickly laying her\r\nlight hand upon his wrist. \xe2\x80\x9cThey will all be coming out directly; let\r\nus get away. O, what a resounding chord! But don\xe2\x80\x99t let us stop to\r\nlisten to it; let us get away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer hurry is over as soon as they have passed out of the Close. They go\r\narm-in-arm now, gravely and deliberately enough, along the old\r\nHigh-street, to the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. At the gate, the street being within\r\nsight empty, Edwin bends down his face to Rosebud\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nShe remonstrates, laughing, and is a childish schoolgirl again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEddy, no! I\xe2\x80\x99m too sticky to be kissed. But give me your hand, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nblow a kiss into that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe does so. She breathes a light breath into it and asks, retaining it\r\nand looking into it:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow say, what do you see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I thought you Egyptian boys could look into a hand and see all\r\nsorts of phantoms. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you see a happy Future?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor certain, neither of them sees a happy Present, as the gate opens\r\nand closes, and one goes in, and the other goes away.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\nMR. SAPSEA\r\n\r\n\r\nAccepting the Jackass as the type of self-sufficient stupidity and\r\nconceit\xe2\x80\x94a custom, perhaps, like some few other customs, more\r\nconventional than fair\xe2\x80\x94then the purest jackass in Cloisterham is Mr.\r\nThomas Sapsea, Auctioneer.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea \xe2\x80\x9cdresses at\xe2\x80\x9d the Dean; has been bowed to for the Dean, in\r\nmistake; has even been spoken to in the street as My Lord, under the\r\nimpression that he was the Bishop come down unexpectedly, without his\r\nchaplain. Mr. Sapsea is very proud of this, and of his voice, and of\r\nhis style. He has even (in selling landed property) tried the\r\nexperiment of slightly intoning in his pulpit, to make himself more\r\nlike what he takes to be the genuine ecclesiastical article. So, in\r\nending a Sale by Public Auction, Mr. Sapsea finishes off with an air of\r\nbestowing a benediction on the assembled brokers, which leaves the real\r\nDean\xe2\x80\x94a modest and worthy gentleman\xe2\x80\x94far behind.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea has many admirers; indeed, the proposition is carried by a\r\nlarge local majority, even including non-believers in his wisdom, that\r\nhe is a credit to Cloisterham. He possesses the great qualities of\r\nbeing portentous and dull, and of having a roll in his speech, and\r\nanother roll in his gait; not to mention a certain gravely flowing\r\naction with his hands, as if he were presently going to Confirm the\r\nindividual with whom he holds discourse. Much nearer sixty years of age\r\nthan fifty, with a flowing outline of stomach, and horizontal creases\r\nin his waistcoat; reputed to be rich; voting at elections in the\r\nstrictly respectable interest; morally satisfied that nothing but he\r\nhimself has grown since he was a baby; how can dunder-headed Mr. Sapsea\r\nbe otherwise than a credit to Cloisterham, and society?\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s premises are in the High-street, over against the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99\r\nHouse. They are of about the period of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, irregularly\r\nmodernised here and there, as steadily deteriorating generations found,\r\nmore and more, that they preferred air and light to Fever and the\r\nPlague. Over the doorway is a wooden effigy, about half life-size,\r\nrepresenting Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s father, in a curly wig and toga, in the act\r\nof selling. The chastity of the idea, and the natural appearance of the\r\nlittle finger, hammer, and pulpit, have been much admired.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea sits in his dull ground-floor sitting-room, giving first on\r\nhis paved back yard; and then on his railed-off garden. Mr. Sapsea has\r\na bottle of port wine on a table before the fire\xe2\x80\x94the fire is an early\r\nluxury, but pleasant on the cool, chilly autumn evening\xe2\x80\x94and is\r\ncharacteristically attended by his portrait, his eight-day clock, and\r\nhis weather-glass. Characteristically, because he would uphold himself\r\nagainst mankind, his weather-glass against weather, and his clock\r\nagainst time.\r\n\r\nBy Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s side on the table are a writing-desk and writing\r\nmaterials. Glancing at a scrap of manuscript, Mr. Sapsea reads it to\r\nhimself with a lofty air, and then, slowly pacing the room with his\r\nthumbs in the arm-holes of his waistcoat, repeats it from memory: so\r\ninternally, though with much dignity, that the word \xe2\x80\x9cEthelinda\xe2\x80\x9d is\r\nalone audible.\r\n\r\nThere are three clean wineglasses in a tray on the table. His\r\nserving-maid entering, and announcing \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Jasper is come, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nSapsea waves \xe2\x80\x9cAdmit him,\xe2\x80\x9d and draws two wineglasses from the rank, as\r\nbeing claimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad to see you, sir. I congratulate myself on having the honour of\r\nreceiving you here for the first time.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea does the honours of\r\nhis house in this wise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very good. The honour is mine and the self-congratulation is\r\nmine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are pleased to say so, sir. But I do assure you that it is a\r\nsatisfaction to me to receive you in my humble home. And that is what I\r\nwould not say to everybody.\xe2\x80\x9d Ineffable loftiness on Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s part\r\naccompanies these words, as leaving the sentence to be understood: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nwill not easily believe that your society can be a satisfaction to a\r\nman like myself; nevertheless, it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have for some time desired to know you, Mr. Sapsea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I, sir, have long known you by reputation as a man of taste. Let\r\nme fill your glass. I will give you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Sapsea, filling his\r\nown:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen the French come over,\r\nMay we meet them at Dover!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThis was a patriotic toast in Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s infancy, and he is therefore\r\nfully convinced of its being appropriate to any subsequent era.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can scarcely be ignorant, Mr. Sapsea,\xe2\x80\x9d observes Jasper, watching\r\nthe auctioneer with a smile as the latter stretches out his legs before\r\nthe fire, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you know the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d is the chuckling reply, \xe2\x80\x9cI think I know something of it;\r\nsomething of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour reputation for that knowledge has always interested and surprised\r\nme, and made me wish to know you. For Cloisterham is a little place.\r\nCooped up in it myself, I know nothing beyond it, and feel it to be a\r\nvery little place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have not gone to foreign countries, young man,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea\r\nbegins, and then stops:\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cYou will excuse me calling you young man, Mr.\r\nJasper? You are much my junior.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have not gone to foreign countries, young man, foreign countries\r\nhave come to me. They have come to me in the way of business, and I\r\nhave improved upon my opportunities. Put it that I take an inventory,\r\nor make a catalogue. I see a French clock. I never saw him before, in\r\nmy life, but I instantly lay my finger on him and say \xe2\x80\x98Paris!\xe2\x80\x99 I see\r\nsome cups and saucers of Chinese make, equally strangers to me\r\npersonally: I put my finger on them, then and there, and I say \xe2\x80\x98Pekin,\r\nNankin, and Canton.\xe2\x80\x99 It is the same with Japan, with Egypt, and with\r\nbamboo and sandalwood from the East Indies; I put my finger on them\r\nall. I have put my finger on the North Pole before now, and said \xe2\x80\x98Spear\r\nof Esquimaux make, for half a pint of pale sherry!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally? A very remarkable way, Mr. Sapsea, of acquiring a knowledge of\r\nmen and things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mention it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea rejoins, with unspeakable complacency,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbecause, as I say, it don\xe2\x80\x99t do to boast of what you are; but show how\r\nyou came to be it, and then you prove it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost interesting. We were to speak of the late Mrs. Sapsea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea fills both glasses, and takes the decanter\r\ninto safe keeping again. \xe2\x80\x9cBefore I consult your opinion as a man of\r\ntaste on this little trifle\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94holding it up\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhich is _but_ a trifle,\r\nand still has required some thought, sir, some little fever of the\r\nbrow, I ought perhaps to describe the character of the late Mrs.\r\nSapsea, now dead three quarters of a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, in the act of yawning behind his wineglass, puts down that\r\nscreen and calls up a look of interest. It is a little impaired in its\r\nexpressiveness by his having a shut-up gape still to dispose of, with\r\nwatering eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a dozen years ago, or so,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea proceeds, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I had\r\nenlarged my mind up to\xe2\x80\x94I will not say to what it now is, for that might\r\nseem to aim at too much, but up to the pitch of wanting another mind to\r\nbe absorbed in it\xe2\x80\x94I cast my eye about me for a nuptial partner.\r\nBecause, as I say, it is not good for man to be alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper appears to commit this original idea to memory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Brobity at that time kept, I will not call it the rival\r\nestablishment to the establishment at the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House opposite, but I\r\nwill call it the other parallel establishment down town. The world did\r\nhave it that she showed a passion for attending my sales, when they\r\ntook place on half holidays, or in vacation time. The world did put it\r\nabout, that she admired my style. The world did notice that as time\r\nflowed by, my style became traceable in the dictation-exercises of Miss\r\nBrobity\xe2\x80\x99s pupils. Young man, a whisper even sprang up in obscure\r\nmalignity, that one ignorant and besotted Churl (a parent) so committed\r\nhimself as to object to it by name. But I do not believe this. For is\r\nit likely that any human creature in his right senses would so lay\r\nhimself open to be pointed at, by what I call the finger of scorn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper shakes his head. Not in the least likely. Mr. Sapsea, in a\r\ngrandiloquent state of absence of mind, seems to refill his visitor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nglass, which is full already; and does really refill his own, which is\r\nempty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Brobity\xe2\x80\x99s Being, young man, was deeply imbued with homage to\r\nMind. She revered Mind, when launched, or, as I say, precipitated, on\r\nan extensive knowledge of the world. When I made my proposal, she did\r\nme the honour to be so overshadowed with a species of Awe, as to be\r\nable to articulate only the two words, \xe2\x80\x98O Thou!\xe2\x80\x99 meaning myself. Her\r\nlimpid blue eyes were fixed upon me, her semi-transparent hands were\r\nclasped together, pallor overspread her aquiline features, and, though\r\nencouraged to proceed, she never did proceed a word further. I disposed\r\nof the parallel establishment by private contract, and we became as\r\nnearly one as could be expected under the circumstances. But she never\r\ncould, and she never did, find a phrase satisfactory to her\r\nperhaps-too-favourable estimate of my intellect. To the very last\r\n(feeble action of liver), she addressed me in the same unfinished\r\nterms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper has closed his eyes as the auctioneer has deepened his\r\nvoice. He now abruptly opens them, and says, in unison with the\r\ndeepened voice \xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94rather as if stopping himself on the extreme verge\r\nof adding\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been since,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Sapsea, with his legs stretched out, and\r\nsolemnly enjoying himself with the wine and the fire, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat you behold\r\nme; I have been since a solitary mourner; I have been since, as I say,\r\nwasting my evening conversation on the desert air. I will not say that\r\nI have reproached myself; but there have been times when I have asked\r\nmyself the question: What if her husband had been nearer on a level\r\nwith her? If she had not had to look up quite so high, what might the\r\nstimulating action have been upon the liver?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper says, with an appearance of having fallen into dreadfully\r\nlow spirits, that he \xe2\x80\x9csupposes it was to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can only suppose so, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea coincides. \xe2\x80\x9cAs I say, Man\r\nproposes, Heaven disposes. It may or may not be putting the same\r\nthought in another form; but that is the way I put it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper murmurs assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Mr. Jasper,\xe2\x80\x9d resumes the auctioneer, producing his scrap of\r\nmanuscript, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s monument having had full time to settle and\r\ndry, let me take your opinion, as a man of taste, on the inscription I\r\nhave (as I before remarked, not without some little fever of the brow)\r\ndrawn out for it. Take it in your own hand. The setting out of the\r\nlines requires to be followed with the eye, as well as the contents\r\nwith the mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper complying, sees and reads as follows:\r\n\r\nETHELINDA,\r\nReverential Wife of\r\nMR.
