THOMAS SAPSEA,\r\nAUCTIONEER, VALUER, ESTATE AGENT, &c.,\r\nOF THIS CITY.\r\nWhose Knowledge of the World,\r\nThough somewhat extensive,\r\nNever brought him acquainted with\r\nA SPIRIT\r\nMore capable of\r\nLOOKING UP TO HIM.\r\nSTRANGER, PAUSE\r\nAnd ask thyself the Question,\r\nCANST THOU DO LIKEWISE?\r\nIf Not,\r\nWITH A BLUSH RETIRE.\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea having risen and stationed himself with his back to the\r\nfire, for the purpose of observing the effect of these lines on the\r\ncountenance of a man of taste, consequently has his face towards the\r\ndoor, when his serving-maid, again appearing, announces, \xe2\x80\x9cDurdles is\r\ncome, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d He promptly draws forth and fills the third wineglass, as\r\nbeing now claimed, and replies, \xe2\x80\x9cShow Durdles in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmirable!\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Mr. Jasper, handing back the paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou approve, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible not to approve. Striking, characteristic, and complete.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe auctioneer inclines his head, as one accepting his due and giving a\r\nreceipt; and invites the entering Durdles to take off that glass of\r\nwine (handing the same), for it will warm him.\r\n\r\nDurdles is a stonemason; chiefly in the gravestone, tomb, and monument\r\nway, and wholly of their colour from head to foot. No man is better\r\nknown in Cloisterham. He is the chartered libertine of the place. Fame\r\ntrumpets him a wonderful workman\xe2\x80\x94which, for aught that anybody knows,\r\nhe may be (as he never works); and a wonderful sot\xe2\x80\x94which everybody\r\nknows he is. With the Cathedral crypt he is better acquainted than any\r\nliving authority; it may even be than any dead one. It is said that the\r\nintimacy of this acquaintance began in his habitually resorting to that\r\nsecret place, to lock-out the Cloisterham boy-populace, and sleep off\r\nfumes of liquor: he having ready access to the Cathedral, as contractor\r\nfor rough repairs. Be this as it may, he does know much about it, and,\r\nin the demolition of impedimental fragments of wall, buttress, and\r\npavement, has seen strange sights. He often speaks of himself in the\r\nthird person; perhaps, being a little misty as to his own identity,\r\nwhen he narrates; perhaps impartially adopting the Cloisterham\r\nnomenclature in reference to a character of acknowledged distinction.\r\nThus he will say, touching his strange sights: \xe2\x80\x9cDurdles come upon the\r\nold chap,\xe2\x80\x9d in reference to a buried magnate of ancient time and high\r\ndegree, \xe2\x80\x9cby striking right into the coffin with his pick. The old chap\r\ngave Durdles a look with his open eyes, as much as to say, \xe2\x80\x98Is your\r\nname Durdles? Why, my man, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been waiting for you a devil of a\r\ntime!\xe2\x80\x99 And then he turned to powder.\xe2\x80\x9d With a two-foot rule always in\r\nhis pocket, and a mason\xe2\x80\x99s hammer all but always in his hand, Durdles\r\ngoes continually sounding and tapping all about and about the\r\nCathedral; and whenever he says to Tope: \xe2\x80\x9cTope, here\xe2\x80\x99s another old \xe2\x80\x99un\r\nin here!\xe2\x80\x9d Tope announces it to the Dean as an established discovery.\r\n\r\nIn a suit of coarse flannel with horn buttons, a yellow neckerchief\r\nwith draggled ends, an old hat more russet-coloured than black, and\r\nlaced boots of the hue of his stony calling, Durdles leads a hazy,\r\ngipsy sort of life, carrying his dinner about with him in a small\r\nbundle, and sitting on all manner of tombstones to dine. This dinner of\r\nDurdles\xe2\x80\x99s has become quite a Cloisterham institution: not only because\r\nof his never appearing in public without it, but because of its having\r\nbeen, on certain renowned occasions, taken into custody along with\r\nDurdles (as drunk and incapable), and exhibited before the Bench of\r\njustices at the townhall. These occasions, however, have been few and\r\nfar apart: Durdles being as seldom drunk as sober. For the rest, he is\r\nan old bachelor, and he lives in a little antiquated hole of a house\r\nthat was never finished: supposed to be built, so far, of stones stolen\r\nfrom the city wall. To this abode there is an approach, ankle-deep in\r\nstone chips, resembling a petrified grove of tombstones, urns,\r\ndraperies, and broken columns, in all stages of sculpture. Herein two\r\njourneymen incessantly chip, while other two journeymen, who face each\r\nother, incessantly saw stone; dipping as regularly in and out of their\r\nsheltering sentry-boxes, as if they were mechanical figures\r\nemblematical of Time and Death.\r\n\r\nTo Durdles, when he had consumed his glass of port, Mr. Sapsea intrusts\r\nthat precious effort of his Muse. Durdles unfeelingly takes out his\r\ntwo-foot rule, and measures the lines calmly, alloying them with\r\nstone-grit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is for the monument, is it, Mr. Sapsea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Inscription. Yes.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea waits for its effect on a common\r\nmind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll come in to a eighth of a inch,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cYour servant, Mr.\r\nJasper. Hope I see you well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you Durdles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a touch of the Tombatism on me, Mr. Jasper, but that I must\r\nexpect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean the Rheumatism,\xe2\x80\x9d says Sapsea, in a sharp tone. (He is nettled\r\nby having his composition so mechanically received.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t. I mean, Mr. Sapsea, the Tombatism. It\xe2\x80\x99s another sort from\r\nRheumatism. Mr. Jasper knows what Durdles means. You get among them\r\nTombs afore it\xe2\x80\x99s well light on a winter morning, and keep on, as the\r\nCatechism says, a-walking in the same all the days of your life, and\r\n_you\xe2\x80\x99ll_ know what Durdles means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a bitter cold place,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper assents, with an antipathetic\r\nshiver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if it\xe2\x80\x99s bitter cold for you, up in the chancel, with a lot of live\r\nbreath smoking out about you, what the bitterness is to Durdles, down\r\nin the crypt among the earthy damps there, and the dead breath of the\r\nold \xe2\x80\x99uns,\xe2\x80\x9d returns that individual, \xe2\x80\x9cDurdles leaves you to judge.\xe2\x80\x94Is\r\nthis to be put in hand at once, Mr. Sapsea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea, with an Author\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety to rush into publication, replies\r\nthat it cannot be out of hand too soon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better let me have the key then,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, man, it is not to be put inside the monument!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDurdles knows where it\xe2\x80\x99s to be put, Mr. Sapsea; no man better. Ask\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ere a man in Cloisterham whether Durdles knows his work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea rises, takes a key from a drawer, unlocks an iron safe let\r\ninto the wall, and takes from it another key.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen Durdles puts a touch or a finish upon his work, no matter where,\r\ninside or outside, Durdles likes to look at his work all round, and see\r\nthat his work is a-doing him credit,\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles explains, doggedly.\r\n\r\nThe key proffered him by the bereaved widower being a large one, he\r\nslips his two-foot rule into a side-pocket of his flannel trousers made\r\nfor it, and deliberately opens his flannel coat, and opens the mouth of\r\na large breast-pocket within it before taking the key to place it in\r\nthat repository.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Durdles!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaims Jasper, looking on amused, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are undermined\r\nwith pockets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I carries weight in \xe2\x80\x99em too, Mr. Jasper. Feel those!\xe2\x80\x9d producing\r\ntwo other large keys.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHand me Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s likewise. Surely this is the heaviest of the\r\nthree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find \xe2\x80\x99em much of a muchness, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cThey all\r\nbelong to monuments. They all open Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s work. Durdles keeps the\r\nkeys of his work mostly. Not that they\xe2\x80\x99re much used.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the bye,\xe2\x80\x9d it comes into Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s mind to say, as he idly examines\r\nthe keys, \xe2\x80\x9cI have been going to ask you, many a day, and have always\r\nforgotten. You know they sometimes call you Stony Durdles, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCloisterham knows me as Durdles, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am aware of that, of course. But the boys sometimes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! if you mind them young imps of boys\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles gruffly interrupts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind them any more than you do. But there was a discussion the\r\nother day among the Choir, whether Stony stood for Tony;\xe2\x80\x9d clinking one\r\nkey against another.\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cTake care of the wards, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr whether Stony stood for Stephen;\xe2\x80\x9d clinking with a change of keys.\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t make a pitch pipe of \xe2\x80\x99em, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr whether the name comes from your trade. How stands the fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper weighs the three keys in his hand, lifts his head from his\r\nidly stooping attitude over the fire, and delivers the keys to Durdles\r\nwith an ingenuous and friendly face.\r\n\r\nBut the stony one is a gruff one likewise, and that hazy state of his\r\nis always an uncertain state, highly conscious of its dignity, and\r\nprone to take offence. He drops his two keys back into his pocket one\r\nby one, and buttons them up; he takes his dinner-bundle from the\r\nchair-back on which he hung it when he came in; he distributes the\r\nweight he carries, by tying the third key up in it, as though he were\r\nan Ostrich, and liked to dine off cold iron; and he gets out of the\r\nroom, deigning no word of answer.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea then proposes a hit at backgammon, which, seasoned with his\r\nown improving conversation, and terminating in a supper of cold roast\r\nbeef and salad, beguiles the golden evening until pretty late. Mr.\r\nSapsea\xe2\x80\x99s wisdom being, in its delivery to mortals, rather of the\r\ndiffuse than the epigrammatic order, is by no means expended even then;\r\nbut his visitor intimates that he will come back for more of the\r\nprecious commodity on future occasions, and Mr. Sapsea lets him off for\r\nthe present, to ponder on the instalment he carries away.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\nMR. DURDLES AND FRIEND\r\n\r\n\r\nJohn Jasper, on his way home through the Close, is brought to a\r\nstand-still by the spectacle of Stony Durdles, dinner-bundle and all,\r\nleaning his back against the iron railing of the burial-ground\r\nenclosing it from the old cloister-arches; and a hideous small boy in\r\nrags flinging stones at him as a well-defined mark in the moonlight.\r\nSometimes the stones hit him, and sometimes they miss him, but Durdles\r\nseems indifferent to either fortune. The hideous small boy, on the\r\ncontrary, whenever he hits Durdles, blows a whistle of triumph through\r\na jagged gap, convenient for the purpose, in the front of his mouth,\r\nwhere half his teeth are wanting; and whenever he misses him, yelps out\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMulled agin!\xe2\x80\x9d and tries to atone for the failure by taking a more\r\ncorrect and vicious aim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing to the man?\xe2\x80\x9d demands Jasper, stepping out into the\r\nmoonlight from the shade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaking a cock-shy of him,\xe2\x80\x9d replies the hideous small boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me those stones in your hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give \xe2\x80\x99em you down your throat, if you come a-ketching hold\r\nof me,\xe2\x80\x9d says the small boy, shaking himself loose, and backing. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsmash your eye, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t look out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBaby-Devil that you are, what has the man done to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t go home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe gives me a \xe2\x80\x99apenny to pelt him home if I ketches him out too late,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsays the boy. And then chants, like a little savage, half stumbling and\r\nhalf dancing among the rags and laces of his dilapidated boots:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWiddy widdy wen!\r\nI\xe2\x80\x94ket\xe2\x80\x94ches\xe2\x80\x94Im\xe2\x80\x94out\xe2\x80\x94ar\xe2\x80\x94ter\xe2\x80\x94ten,\r\nWiddy widdy wy!\r\nThen\xe2\x80\x94E\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94go\xe2\x80\x94then\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94shy\xe2\x80\x94\r\nWiddy Widdy Wake-cock warning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94with a comprehensive sweep on the last word, and one more delivery at\r\nDurdles.\r\n\r\nThis would seem to be a poetical note of preparation, agreed upon, as a\r\ncaution to Durdles to stand clear if he can, or to betake himself\r\nhomeward.\r\n\r\nJohn Jasper invites the boy with a beck of his head to follow him\r\n(feeling it hopeless to drag him, or coax him), and crosses to the iron\r\nrailing where the Stony (and stoned) One is profoundly meditating.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know this thing, this child?\xe2\x80\x9d asks Jasper, at a loss for a word\r\nthat will define this thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeputy,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles, with a nod.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that its\xe2\x80\x94his\xe2\x80\x94name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeputy,\xe2\x80\x9d assents Durdles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m man-servant up at the Travellers\xe2\x80\x99 Twopenny in Gas Works Garding,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthis thing explains. \xe2\x80\x9cAll us man-servants at Travellers\xe2\x80\x99 Lodgings is\r\nnamed Deputy. When we\xe2\x80\x99re chock full and the Travellers is all a-bed I\r\ncome out for my \xe2\x80\x99elth.\xe2\x80\x9d Then withdrawing into the road, and taking aim,\r\nhe resumes:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWiddy widdy wen!\r\nI\xe2\x80\x94ket\xe2\x80\x94ches\xe2\x80\x94Im\xe2\x80\x94out\xe2\x80\x94ar\xe2\x80\x94ter\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold your hand,\xe2\x80\x9d cries Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cand don\xe2\x80\x99t throw while I stand so near\r\nhim, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll kill you! Come, Durdles; let me walk home with you\r\nto-night. Shall I carry your bundle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot on any account,\xe2\x80\x9d replies Durdles, adjusting it. \xe2\x80\x9cDurdles was\r\nmaking his reflections here when you come up, sir, surrounded by his\r\nworks, like a poplar Author.\xe2\x80\x94Your own brother-in-law;\xe2\x80\x9d introducing a\r\nsarcophagus within the railing, white and cold in the moonlight. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs.\r\nSapsea;\xe2\x80\x9d introducing the monument of that devoted wife. \xe2\x80\x9cLate\r\nIncumbent;\xe2\x80\x9d introducing the Reverend Gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s broken column.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeparted Assessed Taxes;\xe2\x80\x9d introducing a vase and towel, standing on\r\nwhat might represent the cake of soap. \xe2\x80\x9cFormer pastrycook and\r\nMuffin-maker, much respected;\xe2\x80\x9d introducing gravestone. \xe2\x80\x9cAll safe and\r\nsound here, sir, and all Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s work. Of the common folk, that is\r\nmerely bundled up in turf and brambles, the less said the better. A\r\npoor lot, soon forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis creature, Deputy, is behind us,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, looking back. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nhe to follow us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe relations between Durdles and Deputy are of a capricious kind; for,\r\non Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s turning himself about with the slow gravity of beery\r\nsuddenness, Deputy makes a pretty wide circuit into the road and stands\r\non the defensive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never cried Widdy Warning before you begun to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d says\r\nDurdles, unexpectedly reminded of, or imagining, an injury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie, I did,\xe2\x80\x9d says Deputy, in his only form of polite\r\ncontradiction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOwn brother, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d observes Durdles, turning himself about again, and\r\nas unexpectedly forgetting his offence as he had recalled or conceived\r\nit; \xe2\x80\x9cown brother to Peter the Wild Boy! But I gave him an object in\r\nlife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt which he takes aim?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper suggests.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Durdles, quite satisfied; \xe2\x80\x9cat which he takes\r\naim. I took him in hand and gave him an object. What was he before? A\r\ndestroyer. What work did he do? Nothing but destruction. What did he\r\nearn by it? Short terms in Cloisterham jail. Not a person, not a piece\r\nof property, not a winder, not a horse, nor a dog, nor a cat, nor a\r\nbird, nor a fowl, nor a pig, but what he stoned, for want of an\r\nenlightened object. I put that enlightened object before him, and now\r\nhe can turn his honest halfpenny by the three penn\xe2\x80\x99orth a week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder he has no competitors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has plenty, Mr. Jasper, but he stones \xe2\x80\x99em all away. Now, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what this scheme of mine comes to,\xe2\x80\x9d pursues Durdles, considering\r\nabout it with the same sodden gravity; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you may\r\nprecisely call it. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t a sort of a\xe2\x80\x94scheme of a\xe2\x80\x94National\r\nEducation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say not,\xe2\x80\x9d replies Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say not,\xe2\x80\x9d assents Durdles; \xe2\x80\x9cthen we won\xe2\x80\x99t try to give it a\r\nname.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe still keeps behind us,\xe2\x80\x9d repeats Jasper, looking over his shoulder;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cis he to follow us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t help going round by the Travellers\xe2\x80\x99 Twopenny, if we go the\r\nshort way, which is the back way,\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles answers, \xe2\x80\x9cand we\xe2\x80\x99ll drop him\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they go on; Deputy, as a rear rank one, taking open order, and\r\ninvading the silence of the hour and place by stoning every wall, post,\r\npillar, and other inanimate object, by the deserted way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there anything new down in the crypt, Durdles?\xe2\x80\x9d asks John Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything old, I think you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d growls Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t a spot for\r\nnovelty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny new discovery on your part, I meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a old \xe2\x80\x99un under the seventh pillar on the left as you go down\r\nthe broken steps of the little underground chapel as formerly was; I\r\nmake him out (so fur as I\xe2\x80\x99ve made him out yet) to be one of them old\r\n\xe2\x80\x99uns with a crook. To judge from the size of the passages in the walls,\r\nand of the steps and doors, by which they come and went, them crooks\r\nmust have been a good deal in the way of the old \xe2\x80\x99uns! Two on \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nmeeting promiscuous must have hitched one another by the mitre pretty\r\noften, I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout any endeavour to correct the literality of this opinion, Jasper\r\nsurveys his companion\xe2\x80\x94covered from head to foot with old mortar, lime,\r\nand stone grit\xe2\x80\x94as though he, Jasper, were getting imbued with a\r\nromantic interest in his weird life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYours is a curious existence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout furnishing the least clue to the question, whether he receives\r\nthis as a compliment or as quite the reverse, Durdles gruffly answers:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYours is another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! inasmuch as my lot is cast in the same old earthy, chilly,\r\nnever-changing place, Yes. But there is much more mystery and interest\r\nin your connection with the Cathedral than in mine. Indeed, I am\r\nbeginning to have some idea of asking you to take me on as a sort of\r\nstudent, or free \xe2\x80\x99prentice, under you, and to let me go about with you\r\nsometimes, and see some of these odd nooks in which you pass your\r\ndays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Stony One replies, in a general way, \xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Everybody knows\r\nwhere to find Durdles, when he\xe2\x80\x99s wanted.\xe2\x80\x9d Which, if not strictly true,\r\nis approximately so, if taken to express that Durdles may always be\r\nfound in a state of vagabondage somewhere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I dwell upon most,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, pursuing his subject of romantic\r\ninterest, \xe2\x80\x9cis the remarkable accuracy with which you would seem to find\r\nout where people are buried.\xe2\x80\x94What is the matter? That bundle is in your\r\nway; let me hold it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDurdles has stopped and backed a little (Deputy, attentive to all his\r\nmovements, immediately skirmishing into the road), and was looking\r\nabout for some ledge or corner to place his bundle on, when thus\r\nrelieved of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust you give me my hammer out of that,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll show\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClink, clink. And his hammer is handed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, lookee here. You pitch your note, don\xe2\x80\x99t you, Mr. Jasper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I sound for mine. I take my hammer, and I tap.\xe2\x80\x9d (Here he strikes\r\nthe pavement, and the attentive Deputy skirmishes at a rather wider\r\nrange, as supposing that his head may be in requisition.) \xe2\x80\x9cI tap, tap,\r\ntap. Solid! I go on tapping. Solid still! Tap again. Holloa! Hollow!\r\nTap again, persevering. Solid in hollow! Tap, tap, tap, to try it\r\nbetter. Solid in hollow; and inside solid, hollow again! There you are!\r\nOld \xe2\x80\x99un crumbled away in stone coffin, in vault!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAstonishing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have even done this,\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles, drawing out his two-foot rule\r\n(Deputy meanwhile skirmishing nearer, as suspecting that Treasure may\r\nbe about to be discovered, which may somehow lead to his own\r\nenrichment, and the delicious treat of the discoverers being hanged by\r\nthe neck, on his evidence, until they are dead). \xe2\x80\x9cSay that hammer of\r\nmine\xe2\x80\x99s a wall\xe2\x80\x94my work. Two; four; and two is six,\xe2\x80\x9d measuring on the\r\npavement. \xe2\x80\x9cSix foot inside that wall is Mrs. Sapsea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot really Mrs. Sapsea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay Mrs. Sapsea. Her wall\xe2\x80\x99s thicker, but say Mrs. Sapsea. Durdles\r\ntaps, that wall represented by that hammer, and says, after good\r\nsounding: \xe2\x80\x98Something betwixt us!\xe2\x80\x99 Sure enough, some rubbish has been\r\nleft in that same six-foot space by Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s men!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper opines that such accuracy \xe2\x80\x9cis a gift.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have it at a gift,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Durdles, by no means receiving\r\nthe observation in good part. \xe2\x80\x9cI worked it out for myself. Durdles\r\ncomes by _his_ knowledge through grubbing deep for it, and having it up\r\nby the roots when it don\xe2\x80\x99t want to come.\xe2\x80\x94Holloa you Deputy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWiddy!\xe2\x80\x9d is Deputy\xe2\x80\x99s shrill response, standing off again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch that ha\xe2\x80\x99penny. And don\xe2\x80\x99t let me see any more of you to-night,\r\nafter we come to the Travellers\xe2\x80\x99 Twopenny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWarning!\xe2\x80\x9d returns Deputy, having caught the halfpenny, and appearing\r\nby this mystic word to express his assent to the arrangement.\r\n\r\nThey have but to cross what was once the vineyard, belonging to what\r\nwas once the Monastery, to come into the narrow back lane wherein\r\nstands the crazy wooden house of two low stories currently known as the\r\nTravellers\xe2\x80\x99 Twopenny:\xe2\x80\x94a house all warped and distorted, like the morals\r\nof the travellers, with scant remains of a lattice-work porch over the\r\ndoor, and also of a rustic fence before its stamped-out garden; by\r\nreason of the travellers being so bound to the premises by a tender\r\nsentiment (or so fond of having a fire by the roadside in the course of\r\nthe day), that they can never be persuaded or threatened into\r\ndeparture, without violently possessing themselves of some wooden\r\nforget-me-not, and bearing it off.\r\n\r\nThe semblance of an inn is attempted to be given to this wretched place\r\nby fragments of conventional red curtaining in the windows, which rags\r\nare made muddily transparent in the night-season by feeble lights of\r\nrush or cotton dip burning dully in the close air of the inside. As\r\nDurdles and Jasper come near, they are addressed by an inscribed paper\r\nlantern over the door, setting forth the purport of the house. They are\r\nalso addressed by some half-dozen other hideous small boys\xe2\x80\x94whether\r\ntwopenny lodgers or followers or hangers-on of such, who knows!\xe2\x80\x94who, as\r\nif attracted by some carrion-scent of Deputy in the air, start into the\r\nmoonlight, as vultures might gather in the desert, and instantly fall\r\nto stoning him and one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, you young brutes,\xe2\x80\x9d cries Jasper angrily, \xe2\x80\x9cand let us go by!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis remonstrance being received with yells and flying stones,\r\naccording to a custom of late years comfortably established among the\r\npolice regulations of our English communities, where Christians are\r\nstoned on all sides, as if the days of Saint Stephen were revived,\r\nDurdles remarks of the young savages, with some point, that \xe2\x80\x9cthey\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t got an object,\xe2\x80\x9d and leads the way down the lane.\r\n\r\nAt the corner of the lane, Jasper, hotly enraged, checks his companion\r\nand looks back. All is silent. Next moment, a stone coming rattling at\r\nhis hat, and a distant yell of \xe2\x80\x9cWake-Cock! Warning!\xe2\x80\x9d followed by a\r\ncrow, as from some infernally-hatched Chanticleer, apprising him under\r\nwhose victorious fire he stands, he turns the corner into safety, and\r\ntakes Durdles home: Durdles stumbling among the litter of his stony\r\nyard as if he were going to turn head foremost into one of the\r\nunfinished tombs.\r\n\r\nJohn Jasper returns by another way to his gatehouse, and entering\r\nsoftly with his key, finds his fire still burning. He takes from a\r\nlocked press a peculiar-looking pipe, which he fills\xe2\x80\x94but not with\r\ntobacco\xe2\x80\x94and, having adjusted the contents of the bowl, very carefully,\r\nwith a little instrument, ascends an inner staircase of only a few\r\nsteps, leading to two rooms. One of these is his own sleeping chamber:\r\nthe other is his nephew\xe2\x80\x99s. There is a light in each.\r\n\r\nHis nephew lies asleep, calm and untroubled. John Jasper stands looking\r\ndown upon him, his unlighted pipe in his hand, for some time, with a\r\nfixed and deep attention. Then, hushing his footsteps, he passes to his\r\nown room, lights his pipe, and delivers himself to the Spectres it\r\ninvokes at midnight.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\nPHILANTHROPY IN MINOR CANON CORNER\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Reverend Septimus Crisparkle (Septimus, because six little brother\r\nCrisparkles before him went out, one by one, as they were born, like\r\nsix weak little rushlights, as they were lighted), having broken the\r\nthin morning ice near Cloisterham Weir with his amiable head, much to\r\nthe invigoration of his frame, was now assisting his circulation by\r\nboxing at a looking-glass with great science and prowess. A fresh and\r\nhealthy portrait the looking-glass presented of the Reverend Septimus,\r\nfeinting and dodging with the utmost artfulness, and hitting out from\r\nthe shoulder with the utmost straightness, while his radiant features\r\nteemed with innocence, and soft-hearted benevolence beamed from his\r\nboxing-gloves.\r\n\r\nIt was scarcely breakfast-time yet, for Mrs. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x94mother, not\r\nwife of the Reverend Septimus\xe2\x80\x94was only just down, and waiting for the\r\nurn. Indeed, the Reverend Septimus left off at this very moment to take\r\nthe pretty old lady\xe2\x80\x99s entering face between his boxing-gloves and kiss\r\nit. Having done so with tenderness, the Reverend Septimus turned to\r\nagain, countering with his left, and putting in his right, in a\r\ntremendous manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, every morning of my life, that you\xe2\x80\x99ll do it at last, Sept,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nremarked the old lady, looking on; \xe2\x80\x9cand so you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo what, Ma dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreak the pier-glass, or burst a blood-vessel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither, please God, Ma dear. Here\xe2\x80\x99s wind, Ma. Look at this!\xe2\x80\x9d In a\r\nconcluding round of great severity, the Reverend Septimus administered\r\nand escaped all sorts of punishment, and wound up by getting the old\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s cap into Chancery\xe2\x80\x94such is the technical term used in scientific\r\ncircles by the learned in the Noble Art\xe2\x80\x94with a lightness of touch that\r\nhardly stirred the lightest lavender or cherry riband on it.\r\nMagnanimously releasing the defeated, just in time to get his gloves\r\ninto a drawer and feign to be looking out of window in a contemplative\r\nstate of mind when a servant entered, the Reverend Septimus then gave\r\nplace to the urn and other preparations for breakfast. These completed,\r\nand the two alone again, it was pleasant to see (or would have been, if\r\nthere had been any one to see it, which there never was), the old lady\r\nstanding to say the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s Prayer aloud, and her son, Minor Canon\r\nnevertheless, standing with bent head to hear it, he being within five\r\nyears of forty: much as he had stood to hear the same words from the\r\nsame lips when he was within five months of four.\r\n\r\nWhat is prettier than an old lady\xe2\x80\x94except a young lady\xe2\x80\x94when her eyes are\r\nbright, when her figure is trim and compact, when her face is cheerful\r\nand calm, when her dress is as the dress of a china shepherdess: so\r\ndainty in its colours, so individually assorted to herself, so neatly\r\nmoulded on her? Nothing is prettier, thought the good Minor Canon\r\nfrequently, when taking his seat at table opposite his long-widowed\r\nmother. Her thought at such times may be condensed into the two words\r\nthat oftenest did duty together in all her conversations: \xe2\x80\x9cMy Sept!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were a good pair to sit breakfasting together in Minor Canon\r\nCorner, Cloisterham. For Minor Canon Corner was a quiet place in the\r\nshadow of the Cathedral, which the cawing of the rooks, the echoing\r\nfootsteps of rare passers, the sound of the Cathedral bell, or the roll\r\nof the Cathedral organ, seemed to render more quiet than absolute\r\nsilence. Swaggering fighting men had had their centuries of ramping and\r\nraving about Minor Canon Corner, and beaten serfs had had their\r\ncenturies of drudging and dying there, and powerful monks had had their\r\ncenturies of being sometimes useful and sometimes harmful there, and\r\nbehold they were all gone out of Minor Canon Corner, and so much the\r\nbetter. Perhaps one of the highest uses of their ever having been\r\nthere, was, that there might be left behind, that blessed air of\r\ntranquillity which pervaded Minor Canon Corner, and that serenely\r\nromantic state of the mind\xe2\x80\x94productive for the most part of pity and\r\nforbearance\xe2\x80\x94which is engendered by a sorrowful story that is all told,\r\nor a pathetic play that is played out.\r\n\r\nRed-brick walls harmoniously toned down in colour by time,\r\nstrong-rooted ivy, latticed windows, panelled rooms, big oaken beams in\r\nlittle places, and stone-walled gardens where annual fruit yet ripened\r\nupon monkish trees, were the principal surroundings of pretty old Mrs.\r\nCrisparkle and the Reverend Septimus as they sat at breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what, Ma dear,\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the Minor Canon, giving proof of a\r\nwholesome and vigorous appetite, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes the letter say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pretty old lady, after reading it, had just laid it down upon the\r\nbreakfast-cloth. She handed it over to her son.\r\n\r\nNow, the old lady was exceedingly proud of her bright eyes being so\r\nclear that she could read writing without spectacles. Her son was also\r\nso proud of the circumstance, and so dutifully bent on her deriving the\r\nutmost possible gratification from it, that he had invented the\r\npretence that he himself could _not_ read writing without spectacles.\r\nTherefore he now assumed a pair, of grave and prodigious proportions,\r\nwhich not only seriously inconvenienced his nose and his breakfast, but\r\nseriously impeded his perusal of the letter. For, he had the eyes of a\r\nmicroscope and a telescope combined, when they were unassisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s from Mr. Honeythunder, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady, folding her\r\narms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d assented her son. He then lamely read on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Haven of Philanthropy,\r\nChief Offices, London, Wednesday.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98DEAR MADAM,\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I write in the\xe2\x80\x94;\xe2\x80\x99 In the what\xe2\x80\x99s this? What does he write in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the chair,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\nThe Reverend Septimus took off his spectacles, that he might see her\r\nface, as he exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what should he write in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless me, bless me, Sept,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t see the\r\ncontext! Give it back to me, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGlad to get his spectacles off (for they always made his eyes water),\r\nher son obeyed: murmuring that his sight for reading manuscript got\r\nworse and worse daily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I write,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d his mother went on, reading very perspicuously and\r\nprecisely, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98from the chair, to which I shall probably be confined for\r\nsome hours.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeptimus looked at the row of chairs against the wall, with a\r\nhalf-protesting and half-appealing countenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98We have,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady read on with a little extra emphasis, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98a\r\nmeeting of our Convened Chief Composite Committee of Central and\r\nDistrict Philanthropists, at our Head Haven as above; and it is their\r\nunanimous pleasure that I take the chair.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeptimus breathed more freely, and muttered: \xe2\x80\x9cO! if he comes to _that_,\r\nlet him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Not to lose a day\xe2\x80\x99s post, I take the opportunity of a long report\r\nbeing read, denouncing a public miscreant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a most extraordinary thing,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed the gentle Minor Canon,\r\nlaying down his knife and fork to rub his ear in a vexed manner, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthese Philanthropists are always denouncing somebody. And it is another\r\nmost extraordinary thing that they are always so violently flush of\r\nmiscreants!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Denouncing a public miscreant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the old lady resumed, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98to get our\r\nlittle affair of business off my mind. I have spoken with my two wards,\r\nNeville and Helena Landless, on the subject of their defective\r\neducation, and they give in to the plan proposed; as I should have\r\ntaken good care they did, whether they liked it or not.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is another most extraordinary thing,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the Minor Canon\r\nin the same tone as before, \xe2\x80\x9cthat these philanthropists are so given to\r\nseizing their fellow-creatures by the scruff of the neck, and (as one\r\nmay say) bumping them into the paths of peace.\xe2\x80\x94I beg your pardon, Ma\r\ndear, for interrupting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Therefore, dear Madam, you will please prepare your son, the Rev. Mr.\r\nSeptimus, to expect Neville as an inmate to be read with, on Monday\r\nnext. On the same day Helena will accompany him to Cloisterham, to take\r\nup her quarters at the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, the establishment recommended by\r\nyourself and son jointly. Please likewise to prepare for her reception\r\nand tuition there. The terms in both cases are understood to be exactly\r\nas stated to me in writing by yourself, when I opened a correspondence\r\nwith you on this subject, after the honour of being introduced to you\r\nat your sister\xe2\x80\x99s house in town here. With compliments to the Rev. Mr.\r\nSeptimus, I am, Dear Madam, Your affectionate brother (In\r\nPhilanthropy), LUKE HONEYTHUNDER.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Ma,\xe2\x80\x9d said Septimus, after a little more rubbing of his ear, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\r\nmust try it. There can be no doubt that we have room for an inmate, and\r\nthat I have time to bestow upon him, and inclination too. I must\r\nconfess to feeling rather glad that he is not Mr. Honeythunder himself.\r\nThough that seems wretchedly prejudiced\xe2\x80\x94does it not?\xe2\x80\x94for I never saw\r\nhim. Is he a large man, Ma?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should call him a large man, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady replied after\r\nsome hesitation, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that his voice is so much larger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThan himself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThan anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Septimus. And finished his breakfast as if the flavour of\r\nthe Superior Family Souchong, and also of the ham and toast and eggs,\r\nwere a little on the wane.\r\n\r\nMrs. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s sister, another piece of Dresden china, and matching\r\nher so neatly that they would have made a delightful pair of ornaments\r\nfor the two ends of any capacious old-fashioned chimneypiece, and by\r\nright should never have been seen apart, was the childless wife of a\r\nclergyman holding Corporation preferment in London City. Mr.\r\nHoneythunder in his public character of Professor of Philanthropy had\r\ncome to know Mrs. Crisparkle during the last re-matching of the china\r\nornaments (in other words during her last annual visit to her sister),\r\nafter a public occasion of a philanthropic nature, when certain devoted\r\norphans of tender years had been glutted with plum buns, and plump\r\nbumptiousness. These were all the antecedents known in Minor Canon\r\nCorner of the coming pupils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you will agree with me, Ma,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, after\r\nthinking the matter over, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the first thing to be done, is, to put\r\nthese young people as much at their ease as possible. There is nothing\r\ndisinterested in the notion, because we cannot be at our ease with them\r\nunless they are at their ease with us. Now, Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s nephew is down\r\nhere at present; and like takes to like, and youth takes to youth. He\r\nis a cordial young fellow, and we will have him to meet the brother and\r\nsister at dinner. That\xe2\x80\x99s three. We can\xe2\x80\x99t think of asking him, without\r\nasking Jasper. That\xe2\x80\x99s four. Add Miss Twinkleton and the fairy bride\r\nthat is to be, and that\xe2\x80\x99s six. Add our two selves, and that\xe2\x80\x99s eight.\r\nWould eight at a friendly dinner at all put you out, Ma?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine would, Sept,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady, visibly nervous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Ma, I particularise eight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe exact size of the table and the room, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo it was settled that way: and when Mr. Crisparkle called with his\r\nmother upon Miss Twinkleton, to arrange for the reception of Miss\r\nHelena Landless at the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, the two other invitations having\r\nreference to that establishment were proffered and accepted. Miss\r\nTwinkleton did, indeed, glance at the globes, as regretting that they\r\nwere not formed to be taken out into society; but became reconciled to\r\nleaving them behind. Instructions were then despatched to the\r\nPhilanthropist for the departure and arrival, in good time for dinner,\r\nof Mr. Neville and Miss Helena; and stock for soup became fragrant in\r\nthe air of Minor Canon Corner.\r\n\r\nIn those days there was no railway to Cloisterham, and Mr. Sapsea said\r\nthere never would be. Mr. Sapsea said more; he said there never should\r\nbe. And yet, marvellous to consider, it has come to pass, in these\r\ndays, that Express Trains don\xe2\x80\x99t think Cloisterham worth stopping at,\r\nbut yell and whirl through it on their larger errands, casting the dust\r\noff their wheels as a testimony against its insignificance. Some remote\r\nfragment of Main Line to somewhere else, there was, which was going to\r\nruin the Money Market if it failed, and Church and State if it\r\nsucceeded, and (of course), the Constitution, whether or no; but even\r\nthat had already so unsettled Cloisterham traffic, that the traffic,\r\ndeserting the high road, came sneaking in from an unprecedented part of\r\nthe country by a back stable-way, for many years labelled at the\r\ncorner: \xe2\x80\x9cBeware of the Dog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this ignominious avenue of approach, Mr. Crisparkle repaired,\r\nawaiting the arrival of a short, squat omnibus, with a disproportionate\r\nheap of luggage on the roof\xe2\x80\x94like a little Elephant with infinitely too\r\nmuch Castle\xe2\x80\x94which was then the daily service between Cloisterham and\r\nexternal mankind. As this vehicle lumbered up, Mr. Crisparkle could\r\nhardly see anything else of it for a large outside passenger seated on\r\nthe box, with his elbows squared, and his hands on his knees,\r\ncompressing the driver into a most uncomfortably small compass, and\r\nglowering about him with a strongly-marked face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this Cloisterham?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the passenger, in a tremendous voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the driver, rubbing himself as if he ached, after\r\nthrowing the reins to the ostler. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I never was so glad to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell your master to make his box-seat wider, then,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the\r\npassenger. \xe2\x80\x9cYour master is morally bound\xe2\x80\x94and ought to be legally, under\r\nruinous penalties\xe2\x80\x94to provide for the comfort of his fellow-man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe driver instituted, with the palms of his hands, a superficial\r\nperquisition into the state of his skeleton; which seemed to make him\r\nanxious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I sat upon you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the passenger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have,\xe2\x80\x9d said the driver, as if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it at all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake that card, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I won\xe2\x80\x99t deprive you on it,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the driver, casting his\r\neyes over it with no great favour, without taking it. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good\r\nof it to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe a Member of that Society,\xe2\x80\x9d said the passenger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I get by it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the driver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrotherhood,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the passenger, in a ferocious voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThankee,\xe2\x80\x9d said the driver, very deliberately, as he got down; \xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\nmother was contented with myself, and so am I. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want no\r\nbrothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must have them,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the passenger, also descending,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhether you like it or not. I am your brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say!\xe2\x80\x9d expostulated the driver, becoming more chafed in temper, \xe2\x80\x9cnot\r\ntoo fur! The worm _will_, when\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut here, Mr. Crisparkle interposed, remonstrating aside, in a friendly\r\nvoice: \xe2\x80\x9cJoe, Joe, Joe! don\xe2\x80\x99t forget yourself, Joe, my good fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nthen, when Joe peaceably touched his hat, accosting the passenger with:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Honeythunder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my name, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Crisparkle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReverend Mr. Septimus? Glad to see you, sir. Neville and Helena are\r\ninside. Having a little succumbed of late, under the pressure of my\r\npublic labours, I thought I would take a mouthful of fresh air, and\r\ncome down with them, and return at night. So you are the Reverend Mr.\r\nSeptimus, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d surveying him on the whole with disappointment, and\r\ntwisting a double eyeglass by its ribbon, as if he were roasting it,\r\nbut not otherwise using it. \xe2\x80\x9cHah! I expected to see you older, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will,\xe2\x80\x9d was the good-humoured reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mr. Honeythunder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a poor little joke. Not worth repeating.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoke? Ay; I never see a joke,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Honeythunder frowningly retorted.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA joke is wasted upon me, sir. Where are they? Helena and Neville,\r\ncome here! Mr. Crisparkle has come down to meet you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn unusually handsome lithe young fellow, and an unusually handsome\r\nlithe girl; much alike; both very dark, and very rich in colour; she of\r\nalmost the gipsy type; something untamed about them both; a certain air\r\nupon them of hunter and huntress; yet withal a certain air of being the\r\nobjects of the chase, rather than the followers. Slender, supple, quick\r\nof eye and limb; half shy, half defiant; fierce of look; an indefinable\r\nkind of pause coming and going on their whole expression, both of face\r\nand form, which might be equally likened to the pause before a crouch\r\nor a bound. The rough mental notes made in the first five minutes by\r\nMr. Crisparkle would have read thus, _verbatim_.\r\n\r\nHe invited Mr. Honeythunder to dinner, with a troubled mind (for the\r\ndiscomfiture of the dear old china shepherdess lay heavy on it), and\r\ngave his arm to Helena Landless. Both she and her brother, as they\r\nwalked all together through the ancient streets, took great delight in\r\nwhat he pointed out of the Cathedral and the Monastery ruin, and\r\nwondered\xe2\x80\x94so his notes ran on\xe2\x80\x94much as if they were beautiful barbaric\r\ncaptives brought from some wild tropical dominion. Mr. Honeythunder\r\nwalked in the middle of the road, shouldering the natives out of his\r\nway, and loudly developing a scheme he had, for making a raid on all\r\nthe unemployed persons in the United Kingdom, laying them every one by\r\nthe heels in jail, and forcing them, on pain of prompt extermination,\r\nto become philanthropists.\r\n\r\nMrs. Crisparkle had need of her own share of philanthropy when she\r\nbeheld this very large and very loud excrescence on the little party.\r\nAlways something in the nature of a Boil upon the face of society, Mr.\r\nHoneythunder expanded into an inflammatory Wen in Minor Canon Corner.\r\nThough it was not literally true, as was facetiously charged against\r\nhim by public unbelievers, that he called aloud to his\r\nfellow-creatures: \xe2\x80\x9cCurse your souls and bodies, come here and be\r\nblessed!\xe2\x80\x9d still his philanthropy was of that gunpowderous sort that the\r\ndifference between it and animosity was hard to determine. You were to\r\nabolish military force, but you were first to bring all commanding\r\nofficers who had done their duty, to trial by court-martial for that\r\noffence, and shoot them. You were to abolish war, but were to make\r\nconverts by making war upon them, and charging them with loving war as\r\nthe apple of their eye. You were to have no capital punishment, but\r\nwere first to sweep off the face of the earth all legislators, jurists,\r\nand judges, who were of the contrary opinion. You were to have\r\nuniversal concord, and were to get it by eliminating all the people who\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, or conscientiously couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, be concordant. You were to love\r\nyour brother as yourself, but after an indefinite interval of maligning\r\nhim (very much as if you hated him), and calling him all manner of\r\nnames. Above all things, you were to do nothing in private, or on your\r\nown account. You were to go to the offices of the Haven of\r\nPhilanthropy, and put your name down as a Member and a Professing\r\nPhilanthropist. Then, you were to pay up your subscription, get your\r\ncard of membership and your riband and medal, and were evermore to live\r\nupon a platform, and evermore to say what Mr. Honeythunder said, and\r\nwhat the Treasurer said, and what the sub-Treasurer said, and what the\r\nCommittee said, and what the sub-Committee said, and what the Secretary\r\nsaid, and what the Vice-Secretary said. And this was usually said in\r\nthe unanimously-carried resolution under hand and seal, to the effect:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat this assembled Body of Professing Philanthropists views, with\r\nindignant scorn and contempt, not unmixed with utter detestation and\r\nloathing abhorrence\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94in short, the baseness of all those who do not\r\nbelong to it, and pledges itself to make as many obnoxious statements\r\nas possible about them, without being at all particular as to facts.\r\n\r\nThe dinner was a most doleful breakdown. The philanthropist deranged\r\nthe symmetry of the table, sat himself in the way of the waiting,\r\nblocked up the thoroughfare, and drove Mr. Tope (who assisted the\r\nparlour-maid) to the verge of distraction by passing plates and dishes\r\non, over his own head. Nobody could talk to anybody, because he held\r\nforth to everybody at once, as if the company had no individual\r\nexistence, but were a Meeting. He impounded the Reverend Mr. Septimus,\r\nas an official personage to be addressed, or kind of human peg to hang\r\nhis oratorical hat on, and fell into the exasperating habit, common\r\namong such orators, of impersonating him as a wicked and weak opponent.\r\nThus, he would ask: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you, sir, now stultify yourself by\r\ntelling me\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and so forth, when the innocent man had not opened his\r\nlips, nor meant to open them. Or he would say: \xe2\x80\x9cNow see, sir, to what a\r\nposition you are reduced. I will leave you no escape. After exhausting\r\nall the resources of fraud and falsehood, during years upon years;\r\nafter exhibiting a combination of dastardly meanness with ensanguined\r\ndaring, such as the world has not often witnessed; you have now the\r\nhypocrisy to bend the knee before the most degraded of mankind, and to\r\nsue and whine and howl for mercy!\xe2\x80\x9d Whereat the unfortunate Minor Canon\r\nwould look, in part indignant and in part perplexed; while his worthy\r\nmother sat bridling, with tears in her eyes, and the remainder of the\r\nparty lapsed into a sort of gelatinous state, in which there was no\r\nflavour or solidity, and very little resistance.\r\n\r\nBut the gush of philanthropy that burst forth when the departure of Mr.\r\nHoneythunder began to impend, must have been highly gratifying to the\r\nfeelings of that distinguished man. His coffee was produced, by the\r\nspecial activity of Mr. Tope, a full hour before he wanted it. Mr.\r\nCrisparkle sat with his watch in his hand for about the same period,\r\nlest he should overstay his time. The four young people were unanimous\r\nin believing that the Cathedral clock struck three-quarters, when it\r\nactually struck but one. Miss Twinkleton estimated the distance to the\r\nomnibus at five-and-twenty minutes\xe2\x80\x99 walk, when it was really five. The\r\naffectionate kindness of the whole circle hustled him into his\r\ngreatcoat, and shoved him out into the moonlight, as if he were a\r\nfugitive traitor with whom they sympathised, and a troop of horse were\r\nat the back door. Mr. Crisparkle and his new charge, who took him to\r\nthe omnibus, were so fervent in their apprehensions of his catching\r\ncold, that they shut him up in it instantly and left him, with still\r\nhalf-an-hour to spare.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\nMORE CONFIDENCES THAN ONE\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know very little of that gentleman, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville to the Minor\r\nCanon as they turned back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know very little of your guardian?\xe2\x80\x9d the Minor Canon repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlmost nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow came he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo _be_ my guardian? I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you, sir. I suppose you know that we\r\ncome (my sister and I) from Ceylon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder at that. We lived with a stepfather there. Our mother died\r\nthere, when we were little children. We have had a wretched existence.\r\nShe made him our guardian, and he was a miserly wretch who grudged us\r\nfood to eat, and clothes to wear. At his death, he passed us over to\r\nthis man; for no better reason that I know of, than his being a friend\r\nor connexion of his, whose name was always in print and catching his\r\nattention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was lately, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite lately, sir. This stepfather of ours was a cruel brute as well\r\nas a grinding one. It is well he died when he did, or I might have\r\nkilled him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle stopped short in the moonlight and looked at his hopeful\r\npupil in consternation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI surprise you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a quick change to a submissive\r\nmanner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shock me; unspeakably shock me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pupil hung his head for a little while, as they walked on, and then\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cYou never saw him beat your sister. I have seen him beat mine,\r\nmore than once or twice, and I never forgot it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cnot even a beloved and beautiful\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x99s tears under dastardly ill-usage;\xe2\x80\x9d he became less severe, in\r\nspite of himself, as his indignation rose; \xe2\x80\x9ccould justify those\r\nhorrible expressions that you used.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry I used them, and especially to you, sir. I beg to recall\r\nthem. But permit me to set you right on one point. You spoke of my\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x99s tears. My sister would have let him tear her to pieces, before\r\nshe would have let him believe that he could make her shed a tear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle reviewed those mental notes of his, and was neither at\r\nall surprised to hear it, nor at all disposed to question it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you will think it strange, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this was said in a hesitating\r\nvoice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat I should so soon ask you to allow me to confide in you, and\r\nto have the kindness to hear a word or two from me in my defence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDefence?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not on your defence, Mr.\r\nNeville.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I am, sir. At least I know I should be, if you were better\r\nacquainted with my character.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d was the rejoinder. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat if you leave me to find\r\nit out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince it is your pleasure, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the young man, with a quick\r\nchange in his manner to sullen disappointment: \xe2\x80\x9csince it is your\r\npleasure to check me in my impulse, I must submit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was that in the tone of this short speech which made the\r\nconscientious man to whom it was addressed uneasy. It hinted to him\r\nthat he might, without meaning it, turn aside a trustfulness beneficial\r\nto a mis-shapen young mind and perhaps to his own power of directing\r\nand improving it. They were within sight of the lights in his windows,\r\nand he stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us turn back and take a turn or two up and down, Mr. Neville, or\r\nyou may not have time to finish what you wish to say to me. You are\r\nhasty in thinking that I mean to check you. Quite the contrary. I\r\ninvite your confidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have invited it, sir, without knowing it, ever since I came here.\r\nI say \xe2\x80\x98ever since,\xe2\x80\x99 as if I had been here a week. The truth is, we came\r\nhere (my sister and I) to quarrel with you, and affront you, and break\r\naway again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, at a dead loss for anything else to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, we could not know what you were beforehand, sir; could we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClearly not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd having liked no one else with whom we have ever been brought into\r\ncontact, we had made up our minds not to like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we do like you, sir, and we see an unmistakable difference between\r\nyour house and your reception of us, and anything else we have ever\r\nknown. This\xe2\x80\x94and my happening to be alone with you\xe2\x80\x94and everything around\r\nus seeming so quiet and peaceful after Mr. Honeythunder\xe2\x80\x99s departure\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nCloisterham being so old and grave and beautiful, with the moon shining\r\non it\xe2\x80\x94these things inclined me to open my heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite understand, Mr. Neville. And it is salutary to listen to such\r\ninfluences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn describing my own imperfections, sir, I must ask you not to suppose\r\nthat I am describing my sister\xe2\x80\x99s. She has come out of the disadvantages\r\nof our miserable life, as much better than I am, as that Cathedral\r\ntower is higher than those chimneys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle in his own breast was not so sure of this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had, sir, from my earliest remembrance, to suppress a deadly\r\nand bitter hatred. This has made me secret and revengeful. I have been\r\nalways tyrannically held down by the strong hand. This has driven me,\r\nin my weakness, to the resource of being false and mean. I have been\r\nstinted of education, liberty, money, dress, the very necessaries of\r\nlife, the commonest pleasures of childhood, the commonest possessions\r\nof youth. This has caused me to be utterly wanting in I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nemotions, or remembrances, or good instincts\xe2\x80\x94I have not even a name for\r\nthe thing, you see!\xe2\x80\x94that you have had to work upon in other young men\r\nto whom you have been accustomed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is evidently true. But this is not encouraging,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Mr.\r\nCrisparkle as they turned again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to finish with, sir: I have been brought up among abject and\r\nservile dependents, of an inferior race, and I may easily have\r\ncontracted some affinity with them. Sometimes, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but that it\r\nmay be a drop of what is tigerish in their blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs in the case of that remark just now,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a last word of reference to my sister, sir (we are twin children),\r\nyou ought to know, to her honour, that nothing in our misery ever\r\nsubdued her, though it often cowed me. When we ran away from it (we ran\r\naway four times in six years, to be soon brought back and cruelly\r\npunished), the flight was always of her planning and leading. Each time\r\nshe dressed as a boy, and showed the daring of a man. I take it we were\r\nseven years old when we first decamped; but I remember, when I lost the\r\npocket-knife with which she was to have cut her hair short, how\r\ndesperately she tried to tear it out, or bite it off. I have nothing\r\nfurther to say, sir, except that I hope you will bear with me and make\r\nallowance for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf that, Mr. Neville, you may be sure,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Minor Canon. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t preach more than I can help, and I will not repay your confidence\r\nwith a sermon. But I entreat you to bear in mind, very seriously and\r\nsteadily, that if I am to do you any good, it can only be with your own\r\nassistance; and that you can only render that, efficiently, by seeking\r\naid from Heaven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try to do my part, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, Mr. Neville, I will try to do mine. Here is my hand on it. May\r\nGod bless our endeavours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were now standing at his house-door, and a cheerful sound of\r\nvoices and laughter was heard within.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will take one more turn before going in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cfor\r\nI want to ask you a question. When you said you were in a changed mind\r\nconcerning me, you spoke, not only for yourself, but for your sister\r\ntoo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly I did, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, Mr. Neville, but I think you have had no opportunity of\r\ncommunicating with your sister, since I met you. Mr. Honeythunder was\r\nvery eloquent; but perhaps I may venture to say, without ill-nature,\r\nthat he rather monopolised the occasion. May you not have answered for\r\nyour sister without sufficient warrant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville shook his head with a proud smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir, yet, what a complete understanding can exist\r\nbetween my sister and me, though no spoken word\xe2\x80\x94perhaps hardly as much\r\nas a look\xe2\x80\x94may have passed between us. She not only feels as I have\r\ndescribed, but she very well knows that I am taking this opportunity of\r\nspeaking to you, both for her and for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle looked in his face, with some incredulity; but his face\r\nexpressed such absolute and firm conviction of the truth of what he\r\nsaid, that Mr. Crisparkle looked at the pavement, and mused, until they\r\ncame to his door again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will ask for one more turn, sir, this time,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man,\r\nwith a rather heightened colour rising in his face. \xe2\x80\x9cBut for Mr.\r\nHoneythunder\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94I think you called it eloquence, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d (somewhat slyly.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94yes, I called it eloquence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for Mr. Honeythunder\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence, I might have had no need to ask\r\nyou what I am going to ask you. This Mr. Edwin Drood, sir: I think\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite correct,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cD-r-double o-d.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he\xe2\x80\x94or did he\xe2\x80\x94read with you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, Mr. Neville. He comes here visiting his relation, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Miss Bud his relation too, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cNow, why should he ask that, with sudden superciliousness?\xe2\x80\x9d thought\r\nMr. Crisparkle.) Then he explained, aloud, what he knew of the little\r\nstory of their betrothal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! _that\xe2\x80\x99s_ it, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cI understand his air of\r\nproprietorship now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was said so evidently to himself, or to anybody rather than Mr.\r\nCrisparkle, that the latter instinctively felt as if to notice it would\r\nbe almost tantamount to noticing a passage in a letter which he had\r\nread by chance over the writer\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. A moment afterwards they\r\nre-entered the house.\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper was seated at the piano as they came into his drawing-room,\r\nand was accompanying Miss Rosebud while she sang. It was a consequence\r\nof his playing the accompaniment without notes, and of her being a\r\nheedless little creature, very apt to go wrong, that he followed her\r\nlips most attentively, with his eyes as well as hands; carefully and\r\nsoftly hinting the key-note from time to time. Standing with an arm\r\ndrawn round her, but with a face far more intent on Mr. Jasper than on\r\nher singing, stood Helena, between whom and her brother an\r\ninstantaneous recognition passed, in which Mr. Crisparkle saw, or\r\nthought he saw, the understanding that had been spoken of, flash out.\r\nMr. Neville then took his admiring station, leaning against the piano,\r\nopposite the singer; Mr. Crisparkle sat down by the china shepherdess;\r\nEdwin Drood gallantly furled and unfurled Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s fan; and\r\nthat lady passively claimed that sort of exhibitor\xe2\x80\x99s proprietorship in\r\nthe accomplishment on view, which Mr. Tope, the Verger, daily claimed\r\nin the Cathedral service.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: At the piano]\r\n\r\n\r\nThe song went on. It was a sorrowful strain of parting, and the fresh\r\nyoung voice was very plaintive and tender. As Jasper watched the pretty\r\nlips, and ever and again hinted the one note, as though it were a low\r\nwhisper from himself, the voice became less steady, until all at once\r\nthe singer broke into a burst of tears, and shrieked out, with her\r\nhands over her eyes: \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t bear this! I am frightened! Take me\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith one swift turn of her lithe figure, Helena laid the little beauty\r\non a sofa, as if she had never caught her up. Then, on one knee beside\r\nher, and with one hand upon her rosy mouth, while with the other she\r\nappealed to all the rest, Helena said to them: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothing; it\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nover; don\xe2\x80\x99t speak to her for one minute, and she is well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper\xe2\x80\x99s hands had, in the same instant, lifted themselves from the\r\nkeys, and were now poised above them, as though he waited to resume. In\r\nthat attitude he yet sat quiet: not even looking round, when all the\r\nrest had changed their places and were reassuring one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPussy\xe2\x80\x99s not used to an audience; that\xe2\x80\x99s the fact,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin Drood.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe got nervous, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hold out. Besides, Jack, you are such a\r\nconscientious master, and require so much, that I believe you make her\r\nafraid of you. No wonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Helena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, Jack, you hear! You would be afraid of him, under similar\r\ncircumstances, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you, Miss Landless?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot under any circumstances,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Helena.\r\n\r\nJasper brought down his hands, looked over his shoulder, and begged to\r\nthank Miss Landless for her vindication of his character. Then he fell\r\nto dumbly playing, without striking the notes, while his little pupil\r\nwas taken to an open window for air, and was otherwise petted and\r\nrestored. When she was brought back, his place was empty. \xe2\x80\x9cJack\xe2\x80\x99s gone,\r\nPussy,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin told her. \xe2\x80\x9cI am more than half afraid he didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to\r\nbe charged with being the Monster who had frightened you.\xe2\x80\x9d But she\r\nanswered never a word, and shivered, as if they had made her a little\r\ntoo cold.\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton now opining that indeed these were late hours, Mrs.\r\nCrisparkle, for finding ourselves outside the walls of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House,\r\nand that we who undertook the formation of the future wives and mothers\r\nof England (the last words in a lower voice, as requiring to be\r\ncommunicated in confidence) were really bound (voice coming up again)\r\nto set a better example than one of rakish habits, wrappers were put in\r\nrequisition, and the two young cavaliers volunteered to see the ladies\r\nhome. It was soon done, and the gate of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House closed upon\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nThe boarders had retired, and only Mrs. Tisher in solitary vigil\r\nawaited the new pupil. Her bedroom being within Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s, very little\r\nintroduction or explanation was necessary, before she was placed in\r\ncharge of her new friend, and left for the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a blessed relief, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been dreading\r\nall day, that I should be brought to bay at this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are not many of us,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Rosa, \xe2\x80\x9cand we are good-natured\r\ngirls; at least the others are; I can answer for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can answer for you,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Helena, searching the lovely little\r\nface with her dark, fiery eyes, and tenderly caressing the small\r\nfigure. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will be a friend to me, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so. But the idea of my being a friend to you seems too absurd,\r\nthough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, I am such a mite of a thing, and you are so womanly and handsome.\r\nYou seem to have resolution and power enough to crush me. I shrink into\r\nnothing by the side of your presence even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a neglected creature, my dear, unacquainted with all\r\naccomplishments, sensitively conscious that I have everything to learn,\r\nand deeply ashamed to own my ignorance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you acknowledge everything to me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy pretty one, can I help it? There is a fascination in you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! is there though?\xe2\x80\x9d pouted Rosa, half in jest and half in earnest.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a pity Master Eddy doesn\xe2\x80\x99t feel it more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course her relations towards that young gentleman had been already\r\nimparted in Minor Canon Corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, surely he must love you with all his heart!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Helena, with\r\nan earnestness that threatened to blaze into ferocity if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh? O, well, I suppose he does,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, pouting again; \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure\r\nI have no right to say he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t. Perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s my fault. Perhaps I am\r\nnot as nice to him as I ought to be. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am. But it _is_ so\r\nridiculous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena\xe2\x80\x99s eyes demanded what was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_We_ are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, answering as if she had spoken. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are such a\r\nridiculous couple. And we are always quarrelling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we both know we are ridiculous, my dear!\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa gave that\r\nanswer as if it were the most conclusive answer in the world.\r\n\r\nHelena\xe2\x80\x99s masterful look was intent upon her face for a few moments, and\r\nthen she impulsively put out both her hands and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will be my friend and help me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, my dear, I will,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Rosa, in a tone of affectionate\r\nchildishness that went straight and true to her heart; \xe2\x80\x9cI will be as\r\ngood a friend as such a mite of a thing can be to such a noble creature\r\nas you. And be a friend to me, please; I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand myself: and I\r\nwant a friend who can understand me, very much indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena Landless kissed her, and retaining both her hands said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Mr. Jasper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa turned aside her head in answering: \xe2\x80\x9cEddy\xe2\x80\x99s uncle, and my\r\nmusic-master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not love him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUgh!\xe2\x80\x9d She put her hands up to her face, and shook with fear or horror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know that he loves you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa, dropping on her knees, and\r\nclinging to her new resource. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me of it! He terrifies me. He\r\nhaunts my thoughts, like a dreadful ghost. I feel that I am never safe\r\nfrom him. I feel as if he could pass in through the wall when he is\r\nspoken of.\xe2\x80\x9d She actually did look round, as if she dreaded to see him\r\nstanding in the shadow behind her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry to tell me more about it, darling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I will, I will. Because you are so strong. But hold me the while,\r\nand stay with me afterwards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy child! You speak as if he had threatened you in some dark way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has never spoken to me about\xe2\x80\x94that. Never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has he done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has made a slave of me with his looks. He has forced me to\r\nunderstand him, without his saying a word; and he has forced me to keep\r\nsilence, without his uttering a threat. When I play, he never moves his\r\neyes from my hands. When I sing, he never moves his eyes from my lips.\r\nWhen he corrects me, and strikes a note, or a chord, or plays a\r\npassage, he himself is in the sounds, whispering that he pursues me as\r\na lover, and commanding me to keep his secret. I avoid his eyes, but he\r\nforces me to see them without looking at them. Even when a glaze comes\r\nover them (which is sometimes the case), and he seems to wander away\r\ninto a frightful sort of dream in which he threatens most, he obliges\r\nme to know it, and to know that he is sitting close at my side, more\r\nterrible to me than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this imagined threatening, pretty one? What is threatened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I have never even dared to think or wonder what it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd was this all, to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis was all; except that to-night when he watched my lips so closely\r\nas I was singing, besides feeling terrified I felt ashamed and\r\npassionately hurt. It was as if he kissed me, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear it,\r\nbut cried out. You must never breathe this to any one. Eddy is devoted\r\nto him. But you said to-night that you would not be afraid of him,\r\nunder any circumstances, and that gives me\xe2\x80\x94who am so much afraid of\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94courage to tell only you. Hold me! Stay with me! I am too\r\nfrightened to be left by myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lustrous gipsy-face drooped over the clinging arms and bosom, and\r\nthe wild black hair fell down protectingly over the childish form.\r\nThere was a slumbering gleam of fire in the intense dark eyes, though\r\nthey were then softened with compassion and admiration. Let whomsoever\r\nit most concerned look well to it!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\nDAGGERS DRAWN\r\n\r\n\r\nThe two young men, having seen the damsels, their charges, enter the\r\ncourtyard of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and finding themselves coldly stared at\r\nby the brazen door-plate, as if the battered old beau with the glass in\r\nhis eye were insolent, look at one another, look along the perspective\r\nof the moonlit street, and slowly walk away together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you stay here long, Mr. Drood?\xe2\x80\x9d says Neville.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot this time,\xe2\x80\x9d is the careless answer. \xe2\x80\x9cI leave for London again,\r\nto-morrow. But I shall be here, off and on, until next Midsummer; then\r\nI shall take my leave of Cloisterham, and England too; for many a long\r\nday, I expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going abroad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to wake up Egypt a little,\xe2\x80\x9d is the condescending answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you reading?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReading?\xe2\x80\x9d repeats Edwin Drood, with a touch of contempt. \xe2\x80\x9cNo. Doing,\r\nworking, engineering. My small patrimony was left a part of the capital\r\nof the Firm I am with, by my father, a former partner; and I am a\r\ncharge upon the Firm until I come of age; and then I step into my\r\nmodest share in the concern. Jack\xe2\x80\x94you met him at dinner\xe2\x80\x94is, until then,\r\nmy guardian and trustee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard from Mr. Crisparkle of your other good fortune.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by my other good fortune?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville has made his remark in a watchfully advancing, and yet furtive\r\nand shy manner, very expressive of that peculiar air already noticed,\r\nof being at once hunter and hunted. Edwin has made his retort with an\r\nabruptness not at all polite. They stop and interchange a rather heated\r\nlook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope,\xe2\x80\x9d says Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no offence, Mr. Drood, in my\r\ninnocently referring to your betrothal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Edwin, leading on again at a somewhat quicker pace;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ceverybody in this chattering old Cloisterham refers to it. I wonder no\r\npublic-house has been set up, with my portrait for the sign of The\r\nBetrothed\xe2\x80\x99s Head. Or Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s portrait. One or the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not accountable for Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s mentioning the matter to me,\r\nquite openly,\xe2\x80\x9d Neville begins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; that\xe2\x80\x99s true; you are not,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin Drood assents.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d resumes Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cI am accountable for mentioning it to you. And\r\nI did so, on the supposition that you could not fail to be highly proud\r\nof it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow, there are these two curious touches of human nature working the\r\nsecret springs of this dialogue. Neville Landless is already enough\r\nimpressed by Little Rosebud, to feel indignant that Edwin Drood (far\r\nbelow her) should hold his prize so lightly. Edwin Drood is already\r\nenough impressed by Helena, to feel indignant that Helena\xe2\x80\x99s brother\r\n(far below her) should dispose of him so coolly, and put him out of the\r\nway so entirely.\r\n\r\nHowever, the last remark had better be answered.
