So, says Edwin:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Mr. Neville\xe2\x80\x9d (adopting that mode of address from Mr.\r\nCrisparkle), \xe2\x80\x9cthat what people are proudest of, they usually talk most\r\nabout; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know either, that what they are proudest of, they most\r\nlike other people to talk about. But I live a busy life, and I speak\r\nunder correction by you readers, who ought to know everything, and I\r\ndaresay do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time they had both become savage; Mr. Neville out in the open;\r\nEdwin Drood under the transparent cover of a popular tune, and a stop\r\nnow and then to pretend to admire picturesque effects in the moonlight\r\nbefore him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not seem to me very civil in you,\xe2\x80\x9d remarks Neville, at length,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cto reflect upon a stranger who comes here, not having had your\r\nadvantages, to try to make up for lost time. But, to be sure, I was not\r\nbrought up in \xe2\x80\x98busy life,\xe2\x80\x99 and my ideas of civility were formed among\r\nHeathens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, the best civility, whatever kind of people we are brought up\r\namong,\xe2\x80\x9d retorts Edwin Drood, \xe2\x80\x9cis to mind our own business. If you will\r\nset me that example, I promise to follow it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know that you take a great deal too much upon yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d is the\r\nangry rejoinder, \xe2\x80\x9cand that in the part of the world I come from, you\r\nwould be called to account for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy whom, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d asks Edwin Drood, coming to a halt, and\r\nsurveying the other with a look of disdain.\r\n\r\nBut, here a startling right hand is laid on Edwin\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and\r\nJasper stands between them. For, it would seem that he, too, has\r\nstrolled round by the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and has come up behind them on the\r\nshadowy side of the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNed, Ned, Ned!\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cwe must have no more of this. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nthis. I have overheard high words between you two. Remember, my dear\r\nboy, you are almost in the position of host to-night. You belong, as it\r\nwere, to the place, and in a manner represent it towards a stranger.\r\nMr. Neville is a stranger, and you should respect the obligations of\r\nhospitality. And, Mr. Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d laying his left hand on the inner\r\nshoulder of that young gentleman, and thus walking on between them,\r\nhand to shoulder on either side: \xe2\x80\x9cyou will pardon me; but I appeal to\r\nyou to govern your temper too. Now, what is amiss? But why ask! Let\r\nthere be nothing amiss, and the question is superfluous. We are all\r\nthree on a good understanding, are we not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a silent struggle between the two young men who shall speak last,\r\nEdwin Drood strikes in with: \xe2\x80\x9cSo far as I am concerned, Jack, there is\r\nno anger in me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor in me,\xe2\x80\x9d says Neville Landless, though not so freely; or perhaps so\r\ncarelessly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if Mr. Drood knew all that lies behind me, far away\r\nfrom here, he might know better how it is that sharp-edged words have\r\nsharp edges to wound me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, in a soothing manner, \xe2\x80\x9cwe had better not\r\nqualify our good understanding. We had better not say anything having\r\nthe appearance of a remonstrance or condition; it might not seem\r\ngenerous. Frankly and freely, you see there is no anger in Ned. Frankly\r\nand freely, there is no anger in you, Mr. Neville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone at all, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d Still, not quite so frankly or so freely;\r\nor, be it said once again, not quite so carelessly perhaps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll over then! Now, my bachelor gatehouse is a few yards from here,\r\nand the heater is on the fire, and the wine and glasses are on the\r\ntable, and it is not a stone\xe2\x80\x99s throw from Minor Canon Corner. Ned, you\r\nare up and away to-morrow. We will carry Mr. Neville in with us, to\r\ntake a stirrup-cup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith all my heart, Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd with all mine, Mr. Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d Neville feels it impossible to say\r\nless, but would rather not go. He has an impression upon him that he\r\nhas lost hold of his temper; feels that Edwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s coolness, so far\r\nfrom being infectious, makes him red-hot.\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper, still walking in the centre, hand to shoulder on either\r\nside, beautifully turns the Refrain of a drinking song, and they all go\r\nup to his rooms. There, the first object visible, when he adds the\r\nlight of a lamp to that of the fire, is the portrait over the\r\nchimneypiece. It is not an object calculated to improve the\r\nunderstanding between the two young men, as rather awkwardly reviving\r\nthe subject of their difference. Accordingly, they both glance at it\r\nconsciously, but say nothing. Jasper, however (who would appear from\r\nhis conduct to have gained but an imperfect clue to the cause of their\r\nlate high words), directly calls attention to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou recognise that picture, Mr. Neville?\xe2\x80\x9d shading the lamp to throw\r\nthe light upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI recognise it, but it is far from flattering the original.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, you are hard upon it! It was done by Ned, who made me a present of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for that, Mr. Drood.\xe2\x80\x9d Neville apologises, with a real\r\nintention to apologise; \xe2\x80\x9cif I had known I was in the artist\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, a joke, sir, a mere joke,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin cuts in, with a provoking yawn. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nlittle humouring of Pussy\xe2\x80\x99s points! I\xe2\x80\x99m going to paint her gravely, one\r\nof these days, if she\xe2\x80\x99s good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe air of leisurely patronage and indifference with which this is\r\nsaid, as the speaker throws himself back in a chair and clasps his\r\nhands at the back of his head, as a rest for it, is very exasperating\r\nto the excitable and excited Neville. Jasper looks observantly from the\r\none to the other, slightly smiles, and turns his back to mix a jug of\r\nmulled wine at the fire. It seems to require much mixing and\r\ncompounding.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose, Mr. Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, quick to resent the indignant\r\nprotest against himself in the face of young Landless, which is fully\r\nas visible as the portrait, or the fire, or the lamp: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that\r\nif you painted the picture of your lady love\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t paint,\xe2\x80\x9d is the hasty interruption.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s your misfortune, and not your fault. You would if you could.\r\nBut if you could, I suppose you would make her (no matter what she was\r\nin reality), Juno, Minerva, Diana, and Venus, all in one. Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no lady love, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were to try my hand,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, with a boyish boastfulness\r\ngetting up in him, \xe2\x80\x9con a portrait of Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x94in earnest, mind\r\nyou; in earnest\xe2\x80\x94you should see what I could do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sister\xe2\x80\x99s consent to sit for it being first got, I suppose? As it\r\nnever will be got, I am afraid I shall never see what you can do. I\r\nmust bear the loss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper turns round from the fire, fills a large goblet glass for\r\nNeville, fills a large goblet glass for Edwin, and hands each his own;\r\nthen fills for himself, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Mr. Neville, we are to drink to my nephew, Ned. As it is his\r\nfoot that is in the stirrup\xe2\x80\x94metaphorically\xe2\x80\x94our stirrup-cup is to be\r\ndevoted to him. Ned, my dearest fellow, my love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper sets the example of nearly emptying his glass, and Neville\r\nfollows it. Edwin Drood says, \xe2\x80\x9cThank you both very much,\xe2\x80\x9d and follows\r\nthe double example.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at him,\xe2\x80\x9d cries Jasper, stretching out his hand admiringly and\r\ntenderly, though rallyingly too. \xe2\x80\x9cSee where he lounges so easily, Mr.\r\nNeville! The world is all before him where to choose. A life of\r\nstirring work and interest, a life of change and excitement, a life of\r\ndomestic ease and love! Look at him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s face has become quickly and remarkably flushed with the\r\nwine; so has the face of Neville Landless. Edwin still sits thrown back\r\nin his chair, making that rest of clasped hands for his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee how little he heeds it all!\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper proceeds in a bantering vein.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is hardly worth his while to pluck the golden fruit that hangs ripe\r\non the tree for him. And yet consider the contrast, Mr. Neville. You\r\nand I have no prospect of stirring work and interest, or of change and\r\nexcitement, or of domestic ease and love. You and I have no prospect\r\n(unless you are more fortunate than I am, which may easily be), but the\r\ntedious unchanging round of this dull place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my soul, Jack,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin, complacently, \xe2\x80\x9cI feel quite\r\napologetic for having my way smoothed as you describe. But you know\r\nwhat I know, Jack, and it may not be so very easy as it seems, after\r\nall. May it, Pussy?\xe2\x80\x9d To the portrait, with a snap of his thumb and\r\nfinger. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have got to hit it off yet; haven\xe2\x80\x99t we, Pussy? You know\r\nwhat I mean, Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: On dangerous ground]\r\n\r\n\r\nHis speech has become thick and indistinct. Jasper, quiet and\r\nself-possessed, looks to Neville, as expecting his answer or comment.\r\nWhen Neville speaks, _his_ speech is also thick and indistinct.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might have been better for Mr. Drood to have known some hardships,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe says, defiantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray,\xe2\x80\x9d retorts Edwin, turning merely his eyes in that direction, \xe2\x80\x9cpray\r\nwhy might it have been better for Mr. Drood to have known some\r\nhardships?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy,\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper assents, with an air of interest; \xe2\x80\x9clet us know why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause they might have made him more sensible,\xe2\x80\x9d says Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cof\r\ngood fortune that is not by any means necessarily the result of his own\r\nmerits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper quickly looks to his nephew for his rejoinder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave _you_ known hardships, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin Drood, sitting\r\nupright.\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper quickly looks to the other for his retort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what have they made you sensible of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s play of eyes between the two holds good throughout the\r\ndialogue, to the end.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have told you once before to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have done nothing of the sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you I have. That you take a great deal too much upon yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou added something else to that, if I remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I did say something else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said that in the part of the world I come from, you would be called\r\nto account for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly there?\xe2\x80\x9d cries Edwin Drood, with a contemptuous laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cA long way\r\noff, I believe? Yes; I see! That part of the world is at a safe\r\ndistance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay here, then,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoins the other, rising in a fury. \xe2\x80\x9cSay anywhere!\r\nYour vanity is intolerable, your conceit is beyond endurance; you talk\r\nas if you were some rare and precious prize, instead of a common\r\nboaster. You are a common fellow, and a common boaster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh, pooh,\xe2\x80\x9d says Edwin Drood, equally furious, but more collected;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chow should you know? You may know a black common fellow, or a black\r\ncommon boaster, when you see him (and no doubt you have a large\r\nacquaintance that way); but you are no judge of white men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis insulting allusion to his dark skin infuriates Neville to that\r\nviolent degree, that he flings the dregs of his wine at Edwin Drood,\r\nand is in the act of flinging the goblet after it, when his arm is\r\ncaught in the nick of time by Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNed, my dear fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d he cries in a loud voice; \xe2\x80\x9cI entreat you, I\r\ncommand you, to be still!\xe2\x80\x9d There has been a rush of all the three, and\r\na clattering of glasses and overturning of chairs. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Neville, for\r\nshame! Give this glass to me. Open your hand, sir. I WILL have it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Neville throws him off, and pauses for an instant, in a raging\r\npassion, with the goblet yet in his uplifted hand. Then, he dashes it\r\ndown under the grate, with such force that the broken splinters fly out\r\nagain in a shower; and he leaves the house.\r\n\r\nWhen he first emerges into the night air, nothing around him is still\r\nor steady; nothing around him shows like what it is; he only knows that\r\nhe stands with a bare head in the midst of a blood-red whirl, waiting\r\nto be struggled with, and to struggle to the death.\r\n\r\nBut, nothing happening, and the moon looking down upon him as if he\r\nwere dead after a fit of wrath, he holds his steam-hammer beating head\r\nand heart, and staggers away. Then, he becomes half-conscious of having\r\nheard himself bolted and barred out, like a dangerous animal; and\r\nthinks what shall he do?\r\n\r\nSome wildly passionate ideas of the river dissolve under the spell of\r\nthe moonlight on the Cathedral and the graves, and the remembrance of\r\nhis sister, and the thought of what he owes to the good man who has but\r\nthat very day won his confidence and given him his pledge. He repairs\r\nto Minor Canon Corner, and knocks softly at the door.\r\n\r\nIt is Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s custom to sit up last of the early household,\r\nvery softly touching his piano and practising his favourite parts in\r\nconcerted vocal music. The south wind that goes where it lists, by way\r\nof Minor Canon Corner on a still night, is not more subdued than Mr.\r\nCrisparkle at such times, regardful of the slumbers of the china\r\nshepherdess.\r\n\r\nHis knock is immediately answered by Mr. Crisparkle himself. When he\r\nopens the door, candle in hand, his cheerful face falls, and\r\ndisappointed amazement is in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Neville! In this disorder! Where have you been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been to Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s, sir. With his nephew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Minor Canon props him by the elbow with a strong hand (in a\r\nstrictly scientific manner, worthy of his morning trainings), and turns\r\nhim into his own little book-room, and shuts the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have begun ill, sir. I have begun dreadfully ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo true. You are not sober, Mr. Neville.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I am not, sir, though I can satisfy you at another time\r\nthat I have had a very little indeed to drink, and that it overcame me\r\nin the strangest and most sudden manner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Neville, Mr. Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d says the Minor Canon, shaking his head with\r\na sorrowful smile; \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard that said before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think\xe2\x80\x94my mind is much confused, but I think\xe2\x80\x94it is equally true of\r\nMr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s nephew, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d is the dry rejoinder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe quarrelled, sir. He insulted me most grossly. He had heated that\r\ntigerish blood I told you of to-day, before then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoins the Minor Canon, mildly, but firmly: \xe2\x80\x9cI request\r\nyou not to speak to me with that clenched right hand. Unclench it, if\r\nyou please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe goaded me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d pursues the young man, instantly obeying, \xe2\x80\x9cbeyond\r\nmy power of endurance. I cannot say whether or no he meant it at first,\r\nbut he did it. He certainly meant it at last. In short, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d with an\r\nirrepressible outburst, \xe2\x80\x9cin the passion into which he lashed me, I\r\nwould have cut him down if I could, and I tried to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have clenched that hand again,\xe2\x80\x9d is Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s quiet\r\ncommentary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know your room, for I showed it you before dinner; but I will\r\naccompany you to it once more. Your arm, if you please. Softly, for the\r\nhouse is all a-bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScooping his hand into the same scientific elbow-rest as before, and\r\nbacking it up with the inert strength of his arm, as skilfully as a\r\nPolice Expert, and with an apparent repose quite unattainable by\r\nnovices, Mr. Crisparkle conducts his pupil to the pleasant and orderly\r\nold room prepared for him. Arrived there, the young man throws himself\r\ninto a chair, and, flinging his arms upon his reading-table, rests his\r\nhead upon them with an air of wretched self-reproach.\r\n\r\nThe gentle Minor Canon has had it in his thoughts to leave the room,\r\nwithout a word. But looking round at the door, and seeing this dejected\r\nfigure, he turns back to it, touches it with a mild hand, says \xe2\x80\x9cGood\r\nnight!\xe2\x80\x9d A sob is his only acknowledgment. He might have had many a\r\nworse; perhaps, could have had few better.\r\n\r\nAnother soft knock at the outer door attracts his attention as he goes\r\ndown-stairs. He opens it to Mr. Jasper, holding in his hand the pupil\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have had an awful scene with him,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, in a low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas it been so bad as that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMurderous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle remonstrates: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no. Do not use such strong\r\nwords.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe might have laid my dear boy dead at my feet. It is no fault of his,\r\nthat he did not. But that I was, through the mercy of God, swift and\r\nstrong with him, he would have cut him down on my hearth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe phrase smites home. \xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d thinks Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9chis own words!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing what I have seen to-night, and hearing what I have heard,\xe2\x80\x9d adds\r\nJasper, with great earnestness, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never know peace of mind when\r\nthere is danger of those two coming together, with no one else to\r\ninterfere. It was horrible. There is something of the tiger in his dark\r\nblood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d thinks Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cso he said!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, my dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d pursues Jasper, taking his hand, \xe2\x80\x9ceven you, have\r\naccepted a dangerous charge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou need have no fear for me, Jasper,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Mr. Crisparkle, with a\r\nquiet smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI have none for myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have none for myself,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Jasper, with an emphasis on the last\r\npronoun, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I am not, nor am I in the way of being, the object of\r\nhis hostility. But you may be, and my dear boy has been. Good night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle goes in, with the hat that has so easily, so almost\r\nimperceptibly, acquired the right to be hung up in his hall; hangs it\r\nup; and goes thoughtfully to bed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\nBIRDS IN THE BUSH\r\n\r\n\r\nRosa, having no relation that she knew of in the world, had, from the\r\nseventh year of her age, known no home but the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and no\r\nmother but Miss Twinkleton. Her remembrance of her own mother was of a\r\npretty little creature like herself (not much older than herself it\r\nseemed to her), who had been brought home in her father\xe2\x80\x99s arms,\r\ndrowned. The fatal accident had happened at a party of pleasure. Every\r\nfold and colour in the pretty summer dress, and even the long wet hair,\r\nwith scattered petals of ruined flowers still clinging to it, as the\r\ndead young figure, in its sad, sad beauty lay upon the bed, were fixed\r\nindelibly in Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s recollection. So were the wild despair and the\r\nsubsequent bowed-down grief of her poor young father, who died\r\nbroken-hearted on the first anniversary of that hard day.\r\n\r\nThe betrothal of Rosa grew out of the soothing of his year of mental\r\ndistress by his fast friend and old college companion, Drood: who\r\nlikewise had been left a widower in his youth. But he, too, went the\r\nsilent road into which all earthly pilgrimages merge, some sooner, and\r\nsome later; and thus the young couple had come to be as they were.\r\n\r\nThe atmosphere of pity surrounding the little orphan girl when she\r\nfirst came to Cloisterham, had never cleared away. It had taken\r\nbrighter hues as she grew older, happier, prettier; now it had been\r\ngolden, now roseate, and now azure; but it had always adorned her with\r\nsome soft light of its own. The general desire to console and caress\r\nher, had caused her to be treated in the beginning as a child much\r\nyounger than her years; the same desire had caused her to be still\r\npetted when she was a child no longer. Who should be her favourite, who\r\nshould anticipate this or that small present, or do her this or that\r\nsmall service; who should take her home for the holidays; who should\r\nwrite to her the oftenest when they were separated, and whom she would\r\nmost rejoice to see again when they were reunited; even these gentle\r\nrivalries were not without their slight dashes of bitterness in the\r\nNuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. Well for the poor Nuns in their day, if they hid no harder\r\nstrife under their veils and rosaries!\r\n\r\nThus Rosa had grown to be an amiable, giddy, wilful, winning little\r\ncreature; spoilt, in the sense of counting upon kindness from all\r\naround her; but not in the sense of repaying it with indifference.\r\nPossessing an exhaustless well of affection in her nature, its\r\nsparkling waters had freshened and brightened the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House for\r\nyears, and yet its depths had never yet been moved: what might betide\r\nwhen that came to pass; what developing changes might fall upon the\r\nheedless head, and light heart, then; remained to be seen.\r\n\r\nBy what means the news that there had been a quarrel between the two\r\nyoung men overnight, involving even some kind of onslaught by Mr.\r\nNeville upon Edwin Drood, got into Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s establishment\r\nbefore breakfast, it is impossible to say. Whether it was brought in by\r\nthe birds of the air, or came blowing in with the very air itself, when\r\nthe casement windows were set open; whether the baker brought it\r\nkneaded into the bread, or the milkman delivered it as part of the\r\nadulteration of his milk; or the housemaids, beating the dust out of\r\ntheir mats against the gateposts, received it in exchange deposited on\r\nthe mats by the town atmosphere; certain it is that the news permeated\r\nevery gable of the old building before Miss Twinkleton was down, and\r\nthat Miss Twinkleton herself received it through Mrs. Tisher, while yet\r\nin the act of dressing; or (as she might have expressed the phrase to a\r\nparent or guardian of a mythological turn) of sacrificing to the\r\nGraces.\r\n\r\nMiss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother had thrown a bottle at Mr. Edwin Drood.\r\n\r\nMiss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother had thrown a knife at Mr. Edwin Drood.\r\n\r\nA knife became suggestive of a fork; and Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother had\r\nthrown a fork at Mr. Edwin Drood.\r\n\r\nAs in the governing precedence of Peter Piper, alleged to have picked\r\nthe peck of pickled pepper, it was held physically desirable to have\r\nevidence of the existence of the peck of pickled pepper which Peter\r\nPiper was alleged to have picked; so, in this case, it was held\r\npsychologically important to know why Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother threw a\r\nbottle, knife, or fork-or bottle, knife, _and_ fork\xe2\x80\x94for the cook had\r\nbeen given to understand it was all three\xe2\x80\x94at Mr. Edwin Drood?\r\n\r\nWell, then. Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother had said he admired Miss Bud. Mr.\r\nEdwin Drood had said to Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother that he had no business\r\nto admire Miss Bud. Miss Landless\xe2\x80\x99s brother had then \xe2\x80\x9cup\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x9d (this was\r\nthe cook\xe2\x80\x99s exact information) with the bottle, knife, fork, and\r\ndecanter (the decanter now coolly flying at everybody\xe2\x80\x99s head, without\r\nthe least introduction), and thrown them all at Mr. Edwin Drood.\r\n\r\nPoor little Rosa put a forefinger into each of her ears when these\r\nrumours began to circulate, and retired into a corner, beseeching not\r\nto be told any more; but Miss Landless, begging permission of Miss\r\nTwinkleton to go and speak with her brother, and pretty plainly showing\r\nthat she would take it if it were not given, struck out the more\r\ndefinite course of going to Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s for accurate intelligence.\r\n\r\nWhen she came back (being first closeted with Miss Twinkleton, in order\r\nthat anything objectionable in her tidings might be retained by that\r\ndiscreet filter), she imparted to Rosa only, what had taken place;\r\ndwelling with a flushed cheek on the provocation her brother had\r\nreceived, but almost limiting it to that last gross affront as crowning\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csome other words between them,\xe2\x80\x9d and, out of consideration for her new\r\nfriend, passing lightly over the fact that the other words had\r\noriginated in her lover\xe2\x80\x99s taking things in general so very easily. To\r\nRosa direct, she brought a petition from her brother that she would\r\nforgive him; and, having delivered it with sisterly earnestness, made\r\nan end of the subject.\r\n\r\nIt was reserved for Miss Twinkleton to tone down the public mind of the\r\nNuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. That lady, therefore, entering in a stately manner what\r\nplebeians might have called the school-room, but what, in the patrician\r\nlanguage of the head of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, was euphuistically, not to say\r\nround-aboutedly, denominated \xe2\x80\x9cthe apartment allotted to study,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nsaying with a forensic air, \xe2\x80\x9cLadies!\xe2\x80\x9d all rose. Mrs. Tisher at the same\r\ntime grouped herself behind her chief, as representing Queen\r\nElizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s first historical female friend at Tilbury fort. Miss\r\nTwinkleton then proceeded to remark that Rumour, Ladies, had been\r\nrepresented by the bard of Avon\xe2\x80\x94needless were it to mention the\r\nimmortal SHAKESPEARE, also called the Swan of his native river, not\r\nimprobably with some reference to the ancient superstition that that\r\nbird of graceful plumage (Miss Jennings will please stand upright) sang\r\nsweetly on the approach of death, for which we have no ornithological\r\nauthority,\xe2\x80\x94Rumour, Ladies, had been represented by that bard\xe2\x80\x94hem!\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n        \xe2\x80\x9cwho drew\r\nThe celebrated Jew,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nas painted full of tongues. Rumour in Cloisterham (Miss Ferdinand will\r\nhonour me with her attention) was no exception to the great limner\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nportrait of Rumour elsewhere. A slight _fracas_ between two young\r\ngentlemen occurring last night within a hundred miles of these peaceful\r\nwalls (Miss Ferdinand, being apparently incorrigible, will have the\r\nkindness to write out this evening, in the original language, the first\r\nfour fables of our vivacious neighbour, Monsieur La Fontaine) had been\r\nvery grossly exaggerated by Rumour\xe2\x80\x99s voice. In the first alarm and\r\nanxiety arising from our sympathy with a sweet young friend, not wholly\r\nto be dissociated from one of the gladiators in the bloodless arena in\r\nquestion (the impropriety of Miss Reynolds\xe2\x80\x99s appearing to stab herself\r\nin the hand with a pin, is far too obvious, and too glaringly\r\nunladylike, to be pointed out), we descended from our maiden elevation\r\nto discuss this uncongenial and this unfit theme. Responsible inquiries\r\nhaving assured us that it was but one of those \xe2\x80\x9cairy nothings\xe2\x80\x9d pointed\r\nat by the Poet (whose name and date of birth Miss Giggles will supply\r\nwithin half an hour), we would now discard the subject, and concentrate\r\nour minds upon the grateful labours of the day.\r\n\r\nBut the subject so survived all day, nevertheless, that Miss Ferdinand\r\ngot into new trouble by surreptitiously clapping on a paper moustache\r\nat dinner-time, and going through the motions of aiming a water-bottle\r\nat Miss Giggles, who drew a table-spoon in defence.\r\n\r\nNow, Rosa thought of this unlucky quarrel a great deal, and thought of\r\nit with an uncomfortable feeling that she was involved in it, as cause,\r\nor consequence, or what not, through being in a false position\r\naltogether as to her marriage engagement. Never free from such\r\nuneasiness when she was with her affianced husband, it was not likely\r\nthat she would be free from it when they were apart. To-day, too, she\r\nwas cast in upon herself, and deprived of the relief of talking freely\r\nwith her new friend, because the quarrel had been with Helena\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbrother, and Helena undisguisedly avoided the subject as a delicate and\r\ndifficult one to herself. At this critical time, of all times, Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nguardian was announced as having come to see her.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious had been well selected for his trust, as a man of\r\nincorruptible integrity, but certainly for no other appropriate quality\r\ndiscernible on the surface. He was an arid, sandy man, who, if he had\r\nbeen put into a grinding-mill, looked as if he would have ground\r\nimmediately into high-dried snuff. He had a scanty flat crop of hair,\r\nin colour and consistency like some very mangy yellow fur tippet; it\r\nwas so unlike hair, that it must have been a wig, but for the\r\nstupendous improbability of anybody\xe2\x80\x99s voluntarily sporting such a head.\r\nThe little play of feature that his face presented, was cut deep into\r\nit, in a few hard curves that made it more like work; and he had\r\ncertain notches in his forehead, which looked as though Nature had been\r\nabout to touch them into sensibility or refinement, when she had\r\nimpatiently thrown away the chisel, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cI really cannot be\r\nworried to finish off this man; let him go as he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith too great length of throat at his upper end, and too much\r\nankle-bone and heel at his lower; with an awkward and hesitating\r\nmanner; with a shambling walk; and with what is called a near\r\nsight\xe2\x80\x94which perhaps prevented his observing how much white cotton\r\nstocking he displayed to the public eye, in contrast with his black\r\nsuit\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Grewgious still had some strange capacity in him of making on\r\nthe whole an agreeable impression.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious was discovered by his ward, much discomfited by being in\r\nMiss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s company in Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s own sacred room. Dim\r\nforebodings of being examined in something, and not coming well out of\r\nit, seemed to oppress the poor gentleman when found in these\r\ncircumstances.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, how do you do? I am glad to see you. My dear, how much\r\nimproved you are. Permit me to hand you a chair, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton rose at her little writing-table, saying, with general\r\nsweetness, as to the polite Universe: \xe2\x80\x9cWill you permit me to retire?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy no means, madam, on my account. I beg that you will not move.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must entreat permission to _move_,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Miss Twinkleton,\r\nrepeating the word with a charming grace; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I will not withdraw,\r\nsince you are so obliging. If I wheel my desk to this corner window,\r\nshall I be in the way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadam! In the way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind.\xe2\x80\x94Rosa, my dear, you will be under no restraint, I am\r\nsure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Mr. Grewgious, left by the fire with Rosa, said again: \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear,\r\nhow do you do? I am glad to see you, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d And having waited for\r\nher to sit down, sat down himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy visits,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9care, like those of the angels\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nthat I compare myself to an angel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by any means,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cI merely refer to my\r\nvisits, which are few and far between. The angels are, we know very\r\nwell, up-stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton looked round with a kind of stiff stare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI refer, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, laying his hand on Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s, as\r\nthe possibility thrilled through his frame of his otherwise seeming to\r\ntake the awful liberty of calling Miss Twinkleton my dear; \xe2\x80\x9cI refer to\r\nthe other young ladies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton resumed her writing.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious, with a sense of not having managed his opening point\r\nquite as neatly as he might have desired, smoothed his head from back\r\nto front as if he had just dived, and were pressing the water out\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nsmoothing action, however superfluous, was habitual with him\xe2\x80\x94and took a\r\npocket-book from his coat-pocket, and a stump of black-lead pencil from\r\nhis waistcoat-pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning the leaves: \xe2\x80\x9cI made a guiding memorandum or\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94as I usually do, for I have no conversational powers whatever\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nwhich I will, with your permission, my dear, refer. \xe2\x80\x98Well and happy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nTruly. You are well and happy, my dear? You look so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor which,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with a bend of his head towards the\r\ncorner window, \xe2\x80\x9cour warmest acknowledgments are due, and I am sure are\r\nrendered, to the maternal kindness and the constant care and\r\nconsideration of the lady whom I have now the honour to see before me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis point, again, made but a lame departure from Mr. Grewgious, and\r\nnever got to its destination; for, Miss Twinkleton, feeling that the\r\ncourtesies required her to be by this time quite outside the\r\nconversation, was biting the end of her pen, and looking upward, as\r\nwaiting for the descent of an idea from any member of the Celestial\r\nNine who might have one to spare.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious smoothed his smooth head again, and then made another\r\nreference to his pocket-book; lining out \xe2\x80\x9cwell and happy,\xe2\x80\x9d as disposed\r\nof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Pounds, shillings, and pence,\xe2\x80\x99 is my next note. A dry subject for a\r\nyoung lady, but an important subject too. Life is pounds, shillings,\r\nand pence. Death is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d A sudden recollection of the death of her two\r\nparents seemed to stop him, and he said in a softer tone, and evidently\r\ninserting the negative as an after-thought: \xe2\x80\x9cDeath is _not_ pounds,\r\nshillings, and pence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice was as hard and dry as himself, and Fancy might have ground\r\nit straight, like himself, into high-dried snuff. And yet, through the\r\nvery limited means of expression that he possessed, he seemed to\r\nexpress kindness. If Nature had but finished him off, kindness might\r\nhave been recognisable in his face at this moment. But if the notches\r\nin his forehead wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t fuse together, and if his face would work and\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t play, what could he do, poor man!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Pounds, shillings, and pence.\xe2\x80\x99 You find your allowance always\r\nsufficient for your wants, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa wanted for nothing, and therefore it was ample.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are not in debt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa laughed at the idea of being in debt. It seemed, to her\r\ninexperience, a comical vagary of the imagination. Mr. Grewgious\r\nstretched his near sight to be sure that this was her view of the case.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as comment, with a furtive glance towards Miss\r\nTwinkleton, and lining out pounds, shillings, and pence: \xe2\x80\x9cI spoke of\r\nhaving got among the angels! So I did!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa felt what his next memorandum would prove to be, and was blushing\r\nand folding a crease in her dress with one embarrassed hand, long\r\nbefore he found it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Marriage.\xe2\x80\x99 Hem!\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Grewgious carried his smoothing hand down over\r\nhis eyes and nose, and even chin, before drawing his chair a little\r\nnearer, and speaking a little more confidentially: \xe2\x80\x9cI now touch, my\r\ndear, upon the point that is the direct cause of my troubling you with\r\nthe present visit. Otherwise, being a particularly Angular man, I\r\nshould not have intruded here. I am the last man to intrude into a\r\nsphere for which I am so entirely unfitted. I feel, on these premises,\r\nas if I was a bear\xe2\x80\x94with the cramp\xe2\x80\x94in a youthful Cotillon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis ungainliness gave him enough of the air of his simile to set Rosa\r\noff laughing heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt strikes you in the same light,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with perfect\r\ncalmness. \xe2\x80\x9cJust so. To return to my memorandum. Mr. Edwin has been to\r\nand fro here, as was arranged. You have mentioned that, in your\r\nquarterly letters to me. And you like him, and he likes you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _like_ him very much, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I said, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d returned her guardian, for whose ear the timid\r\nemphasis was much too fine. \xe2\x80\x9cGood. And you correspond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe write to one another,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, pouting, as she recalled their\r\nepistolary differences.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch is the meaning that I attach to the word \xe2\x80\x98correspond\xe2\x80\x99 in this\r\napplication, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cGood. All goes well, time\r\nworks on, and at this next Christmas-time it will become necessary, as\r\na matter of form, to give the exemplary lady in the corner window, to\r\nwhom we are so much indebted, business notice of your departure in the\r\nensuing half-year. Your relations with her are far more than business\r\nrelations, no doubt; but a residue of business remains in them, and\r\nbusiness is business ever. I am a particularly Angular man,\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded\r\nMr. Grewgious, as if it suddenly occurred to him to mention it, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\nam not used to give anything away. If, for these two reasons, some\r\ncompetent Proxy would give _you_ away, I should take it very kindly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa intimated, with her eyes on the ground, that she thought a\r\nsubstitute might be found, if required.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, surely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cFor instance, the gentleman who\r\nteaches Dancing here\xe2\x80\x94he would know how to do it with graceful\r\npropriety. He would advance and retire in a manner satisfactory to the\r\nfeelings of the officiating clergyman, and of yourself, and the\r\nbridegroom, and all parties concerned. I am\xe2\x80\x94I am a particularly Angular\r\nman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, as if he had made up his mind to screw it out\r\nat last: \xe2\x80\x9cand should only blunder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa sat still and silent. Perhaps her mind had not got quite so far as\r\nthe ceremony yet, but was lagging on the way there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMemorandum, \xe2\x80\x98Will.\xe2\x80\x99 Now, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, referring to\r\nhis notes, disposing of \xe2\x80\x9cMarriage\xe2\x80\x9d with his pencil, and taking a paper\r\nfrom his pocket; \xe2\x80\x9calthough. I have before possessed you with the\r\ncontents of your father\xe2\x80\x99s will, I think it right at this time to leave\r\na certified copy of it in your hands. And although Mr. Edwin is also\r\naware of its contents, I think it right at this time likewise to place\r\na certified copy of it in Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s hand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in his own!\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa, looking up quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cCannot the copy go\r\nto Eddy himself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes, my dear, if you particularly wish it; but I spoke of Mr.\r\nJasper as being his trustee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do particularly wish it, if you please,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, hurriedly and\r\nearnestly; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like Mr. Jasper to come between us, in any way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is natural, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cthat your young\r\nhusband should be all in all. Yes. You observe that I say, I suppose.\r\nThe fact is, I am a particularly Unnatural man, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know from\r\nmy own knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa looked at him with some wonder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained, \xe2\x80\x9cthat young ways were never my ways. I was the\r\nonly offspring of parents far advanced in life, and I half believe I\r\nwas born advanced in life myself. No personality is intended towards\r\nthe name you will so soon change, when I remark that while the general\r\ngrowth of people seem to have come into existence, buds, I seem to have\r\ncome into existence a chip. I was a chip\xe2\x80\x94and a very dry one\xe2\x80\x94when I\r\nfirst became aware of myself. Respecting the other certified copy, your\r\nwish shall be complied with. Respecting your inheritance, I think you\r\nknow all. It is an annuity of two hundred and fifty pounds. The savings\r\nupon that annuity, and some other items to your credit, all duly\r\ncarried to account, with vouchers, will place you in possession of a\r\nlump-sum of money, rather exceeding Seventeen Hundred Pounds. I am\r\nempowered to advance the cost of your preparations for your marriage\r\nout of that fund. All is told.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you please tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, taking the paper with a prettily\r\nknitted brow, but not opening it: \xe2\x80\x9cwhether I am right in what I am\r\ngoing to say? I can understand what you tell me, so very much better\r\nthan what I read in law-writings. My poor papa and Eddy\xe2\x80\x99s father made\r\ntheir agreement together, as very dear and firm and fast friends, in\r\norder that we, too, might be very dear and firm and fast friends after\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the lasting good of both of us, and the lasting happiness of both\r\nof us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat we might be to one another even much more than they had been to\r\none another?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not bound upon Eddy, and it was not bound upon me, by any\r\nforfeit, in case\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be agitated, my dear. In the case that it brings tears into your\r\naffectionate eyes even to picture to yourself\xe2\x80\x94in the case of your not\r\nmarrying one another\xe2\x80\x94no, no forfeiture on either side. You would then\r\nhave been my ward until you were of age. No worse would have befallen\r\nyou. Bad enough perhaps!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Eddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would have come into his partnership derived from his father, and\r\ninto its arrears to his credit (if any), on attaining his majority,\r\njust as now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa, with her perplexed face and knitted brow, bit the corner of her\r\nattested copy, as she sat with her head on one side, looking\r\nabstractedly on the floor, and smoothing it with her foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn short,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cthis betrothal is a wish, a sentiment,\r\na friendly project, tenderly expressed on both sides. That it was\r\nstrongly felt, and that there was a lively hope that it would prosper,\r\nthere can be no doubt. When you were both children, you began to be\r\naccustomed to it, and it _has_ prospered. But circumstances alter\r\ncases; and I made this visit to-day, partly, indeed principally, to\r\ndischarge myself of the duty of telling you, my dear, that two young\r\npeople can only be betrothed in marriage (except as a matter of\r\nconvenience, and therefore mockery and misery) of their own free will,\r\ntheir own attachment, and their own assurance (it may or it may not\r\nprove a mistaken one, but we must take our chance of that), that they\r\nare suited to each other, and will make each other happy. Is it to be\r\nsupposed, for example, that if either of your fathers were living now,\r\nand had any mistrust on that subject, his mind would not be changed by\r\nthe change of circumstances involved in the change of your years?\r\nUntenable, unreasonable, inconclusive, and preposterous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious said all this, as if he were reading it aloud; or, still\r\nmore, as if he were repeating a lesson. So expressionless of any\r\napproach to spontaneity were his face and manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have now, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, blurring out \xe2\x80\x9cWill\xe2\x80\x9d with his pencil,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdischarged myself of what is doubtless a formal duty in this case, but\r\nstill a duty in such a case. Memorandum, \xe2\x80\x98Wishes.\xe2\x80\x99 My dear, is there\r\nany wish of yours that I can further?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa shook her head, with an almost plaintive air of hesitation in want\r\nof help.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there any instruction that I can take from you with reference to\r\nyour affairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I should like to settle them with Eddy first, if you please,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRosa, plaiting the crease in her dress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, surely,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cYou two should be of one\r\nmind in all things. Is the young gentleman expected shortly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has gone away only this morning. He will be back at Christmas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing could happen better. You will, on his return at Christmas,\r\narrange all matters of detail with him; you will then communicate with\r\nme; and I will discharge myself (as a mere business acquaintance) of my\r\nbusiness responsibilities towards the accomplished lady in the corner\r\nwindow. They will accrue at that season.\xe2\x80\x9d Blurring pencil once again.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMemorandum, \xe2\x80\x98Leave.\xe2\x80\x99 Yes. I will now, my dear, take my leave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, rising, as he jerked out of his chair in his\r\nungainly way: \xe2\x80\x9ccould I ask you, most kindly to come to me at Christmas,\r\nif I had anything particular to say to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, certainly, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined; apparently\xe2\x80\x94if such a word can\r\nbe used of one who had no apparent lights or shadows about\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94complimented by the question. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a particularly Angular man, I do\r\nnot fit smoothly into the social circle, and consequently I have no\r\nother engagement at Christmas-time than to partake, on the\r\ntwenty-fifth, of a boiled turkey and celery sauce with a\xe2\x80\x94with a\r\nparticularly Angular clerk I have the good fortune to possess, whose\r\nfather, being a Norfolk farmer, sends him up (the turkey up), as a\r\npresent to me, from the neighbourhood of Norwich. I should be quite\r\nproud of your wishing to see me, my dear. As a professional Receiver of\r\nrents, so very few people _do_ wish to see me, that the novelty would\r\nbe bracing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor his ready acquiescence, the grateful Rosa put her hands upon his\r\nshoulders, stood on tiptoe, and instantly kissed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord bless me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you, my dear! The honour\r\nis almost equal to the pleasure. Miss Twinkleton, madam, I have had a\r\nmost satisfactory conversation with my ward, and I will now release you\r\nfrom the incumbrance of my presence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Miss Twinkleton, rising with a gracious\r\ncondescension: \xe2\x80\x9csay not incumbrance. Not so, by any means. I cannot\r\npermit you to say so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, madam. I have read in the newspapers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\nstammering a little, \xe2\x80\x9cthat when a distinguished visitor (not that I am\r\none: far from it) goes to a school (not that this is one: far from it),\r\nhe asks for a holiday, or some sort of grace. It being now the\r\nafternoon in the\xe2\x80\x94College\xe2\x80\x94of which you are the eminent head, the young\r\nladies might gain nothing, except in name, by having the rest of the\r\nday allowed them. But if there is any young lady at all under a cloud,\r\nmight I solicit\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mr. Grewgious, Mr. Grewgious!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Miss Twinkleton, with a\r\nchastely-rallying forefinger. \xe2\x80\x9cO you gentlemen, you gentlemen! Fie for\r\nshame, that you are so hard upon us poor maligned disciplinarians of\r\nour sex, for your sakes! But as Miss Ferdinand is at present weighed\r\ndown by an incubus\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Miss Twinkleton might have said a pen-and-ink-ubus\r\nof writing out Monsieur La Fontaine\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cgo to her, Rosa my dear, and tell\r\nher the penalty is remitted, in deference to the intercession of your\r\nguardian, Mr. Grewgious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton here achieved a curtsey, suggestive of marvels\r\nhappening to her respected legs, and which she came out of nobly, three\r\nyards behind her starting-point.\r\n\r\nAs he held it incumbent upon him to call on Mr. Jasper before leaving\r\nCloisterham, Mr. Grewgious went to the gatehouse, and climbed its\r\npostern stair. But Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s door being closed, and presenting on a\r\nslip of paper the word \xe2\x80\x9cCathedral,\xe2\x80\x9d the fact of its being service-time\r\nwas borne into the mind of Mr. Grewgious. So he descended the stair\r\nagain, and, crossing the Close, paused at the great western\r\nfolding-door of the Cathedral, which stood open on the fine and bright,\r\nthough short-lived, afternoon, for the airing of the place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, peeping in, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s like looking down the\r\nthroat of Old Time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOld Time heaved a mouldy sigh from tomb and arch and vault; and gloomy\r\nshadows began to deepen in corners; and damps began to rise from green\r\npatches of stone; and jewels, cast upon the pavement of the nave from\r\nstained glass by the declining sun, began to perish. Within the\r\ngrill-gate of the chancel, up the steps surmounted loomingly by the\r\nfast-darkening organ, white robes could be dimly seen, and one feeble\r\nvoice, rising and falling in a cracked, monotonous mutter, could at\r\nintervals be faintly heard. In the free outer air, the river, the green\r\npastures, and the brown arable lands, the teeming hills and dales, were\r\nreddened by the sunset: while the distant little windows in windmills\r\nand farm homesteads, shone, patches of bright beaten gold. In the\r\nCathedral, all became gray, murky, and sepulchral, and the cracked\r\nmonotonous mutter went on like a dying voice, until the organ and the\r\nchoir burst forth, and drowned it in a sea of music. Then, the sea\r\nfell, and the dying voice made another feeble effort, and then the sea\r\nrose high, and beat its life out, and lashed the roof, and surged among\r\nthe arches, and pierced the heights of the great tower; and then the\r\nsea was dry, and all was still.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious had by that time walked to the chancel-steps, where he\r\nmet the living waters coming out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d Thus Jasper accosted him, rather quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nhave not been sent for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, not at all. I came down of my own accord. I have been to\r\nmy pretty ward\xe2\x80\x99s, and am now homeward bound again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou found her thriving?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlooming indeed. Most blooming. I merely came to tell her, seriously,\r\nwhat a betrothal by deceased parents is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is it\xe2\x80\x94according to your judgment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious noticed the whiteness of the lips that asked the\r\nquestion, and put it down to the chilling account of the Cathedral.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI merely came to tell her that it could not be considered binding,\r\nagainst any such reason for its dissolution as a want of affection, or\r\nwant of disposition to carry it into effect, on the side of either\r\nparty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask, had you any especial reason for telling her that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious answered somewhat sharply: \xe2\x80\x9cThe especial reason of doing\r\nmy duty, sir. Simply that.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he added: \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Mr. Jasper; I know\r\nyour affection for your nephew, and that you are quick to feel on his\r\nbehalf. I assure you that this implies not the least doubt of, or\r\ndisrespect to, your nephew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou could not,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Jasper, with a friendly pressure of his arm,\r\nas they walked on side by side, \xe2\x80\x9cspeak more handsomely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious pulled off his hat to smooth his head, and, having\r\nsmoothed it, nodded it contentedly, and put his hat on again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will wager,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, smiling\xe2\x80\x94his lips were still so white that\r\nhe was conscious of it, and bit and moistened them while speaking: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwill wager that she hinted no wish to be released from Ned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will win your wager, if you do,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nshould allow some margin for little maidenly delicacies in a young\r\nmotherless creature, under such circumstances, I suppose; it is not in\r\nmy line; what do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere can be no doubt of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you say so. Because,\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded Mr. Grewgious, who had all\r\nthis time very knowingly felt his way round to action on his\r\nremembrance of what she had said of Jasper himself: \xe2\x80\x9cbecause she seems\r\nto have some little delicate instinct that all preliminary arrangements\r\nhad best be made between Mr. Edwin Drood and herself, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see?\r\nShe don\xe2\x80\x99t want us, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper touched himself on the breast, and said, somewhat indistinctly:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious touched himself on the breast, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cI mean us.\r\nTherefore, let them have their little discussions and councils\r\ntogether, when Mr. Edwin Drood comes back here at Christmas; and then\r\nyou and I will step in, and put the final touches to the business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, you settled with her that you would come back at Christmas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nobserved Jasper. \xe2\x80\x9cI see! Mr. Grewgious, as you quite fairly said just\r\nnow, there is such an exceptional attachment between my nephew and me,\r\nthat I am more sensitive for the dear, fortunate, happy, happy fellow\r\nthan for myself. But it is only right that the young lady should be\r\nconsidered, as you have pointed out, and that I should accept my cue\r\nfrom you. I accept it. I understand that at Christmas they will\r\ncomplete their preparations for May, and that their marriage will be\r\nput in final train by themselves, and that nothing will remain for us\r\nbut to put ourselves in train also, and have everything ready for our\r\nformal release from our trusts, on Edwin\xe2\x80\x99s birthday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my understanding,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Mr. Grewgious, as they shook hands\r\nto part. \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless them both!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod save them both!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said, bless them,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the former, looking back over his\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said, save them,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the latter. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there any difference?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\nSMOOTHING THE WAY\r\n\r\n\r\nIt has been often enough remarked that women have a curious power of\r\ndivining the characters of men, which would seem to be innate and\r\ninstinctive; seeing that it is arrived at through no patient process of\r\nreasoning, that it can give no satisfactory or sufficient account of\r\nitself, and that it pronounces in the most confident manner even\r\nagainst accumulated observation on the part of the other sex. But it\r\nhas not been quite so often remarked that this power (fallible, like\r\nevery other human attribute) is for the most part absolutely incapable\r\nof self-revision; and that when it has delivered an adverse opinion\r\nwhich by all human lights is subsequently proved to have failed, it is\r\nundistinguishable from prejudice, in respect of its determination not\r\nto be corrected. Nay, the very possibility of contradiction or\r\ndisproof, however remote, communicates to this feminine judgment from\r\nthe first, in nine cases out of ten, the weakness attendant on the\r\ntestimony of an interested witness; so personally and strongly does the\r\nfair diviner connect herself with her divination.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think, Ma dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon to his mother one\r\nday as she sat at her knitting in his little book-room, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are\r\nrather hard on Mr. Neville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do _not_, Sept,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us discuss it, Ma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no objection to discuss it, Sept. I trust, my dear, I am always\r\nopen to discussion.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a vibration in the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s cap, as\r\nthough she internally added: \xe2\x80\x9cand I should like to see the discussion\r\nthat would change _my_ mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, Ma,\xe2\x80\x9d said her conciliatory son. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing like\r\nbeing open to discussion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady, evidently shut to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! Mr. Neville, on that unfortunate occasion, commits himself under\r\nprovocation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd under mulled wine,\xe2\x80\x9d added the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must admit the wine. Though I believe the two young men were much\r\nalike in that regard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, Ma?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I _don\xe2\x80\x99t_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady. \xe2\x80\x9cStill, I am quite open to\r\ndiscussion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my dear Ma, I cannot see how we are to discuss, if you take that\r\nline.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame Mr. Neville for it, Sept, and not me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady, with\r\nstately severity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Ma! why Mr. Neville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Crisparkle, retiring on first principles, \xe2\x80\x9che came\r\nhome intoxicated, and did great discredit to this house, and showed\r\ngreat disrespect to this family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not to be denied, Ma. He was then, and he is now, very sorry\r\nfor it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s well-bred consideration in coming up to me, next\r\nday, after service, in the Nave itself, with his gown still on, and\r\nexpressing his hope that I had not been greatly alarmed or had my rest\r\nviolently broken, I believe I might never have heard of that\r\ndisgraceful transaction,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be candid, Ma, I think I should have kept it from you if I could:\r\nthough I had not decidedly made up my mind. I was following Jasper out,\r\nto confer with him on the subject, and to consider the expediency of\r\nhis and my jointly hushing the thing up on all accounts, when I found\r\nhim speaking to you. Then it was too late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo late, indeed, Sept. He was still as pale as gentlemanly ashes at\r\nwhat had taken place in his rooms overnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I _had_ kept it from you, Ma, you may be sure it would have been\r\nfor your peace and quiet, and for the good of the young men, and in my\r\nbest discharge of my duty according to my lights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady immediately walked across the room and kissed him: saying,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, my dear Sept, I am sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever, it became the town-talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, rubbing his\r\near, as his mother resumed her seat, and her knitting, \xe2\x80\x9cand passed out\r\nof my power.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I said then, Sept,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I thought ill of\r\nMr. Neville. And I say now, that I think ill of Mr. Neville. And I said\r\nthen, and I say now, that I hope Mr. Neville may come to good, but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe he will.\xe2\x80\x9d Here the cap vibrated again considerably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear you say so, Ma\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to say so, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed the old lady, knitting on\r\nfirmly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94For,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Minor Canon, \xe2\x80\x9cit is undeniable that Mr. Neville is\r\nexceedingly industrious and attentive, and that he improves apace, and\r\nthat he has\xe2\x80\x94I hope I may say\xe2\x80\x94an attachment to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no merit in the last article, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady,\r\nquickly; \xe2\x80\x9cand if he says there is, I think the worse of him for the\r\nboast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my dear Ma, he never said there was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the old lady; \xe2\x80\x9cstill, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that it\r\ngreatly signifies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no impatience in the pleasant look with which Mr. Crisparkle\r\ncontemplated the pretty old piece of china as it knitted; but there\r\nwas, certainly, a humorous sense of its not being a piece of china to\r\nargue with very closely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides, Sept, ask yourself what he would be without his sister. You\r\nknow what an influence she has over him; you know what a capacity she\r\nhas; you know that whatever he reads with you, he reads with her. Give\r\nher her fair share of your praise, and how much do you leave for him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt these words Mr. Crisparkle fell into a little reverie, in which he\r\nthought of several things. He thought of the times he had seen the\r\nbrother and sister together in deep converse over one of his own old\r\ncollege books; now, in the rimy mornings, when he made those sharpening\r\npilgrimages to Cloisterham Weir; now, in the sombre evenings, when he\r\nfaced the wind at sunset, having climbed his favourite outlook, a\r\nbeetling fragment of monastery ruin; and the two studious figures\r\npassed below him along the margin of the river, in which the town fires\r\nand lights already shone, making the landscape bleaker. He thought how\r\nthe consciousness had stolen upon him that in teaching one, he was\r\nteaching two; and how he had almost insensibly adapted his explanations\r\nto both minds\xe2\x80\x94that with which his own was daily in contact, and that\r\nwhich he only approached through it. He thought of the gossip that had\r\nreached him from the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, to the effect that Helena, whom he\r\nhad mistrusted as so proud and fierce, submitted herself to the\r\nfairy-bride (as he called her), and learnt from her what she knew. He\r\nthought of the picturesque alliance between those two, externally so\r\nvery different. He thought\xe2\x80\x94perhaps most of all\xe2\x80\x94could it be that these\r\nthings were yet but so many weeks old, and had become an integral part\r\nof his life?\r\n\r\nAs, whenever the Reverend Septimus fell a-musing, his good mother took\r\nit to be an infallible sign that he \xe2\x80\x9cwanted support,\xe2\x80\x9d the blooming old\r\nlady made all haste to the dining-room closet, to produce from it the\r\nsupport embodied in a glass of Constantia and a home-made biscuit. It\r\nwas a most wonderful closet, worthy of Cloisterham and of Minor Canon\r\nCorner. Above it, a portrait of Handel in a flowing wig beamed down at\r\nthe spectator, with a knowing air of being up to the contents of the\r\ncloset, and a musical air of intending to combine all its harmonies in\r\none delicious fugue. No common closet with a vulgar door on hinges,\r\nopenable all at once, and leaving nothing to be disclosed by degrees,\r\nthis rare closet had a lock in mid-air, where two perpendicular slides\r\nmet; the one falling down, and the other pushing up. The upper slide,\r\non being pulled down (leaving the lower a double mystery), revealed\r\ndeep shelves of pickle-jars, jam-pots, tin canisters, spice-boxes, and\r\nagreeably outlandish vessels of blue and white, the luscious lodgings\r\nof preserved tamarinds and ginger. Every benevolent inhabitant of this\r\nretreat had his name inscribed upon his stomach. The pickles, in a\r\nuniform of rich brown double-breasted buttoned coat, and yellow or\r\nsombre drab continuations, announced their portly forms, in printed\r\ncapitals, as Walnut, Gherkin, Onion, Cabbage, Cauliflower, Mixed, and\r\nother members of that noble family. The jams, as being of a less\r\nmasculine temperament, and as wearing curlpapers, announced themselves\r\nin feminine caligraphy, like a soft whisper, to be Raspberry,\r\nGooseberry, Apricot, Plum, Damson, Apple, and Peach. The scene closing\r\non these charmers, and the lower slide ascending, oranges were\r\nrevealed, attended by a mighty japanned sugar-box, to temper their\r\nacerbity if unripe. Home-made biscuits waited at the Court of these\r\nPowers, accompanied by a goodly fragment of plum-cake, and various\r\nslender ladies\xe2\x80\x99 fingers, to be dipped into sweet wine and kissed.\r\nLowest of all, a compact leaden-vault enshrined the sweet wine and a\r\nstock of cordials: whence issued whispers of Seville Orange, Lemon,\r\nAlmond, and Caraway-seed. There was a crowning air upon this closet of\r\nclosets, of having been for ages hummed through by the Cathedral bell\r\nand organ, until those venerable bees had made sublimated honey of\r\neverything in store; and it was always observed that every dipper among\r\nthe shelves (deep, as has been noticed, and swallowing up head,\r\nshoulders, and elbows) came forth again mellow-faced, and seeming to\r\nhave undergone a saccharine transfiguration.\r\n\r\nThe Reverend Septimus yielded himself up quite as willing a victim to a\r\nnauseous medicinal herb-closet, also presided over by the china\r\nshepherdess, as to this glorious cupboard. To what amazing infusions of\r\ngentian, peppermint, gilliflower, sage, parsley, thyme, rue, rosemary,\r\nand dandelion, did his courageous stomach submit itself! In what\r\nwonderful wrappers, enclosing layers of dried leaves, would he swathe\r\nhis rosy and contented face, if his mother suspected him of a\r\ntoothache! What botanical blotches would he cheerfully stick upon his\r\ncheek, or forehead, if the dear old lady convicted him of an\r\nimperceptible pimple there! Into this herbaceous penitentiary, situated\r\non an upper staircase-landing: a low and narrow whitewashed cell, where\r\nbunches of dried leaves hung from rusty hooks in the ceiling, and were\r\nspread out upon shelves, in company with portentous bottles: would the\r\nReverend Septimus submissively be led, like the highly popular lamb who\r\nhas so long and unresistingly been led to the slaughter, and there\r\nwould he, unlike that lamb, bore nobody but himself. Not even doing\r\nthat much, so that the old lady were busy and pleased, he would quietly\r\nswallow what was given him, merely taking a corrective dip of hands and\r\nface into the great bowl of dried rose-leaves, and into the other great\r\nbowl of dried lavender, and then would go out, as confident in the\r\nsweetening powers of Cloisterham Weir and a wholesome mind, as Lady\r\nMacbeth was hopeless of those of all the seas that roll.\r\n\r\nIn the present instance the good Minor Canon took his glass of\r\nConstantia with an excellent grace, and, so supported to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsatisfaction, applied himself to the remaining duties of the day. In\r\ntheir orderly and punctual progress they brought round Vesper Service\r\nand twilight. The Cathedral being very cold, he set off for a brisk\r\ntrot after service; the trot to end in a charge at his favourite\r\nfragment of ruin, which was to be carried by storm, without a pause for\r\nbreath.\r\n\r\nHe carried it in a masterly manner, and, not breathed even then, stood\r\nlooking down upon the river. The river at Cloisterham is sufficiently\r\nnear the sea to throw up oftentimes a quantity of seaweed. An unusual\r\nquantity had come in with the last tide, and this, and the confusion of\r\nthe water, and the restless dipping and flapping of the noisy gulls,\r\nand an angry light out seaward beyond the brown-sailed barges that were\r\nturning black, foreshadowed a stormy night. In his mind he was\r\ncontrasting the wild and noisy sea with the quiet harbour of Minor\r\nCanon Corner, when Helena and Neville Landless passed below him. He had\r\nhad the two together in his thoughts all day, and at once climbed down\r\nto speak to them together. The footing was rough in an uncertain light\r\nfor any tread save that of a good climber; but the Minor Canon was as\r\ngood a climber as most men, and stood beside them before many good\r\nclimbers would have been half-way down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA wild evening, Miss Landless! Do you not find your usual walk with\r\nyour brother too exposed and cold for the time of year? Or at all\r\nevents, when the sun is down, and the weather is driving in from the\r\nsea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena thought not. It was their favourite walk. It was very retired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very retired,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Mr. Crisparkle, laying hold of his\r\nopportunity straightway, and walking on with them. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a place of\r\nall others where one can speak without interruption, as I wish to do.\r\nMr. Neville, I believe you tell your sister everything that passes\r\nbetween us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsequently,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cyour sister is aware that I have\r\nrepeatedly urged you to make some kind of apology for that unfortunate\r\noccurrence which befell on the night of your arrival here.\xe2\x80\x9d In saying\r\nit he looked to her, and not to him; therefore it was she, and not he,\r\nwho replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI call it unfortunate, Miss Helena,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Mr. Crisparkle,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cforasmuch as it certainly has engendered a prejudice against Neville.\r\nThere is a notion about, that he is a dangerously passionate fellow, of\r\nan uncontrollable and furious temper: he is really avoided as such.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt he is, poor fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, with a look of proud\r\ncompassion at her brother, expressing a deep sense of his being\r\nungenerously treated. \xe2\x80\x9cI should be quite sure of it, from your saying\r\nso; but what you tell me is confirmed by suppressed hints and\r\nreferences that I meet with every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle again resumed, in a tone of mild though firm\r\npersuasion, \xe2\x80\x9cis not this to be regretted, and ought it not to be\r\namended? These are early days of Neville\xe2\x80\x99s in Cloisterham, and I have\r\nno fear of his outliving such a prejudice, and proving himself to have\r\nbeen misunderstood. But how much wiser to take action at once, than to\r\ntrust to uncertain time! Besides, apart from its being politic, it is\r\nright. For there can be no question that Neville was wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was provoked,\xe2\x80\x9d Helena submitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was the assailant,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle submitted.\r\n\r\nThey walked on in silence, until Helena raised her eyes to the Minor\r\nCanon\xe2\x80\x99s face, and said, almost reproachfully: \xe2\x80\x9cO Mr. Crisparkle, would\r\nyou have Neville throw himself at young Drood\xe2\x80\x99s feet, or at Mr.\r\nJasper\xe2\x80\x99s, who maligns him every day? In your heart you cannot mean it.\r\nFrom your heart you could not do it, if his case were yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have represented to Mr. Crisparkle, Helena,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville, with a\r\nglance of deference towards his tutor, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if I could do it from my\r\nheart, I would. But I cannot, and I revolt from the pretence. You\r\nforget however, that to put the case to Mr. Crisparkle as his own, is\r\nto suppose to have done what I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ask his pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Mr. Crisparkle, again laying hold of his\r\nopportunity, though with a moderate and delicate touch, \xe2\x80\x9cyou both\r\ninstinctively acknowledge that Neville did wrong. Then why stop short,\r\nand not otherwise acknowledge it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there no difference,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Helena, with a little faltering in her\r\nmanner; \xe2\x80\x9cbetween submission to a generous spirit, and submission to a\r\nbase or trivial one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore the worthy Minor Canon was quite ready with his argument in\r\nreference to this nice distinction, Neville struck in:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelp me to clear myself with Mr. Crisparkle, Helena. Help me to\r\nconvince him that I cannot be the first to make concessions without\r\nmockery and falsehood. My nature must be changed before I can do so,\r\nand it is not changed. I am sensible of inexpressible affront, and\r\ndeliberate aggravation of inexpressible affront, and I am angry. The\r\nplain truth is, I am still as angry when I recall that night as I was\r\nthat night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeville,\xe2\x80\x9d hinted the Minor Canon, with a steady countenance, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have\r\nrepeated that former action of your hands, which I so much dislike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for it, sir, but it was involuntary. I confessed that I was\r\nstill as angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I confess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I hoped for better things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to disappoint you, sir, but it would be far worse to\r\ndeceive you, and I should deceive you grossly if I pretended that you\r\nhad softened me in this respect. The time may come when your powerful\r\ninfluence will do even that with the difficult pupil whose antecedents\r\nyou know; but it has not come yet. Is this so, and in spite of my\r\nstruggles against myself, Helena?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe, whose dark eyes were watching the effect of what he said on Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s face, replied\xe2\x80\x94to Mr. Crisparkle, not to him: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAfter a short pause, she answered the slightest look of inquiry\r\nconceivable, in her brother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, with as slight an affirmative bend\r\nof her own head; and he went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never yet had the courage to say to you, sir, what in full\r\nopenness I ought to have said when you first talked with me on this\r\nsubject. It is not easy to say, and I have been withheld by a fear of\r\nits seeming ridiculous, which is very strong upon me down to this last\r\nmoment, and might, but for my sister, prevent my being quite open with\r\nyou even now.\xe2\x80\x94I admire Miss Bud, sir, so very much, that I cannot bear\r\nher being treated with conceit or indifference; and even if I did not\r\nfeel that I had an injury against young Drood on my own account, I\r\nshould feel that I had an injury against him on hers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, in utter amazement, looked at Helena for corroboration,\r\nand met in her expressive face full corroboration, and a plea for\r\nadvice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young lady of whom you speak is, as you know, Mr. Neville, shortly\r\nto be married,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, gravely; \xe2\x80\x9ctherefore your\r\nadmiration, if it be of that special nature which you seem to indicate,\r\nis outrageously misplaced. Moreover, it is monstrous that you should\r\ntake upon yourself to be the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s champion against her chosen\r\nhusband. Besides, you have seen them only once. The young lady has\r\nbecome your sister\xe2\x80\x99s friend; and I wonder that your sister, even on her\r\nbehalf, has not checked you in this irrational and culpable fancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has tried, sir, but uselessly. Husband or no husband, that fellow\r\nis incapable of the feeling with which I am inspired towards the\r\nbeautiful young creature whom he treats like a doll. I say he is as\r\nincapable of it, as he is unworthy of her. I say she is sacrificed in\r\nbeing bestowed upon him.
