I say that I love her, and despise and hate\r\nhim!\xe2\x80\x9d This with a face so flushed, and a gesture so violent, that his\r\nsister crossed to his side, and caught his arm, remonstrating,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeville, Neville!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus recalled to himself, he quickly became sensible of having lost the\r\nguard he had set upon his passionate tendency, and covered his face\r\nwith his hand, as one repentant and wretched.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, watching him attentively, and at the same time\r\nmeditating how to proceed, walked on for some paces in silence. Then he\r\nspoke:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Neville, Mr. Neville, I am sorely grieved to see in you more\r\ntraces of a character as sullen, angry, and wild, as the night now\r\nclosing in. They are of too serious an aspect to leave me the resource\r\nof treating the infatuation you have disclosed, as undeserving serious\r\nconsideration. I give it very serious consideration, and I speak to you\r\naccordingly. This feud between you and young Drood must not go on. I\r\ncannot permit it to go on any longer, knowing what I now know from you,\r\nand you living under my roof. Whatever prejudiced and unauthorised\r\nconstructions your blind and envious wrath may put upon his character,\r\nit is a frank, good-natured character. I know I can trust to it for\r\nthat. Now, pray observe what I am about to say. On reflection, and on\r\nyour sister\xe2\x80\x99s representation, I am willing to admit that, in making\r\npeace with young Drood, you have a right to be met half-way. I will\r\nengage that you shall be, and even that young Drood shall make the\r\nfirst advance. This condition fulfilled, you will pledge me the honour\r\nof a Christian gentleman that the quarrel is for ever at an end on your\r\nside. What may be in your heart when you give him your hand, can only\r\nbe known to the Searcher of all hearts; but it will never go well with\r\nyou, if there be any treachery there. So far, as to that; next as to\r\nwhat I must again speak of as your infatuation. I understand it to have\r\nbeen confided to me, and to be known to no other person save your\r\nsister and yourself. Do I understand aright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena answered in a low voice: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is only known to us three who are\r\nhere together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not at all known to the young lady, your friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn my soul, no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI require you, then, to give me your similar and solemn pledge, Mr.\r\nNeville, that it shall remain the secret it is, and that you will take\r\nno other action whatsoever upon it than endeavouring (and that most\r\nearnestly) to erase it from your mind. I will not tell you that it will\r\nsoon pass; I will not tell you that it is the fancy of the moment; I\r\nwill not tell you that such caprices have their rise and fall among the\r\nyoung and ardent every hour; I will leave you undisturbed in the belief\r\nthat it has few parallels or none, that it will abide with you a long\r\ntime, and that it will be very difficult to conquer. So much the more\r\nweight shall I attach to the pledge I require from you, when it is\r\nunreservedly given.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man twice or thrice essayed to speak, but failed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me leave you with your sister, whom it is time you took home,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will find me alone in my room by-and-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray do not leave us yet,\xe2\x80\x9d Helena implored him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnother minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville, pressing his hand upon his face, \xe2\x80\x9chave\r\nneeded so much as another minute, if you had been less patient with me,\r\nMr. Crisparkle, less considerate of me, and less unpretendingly good\r\nand true. O, if in my childhood I had known such a guide!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow your guide now, Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Helena, \xe2\x80\x9cand follow him to\r\nHeaven!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was that in her tone which broke the good Minor Canon\xe2\x80\x99s voice, or\r\nit would have repudiated her exaltation of him. As it was, he laid a\r\nfinger on his lips, and looked towards her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo say that I give both pledges, Mr. Crisparkle, out of my innermost\r\nheart, and to say that there is no treachery in it, is to say nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThus Neville, greatly moved. \xe2\x80\x9cI beg your forgiveness for my miserable\r\nlapse into a burst of passion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot mine, Neville, not mine. You know with whom forgiveness lies, as\r\nthe highest attribute conceivable. Miss Helena, you and your brother\r\nare twin children. You came into this world with the same dispositions,\r\nand you passed your younger days together surrounded by the same\r\nadverse circumstances. What you have overcome in yourself, can you not\r\novercome in him? You see the rock that lies in his course. Who but you\r\ncan keep him clear of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho but you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Helena. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is my influence, or my weak\r\nwisdom, compared with yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have the wisdom of Love,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Minor Canon, \xe2\x80\x9cand it was\r\nthe highest wisdom ever known upon this earth, remember. As to mine\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nthe less said of that commonplace commodity the better. Good night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took the hand he offered her, and gratefully and almost reverently\r\nraised it to her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon softly, \xe2\x80\x9cI am much overpaid!\xe2\x80\x9d and turned\r\naway.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Mr. Crisparkle is overpaid]\r\n\r\n\r\nRetracing his steps towards the Cathedral Close, he tried, as he went\r\nalong in the dark, to think out the best means of bringing to pass what\r\nhe had promised to effect, and what must somehow be done. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nprobably be asked to marry them,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cand I would they were\r\nmarried and gone! But this presses first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe debated principally whether he should write to young Drood, or\r\nwhether he should speak to Jasper. The consciousness of being popular\r\nwith the whole Cathedral establishment inclined him to the latter\r\ncourse, and the well-timed sight of the lighted gatehouse decided him\r\nto take it. \xe2\x80\x9cI will strike while the iron is hot,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand see\r\nhim now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper was lying asleep on a couch before the fire, when, having\r\nascended the postern-stair, and received no answer to his knock at the\r\ndoor, Mr. Crisparkle gently turned the handle and looked in. Long\r\nafterwards he had cause to remember how Jasper sprang from the couch in\r\na delirious state between sleeping and waking, and crying out: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is\r\nthe matter? Who did it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only I, Jasper. I am sorry to have disturbed you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe glare of his eyes settled down into a look of recognition, and he\r\nmoved a chair or two, to make a way to the fireside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was dreaming at a great rate, and am glad to be disturbed from an\r\nindigestive after-dinner sleep. Not to mention that you are always\r\nwelcome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. I am not confident,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Crisparkle, as he sat\r\nhimself down in the easy-chair placed for him, \xe2\x80\x9cthat my subject will at\r\nfirst sight be quite as welcome as myself; but I am a minister of\r\npeace, and I pursue my subject in the interests of peace. In a word,\r\nJasper, I want to establish peace between these two young fellows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA very perplexed expression took hold of Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s face; a very\r\nperplexing expression too, for Mr. Crisparkle could make nothing of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d was Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s inquiry, in a low and slow voice, after a silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the \xe2\x80\x98How\xe2\x80\x99 I come to you. I want to ask you to do me the great\r\nfavour and service of interposing with your nephew (I have already\r\ninterposed with Mr. Neville), and getting him to write you a short\r\nnote, in his lively way, saying that he is willing to shake hands. I\r\nknow what a good-natured fellow he is, and what influence you have with\r\nhim. And without in the least defending Mr. Neville, we must all admit\r\nthat he was bitterly stung.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper turned that perplexed face towards the fire. Mr. Crisparkle\r\ncontinuing to observe it, found it even more perplexing than before,\r\ninasmuch as it seemed to denote (which could hardly be) some close\r\ninternal calculation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that you are not prepossessed in Mr. Neville\xe2\x80\x99s favour,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nMinor Canon was going on, when Jasper stopped him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have cause to say so. I am not, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly; and I admit his lamentable violence of temper, though I\r\nhope he and I will get the better of it between us. But I have exacted\r\na very solemn promise from him as to his future demeanour towards your\r\nnephew, if you do kindly interpose; and I am sure he will keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are always responsible and trustworthy, Mr. Crisparkle. Do you\r\nreally feel sure that you can answer for him so confidently?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe perplexed and perplexing look vanished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you relieve my mind of a great dread, and a heavy weight,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJasper; \xe2\x80\x9cI will do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, delighted by the swiftness and completeness of his\r\nsuccess, acknowledged it in the handsomest terms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will do it,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cfor the comfort of having your\r\nguarantee against my vague and unfounded fears. You will laugh\xe2\x80\x94but do\r\nyou keep a Diary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA line for a day; not more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA line for a day would be quite as much as my uneventful life would\r\nneed, Heaven knows,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, taking a book from a desk, \xe2\x80\x9cbut that\r\nmy Diary is, in fact, a Diary of Ned\xe2\x80\x99s life too. You will laugh at this\r\nentry; you will guess when it was made:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Past midnight.\xe2\x80\x94After what I have just now seen, I have a morbid dread\r\nupon me of some horrible consequences resulting to my dear boy, that I\r\ncannot reason with or in any way contend against. All my efforts are\r\nvain. The demoniacal passion of this Neville Landless, his strength in\r\nhis fury, and his savage rage for the destruction of its object, appal\r\nme. So profound is the impression, that twice since I have gone into my\r\ndear boy\xe2\x80\x99s room, to assure myself of his sleeping safely, and not lying\r\ndead in his blood.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is another entry next morning:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ned up and away. Light-hearted and unsuspicious as ever. He laughed\r\nwhen I cautioned him, and said he was as good a man as Neville Landless\r\nany day. I told him that might be, but he was not as bad a man. He\r\ncontinued to make light of it, but I travelled with him as far as I\r\ncould, and left him most unwillingly. I am unable to shake off these\r\ndark intangible presentiments of evil\xe2\x80\x94if feelings founded upon staring\r\nfacts are to be so called.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgain and again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, in conclusion, twirling the leaves of\r\nthe book before putting it by, \xe2\x80\x9cI have relapsed into these moods, as\r\nother entries show. But I have now your assurance at my back, and shall\r\nput it in my book, and make it an antidote to my black humours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch an antidote, I hope,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cas will induce\r\nyou before long to consign the black humours to the flames. I ought to\r\nbe the last to find any fault with you this evening, when you have met\r\nmy wishes so freely; but I must say, Jasper, that your devotion to your\r\nnephew has made you exaggerative here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are my witness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, shrugging his shoulders, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat my\r\nstate of mind honestly was, that night, before I sat down to write, and\r\nin what words I expressed it. You remember objecting to a word I used,\r\nas being too strong? It was a stronger word than any in my Diary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well. Try the antidote,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Mr. Crisparkle; \xe2\x80\x9cand may it\r\ngive you a brighter and better view of the case! We will discuss it no\r\nmore now. I have to thank you for myself, thank you sincerely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall find,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, as they shook hands, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I will not do\r\nthe thing you wish me to do, by halves. I will take care that Ned,\r\ngiving way at all, shall give way thoroughly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the third day after this conversation, he called on Mr. Crisparkle\r\nwith the following letter:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMY DEAR JACK,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cI am touched by your account of your interview with Mr.\r\n    Crisparkle, whom I much respect and esteem. At once I openly say\r\n    that I forgot myself on that occasion quite as much as Mr. Landless\r\n    did, and that I wish that bygone to be a bygone, and all to be\r\n    right again.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cLook here, dear old boy. Ask Mr. Landless to dinner on Christmas\r\n    Eve (the better the day the better the deed), and let there be only\r\n    we three, and let us shake hands all round there and then, and say\r\n    no more about it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Jack,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEver your most affectionate,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEDWIN DROOD.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cP.S. Love to Miss Pussy at the next music-lesson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou expect Mr. Neville, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI count upon his coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Jasper.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\nA PICTURE AND A RING\r\n\r\n\r\nBehind the most ancient part of Holborn, London, where certain gabled\r\nhouses some centuries of age still stand looking on the public way, as\r\nif disconsolately looking for the Old Bourne that has long run dry, is\r\na little nook composed of two irregular quadrangles, called Staple Inn.\r\nIt is one of those nooks, the turning into which out of the clashing\r\nstreet, imparts to the relieved pedestrian the sensation of having put\r\ncotton in his ears, and velvet soles on his boots. It is one of those\r\nnooks where a few smoky sparrows twitter in smoky trees, as though they\r\ncalled to one another, \xe2\x80\x9cLet us play at country,\xe2\x80\x9d and where a few feet\r\nof garden-mould and a few yards of gravel enable them to do that\r\nrefreshing violence to their tiny understandings. Moreover, it is one\r\nof those nooks which are legal nooks; and it contains a little Hall,\r\nwith a little lantern in its roof: to what obstructive purposes\r\ndevoted, and at whose expense, this history knoweth not.\r\n\r\nIn the days when Cloisterham took offence at the existence of a\r\nrailroad afar off, as menacing that sensitive constitution, the\r\nproperty of us Britons: the odd fortune of which sacred institution it\r\nis to be in exactly equal degrees croaked about, trembled for, and\r\nboasted of, whatever happens to anything, anywhere in the world: in\r\nthose days no neighbouring architecture of lofty proportions had arisen\r\nto overshadow Staple Inn. The westering sun bestowed bright glances on\r\nit, and the south-west wind blew into it unimpeded.\r\n\r\nNeither wind nor sun, however, favoured Staple Inn one December\r\nafternoon towards six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when it was filled with fog, and candles\r\nshed murky and blurred rays through the windows of all its\r\nthen-occupied sets of chambers; notably from a set of chambers in a\r\ncorner house in the little inner quadrangle, presenting in black and\r\nwhite over its ugly portal the mysterious inscription:\r\n\r\nP\r\nJ\r\nT\r\n1747\r\n\r\n\r\nIn which set of chambers, never having troubled his head about the\r\ninscription, unless to bethink himself at odd times on glancing up at\r\nit, that haply it might mean Perhaps John Thomas, or Perhaps Joe Tyler,\r\nsat Mr. Grewgious writing by his fire.\r\n\r\nWho could have told, by looking at Mr. Grewgious, whether he had ever\r\nknown ambition or disappointment? He had been bred to the Bar, and had\r\nlaid himself out for chamber practice; to draw deeds; \xe2\x80\x9cconvey the wise\r\nit call,\xe2\x80\x9d as Pistol says. But Conveyancing and he had made such a very\r\nindifferent marriage of it that they had separated by consent\xe2\x80\x94if there\r\ncan be said to be separation where there has never been coming\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\nNo. Coy Conveyancing would not come to Mr. Grewgious. She was wooed,\r\nnot won, and they went their several ways. But an Arbitration being\r\nblown towards him by some unaccountable wind, and he gaining great\r\ncredit in it as one indefatigable in seeking out right and doing right,\r\na pretty fat Receivership was next blown into his pocket by a wind more\r\ntraceable to its source. So, by chance, he had found his niche.\r\nReceiver and Agent now, to two rich estates, and deputing their legal\r\nbusiness, in an amount worth having, to a firm of solicitors on the\r\nfloor below, he had snuffed out his ambition (supposing him to have\r\never lighted it), and had settled down with his snuffers for the rest\r\nof his life under the dry vine and fig-tree of P. J. T., who planted in\r\nseventeen-forty-seven.\r\n\r\nMany accounts and account-books, many files of correspondence, and\r\nseveral strong boxes, garnished Mr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x99s room. They can scarcely\r\nbe represented as having lumbered it, so conscientious and precise was\r\ntheir orderly arrangement. The apprehension of dying suddenly, and\r\nleaving one fact or one figure with any incompleteness or obscurity\r\nattaching to it, would have stretched Mr. Grewgious stone-dead any day.\r\nThe largest fidelity to a trust was the life-blood of the man. There\r\nare sorts of life-blood that course more quickly, more gaily, more\r\nattractively; but there is no better sort in circulation.\r\n\r\nThere was no luxury in his room. Even its comforts were limited to its\r\nbeing dry and warm, and having a snug though faded fireside. What may\r\nbe called its private life was confined to the hearth, and an\r\neasy-chair, and an old-fashioned occasional round table that was\r\nbrought out upon the rug after business hours, from a corner where it\r\nelsewise remained turned up like a shining mahogany shield. Behind it,\r\nwhen standing thus on the defensive, was a closet, usually containing\r\nsomething good to drink. An outer room was the clerk\xe2\x80\x99s room; Mr.\r\nGrewgious\xe2\x80\x99s sleeping-room was across the common stair; and he held some\r\nnot empty cellarage at the bottom of the common stair. Three hundred\r\ndays in the year, at least, he crossed over to the hotel in Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nInn for his dinner, and after dinner crossed back again, to make the\r\nmost of these simplicities until it should become broad business day\r\nonce more, with P. J. T., date seventeen-forty-seven.\r\n\r\nAs Mr. Grewgious sat and wrote by his fire that afternoon, so did the\r\nclerk of Mr. Grewgious sit and write by _his_ fire. A pale,\r\npuffy-faced, dark-haired person of thirty, with big dark eyes that\r\nwholly wanted lustre, and a dissatisfied doughy complexion, that seemed\r\nto ask to be sent to the baker\xe2\x80\x99s, this attendant was a mysterious\r\nbeing, possessed of some strange power over Mr. Grewgious. As though he\r\nhad been called into existence, like a fabulous Familiar, by a magic\r\nspell which had failed when required to dismiss him, he stuck tight to\r\nMr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x99s stool, although Mr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x99s comfort and convenience\r\nwould manifestly have been advanced by dispossessing him. A gloomy\r\nperson with tangled locks, and a general air of having been reared\r\nunder the shadow of that baleful tree of Java which has given shelter\r\nto more lies than the whole botanical kingdom, Mr. Grewgious,\r\nnevertheless, treated him with unaccountable consideration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bazzard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, on the entrance of his clerk:\r\nlooking up from his papers as he arranged them for the night: \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is\r\nin the wind besides fog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Drood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat of him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas called,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might have shown him in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am doing it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard.\r\n\r\nThe visitor came in accordingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, looking round his pair of office\r\ncandles. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had called and merely left your name and gone.\r\nHow do you do, Mr. Edwin? Dear me, you\xe2\x80\x99re choking!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s this fog,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Edwin; \xe2\x80\x9cand it makes my eyes smart, like\r\nCayenne pepper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it really so bad as that? Pray undo your wrappers. It\xe2\x80\x99s fortunate I\r\nhave so good a fire; but Mr. Bazzard has taken care of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Bazzard at the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! then it follows that I must have taken care of myself without\r\nobserving it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cPray be seated in my chair. No. I\r\nbeg! Coming out of such an atmosphere, in _my_ chair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin took the easy-chair in the corner; and the fog he had brought in\r\nwith him, and the fog he took off with his greatcoat and neck-shawl,\r\nwas speedily licked up by the eager fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI look,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cas if I had come to stop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94By the by,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious; \xe2\x80\x9cexcuse my interrupting you; do\r\nstop. The fog may clear in an hour or two. We can have dinner in from\r\njust across Holborn. You had better take your Cayenne pepper here than\r\noutside; pray stop and dine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin, glancing about him as though attracted\r\nby the notion of a new and relishing sort of gipsy-party.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious; \xe2\x80\x9c_you_ are very kind to join issue\r\nwith a bachelor in chambers, and take pot-luck. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll ask,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nGrewgious, dropping his voice, and speaking with a twinkling eye, as if\r\ninspired with a bright thought: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll ask Bazzard. He mightn\xe2\x80\x99t like it\r\nelse.\xe2\x80\x94Bazzard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBazzard reappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDine presently with Mr. Drood and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am ordered to dine, of course I will, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d was the gloomy\r\nanswer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSave the man!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not ordered; you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ninvited.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard; \xe2\x80\x9cin that case I don\xe2\x80\x99t care if I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s arranged. And perhaps you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cstepping over to the hotel in Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s, and asking them to send in\r\nmaterials for laying the cloth. For dinner we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a tureen of the\r\nhottest and strongest soup available, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll have the best made-dish\r\nthat can be recommended, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a joint (such as a haunch of\r\nmutton), and we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a goose, or a turkey, or any little stuffed\r\nthing of that sort that may happen to be in the bill of fare\xe2\x80\x94in short,\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll have whatever there is on hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese liberal directions Mr. Grewgious issued with his usual air of\r\nreading an inventory, or repeating a lesson, or doing anything else by\r\nrote. Bazzard, after drawing out the round table, withdrew to execute\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was a little delicate, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, in a lower\r\ntone, after his clerk\xe2\x80\x99s departure, \xe2\x80\x9cabout employing him in the foraging\r\nor commissariat department. Because he mightn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seems to have his own way, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Edwin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis own way?\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cO dear no! Poor fellow, you\r\nquite mistake him. If he had his own way, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder where he would be!\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin thought. But he only thought it,\r\nbecause Mr. Grewgious came and stood himself with his back to the other\r\ncorner of the fire, and his shoulder-blades against the chimneypiece,\r\nand collected his skirts for easy conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take it, without having the gift of prophecy, that you have done me\r\nthe favour of looking in to mention that you are going down\r\nyonder\xe2\x80\x94where I can tell you, you are expected\xe2\x80\x94and to offer to execute\r\nany little commission from me to my charming ward, and perhaps to\r\nsharpen me up a bit in any proceedings? Eh, Mr. Edwin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI called, sir, before going down, as an act of attention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf attention!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! of course, not of impatience?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpatience, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious had meant to be arch\xe2\x80\x94not that he in the remotest degree\r\nexpressed that meaning\xe2\x80\x94and had brought himself into scarcely\r\nsupportable proximity with the fire, as if to burn the fullest effect\r\nof his archness into himself, as other subtle impressions are burnt\r\ninto hard metals. But his archness suddenly flying before the composed\r\nface and manner of his visitor, and only the fire remaining, he started\r\nand rubbed himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have lately been down yonder,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, rearranging his\r\nskirts; \xe2\x80\x9cand that was what I referred to, when I said I could tell you\r\nyou are expected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, sir! Yes; I knew that Pussy was looking out for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you keep a cat down there?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\nEdwin coloured a little as he explained: \xe2\x80\x9cI call Rosa Pussy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, smoothing down his head; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\naffable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin glanced at his face, uncertain whether or no he seriously\r\nobjected to the appellation. But Edwin might as well have glanced at\r\nthe face of a clock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA pet name, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmps,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with a nod. But with such an extraordinary\r\ncompromise between an unqualified assent and a qualified dissent, that\r\nhis visitor was much disconcerted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid PRosa\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin began by way of recovering himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPRosa?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was going to say Pussy, and changed my mind;\xe2\x80\x94did she tell you\r\nanything about the Landlesses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the Landlesses? An estate? A villa?\r\nA farm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA brother and sister. The sister is at the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and has become\r\na great friend of P\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPRosa\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Grewgious struck in, with a fixed face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is a strikingly handsome girl, sir, and I thought she might have\r\nbeen described to you, or presented to you perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cBut here is Bazzard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBazzard returned, accompanied by two waiters\xe2\x80\x94an immovable waiter, and a\r\nflying waiter; and the three brought in with them as much fog as gave a\r\nnew roar to the fire. The flying waiter, who had brought everything on\r\nhis shoulders, laid the cloth with amazing rapidity and dexterity;\r\nwhile the immovable waiter, who had brought nothing, found fault with\r\nhim. The flying waiter then highly polished all the glasses he had\r\nbrought, and the immovable waiter looked through them. The flying\r\nwaiter then flew across Holborn for the soup, and flew back again, and\r\nthen took another flight for the made-dish, and flew back again, and\r\nthen took another flight for the joint and poultry, and flew back\r\nagain, and between whiles took supplementary flights for a great\r\nvariety of articles, as it was discovered from time to time that the\r\nimmovable waiter had forgotten them all. But let the flying waiter\r\ncleave the air as he might, he was always reproached on his return by\r\nthe immovable waiter for bringing fog with him, and being out of\r\nbreath. At the conclusion of the repast, by which time the flying\r\nwaiter was severely blown, the immovable waiter gathered up the\r\ntablecloth under his arm with a grand air, and having sternly (not to\r\nsay with indignation) looked on at the flying waiter while he set the\r\nclean glasses round, directed a valedictory glance towards Mr.\r\nGrewgious, conveying: \xe2\x80\x9cLet it be clearly understood between us that the\r\nreward is mine, and that Nil is the claim of this slave,\xe2\x80\x9d and pushed\r\nthe flying waiter before him out of the room.\r\n\r\nIt was like a highly-finished miniature painting representing My Lords\r\nof the Circumlocution Department, Commandership-in-Chief of any sort,\r\nGovernment. It was quite an edifying little picture to be hung on the\r\nline in the National Gallery.\r\n\r\nAs the fog had been the proximate cause of this sumptuous repast, so\r\nthe fog served for its general sauce. To hear the out-door clerks\r\nsneezing, wheezing, and beating their feet on the gravel was a zest far\r\nsurpassing Doctor Kitchener\xe2\x80\x99s. To bid, with a shiver, the unfortunate\r\nflying waiter shut the door before he had opened it, was a condiment of\r\na profounder flavour than Harvey. And here let it be noticed,\r\nparenthetically, that the leg of this young man, in its application to\r\nthe door, evinced the finest sense of touch: always preceding himself\r\nand tray (with something of an angling air about it), by some seconds:\r\nand always lingering after he and the tray had disappeared, like\r\nMacbeth\xe2\x80\x99s leg when accompanying him off the stage with reluctance to\r\nthe assassination of Duncan.\r\n\r\nThe host had gone below to the cellar, and had brought up bottles of\r\nruby, straw-coloured, and golden drinks, which had ripened long ago in\r\nlands where no fogs are, and had since lain slumbering in the shade.\r\nSparkling and tingling after so long a nap, they pushed at their corks\r\nto help the corkscrew (like prisoners helping rioters to force their\r\ngates), and danced out gaily. If P. J. T. in seventeen-forty-seven, or\r\nin any other year of his period, drank such wines\xe2\x80\x94then, for a\r\ncertainty, P. J. T. was Pretty Jolly Too.\r\n\r\nExternally, Mr. Grewgious showed no signs of being mellowed by these\r\nglowing vintages. Instead of his drinking them, they might have been\r\npoured over him in his high-dried snuff form, and run to waste, for any\r\nlights and shades they caused to flicker over his face. Neither was his\r\nmanner influenced. But, in his wooden way, he had observant eyes for\r\nEdwin; and when at the end of dinner, he motioned Edwin back to his own\r\neasy-chair in the fireside corner, and Edwin sank luxuriously into it\r\nafter very brief remonstrance, Mr. Grewgious, as he turned his seat\r\nround towards the fire too, and smoothed his head and face, might have\r\nbeen seen looking at his visitor between his smoothing fingers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBazzard!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, suddenly turning to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI follow you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Bazzard; who had done his work of\r\nconsuming meat and drink in a workmanlike manner, though mostly in\r\nspeechlessness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI drink to you, Bazzard; Mr. Edwin, success to Mr. Bazzard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuccess to Mr. Bazzard!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed Edwin, with a totally unfounded\r\nappearance of enthusiasm, and with the unspoken addition: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat in, I\r\nwonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd May!\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI am not at liberty to be\r\ndefinite\xe2\x80\x94May!\xe2\x80\x94my conversational powers are so very limited that I know\r\nI shall not come well out of this\xe2\x80\x94May!\xe2\x80\x94it ought to be put\r\nimaginatively, but I have no imagination\xe2\x80\x94May!\xe2\x80\x94the thorn of anxiety is\r\nas nearly the mark as I am likely to get\xe2\x80\x94May it come out at last!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Bazzard, with a frowning smile at the fire, put a hand into his\r\ntangled locks, as if the thorn of anxiety were there; then into his\r\nwaistcoat, as if it were there; then into his pockets, as if it were\r\nthere. In all these movements he was closely followed by the eyes of\r\nEdwin, as if that young gentleman expected to see the thorn in action.\r\nIt was not produced, however, and Mr. Bazzard merely said: \xe2\x80\x9cI follow\r\nyou, sir, and I thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, jingling his glass on the table with\r\none hand, and bending aside under cover of the other, to whisper to\r\nEdwin, \xe2\x80\x9cto drink to my ward. But I put Bazzard first. He mightn\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nit else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was said with a mysterious wink; or what would have been a wink,\r\nif, in Mr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x99s hands, it could have been quick enough. So Edwin\r\nwinked responsively, without the least idea what he meant by doing so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cI devote a bumper to the fair and\r\nfascinating Miss Rosa. Bazzard, the fair and fascinating Miss Rosa!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI follow you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard, \xe2\x80\x9cand I pledge you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so do I!\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord bless me,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious, breaking the blank silence which\r\nof course ensued: though why these pauses _should_ come upon us when we\r\nhave performed any small social rite, not directly inducive of\r\nself-examination or mental despondency, who can tell? \xe2\x80\x9cI am a\r\nparticularly Angular man, and yet I fancy (if I may use the word, not\r\nhaving a morsel of fancy), that I could draw a picture of a true\r\nlover\xe2\x80\x99s state of mind, to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us follow you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard, \xe2\x80\x9cand have the picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Edwin will correct it where it\xe2\x80\x99s wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Mr. Grewgious,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand will throw in a few touches from the life. I dare say it is wrong\r\nin many particulars, and wants many touches from the life, for I was\r\nborn a Chip, and have neither soft sympathies nor soft experiences.\r\nWell! I hazard the guess that the true lover\xe2\x80\x99s mind is completely\r\npermeated by the beloved object of his affections. I hazard the guess\r\nthat her dear name is precious to him, cannot be heard or repeated\r\nwithout emotion, and is preserved sacred. If he has any distinguishing\r\nappellation of fondness for her, it is reserved for her, and is not for\r\ncommon ears. A name that it would be a privilege to call her by, being\r\nalone with her own bright self, it would be a liberty, a coldness, an\r\ninsensibility, almost a breach of good faith, to flaunt elsewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was wonderful to see Mr. Grewgious sitting bolt upright, with his\r\nhands on his knees, continuously chopping this discourse out of\r\nhimself: much as a charity boy with a very good memory might get his\r\ncatechism said: and evincing no correspondent emotion whatever, unless\r\nin a certain occasional little tingling perceptible at the end of his\r\nnose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy picture,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Grewgious proceeded, \xe2\x80\x9cgoes on to represent (under\r\ncorrection from you, Mr. Edwin), the true lover as ever impatient to be\r\nin the presence or vicinity of the beloved object of his affections; as\r\ncaring very little for his case in any other society; and as constantly\r\nseeking that. If I was to say seeking that, as a bird seeks its nest, I\r\nshould make an ass of myself, because that would trench upon what I\r\nunderstand to be poetry; and I am so far from trenching upon poetry at\r\nany time, that I never, to my knowledge, got within ten thousand miles\r\nof it. And I am besides totally unacquainted with the habits of birds,\r\nexcept the birds of Staple Inn, who seek their nests on ledges, and in\r\ngutter-pipes and chimneypots, not constructed for them by the\r\nbeneficent hand of Nature. I beg, therefore, to be understood as\r\nforegoing the bird\xe2\x80\x99s-nest. But my picture does represent the true lover\r\nas having no existence separable from that of the beloved object of his\r\naffections, and as living at once a doubled life and a halved life. And\r\nif I do not clearly express what I mean by that, it is either for the\r\nreason that having no conversational powers, I cannot express what I\r\nmean, or that having no meaning, I do not mean what I fail to express.\r\nWhich, to the best of my belief, is not the case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin had turned red and turned white, as certain points of this\r\npicture came into the light. He now sat looking at the fire, and bit\r\nhis lip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe speculations of an Angular man,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Mr. Grewgious, still\r\nsitting and speaking exactly as before, \xe2\x80\x9care probably erroneous on so\r\nglobular a topic. But I figure to myself (subject, as before, to Mr.\r\nEdwin\xe2\x80\x99s correction), that there can be no coolness, no lassitude, no\r\ndoubt, no indifference, no half fire and half smoke state of mind, in a\r\nreal lover. Pray am I at all near the mark in my picture?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs abrupt in his conclusion as in his commencement and progress, he\r\njerked this inquiry at Edwin, and stopped when one might have supposed\r\nhim in the middle of his oration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Edwin, \xe2\x80\x9cas you refer the question to\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cI refer it to you, as an authority.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say, then, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Edwin went on, embarrassed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the\r\npicture you have drawn is generally correct; but I submit that perhaps\r\nyou may be rather hard upon the unlucky lover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLikely so,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9clikely so. I am a hard man in the\r\ngrain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe may not show,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin, \xe2\x80\x9call he feels; or he may not\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere he stopped so long, to find the rest of his sentence, that Mr.\r\nGrewgious rendered his difficulty a thousand times the greater by\r\nunexpectedly striking in with:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo to be sure; he _may_ not!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter that, they all sat silent; the silence of Mr. Bazzard being\r\noccasioned by slumber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis responsibility is very great, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious at\r\nlength, with his eyes on the fire.\r\n\r\nEdwin nodded assent, with _his_ eyes on the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd let him be sure that he trifles with no one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cneither with himself, nor with any other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin bit his lip again, and still sat looking at the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must not make a plaything of a treasure. Woe betide him if he does!\r\nLet him take that well to heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\nThough he said these things in short sentences, much as the\r\nsupposititious charity boy just now referred to might have repeated a\r\nverse or two from the Book of Proverbs, there was something dreamy (for\r\nso literal a man) in the way in which he now shook his right forefinger\r\nat the live coals in the grate, and again fell silent.\r\n\r\nBut not for long. As he sat upright and stiff in his chair, he suddenly\r\nrapped his knees, like the carved image of some queer Joss or other\r\ncoming out of its reverie, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe must finish this bottle, Mr.\r\nEdwin. Let me help you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll help Bazzard too, though he _is_ asleep.\r\nHe mightn\xe2\x80\x99t like it else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe helped them both, and helped himself, and drained his glass, and\r\nstood it bottom upward on the table, as though he had just caught a\r\nbluebottle in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Mr. Edwin,\xe2\x80\x9d he proceeded, wiping his mouth and hands upon his\r\nhandkerchief: \xe2\x80\x9cto a little piece of business. You received from me, the\r\nother day, a certified copy of Miss Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s father\xe2\x80\x99s will. You knew its\r\ncontents before, but you received it from me as a matter of business. I\r\nshould have sent it to Mr. Jasper, but for Miss Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s wishing it to\r\ncome straight to you, in preference. You received it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite safely, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have acknowledged its receipt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbusiness being business all the world over. However, you did not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI meant to have acknowledged it when I first came in this evening,\r\nsir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a business-like acknowledgment,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Grewgious; \xe2\x80\x9chowever,\r\nlet that pass. Now, in that document you have observed a few words of\r\nkindly allusion to its being left to me to discharge a little trust,\r\nconfided to me in conversation, at such time as I in my discretion may\r\nthink best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Edwin, it came into my mind just now, when I was looking at the\r\nfire, that I could, in my discretion, acquit myself of that trust at no\r\nbetter time than the present. Favour me with your attention, half a\r\nminute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took a bunch of keys from his pocket, singled out by the\r\ncandle-light the key he wanted, and then, with a candle in his hand,\r\nwent to a bureau or escritoire, unlocked it, touched the spring of a\r\nlittle secret drawer, and took from it an ordinary ring-case made for a\r\nsingle ring. With this in his hand, he returned to his chair. As he\r\nheld it up for the young man to see, his hand trembled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Edwin, this rose of diamonds and rubies delicately set in gold,\r\nwas a ring belonging to Miss Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s mother. It was removed from her\r\ndead hand, in my presence, with such distracted grief as I hope it may\r\nnever be my lot to contemplate again. Hard man as I am, I am not hard\r\nenough for that. See how bright these stones shine!\xe2\x80\x9d opening the case.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet the eyes that were so much brighter, and that so often looked\r\nupon them with a light and a proud heart, have been ashes among ashes,\r\nand dust among dust, some years! If I had any imagination (which it is\r\nneedless to say I have not), I might imagine that the lasting beauty of\r\nthese stones was almost cruel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed the case again as he spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis ring was given to the young lady who was drowned so early in her\r\nbeautiful and happy career, by her husband, when they first plighted\r\ntheir faith to one another. It was he who removed it from her\r\nunconscious hand, and it was he who, when his death drew very near,\r\nplaced it in mine. The trust in which I received it, was, that, you and\r\nMiss Rosa growing to manhood and womanhood, and your betrothal\r\nprospering and coming to maturity, I should give it to you to place\r\nupon her finger. Failing those desired results, it was to remain in my\r\npossession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome trouble was in the young man\xe2\x80\x99s face, and some indecision was in\r\nthe action of his hand, as Mr. Grewgious, looking steadfastly at him,\r\ngave him the ring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour placing it on her finger,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cwill be the\r\nsolemn seal upon your strict fidelity to the living and the dead. You\r\nare going to her, to make the last irrevocable preparations for your\r\nmarriage. Take it with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man took the little case, and placed it in his breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf anything should be amiss, if anything should be even slightly\r\nwrong, between you; if you should have any secret consciousness that\r\nyou are committing yourself to this step for no higher reason than\r\nbecause you have long been accustomed to look forward to it; then,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cI charge you once more, by the living and by the\r\ndead, to bring that ring back to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Bazzard awoke himself by his own snoring; and, as is usual in such\r\ncases, sat apoplectically staring at vacancy, as defying vacancy to\r\naccuse him of having been asleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBazzard!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, harder than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI follow you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bazzard, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have been following you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn discharge of a trust, I have handed Mr. Edwin Drood a ring of\r\ndiamonds and rubies. You see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEdwin reproduced the little case, and opened it; and Bazzard looked\r\ninto it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI follow you both, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Bazzard, \xe2\x80\x9cand I witness the\r\ntransaction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvidently anxious to get away and be alone, Edwin Drood now resumed his\r\nouter clothing, muttering something about time and appointments. The\r\nfog was reported no clearer (by the flying waiter, who alighted from a\r\nspeculative flight in the coffee interest), but he went out into it;\r\nand Bazzard, after his manner, \xe2\x80\x9cfollowed\xe2\x80\x9d him.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious, left alone, walked softly and slowly to and fro, for an\r\nhour and more. He was restless to-night, and seemed dispirited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I have done right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe appeal to him seemed\r\nnecessary. It was hard to lose the ring, and yet it must have gone from\r\nme very soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed the empty little drawer with a sigh, and shut and locked the\r\nescritoire, and came back to the solitary fireside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer ring,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cWill it come back to me? My mind hangs about\r\nher ring very uneasily to-night. But that is explainable. I have had it\r\nso long, and I have prized it so much! I wonder\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was in a wondering mood as well as a restless; for, though he\r\nchecked himself at that point, and took another walk, he resumed his\r\nwondering when he sat down again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder (for the ten-thousandth time, and what a weak fool I, for\r\nwhat can it signify now!) whether he confided the charge of their\r\norphan child to me, because he knew\xe2\x80\x94Good God, how like her mother she\r\nhas become!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder whether he ever so much as suspected that some one doted on\r\nher, at a hopeless, speechless distance, when he struck in and won her.\r\nI wonder whether it ever crept into his mind who that unfortunate some\r\none was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder whether I shall sleep to-night! At all events, I will shut\r\nout the world with the bedclothes, and try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious crossed the staircase to his raw and foggy bedroom, and\r\nwas soon ready for bed. Dimly catching sight of his face in the misty\r\nlooking-glass, he held his candle to it for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA likely some one, _you_, to come into anybody\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts in such an\r\naspect!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThere! there! there! Get to bed, poor man, and\r\ncease to jabber!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith that, he extinguished his light, pulled up the bedclothes around\r\nhim, and with another sigh shut out the world. And yet there are such\r\nunexplored romantic nooks in the unlikeliest men, that even old\r\ntinderous and touchwoody P. J. T. Possibly Jabbered Thus, at some odd\r\ntimes, in or about seventeen-forty-seven.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\nA NIGHT WITH DURDLES\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Mr. Sapsea has nothing better to do, towards evening, and finds\r\nthe contemplation of his own profundity becoming a little monotonous in\r\nspite of the vastness of the subject, he often takes an airing in the\r\nCathedral Close and thereabout. He likes to pass the churchyard with a\r\nswelling air of proprietorship, and to encourage in his breast a sort\r\nof benignant-landlord feeling, in that he has been bountiful towards\r\nthat meritorious tenant, Mrs. Sapsea, and has publicly given her a\r\nprize. He likes to see a stray face or two looking in through the\r\nrailings, and perhaps reading his inscription. Should he meet a\r\nstranger coming from the churchyard with a quick step, he is morally\r\nconvinced that the stranger is \xe2\x80\x9cwith a blush retiring,\xe2\x80\x9d as monumentally\r\ndirected.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s importance has received enhancement, for he has become\r\nMayor of Cloisterham. Without mayors, and many of them, it cannot be\r\ndisputed that the whole framework of society\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Sapsea is confident\r\nthat he invented that forcible figure\xe2\x80\x94would fall to pieces. Mayors have\r\nbeen knighted for \xe2\x80\x9cgoing up\xe2\x80\x9d with addresses: explosive machines\r\nintrepidly discharging shot and shell into the English Grammar. Mr.\r\nSapsea may \xe2\x80\x9cgo up\xe2\x80\x9d with an address. Rise, Sir Thomas Sapsea! Of such is\r\nthe salt of the earth.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea has improved the acquaintance of Mr. Jasper, since their\r\nfirst meeting to partake of port, epitaph, backgammon, beef, and salad.\r\nMr. Sapsea has been received at the gatehouse with kindred hospitality;\r\nand on that occasion Mr. Jasper seated himself at the piano, and sang\r\nto him, tickling his ears\xe2\x80\x94figuratively\xe2\x80\x94long enough to present a\r\nconsiderable area for tickling. What Mr. Sapsea likes in that young man\r\nis, that he is always ready to profit by the wisdom of his elders, and\r\nthat he is sound, sir, at the core. In proof of which, he sang to Mr.\r\nSapsea that evening, no kickshaw ditties, favourites with national\r\nenemies, but gave him the genuine George the Third home-brewed;\r\nexhorting him (as \xe2\x80\x9cmy brave boys\xe2\x80\x9d) to reduce to a smashed condition all\r\nother islands but this island, and all continents, peninsulas,\r\nisthmuses, promontories, and other geographical forms of land soever,\r\nbesides sweeping the seas in all directions. In short, he rendered it\r\npretty clear that Providence made a distinct mistake in originating so\r\nsmall a nation of hearts of oak, and so many other verminous peoples.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea, walking slowly this moist evening near the churchyard with\r\nhis hands behind him, on the look-out for a blushing and retiring\r\nstranger, turns a corner, and comes instead into the goodly presence of\r\nthe Dean, conversing with the Verger and Mr. Jasper. Mr. Sapsea makes\r\nhis obeisance, and is instantly stricken far more ecclesiastical than\r\nany Archbishop of York or Canterbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are evidently going to write a book about us, Mr. Jasper,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth\r\nthe Dean; \xe2\x80\x9cto write a book about us. Well! We are very ancient, and we\r\nought to make a good book. We are not so richly endowed in possessions\r\nas in age; but perhaps you will put _that_ in your book, among other\r\nthings, and call attention to our wrongs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Tope, as in duty bound, is greatly entertained by this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really have no intention at all, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replies Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cof turning\r\nauthor or arch\xc3\xa6ologist. It is but a whim of mine. And even for my whim,\r\nMr. Sapsea here is more accountable than I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow so, Mr. Mayor?\xe2\x80\x9d says the Dean, with a nod of good-natured\r\nrecognition of his Fetch. \xe2\x80\x9cHow is that, Mr. Mayor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not aware,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Sapsea remarks, looking about him for\r\ninformation, \xe2\x80\x9cto what the Very Reverend the Dean does me the honour of\r\nreferring.\xe2\x80\x9d And then falls to studying his original in minute points of\r\ndetail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDurdles,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Tope hints.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy!\xe2\x80\x9d the Dean echoes; \xe2\x80\x9cDurdles, Durdles!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe truth is, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d explains Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cthat my curiosity in the man was\r\nfirst really stimulated by Mr. Sapsea. Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge of\r\nmankind and power of drawing out whatever is recluse or odd around him,\r\nfirst led to my bestowing a second thought upon the man: though of\r\ncourse I had met him constantly about. You would not be surprised by\r\nthis, Mr. Dean, if you had seen Mr. Sapsea deal with him in his own\r\nparlour, as I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Sapsea, picking up the ball thrown to him with ineffable\r\ncomplacency and pomposity; \xe2\x80\x9cyes, yes. The Very Reverend the Dean refers\r\nto that? Yes. I happened to bring Durdles and Mr. Jasper together. I\r\nregard Durdles as a Character.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA character, Mr. Sapsea, that with a few skilful touches you turn\r\ninside out,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, not quite that,\xe2\x80\x9d returns the lumbering auctioneer. \xe2\x80\x9cI may have a\r\nlittle influence over him, perhaps; and a little insight into his\r\ncharacter, perhaps. The Very Reverend the Dean will please to bear in\r\nmind that I have seen the world.\xe2\x80\x9d Here Mr. Sapsea gets a little behind\r\nthe Dean, to inspect his coat-buttons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d says the Dean, looking about him to see what has become of his\r\ncopyist: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope, Mr. Mayor, you will use your study and knowledge of\r\nDurdles to the good purpose of exhorting him not to break our worthy\r\nand respected Choir-Master\xe2\x80\x99s neck; we cannot afford it; his head and\r\nvoice are much too valuable to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Tope is again highly entertained, and, having fallen into\r\nrespectful convulsions of laughter, subsides into a deferential murmur,\r\nimporting that surely any gentleman would deem it a pleasure and an\r\nhonour to have his neck broken, in return for such a compliment from\r\nsuch a source.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take it upon myself, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d observes Sapsea loftily, \xe2\x80\x9cto answer\r\nfor Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s neck. I will tell Durdles to be careful of it. He will\r\nmind what _I_ say. How is it at present endangered?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquires,\r\nlooking about him with magnificent patronage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly by my making a moonlight expedition with Durdles among the tombs,\r\nvaults, towers, and ruins,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Jasper. \xe2\x80\x9cYou remember suggesting,\r\nwhen you brought us together, that, as a lover of the picturesque, it\r\nmight be worth my while?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember!\xe2\x80\x9d replies the auctioneer. And the solemn idiot really\r\nbelieves that he does remember.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProfiting by your hint,\xe2\x80\x9d pursues Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cI have had some day-rambles\r\nwith the extraordinary old fellow, and we are to make a moonlight\r\nhole-and-corner exploration to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here he is,\xe2\x80\x9d says the Dean.\r\n\r\nDurdles with his dinner-bundle in his hand, is indeed beheld slouching\r\ntowards them. Slouching nearer, and perceiving the Dean, he pulls off\r\nhis hat, and is slouching away with it under his arm, when Mr. Sapsea\r\nstops him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind you take care of my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d is the injunction Mr. Sapsea lays\r\nupon him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat friend o\xe2\x80\x99 yourn is dead?\xe2\x80\x9d asks Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cNo orders has come in\r\nfor any friend o\xe2\x80\x99 yourn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean my live friend there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! him?\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cHe can take care of himself, can Mister\r\nJarsper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do you take care of him too,\xe2\x80\x9d says Sapsea.\r\n\r\nWhom Durdles (there being command in his tone) surlily surveys from\r\nhead to foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith submission to his Reverence the Dean, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll mind what\r\nconcerns you, Mr. Sapsea, Durdles he\xe2\x80\x99ll mind what concerns him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re out of temper,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Sapsea, winking to the company to\r\nobserve how smoothly he will manage him. \xe2\x80\x9cMy friend concerns me, and\r\nMr. Jasper is my friend. And you are my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you get into a bad habit of boasting,\xe2\x80\x9d retorts Durdles, with a\r\ngrave cautionary nod. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll grow upon you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Durdles cautions Mr. Sapsea against boasting]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are out of temper,\xe2\x80\x9d says Sapsea again; reddening, but again\r\nsinking to the company.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI own to it,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Durdles; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like liberties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea winks a third wink to the company, as who should say: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink you will agree with me that I have settled _his_ business;\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nstalks out of the controversy.\r\n\r\nDurdles then gives the Dean a good evening, and adding, as he puts his\r\nhat on, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find me at home, Mister Jarsper, as agreed, when you\r\nwant me; I\xe2\x80\x99m a-going home to clean myself,\xe2\x80\x9d soon slouches out of sight.\r\nThis going home to clean himself is one of the man\xe2\x80\x99s incomprehensible\r\ncompromises with inexorable facts; he, and his hat, and his boots, and\r\nhis clothes, never showing any trace of cleaning, but being uniformly\r\nin one condition of dust and grit.\r\n\r\nThe lamplighter now dotting the quiet Close with specks of light, and\r\nrunning at a great rate up and down his little ladder with that\r\nobject\xe2\x80\x94his little ladder under the sacred shadow of whose inconvenience\r\ngenerations had grown up, and which all Cloisterham would have stood\r\naghast at the idea of abolishing\xe2\x80\x94the Dean withdraws to his dinner, Mr.\r\nTope to his tea, and Mr. Jasper to his piano. There, with no light but\r\nthat of the fire, he sits chanting choir-music in a low and beautiful\r\nvoice, for two or three hours; in short, until it has been for some\r\ntime dark, and the moon is about to rise.\r\n\r\nThen he closes his piano softly, softly changes his coat for a\r\npea-jacket, with a goodly wicker-cased bottle in its largest pocket,\r\nand putting on a low-crowned, flap-brimmed hat, goes softly out. Why\r\ndoes he move so softly to-night? No outward reason is apparent for it.\r\nCan there be any sympathetic reason crouching darkly within him?\r\n\r\nRepairing to Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s unfinished house, or hole in the city wall, and\r\nseeing a light within it, he softly picks his course among the\r\ngravestones, monuments, and stony lumber of the yard, already touched\r\nhere and there, sidewise, by the rising moon. The two journeymen have\r\nleft their two great saws sticking in their blocks of stone; and two\r\nskeleton journeymen out of the Dance of Death might be grinning in the\r\nshadow of their sheltering sentry-boxes, about to slash away at cutting\r\nout the gravestones of the next two people destined to die in\r\nCloisterham. Likely enough, the two think little of that now, being\r\nalive, and perhaps merry. Curious, to make a guess at the two;\xe2\x80\x94or say\r\none of the two!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo! Durdles!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe light moves, and he appears with it at the door. He would seem to\r\nhave been \xe2\x80\x9ccleaning himself\xe2\x80\x9d with the aid of a bottle, jug, and\r\ntumbler; for no other cleansing instruments are visible in the bare\r\nbrick room with rafters overhead and no plastered ceiling, into which\r\nhe shows his visitor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ready, Mister Jarsper. Let the old \xe2\x80\x99uns come out if they dare,\r\nwhen we go among their tombs. My spirit is ready for \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean animal spirits, or ardent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one\xe2\x80\x99s the t\xe2\x80\x99other,\xe2\x80\x9d answers Durdles, \xe2\x80\x9cand I mean \xe2\x80\x99em both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe takes a lantern from a hook, puts a match or two in his pocket\r\nwherewith to light it, should there be need; and they go out together,\r\ndinner-bundle and all.\r\n\r\nSurely an unaccountable sort of expedition! That Durdles himself, who\r\nis always prowling among old graves, and ruins, like a Ghoul\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\nshould be stealing forth to climb, and dive, and wander without an\r\nobject, is nothing extraordinary; but that the Choir-Master or any one\r\nelse should hold it worth his while to be with him, and to study\r\nmoonlight effects in such company is another affair. Surely an\r\nunaccountable sort of expedition, therefore!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Ware that there mound by the yard-gate, Mister Jarsper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see it. What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper stops, and waits for him to come up, for he lags behind.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you call quick-lime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy!\xe2\x80\x9d says Durdles; \xe2\x80\x9cquick enough to eat your boots. With a little\r\nhandy stirring, quick enough to eat your bones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey go on, presently passing the red windows of the Travellers\xe2\x80\x99\r\nTwopenny, and emerging into the clear moonlight of the Monks\xe2\x80\x99 Vineyard.\r\nThis crossed, they come to Minor Canon Corner: of which the greater\r\npart lies in shadow until the moon shall rise higher in the sky.\r\n\r\nThe sound of a closing house-door strikes their ears, and two men come\r\nout. These are Mr. Crisparkle and Neville. Jasper, with a strange and\r\nsudden smile upon his face, lays the palm of his hand upon the breast\r\nof Durdles, stopping him where he stands.\r\n\r\nAt that end of Minor Canon Corner the shadow is profound in the\r\nexisting state of the light: at that end, too, there is a piece of old\r\ndwarf wall, breast high, the only remaining boundary of what was once a\r\ngarden, but is now the thoroughfare. Jasper and Durdles would have\r\nturned this wall in another instant; but, stopping so short, stand\r\nbehind it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose two are only sauntering,\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper whispers; \xe2\x80\x9cthey will go out\r\ninto the moonlight soon. Let us keep quiet here, or they will detain\r\nus, or want to join us, or what not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDurdles nods assent, and falls to munching some fragments from his\r\nbundle. Jasper folds his arms upon the top of the wall, and, with his\r\nchin resting on them, watches. He takes no note whatever of the Minor\r\nCanon, but watches Neville, as though his eye were at the trigger of a\r\nloaded rifle, and he had covered him, and were going to fire. A sense\r\nof destructive power is so expressed in his face, that even Durdles\r\npauses in his munching, and looks at him, with an unmunched something\r\nin his cheek.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Mr. Crisparkle and Neville walk to and fro, quietly talking\r\ntogether. What they say, cannot be heard consecutively; but Mr. Jasper\r\nhas already distinguished his own name more than once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the first day of the week,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle can be distinctly\r\nheard to observe, as they turn back; \xe2\x80\x9cand the last day of the week is\r\nChristmas Eve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may be certain of me, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe echoes were favourable at those points, but as the two approach,\r\nthe sound of their talking becomes confused again. The word\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cconfidence,\xe2\x80\x9d shattered by the echoes, but still capable of being\r\npieced together, is uttered by Mr. Crisparkle. As they draw still\r\nnearer, this fragment of a reply is heard: \xe2\x80\x9cNot deserved yet, but shall\r\nbe, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d As they turn away again, Jasper again hears his own name, in\r\nconnection with the words from Mr. Crisparkle: \xe2\x80\x9cRemember that I said I\r\nanswered for you confidently.\xe2\x80\x9d Then the sound of their talk becomes\r\nconfused again; they halting for a little while, and some earnest\r\naction on the part of Neville succeeding. When they move once more, Mr.\r\nCrisparkle is seen to look up at the sky, and to point before him. They\r\nthen slowly disappear; passing out into the moonlight at the opposite\r\nend of the Corner.\r\n\r\nIt is not until they are gone, that Mr. Jasper moves. But then he turns\r\nto Durdles, and bursts into a fit of laughter. Durdles, who still has\r\nthat suspended something in his cheek, and who sees nothing to laugh\r\nat, stares at him until Mr. Jasper lays his face down on his arms to\r\nhave his laugh out. Then Durdles bolts the something, as if desperately\r\nresigning himself to indigestion.\r\n\r\nAmong those secluded nooks there is very little stir or movement after\r\ndark. There is little enough in the high tide of the day, but there is\r\nnext to none at night. Besides that the cheerfully frequented High\r\nStreet lies nearly parallel to the spot (the old Cathedral rising\r\nbetween the two), and is the natural channel in which the Cloisterham\r\ntraffic flows, a certain awful hush pervades the ancient pile, the\r\ncloisters, and the churchyard, after dark, which not many people care\r\nto encounter. Ask the first hundred citizens of Cloisterham, met at\r\nrandom in the streets at noon, if they believed in Ghosts, they would\r\ntell you no; but put them to choose at night between these eerie\r\nPrecincts and the thoroughfare of shops, and you would find that\r\nninety-nine declared for the longer round and the more frequented way.\r\nThe cause of this is not to be found in any local superstition that\r\nattaches to the Precincts\xe2\x80\x94albeit a mysterious lady, with a child in her\r\narms and a rope dangling from her neck, has been seen flitting about\r\nthere by sundry witnesses as intangible as herself\xe2\x80\x94but it is to be\r\nsought in the innate shrinking of dust with the breath of life in it\r\nfrom dust out of which the breath of life has passed; also, in the\r\nwidely diffused, and almost as widely unacknowledged, reflection: \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nthe dead do, under any circumstances, become visible to the living,\r\nthese are such likely surroundings for the purpose that I, the living,\r\nwill get out of them as soon as I can.\xe2\x80\x9d Hence, when Mr. Jasper and\r\nDurdles pause to glance around them, before descending into the crypt\r\nby a small side door, of which the latter has a key, the whole expanse\r\nof moonlight in their view is utterly deserted. One might fancy that\r\nthe tide of life was stemmed by Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s own gatehouse. The murmur\r\nof the tide is heard beyond; but no wave passes the archway, over which\r\nhis lamp burns red behind his curtain, as if the building were a\r\nLighthouse.\r\n\r\nThey enter, locking themselves in, descend the rugged steps, and are\r\ndown in the Crypt. The lantern is not wanted, for the moonlight strikes\r\nin at the groined windows, bare of glass, the broken frames for which\r\ncast patterns on the ground. The heavy pillars which support the roof\r\nengender masses of black shade, but between them there are lanes of\r\nlight. Up and down these lanes they walk, Durdles discoursing of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cold uns\xe2\x80\x9d he yet counts on disinterring, and slapping a wall, in which\r\nhe considers \xe2\x80\x9ca whole family on \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x9d to be stoned and earthed up, just\r\nas if he were a familiar friend of the family. The taciturnity of\r\nDurdles is for the time overcome by Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s wicker bottle, which\r\ncirculates freely;\xe2\x80\x94in the sense, that is to say, that its contents\r\nenter freely into Mr. Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s circulation, while Mr. Jasper only\r\nrinses his mouth once, and casts forth the rinsing.\r\n\r\nThey are to ascend the great Tower. On the steps by which they rise to\r\nthe Cathedral, Durdles pauses for new store of breath. The steps are\r\nvery dark, but out of the darkness they can see the lanes of light they\r\nhave traversed. Durdles seats himself upon a step. Mr. Jasper seats\r\nhimself upon another. The odour from the wicker bottle (which has\r\nsomehow passed into Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s keeping) soon intimates that the cork has\r\nbeen taken out; but this is not ascertainable through the sense of\r\nsight, since neither can descry the other. And yet, in talking, they\r\nturn to one another, as though their faces could commune together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is good stuff, Mister Jarsper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very good stuff, I hope.\xe2\x80\x94I bought it on purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t show, you see, the old uns don\xe2\x80\x99t, Mister Jarsper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a more confused world than it is, if they could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it _would_ lead towards a mixing of things,\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles acquiesces:\r\npausing on the remark, as if the idea of ghosts had not previously\r\npresented itself to him in a merely inconvenient light, domestically or\r\nchronologically. \xe2\x80\x9cBut do you think there may be Ghosts of other things,\r\nthough not of men and women?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat things? Flower-beds and watering-pots? horses and harness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Sounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sounds?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat cries do you mean? Chairs to mend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I mean screeches. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you, Mr. Jarsper. Wait a bit till\r\nI put the bottle right.\xe2\x80\x9d Here the cork is evidently taken out again,\r\nand replaced again. \xe2\x80\x9cThere! _Now_ it\xe2\x80\x99s right! This time last year, only\r\na few days later, I happened to have been doing what was correct by the\r\nseason, in the way of giving it the welcome it had a right to expect,\r\nwhen them town-boys set on me at their worst. At length I gave \xe2\x80\x99em the\r\nslip, and turned in here. And here I fell asleep. And what woke me? The\r\nghost of a cry. The ghost of one terrific shriek, which shriek was\r\nfollowed by the ghost of the howl of a dog: a long, dismal, woeful\r\nhowl, such as a dog gives when a person\xe2\x80\x99s dead. That was _my_ last\r\nChristmas Eve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d is the very abrupt, and, one might say, fierce\r\nretort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that I made inquiries everywhere about, and, that no living\r\nears but mine heard either that cry or that howl. So I say they was\r\nboth ghosts; though why they came to me, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never made out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you were another kind of man,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, scornfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I thought myself,\xe2\x80\x9d answers Durdles with his usual composure; \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nyet I was picked out for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper had risen suddenly, when he asked him what he meant, and he now\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x9cCome; we shall freeze here; lead the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDurdles complies, not over-steadily; opens the door at the top of the\r\nsteps with the key he has already used; and so emerges on the Cathedral\r\nlevel, in a passage at the side of the chancel. Here, the moonlight is\r\nso very bright again that the colours of the nearest stained-glass\r\nwindow are thrown upon their faces. The appearance of the unconscious\r\nDurdles, holding the door open for his companion to follow, as if from\r\nthe grave, is ghastly enough, with a purple hand across his face, and a\r\nyellow splash upon his brow; but he bears the close scrutiny of his\r\ncompanion in an insensible way, although it is prolonged while the\r\nlatter fumbles among his pockets for a key confided to him that will\r\nopen an iron gate, so to enable them to pass to the staircase of the\r\ngreat tower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat and the bottle are enough for you to carry,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, giving it\r\nto Durdles; \xe2\x80\x9chand your bundle to me; I am younger and longer-winded\r\nthan you.\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles hesitates for a moment between bundle and bottle;\r\nbut gives the preference to the bottle as being by far the better\r\ncompany, and consigns the dry weight to his fellow-explorer.\r\n\r\nThen they go up the winding staircase of the great tower, toilsomely,\r\nturning and turning, and lowering their heads to avoid the stairs\r\nabove, or the rough stone pivot around which they twist. Durdles has\r\nlighted his lantern, by drawing from the cold, hard wall a spark of\r\nthat mysterious fire which lurks in everything, and, guided by this\r\nspeck, they clamber up among the cobwebs and the dust. Their way lies\r\nthrough strange places. Twice or thrice they emerge into level,\r\nlow-arched galleries, whence they can look down into the moon-lit nave;\r\nand where Durdles, waving his lantern, waves the dim angels\xe2\x80\x99 heads upon\r\nthe corbels of the roof, seeming to watch their progress. Anon they\r\nturn into narrower and steeper staircases, and the night-air begins to\r\nblow upon them, and the chirp of some startled jackdaw or frightened\r\nrook precedes the heavy beating of wings in a confined space, and the\r\nbeating down of dust and straws upon their heads. At last, leaving\r\ntheir light behind a stair\xe2\x80\x94for it blows fresh up here\xe2\x80\x94they look down on\r\nCloisterham, fair to see in the moonlight: its ruined habitations and\r\nsanctuaries of the dead, at the tower\xe2\x80\x99s base: its moss-softened\r\nred-tiled roofs and red-brick houses of the living, clustered beyond:\r\nits river winding down from the mist on the horizon, as though that\r\nwere its source, and already heaving with a restless knowledge of its\r\napproach towards the sea.\r\n\r\nOnce again, an unaccountable expedition this! Jasper (always moving\r\nsoftly with no visible reason) contemplates the scene, and especially\r\nthat stillest part of it which the Cathedral overshadows. But he\r\ncontemplates Durdles quite as curiously, and Durdles is by times\r\nconscious of his watchful eyes.\r\n\r\nOnly by times, because Durdles is growing drowsy. As a\xc3\xabronauts lighten\r\nthe load they carry, when they wish to rise, similarly Durdles has\r\nlightened the wicker bottle in coming up. Snatches of sleep surprise\r\nhim on his legs, and stop him in his talk. A mild fit of calenture\r\nseizes him, in which he deems that the ground so far below, is on a\r\nlevel with the tower, and would as lief walk off the tower into the air\r\nas not. Such is his state when they begin to come down. And as\r\na\xc3\xabronauts make themselves heavier when they wish to descend, similarly\r\nDurdles charges himself with more liquid from the wicker bottle, that\r\nhe may come down the better.\r\n\r\nThe iron gate attained and locked\xe2\x80\x94but not before Durdles has tumbled\r\ntwice, and cut an eyebrow open once\xe2\x80\x94they descend into the crypt again,\r\nwith the intent of issuing forth as they entered. But, while returning\r\namong those lanes of light, Durdles becomes so very uncertain, both of\r\nfoot and speech, that he half drops, half throws himself down, by one\r\nof the heavy pillars, scarcely less heavy than itself, and indistinctly\r\nappeals to his companion for forty winks of a second each.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you will have it so, or must have it so,\xe2\x80\x9d replies Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll not\r\nleave you here. Take them, while I walk to and fro.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDurdles is asleep at once; and in his sleep he dreams a dream.\r\n\r\nIt is not much of a dream, considering the vast extent of the domains\r\nof dreamland, and their wonderful productions; it is only remarkable\r\nfor being unusually restless and unusually real. He dreams of lying\r\nthere, asleep, and yet counting his companion\xe2\x80\x99s footsteps as he walks\r\nto and fro. He dreams that the footsteps die away into distance of time\r\nand of space, and that something touches him, and that something falls\r\nfrom his hand. Then something clinks and gropes about, and he dreams\r\nthat he is alone for so long a time, that the lanes of light take new\r\ndirections as the moon advances in her course. From succeeding\r\nunconsciousness he passes into a dream of slow uneasiness from cold;\r\nand painfully awakes to a perception of the lanes of light\xe2\x80\x94really\r\nchanged, much as he had dreamed\xe2\x80\x94and Jasper walking among them, beating\r\nhis hands and feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHolloa!\xe2\x80\x9d Durdles cries out, unmeaningly alarmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAwake at last?\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, coming up to him. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know that your\r\nforties have stretched into thousands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark! The bells are going in the Tower!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey strike four quarters, and then the great bell strikes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Durdles, scrambling up; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you try to wake me,\r\nMister Jarsper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did. I might as well have tried to wake the dead\xe2\x80\x94your own family of\r\ndead, up in the corner there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you touch me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTouch you! Yes.
