Shook you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Durdles recalls that touching something in his dream, he looks down\r\non the pavement, and sees the key of the crypt door lying close to\r\nwhere he himself lay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dropped you, did I?\xe2\x80\x9d he says, picking it up, and recalling that part\r\nof his dream. As he gathers himself up again into an upright position,\r\nor into a position as nearly upright as he ever maintains, he is again\r\nconscious of being watched by his companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9care you quite ready? Pray don\xe2\x80\x99t hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me get my bundle right, Mister Jarsper, and I\xe2\x80\x99m with you.\xe2\x80\x9d As he\r\nties it afresh, he is once more conscious that he is very narrowly\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you suspect me of, Mister Jarsper?\xe2\x80\x9d he asks, with drunken\r\ndispleasure. \xe2\x80\x9cLet them as has any suspicions of Durdles name \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve no suspicions of you, my good Mr. Durdles; but I have suspicions\r\nthat my bottle was filled with something stiffer than either of us\r\nsupposed. And I also have suspicions,\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper adds, taking it from the\r\npavement and turning it bottom upwards, \xe2\x80\x9cthat it\xe2\x80\x99s empty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDurdles condescends to laugh at this. Continuing to chuckle when his\r\nlaugh is over, as though remonstrant with himself on his drinking\r\npowers, he rolls to the door and unlocks it. They both pass out, and\r\nDurdles relocks it, and pockets his key.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA thousand thanks for a curious and interesting night,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper,\r\ngiving him his hand; \xe2\x80\x9cyou can make your own way home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so!\xe2\x80\x9d answers Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you was to offer Durdles the\r\naffront to show him his way home, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go home.\r\n\r\nDurdles wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go home till morning;\r\nAnd _then_ Durdles wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go home,\r\n\r\n\r\nDurdles wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d This with the utmost defiance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, Mister Jarsper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEach is turning his own way, when a sharp whistle rends the silence,\r\nand the jargon is yelped out:\r\n\r\nWiddy widdy wen!\r\nI\xe2\x80\x94ket\xe2\x80\x94ches\xe2\x80\x94Im\xe2\x80\x94out\xe2\x80\x94ar\xe2\x80\x94ter\xe2\x80\x94ten.\r\nWiddy widdy wy!\r\nThen\xe2\x80\x94E\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94go\xe2\x80\x94then\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94shy\xe2\x80\x94\r\nWiddy Widdy Wake-cock warning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nInstantly afterwards, a rapid fire of stones rattles at the Cathedral\r\nwall, and the hideous small boy is beheld opposite, dancing in the\r\nmoonlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Is that baby-devil on the watch there!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Jasper in a fury:\r\nso quickly roused, and so violent, that he seems an older devil\r\nhimself. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall shed the blood of that impish wretch! I know I shall\r\ndo it!\xe2\x80\x9d Regardless of the fire, though it hits him more than once, he\r\nrushes at Deputy, collars him, and tries to bring him across. But\r\nDeputy is not to be so easily brought across. With a diabolical insight\r\ninto the strongest part of his position, he is no sooner taken by the\r\nthroat than he curls up his legs, forces his assailant to hang him, as\r\nit were, and gurgles in his throat, and screws his body, and twists, as\r\nalready undergoing the first agonies of strangulation. There is nothing\r\nfor it but to drop him. He instantly gets himself together, backs over\r\nto Durdles, and cries to his assailant, gnashing the great gap in front\r\nof his mouth with rage and malice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll blind yer, s\xe2\x80\x99elp me! I\xe2\x80\x99ll stone yer eyes out, s\xe2\x80\x99elp me! If I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t have yer eyesight, bellows me!\xe2\x80\x9d At the same time dodging behind\r\nDurdles, and snarling at Jasper, now from this side of him, and now\r\nfrom that: prepared, if pounced upon, to dart away in all manner of\r\ncurvilinear directions, and, if run down after all, to grovel in the\r\ndust, and cry: \xe2\x80\x9cNow, hit me when I\xe2\x80\x99m down! Do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t hurt the boy, Mister Jarsper,\xe2\x80\x9d urges Durdles, shielding him.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRecollect yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe followed us to-night, when we first came here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d replies Deputy, in his one form of polite\r\ncontradiction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been prowling near us ever since!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Deputy. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d only jist come out for my\r\n\xe2\x80\x99elth when I see you two a-coming out of the Kin-freederel. If\r\n\r\nI\xe2\x80\x94ket\xe2\x80\x94ches\xe2\x80\x94Im\xe2\x80\x94out\xe2\x80\x94ar\xe2\x80\x94ter\xe2\x80\x94ten!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n(with the usual rhythm and dance, though dodging behind Durdles), \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t _any_ fault, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake him home, then,\xe2\x80\x9d retorts Jasper, ferociously, though with a\r\nstrong check upon himself, \xe2\x80\x9cand let my eyes be rid of the sight of\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDeputy, with another sharp whistle, at once expressing his relief, and\r\nhis commencement of a milder stoning of Mr. Durdles, begins stoning\r\nthat respectable gentleman home, as if he were a reluctant ox. Mr.\r\nJasper goes to his gatehouse, brooding. And thus, as everything comes\r\nto an end, the unaccountable expedition comes to an end\xe2\x80\x94for the time.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\nBOTH AT THEIR BEST\r\n\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s establishment was about to undergo a serene hush. The\r\nChristmas recess was at hand. What had once, and at no remote period,\r\nbeen called, even by the erudite Miss Twinkleton herself, \xe2\x80\x9cthe half,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbut what was now called, as being more elegant, and more strictly\r\ncollegiate, \xe2\x80\x9cthe term,\xe2\x80\x9d would expire to-morrow. A noticeable relaxation\r\nof discipline had for some few days pervaded the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. Club\r\nsuppers had occurred in the bedrooms, and a dressed tongue had been\r\ncarved with a pair of scissors, and handed round with the curling\r\ntongs. Portions of marmalade had likewise been distributed on a service\r\nof plates constructed of curlpaper; and cowslip wine had been quaffed\r\nfrom the small squat measuring glass in which little Rickitts (a junior\r\nof weakly constitution) took her steel drops daily. The housemaids had\r\nbeen bribed with various fragments of riband, and sundry pairs of shoes\r\nmore or less down at heel, to make no mention of crumbs in the beds;\r\nthe airiest costumes had been worn on these festive occasions; and the\r\ndaring Miss Ferdinand had even surprised the company with a sprightly\r\nsolo on the comb-and-curlpaper, until suffocated in her own pillow by\r\ntwo flowing-haired executioners.\r\n\r\nNor were these the only tokens of dispersal. Boxes appeared in the\r\nbedrooms (where they were capital at other times), and a surprising\r\namount of packing took place, out of all proportion to the amount\r\npacked. Largess, in the form of odds and ends of cold cream and\r\npomatum, and also of hairpins, was freely distributed among the\r\nattendants. On charges of inviolable secrecy, confidences were\r\ninterchanged respecting golden youth of England expected to call, \xe2\x80\x9cat\r\nhome,\xe2\x80\x9d on the first opportunity. Miss Giggles (deficient in sentiment)\r\ndid indeed profess that she, for her part, acknowledged such homage by\r\nmaking faces at the golden youth; but this young lady was outvoted by\r\nan immense majority.\r\n\r\nOn the last night before a recess, it was always expressly made a point\r\nof honour that nobody should go to sleep, and that Ghosts should be\r\nencouraged by all possible means. This compact invariably broke down,\r\nand all the young ladies went to sleep very soon, and got up very\r\nearly.\r\n\r\nThe concluding ceremony came off at twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the day of\r\ndeparture; when Miss Twinkleton, supported by Mrs. Tisher, held a\r\ndrawing-room in her own apartment (the globes already covered with\r\nbrown Holland), where glasses of white-wine and plates of cut\r\npound-cake were discovered on the table. Miss Twinkleton then said:\r\nLadies, another revolving year had brought us round to that festive\r\nperiod at which the first feelings of our nature bounded in our\xe2\x80\x94Miss\r\nTwinkleton was annually going to add \xe2\x80\x9cbosoms,\xe2\x80\x9d but annually stopped on\r\nthe brink of that expression, and substituted \xe2\x80\x9chearts.\xe2\x80\x9d Hearts; our\r\nhearts. Hem! Again a revolving year, ladies, had brought us to a pause\r\nin our studies\xe2\x80\x94let us hope our greatly advanced studies\xe2\x80\x94and, like the\r\nmariner in his bark, the warrior in his tent, the captive in his\r\ndungeon, and the traveller in his various conveyances, we yearned for\r\nhome. Did we say, on such an occasion, in the opening words of Mr.\r\nAddison\xe2\x80\x99s impressive tragedy:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dawn is overcast, the morning lowers,\r\nAnd heavily in clouds brings on the day,\r\nThe great, th\xe2\x80\x99 important day\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nNot so. From horizon to zenith all was _couleur de rose_, for all was\r\nredolent of our relations and friends. Might _we_ find _them_\r\nprospering as _we_ expected; might _they_ find _us_ prospering as\r\n_they_ expected! Ladies, we would now, with our love to one another,\r\nwish one another good-bye, and happiness, until we met again. And when\r\nthe time should come for our resumption of those pursuits which (here a\r\ngeneral depression set in all round), pursuits which, pursuits\r\nwhich;\xe2\x80\x94then let us ever remember what was said by the Spartan General,\r\nin words too trite for repetition, at the battle it were superfluous to\r\nspecify.\r\n\r\nThe handmaidens of the establishment, in their best caps, then handed\r\nthe trays, and the young ladies sipped and crumbled, and the bespoken\r\ncoaches began to choke the street. Then leave-taking was not long\r\nabout; and Miss Twinkleton, in saluting each young lady\xe2\x80\x99s cheek,\r\nconfided to her an exceedingly neat letter, addressed to her next\r\nfriend at law, \xe2\x80\x9cwith Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s best compliments\xe2\x80\x9d in the corner.\r\nThis missive she handed with an air as if it had not the least\r\nconnexion with the bill, but were something in the nature of a delicate\r\nand joyful surprise.\r\n\r\nSo many times had Rosa seen such dispersals, and so very little did she\r\nknow of any other Home, that she was contented to remain where she was,\r\nand was even better contented than ever before, having her latest\r\nfriend with her. And yet her latest friendship had a blank place in it\r\nof which she could not fail to be sensible. Helena Landless, having\r\nbeen a party to her brother\xe2\x80\x99s revelation about Rosa, and having entered\r\ninto that compact of silence with Mr. Crisparkle, shrank from any\r\nallusion to Edwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s name. Why she so avoided it, was mysterious\r\nto Rosa, but she perfectly perceived the fact. But for the fact, she\r\nmight have relieved her own little perplexed heart of some of its\r\ndoubts and hesitations, by taking Helena into her confidence. As it\r\nwas, she had no such vent: she could only ponder on her own\r\ndifficulties, and wonder more and more why this avoidance of Edwin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname should last, now that she knew\xe2\x80\x94for so much Helena had told\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94that a good understanding was to be re\xc3\xabstablished between the two\r\nyoung men, when Edwin came down.\r\n\r\nIt would have made a pretty picture, so many pretty girls kissing Rosa\r\nin the cold porch of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and that sunny little creature\r\npeeping out of it (unconscious of sly faces carved on spout and gable\r\npeeping at her), and waving farewells to the departing coaches, as if\r\nshe represented the spirit of rosy youth abiding in the place to keep\r\nit bright and warm in its desertion. The hoarse High Street became\r\nmusical with the cry, in various silvery voices, \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, Rosebud\r\ndarling!\xe2\x80\x9d and the effigy of Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s father over the opposite\r\ndoorway seemed to say to mankind: \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen, favour me with your\r\nattention to this charming little last lot left behind, and bid with a\r\nspirit worthy of the occasion!\xe2\x80\x9d Then the staid street, so unwontedly\r\nsparkling, youthful, and fresh for a few rippling moments, ran dry, and\r\nCloisterham was itself again.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, Rosebud darling\xe2\x80\x9d]\r\n\r\n\r\nIf Rosebud in her bower now waited Edwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s coming with an uneasy\r\nheart, Edwin for his part was uneasy too. With far less force of\r\npurpose in his composition than the childish beauty, crowned by\r\nacclamation fairy queen of Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s establishment, he had a\r\nconscience, and Mr. Grewgious had pricked it. That gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s steady\r\nconvictions of what was right and what was wrong in such a case as his,\r\nwere neither to be frowned aside nor laughed aside. They would not be\r\nmoved. But for the dinner in Staple Inn, and but for the ring he\r\ncarried in the breast pocket of his coat, he would have drifted into\r\ntheir wedding-day without another pause for real thought, loosely\r\ntrusting that all would go well, left alone. But that serious putting\r\nhim on his truth to the living and the dead had brought him to a check.\r\nHe must either give the ring to Rosa, or he must take it back. Once put\r\ninto this narrowed way of action, it was curious that he began to\r\nconsider Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s claims upon him more unselfishly than he had ever\r\nconsidered them before, and began to be less sure of himself than he\r\nhad ever been in all his easy-going days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will be guided by what she says, and by how we get on,\xe2\x80\x9d was his\r\ndecision, walking from the gatehouse to the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever\r\ncomes of it, I will bear his words in mind, and try to be true to the\r\nliving and the dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa was dressed for walking. She expected him. It was a bright, frosty\r\nday, and Miss Twinkleton had already graciously sanctioned fresh air.\r\nThus they got out together before it became necessary for either Miss\r\nTwinkleton, or the deputy high-priest Mrs. Tisher, to lay even so much\r\nas one of those usual offerings on the shrine of Propriety.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Eddy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, when they had turned out of the High Street,\r\nand had got among the quiet walks in the neighbourhood of the Cathedral\r\nand the river: \xe2\x80\x9cI want to say something very serious to you. I have\r\nbeen thinking about it for a long, long time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to be serious with you too, Rosa dear. I mean to be serious and\r\nearnest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Eddy. And you will not think me unkind because I begin,\r\nwill you? You will not think I speak for myself only, because I speak\r\nfirst? That would not be generous, would it? And I know you are\r\ngenerous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope I am not ungenerous to you, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d He called her Pussy\r\nno more. Never again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there is no fear,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Rosa, \xe2\x80\x9cof our quarrelling, is there?\r\nBecause, Eddy,\xe2\x80\x9d clasping her hand on his arm, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have so much reason\r\nto be very lenient to each other!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will be, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a dear good boy! Eddy, let us be courageous. Let us change to\r\nbrother and sister from this day forth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever be husband and wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither spoke again for a little while. But after that pause he said,\r\nwith some effort:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I know that this has been in both our minds, Rosa, and of\r\ncourse I am in honour bound to confess freely that it does not\r\noriginate with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, nor with you, dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned, with pathetic earnestness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat sprung up between us. You are not truly happy in our engagement;\r\nI am not truly happy in it. O, I am so sorry, so sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d And there she\r\nbroke into tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am deeply sorry too, Rosa. Deeply sorry for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I for you, poor boy! And I for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis pure young feeling, this gentle and forbearing feeling of each\r\ntowards the other, brought with it its reward in a softening light that\r\nseemed to shine on their position. The relations between them did not\r\nlook wilful, or capricious, or a failure, in such a light; they became\r\nelevated into something more self-denying, honourable, affectionate,\r\nand true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we knew yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, as she dried her eyes, \xe2\x80\x9cand we did\r\nknow yesterday, and on many, many yesterdays, that we were far from\r\nright together in those relations which were not of our own choosing,\r\nwhat better could we do to-day than change them? It is natural that we\r\nshould be sorry, and you see how sorry we both are; but how much better\r\nto be sorry now than then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen it would be too late. And then we should be angry, besides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother silence fell upon them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa innocently, \xe2\x80\x9cyou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t like me then; and\r\nyou can always like me now, for I shall not be a drag upon you, or a\r\nworry to you. And I can always like you now, and your sister will not\r\ntease or trifle with you. I often did when I was not your sister, and I\r\nbeg your pardon for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let us come to that, Rosa; or I shall want more pardoning than I\r\nlike to think of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed, Eddy; you are too hard, my generous boy, upon yourself.\r\nLet us sit down, brother, on these ruins, and let me tell you how it\r\nwas with us. I think I know, for I have considered about it very much\r\nsince you were here last time. You liked me, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you? You thought I\r\nwas a nice little thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody thinks that, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they?\xe2\x80\x9d She knitted her brow musingly for a moment, and then flashed\r\nout with the bright little induction: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, but say they do. Surely it\r\nwas not enough that you should think of me only as other people did;\r\nnow, was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe point was not to be got over. It was not enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is just what I mean; that is just how it was with us,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nRosa. \xe2\x80\x9cYou liked me very well, and you had grown used to me, and had\r\ngrown used to the idea of our being married. You accepted the situation\r\nas an inevitable kind of thing, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you? It was to be, you thought,\r\nand why discuss or dispute it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was new and strange to him to have himself presented to himself so\r\nclearly, in a glass of her holding up. He had always patronised her, in\r\nhis superiority to her share of woman\xe2\x80\x99s wit. Was that but another\r\ninstance of something radically amiss in the terms on which they had\r\nbeen gliding towards a life-long bondage?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll this that I say of you is true of me as well, Eddy. Unless it was,\r\nI might not be bold enough to say it. Only, the difference between us\r\nwas, that by little and little there crept into my mind a habit of\r\nthinking about it, instead of dismissing it. My life is not so busy as\r\nyours, you see, and I have not so many things to think of. So I thought\r\nabout it very much, and I cried about it very much too (though that was\r\nnot your fault, poor boy); when all at once my guardian came down, to\r\nprepare for my leaving the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. I tried to hint to him that I\r\nwas not quite settled in my mind, but I hesitated and failed, and he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t understand me. But he is a good, good man. And he put before me\r\nso kindly, and yet so strongly, how seriously we ought to consider, in\r\nour circumstances, that I resolved to speak to you the next moment we\r\nwere alone and grave. And if I seemed to come to it easily just now,\r\nbecause I came to it all at once, don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was so really, Eddy,\r\nfor O, it was very, very hard, and O, I am very, very sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer full heart broke into tears again. He put his arm about her waist,\r\nand they walked by the river-side together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour guardian has spoken to me too, Rosa dear. I saw him before I left\r\nLondon.\xe2\x80\x9d His right hand was in his breast, seeking the ring; but he\r\nchecked it, as he thought: \xe2\x80\x9cIf I am to take it back, why should I tell\r\nher of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that made you more serious about it, didn\xe2\x80\x99t it, Eddy? And if I had\r\nnot spoken to you, as I have, you would have spoken to me? I hope you\r\ncan tell me so? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it to be _all_ my doing, though it _is_ so\r\nmuch better for us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I should have spoken; I should have put everything before you; I\r\ncame intending to do it. But I never could have spoken to you as you\r\nhave spoken to me, Rosa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say you mean so coldly or unkindly, Eddy, please, if you can\r\nhelp it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean so sensibly and delicately, so wisely and affectionately.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my dear brother!\xe2\x80\x9d She kissed his hand in a little rapture. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\ndear girls will be dreadfully disappointed,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rosa, laughing, with\r\nthe dewdrops glistening in her bright eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have looked forward\r\nto it so, poor pets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but I fear it will be a worse disappointment to Jack,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin\r\nDrood, with a start. \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of Jack!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer swift and intent look at him as he said the words could no more be\r\nrecalled than a flash of lightning can. But it appeared as though she\r\nwould have instantly recalled it, if she could; for she looked down,\r\nconfused, and breathed quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t doubt its being a blow to Jack, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe merely replied, and that evasively and hurriedly: Why should she?\r\nShe had not thought about it. He seemed, to her, to have so little to\r\ndo with it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear child! can you suppose that any one so wrapped up in\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s expression: not mine\xe2\x80\x94as Jack is in me, could fail\r\nto be struck all of a heap by such a sudden and complete change in my\r\nlife? I say sudden, because it will be sudden to _him_, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded twice or thrice, and her lips parted as if she would have\r\nassented. But she uttered no sound, and her breathing was no slower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow shall I tell Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin, ruminating. If he had been less\r\noccupied with the thought, he must have seen her singular emotion. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nnever thought of Jack. It must be broken to him, before the town-crier\r\nknows it. I dine with the dear fellow to-morrow and next day\xe2\x80\x94Christmas\r\nEve and Christmas Day\xe2\x80\x94but it would never do to spoil his feast-days. He\r\nalways worries about me, and moddley-coddleys in the merest trifles.\r\nThe news is sure to overset him. How on earth shall this be broken to\r\nJack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must be told, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Rosa! who ought to be in our confidence, if not Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy guardian promised to come down, if I should write and ask him. I am\r\ngoing to do so. Would you like to leave it to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bright idea!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Edwin. \xe2\x80\x9cThe other trustee. Nothing more natural.\r\nHe comes down, he goes to Jack, he relates what we have agreed upon,\r\nand he states our case better than we could. He has already spoken\r\nfeelingly to you, he has already spoken feelingly to me, and he\xe2\x80\x99ll put\r\nthe whole thing feelingly to Jack. That\xe2\x80\x99s it! I am not a coward, Rosa,\r\nbut to tell you a secret, I am a little afraid of Jack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! you are not afraid of him!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa, turning white, and\r\nclasping her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, sister Rosa, sister Rosa, what do you see from the turret?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nEdwin, rallying her. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou frightened me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost unintentionally, but I am as sorry as if I had meant to do it.\r\nCould you possibly suppose for a moment, from any loose way of speaking\r\nof mine, that I was literally afraid of the dear fond fellow? What I\r\nmean is, that he is subject to a kind of paroxysm, or fit\xe2\x80\x94I saw him in\r\nit once\xe2\x80\x94and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but that so great a surprise, coming upon him\r\ndirect from me whom he is so wrapped up in, might bring it on perhaps.\r\nWhich\xe2\x80\x94and this is the secret I was going to tell you\xe2\x80\x94is another reason\r\nfor your guardian\xe2\x80\x99s making the communication. He is so steady, precise,\r\nand exact, that he will talk Jack\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts into shape, in no time:\r\nwhereas with me Jack is always impulsive and hurried, and, I may say,\r\nalmost womanish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa seemed convinced. Perhaps from her own very different point of\r\nview of \xe2\x80\x9cJack,\xe2\x80\x9d she felt comforted and protected by the interposition\r\nof Mr. Grewgious between herself and him.\r\n\r\nAnd now, Edwin Drood\xe2\x80\x99s right hand closed again upon the ring in its\r\nlittle case, and again was checked by the consideration: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\ncertain, now, that I am to give it back to him; then why should I tell\r\nher of it?\xe2\x80\x9d That pretty sympathetic nature which could be so sorry for\r\nhim in the blight of their childish hopes of happiness together, and\r\ncould so quietly find itself alone in a new world to weave fresh\r\nwreaths of such flowers as it might prove to bear, the old world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nflowers being withered, would be grieved by those sorrowful jewels; and\r\nto what purpose? Why should it be? They were but a sign of broken joys\r\nand baseless projects; in their very beauty they were (as the\r\nunlikeliest of men had said) almost a cruel satire on the loves, hopes,\r\nplans, of humanity, which are able to forecast nothing, and are so much\r\nbrittle dust. Let them be. He would restore them to her guardian when\r\nhe came down; he in his turn would restore them to the cabinet from\r\nwhich he had unwillingly taken them; and there, like old letters or old\r\nvows, or other records of old aspirations come to nothing, they would\r\nbe disregarded, until, being valuable, they were sold into circulation\r\nagain, to repeat their former round.\r\n\r\nLet them be. Let them lie unspoken of, in his breast. However\r\ndistinctly or indistinctly he entertained these thoughts, he arrived at\r\nthe conclusion, Let them be. Among the mighty store of wonderful chains\r\nthat are for ever forging, day and night, in the vast iron-works of\r\ntime and circumstance, there was one chain forged in the moment of that\r\nsmall conclusion, riveted to the foundations of heaven and earth, and\r\ngifted with invincible force to hold and drag.\r\n\r\nThey walked on by the river. They began to speak of their separate\r\nplans. He would quicken his departure from England, and she would\r\nremain where she was, at least as long as Helena remained. The poor\r\ndear girls should have their disappointment broken to them gently, and,\r\nas the first preliminary, Miss Twinkleton should be confided in by\r\nRosa, even in advance of the reappearance of Mr. Grewgious. It should\r\nbe made clear in all quarters that she and Edwin were the best of\r\nfriends. There had never been so serene an understanding between them\r\nsince they were first affianced. And yet there was one reservation on\r\neach side; on hers, that she intended through her guardian to withdraw\r\nherself immediately from the tuition of her music-master; on his, that\r\nhe did already entertain some wandering speculations whether it might\r\never come to pass that he would know more of Miss Landless.\r\n\r\nThe bright, frosty day declined as they walked and spoke together. The\r\nsun dipped in the river far behind them, and the old city lay red\r\nbefore them, as their walk drew to a close. The moaning water cast its\r\nseaweed duskily at their feet, when they turned to leave its margin;\r\nand the rooks hovered above them with hoarse cries, darker splashes in\r\nthe darkening air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will prepare Jack for my flitting soon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edwin, in a low voice,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand I will but see your guardian when he comes, and then go before\r\nthey speak together. It will be better done without my being by. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe know we have done right, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe know we are better so, even now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd shall be far, far better so by-and-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill there was that lingering tenderness in their hearts towards the\r\nold positions they were relinquishing, that they prolonged their\r\nparting. When they came among the elm-trees by the Cathedral, where\r\nthey had last sat together, they stopped as by consent, and Rosa raised\r\nher face to his, as she had never raised it in the old days;\xe2\x80\x94for they\r\nwere old already.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you, dear! Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you, dear! Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey kissed each other fervently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, please take me home, Eddy, and let me be by myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t look round, Rosa,\xe2\x80\x9d he cautioned her, as he drew her arm through\r\nhis, and led her away. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you see Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the trees. He saw us, as we took leave of each other. Poor\r\nfellow! he little thinks we have parted. This will be a blow to him, I\r\nam much afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hurried on, without resting, and hurried on until they had passed\r\nunder the gatehouse into the street; once there, she asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he followed us? You can look without seeming to. Is he behind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Yes, he is! He has just passed out under the gateway. The dear,\r\nsympathetic old fellow likes to keep us in sight. I am afraid he will\r\nbe bitterly disappointed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe pulled hurriedly at the handle of the hoarse old bell, and the gate\r\nsoon opened. Before going in, she gave him one last, wide, wondering\r\nlook, as if she would have asked him with imploring emphasis: \xe2\x80\x9cO! don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou understand?\xe2\x80\x9d And out of that look he vanished from her view.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\nWHEN SHALL THESE THREE MEET AGAIN?\r\n\r\n\r\nChristmas Eve in Cloisterham. A few strange faces in the streets; a few\r\nother faces, half strange and half familiar, once the faces of\r\nCloisterham children, now the faces of men and women who come back from\r\nthe outer world at long intervals to find the city wonderfully shrunken\r\nin size, as if it had not washed by any means well in the meanwhile. To\r\nthese, the striking of the Cathedral clock, and the cawing of the rooks\r\nfrom the Cathedral tower, are like voices of their nursery time. To\r\nsuch as these, it has happened in their dying hours afar off, that they\r\nhave imagined their chamber-floor to be strewn with the autumnal leaves\r\nfallen from the elm-trees in the Close: so have the rustling sounds and\r\nfresh scents of their earliest impressions revived when the circle of\r\ntheir lives was very nearly traced, and the beginning and the end were\r\ndrawing close together.\r\n\r\nSeasonable tokens are about. Red berries shine here and there in the\r\nlattices of Minor Canon Corner; Mr. and Mrs. Tope are daintily sticking\r\nsprigs of holly into the carvings and sconces of the Cathedral stalls,\r\nas if they were sticking them into the coat-button-holes of the Dean\r\nand Chapter. Lavish profusion is in the shops: particularly in the\r\narticles of currants, raisins, spices, candied peel, and moist sugar.\r\nAn unusual air of gallantry and dissipation is abroad; evinced in an\r\nimmense bunch of mistletoe hanging in the greengrocer\xe2\x80\x99s shop doorway,\r\nand a poor little Twelfth Cake, culminating in the figure of a\r\nHarlequin\xe2\x80\x94such a very poor little Twelfth Cake, that one would rather\r\ncalled it a Twenty-fourth Cake or a Forty-eighth Cake\xe2\x80\x94to be raffled for\r\nat the pastrycook\xe2\x80\x99s, terms one shilling per member. Public amusements\r\nare not wanting. The Wax-Work which made so deep an impression on the\r\nreflective mind of the Emperor of China is to be seen by particular\r\ndesire during Christmas Week only, on the premises of the bankrupt\r\nlivery-stable-keeper up the lane; and a new grand comic Christmas\r\npantomime is to be produced at the Theatre: the latter heralded by the\r\nportrait of Signor Jacksonini the clown, saying \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do\r\nto-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d quite as large as life, and almost as miserably. In short,\r\nCloisterham is up and doing: though from this description the High\r\nSchool and Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s are to be excluded. From the former\r\nestablishment the scholars have gone home, every one of them in love\r\nwith one of Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s young ladies (who knows nothing about\r\nit); and only the handmaidens flutter occasionally in the windows of\r\nthe latter. It is noticed, by the bye, that these damsels become,\r\nwithin the limits of decorum, more skittish when thus intrusted with\r\nthe concrete representation of their sex, than when dividing the\r\nrepresentation with Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s young ladies.\r\n\r\nThree are to meet at the gatehouse to-night. How does each one of the\r\nthree get through the day?\r\n\r\nNeville Landless, though absolved from his books for the time by Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x94whose fresh nature is by no means insensible to the charms\r\nof a holiday\xe2\x80\x94reads and writes in his quiet room, with a concentrated\r\nair, until it is two hours past noon. He then sets himself to clearing\r\nhis table, to arranging his books, and to tearing up and burning his\r\nstray papers. He makes a clean sweep of all untidy accumulations, puts\r\nall his drawers in order, and leaves no note or scrap of paper\r\nundestroyed, save such memoranda as bear directly on his studies. This\r\ndone, he turns to his wardrobe, selects a few articles of ordinary\r\nwear\xe2\x80\x94among them, change of stout shoes and socks for walking\xe2\x80\x94and packs\r\nthese in a knapsack. This knapsack is new, and he bought it in the High\r\nStreet yesterday. He also purchased, at the same time and at the same\r\nplace, a heavy walking-stick; strong in the handle for the grip of the\r\nhand, and iron-shod. He tries this, swings it, poises it, and lays it\r\nby, with the knapsack, on a window-seat. By this time his arrangements\r\nare complete.\r\n\r\nHe dresses for going out, and is in the act of going\xe2\x80\x94indeed has left\r\nhis room, and has met the Minor Canon on the staircase, coming out of\r\nhis bedroom upon the same story\xe2\x80\x94when he turns back again for his\r\nwalking-stick, thinking he will carry it now. Mr. Crisparkle, who has\r\npaused on the staircase, sees it in his hand on his immediately\r\nreappearing, takes it from him, and asks him with a smile how he\r\nchooses a stick?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I understand the subject,\xe2\x80\x9d he answers. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nchose it for its weight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch too heavy, Neville; _much_ too heavy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo rest upon in a long walk, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest upon?\xe2\x80\x9d repeats Mr. Crisparkle, throwing himself into pedestrian\r\nform. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t rest upon it; you merely balance with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall know better, with practice, sir. I have not lived in a walking\r\ncountry, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cGet into a little training, and we will\r\nhave a few score miles together. I should leave you nowhere now. Do you\r\ncome back before dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think not, as we dine early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle gives him a bright nod and a cheerful good-bye;\r\nexpressing (not without intention) absolute confidence and ease.\r\n\r\nNeville repairs to the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and requests that Miss Landless may\r\nbe informed that her brother is there, by appointment. He waits at the\r\ngate, not even crossing the threshold; for he is on his parole not to\r\nput himself in Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s way.\r\n\r\nHis sister is at least as mindful of the obligation they have taken on\r\nthemselves as he can be, and loses not a moment in joining him. They\r\nmeet affectionately, avoid lingering there, and walk towards the upper\r\ninland country.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to tread upon forbidden ground, Helena,\xe2\x80\x9d says Neville,\r\nwhen they have walked some distance and are turning; \xe2\x80\x9cyou will\r\nunderstand in another moment that I cannot help referring to\xe2\x80\x94what shall\r\nI say?\xe2\x80\x94my infatuation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad you not better avoid it, Neville? You know that I can hear\r\nnothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can hear, my dear, what Mr. Crisparkle has heard, and heard with\r\napproval.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I can hear so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is this. I am not only unsettled and unhappy myself, but I am\r\nconscious of unsettling and interfering with other people. How do I\r\nknow that, but for my unfortunate presence, you, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94the rest of\r\nthat former party, our engaging guardian excepted, might be dining\r\ncheerfully in Minor Canon Corner to-morrow? Indeed it probably would be\r\nso. I can see too well that I am not high in the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s opinion,\r\nand it is easy to understand what an irksome clog I must be upon the\r\nhospitalities of her orderly house\xe2\x80\x94especially at this time of year\xe2\x80\x94when\r\nI must be kept asunder from this person, and there is such a reason for\r\nmy not being brought into contact with that person, and an unfavourable\r\nreputation has preceded me with such another person; and so on. I have\r\nput this very gently to Mr. Crisparkle, for you know his self-denying\r\nways; but still I have put it. What I have laid much greater stress\r\nupon at the same time is, that I am engaged in a miserable struggle\r\nwith myself, and that a little change and absence may enable me to come\r\nthrough it the better. So, the weather being bright and hard, I am\r\ngoing on a walking expedition, and intend taking myself out of\r\neverybody\xe2\x80\x99s way (my own included, I hope) to-morrow morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen to come back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a fortnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd going quite alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much better without company, even if there were any one but you\r\nto bear me company, my dear Helena.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Crisparkle entirely agrees, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEntirely. I am not sure but that at first he was inclined to think it\r\nrather a moody scheme, and one that might do a brooding mind harm. But\r\nwe took a moonlight walk last Monday night, to talk it over at leisure,\r\nand I represented the case to him as it really is. I showed him that I\r\ndo want to conquer myself, and that, this evening well got over, it is\r\nsurely better that I should be away from here just now, than here. I\r\ncould hardly help meeting certain people walking together here, and\r\nthat could do no good, and is certainly not the way to forget. A\r\nfortnight hence, that chance will probably be over, for the time; and\r\nwhen it again arises for the last time, why, I can again go away.\r\nFarther, I really do feel hopeful of bracing exercise and wholesome\r\nfatigue. You know that Mr. Crisparkle allows such things their full\r\nweight in the preservation of his own sound mind in his own sound body,\r\nand that his just spirit is not likely to maintain one set of natural\r\nlaws for himself and another for me. He yielded to my view of the\r\nmatter, when convinced that I was honestly in earnest; and so, with his\r\nfull consent, I start to-morrow morning. Early enough to be not only\r\nout of the streets, but out of hearing of the bells, when the good\r\npeople go to church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena thinks it over, and thinks well of it. And she does\r\nencourage him.\r\n\r\nHe will write to her?\r\n\r\nHe will write to her every alternate day, and tell her all his\r\nadventures.\r\n\r\nDoes he send clothes on in advance of him?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Helena, no. What a strange\r\ndead weight there is in the air!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe calls his attention to those copperous clouds beyond the river, and\r\nsays that the wind is rising. He scarcely speaks again, until he takes\r\nleave of her, at the gate of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House. She does not immediately\r\nenter, when they have parted, but remains looking after him along the\r\nstreet. Twice he passes the gatehouse, reluctant to enter. At length,\r\nthe Cathedral clock chiming one quarter, with a rapid turn he hurries\r\nin.\r\n\r\nAnd so _he_ goes up the postern stair.\r\n\r\nEdwin Drood passes a solitary day. Something of deeper moment than he\r\nhad thought, has gone out of his life; and in the silence of his own\r\nchamber he wept for it last night. Though the image of Miss Landless\r\nstill hovers in the background of his mind, the pretty little\r\naffectionate creature, so much firmer and wiser than he had supposed,\r\noccupies its stronghold. It is with some misgiving of his own\r\nunworthiness that he thinks of her, and of what they might have been to\r\none another, if he had been more in earnest some time ago; if he had\r\nset a higher value on her; if, instead of accepting his lot in life as\r\nan inheritance of course, he had studied the right way to its\r\nappreciation and enhancement. And still, for all this, and though there\r\nis a sharp heartache in all this, the vanity and caprice of youth\r\nsustain that handsome figure of Miss Landless in the background of his\r\nmind.\r\n\r\nThat was a curious look of Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s when they parted at the gate. Did it\r\nmean that she saw below the surface of his thoughts, and down into\r\ntheir twilight depths? Scarcely that, for it was a look of astonished\r\nand keen inquiry. He decides that he cannot understand it, though it\r\nwas remarkably expressive.\r\n\r\nAs he only waits for Mr. Grewgious now, and will depart immediately\r\nafter having seen him, he takes a sauntering leave of the ancient city\r\nand its neighbourhood. He recalls the time when Rosa and he walked here\r\nor there, mere children, full of the dignity of being engaged. Poor\r\nchildren! he thinks, with a pitying sadness.\r\n\r\nFinding that his watch has stopped, he turns into the jeweller\xe2\x80\x99s shop,\r\nto have it wound and set. The jeweller is knowing on the subject of a\r\nbracelet, which he begs leave to submit, in a general and quite aimless\r\nway. It would suit (he considers) a young bride, to perfection;\r\nespecially if of a rather diminutive style of beauty. Finding the\r\nbracelet but coldly looked at, the jeweller invites attention to a tray\r\nof rings for gentlemen; here is a style of ring, now, he remarks\xe2\x80\x94a very\r\nchaste signet\xe2\x80\x94which gentlemen are much given to purchasing, when\r\nchanging their condition. A ring of a very responsible appearance. With\r\nthe date of their wedding-day engraved inside, several gentlemen have\r\npreferred it to any other kind of memento.\r\n\r\nThe rings are as coldly viewed as the bracelet. Having no music-lessons to give in the holiday season, his time\r\nis his own, but for the Cathedral services. He is early among the\r\nshopkeepers, ordering little table luxuries that his nephew likes. His\r\nnephew will not be with him long, he tells his provision-dealers, and\r\nso must be petted and made much of. While out on his hospitable\r\npreparations, he looks in on Mr. Sapsea; and mentions that dear Ned,\r\nand that inflammable young spark of Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s, are to dine at\r\nthe gatehouse to-day, and make up their difference. Mr. Sapsea is by no\r\nmeans friendly towards the inflammable young spark. He says that his\r\ncomplexion is \xe2\x80\x9cUn-English.\xe2\x80\x9d And when Mr. Sapsea has once declared\r\nanything to be Un-English, he considers that thing everlastingly sunk\r\nin the bottomless pit.\r\n\r\nJohn Jasper is truly sorry to hear Mr. Sapsea speak thus, for he knows\r\nright well that Mr. Sapsea never speaks without a meaning, and that he\r\nhas a subtle trick of being right. Mr. Sapsea (by a very remarkable\r\ncoincidence) is of exactly that opinion.\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper is in beautiful voice this day. In the pathetic supplication\r\nto have his heart inclined to keep this law, he quite astonishes his\r\nfellows by his melodious power. He has never sung difficult music with\r\nsuch skill and harmony, as in this day\xe2\x80\x99s Anthem. His nervous\r\ntemperament is occasionally prone to take difficult music a little too\r\nquickly; to-day, his time is perfect.\r\n\r\nThese results are probably attained through a grand composure of the\r\nspirits. The mere mechanism of his throat is a little tender, for he\r\nwears, both with his singing-robe and with his ordinary dress, a large\r\nblack scarf of strong close-woven silk, slung loosely round his neck.\r\nBut his composure is so noticeable, that Mr. Crisparkle speaks of it as\r\nthey come out from Vespers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must thank you, Jasper, for the pleasure with which I have heard you\r\nto-day. I will walk round to the Corner with you, if you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t object; I have plenty of time before my company come; and I want\r\nto say a word to you, which I think you will not be displeased to\r\nhear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell. We were speaking, the other evening, of my black humours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s face falls, and he shakes his head deploringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said, you know, that I should make you an antidote to those black\r\nhumours; and you said you hoped I would consign them to the flames.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I still hope so, Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith the best reason in the world! I mean to burn this year\xe2\x80\x99s Diary at\r\nthe year\xe2\x80\x99s end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle brightens greatly as he thus begins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou anticipate me. Because I feel that I have been out of sorts,\r\ngloomy, bilious, brain-oppressed, whatever it may be. You said I had\r\nbeen exaggerative. So I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s brightened face brightens still more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see it then, because I _was_ out of sorts; but I am in a\r\nhealthier state now, and I acknowledge it with genuine pleasure. I made\r\na great deal of a very little; that\xe2\x80\x99s the fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does me good,\xe2\x80\x9d cries Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cto hear you say it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man leading a monotonous life,\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper proceeds, \xe2\x80\x9cand getting his\r\nnerves, or his stomach, out of order, dwells upon an idea until it\r\nloses its proportions. That was my case with the idea in question. So I\r\nshall burn the evidence of my case, when the book is full, and begin\r\nthe next volume with a clearer vision.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is better,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Crisparkle, stopping at the steps of his own\r\ndoor to shake hands, \xe2\x80\x9cthan I could have hoped.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, naturally,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Jasper. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had but little reason to hope\r\nthat I should become more like yourself. You are always training\r\nyourself to be, mind and body, as clear as crystal, and you always are,\r\nand never change; whereas I am a muddy, solitary, moping weed. However,\r\nI have got over that mope. Shall I wait, while you ask if Mr. Neville\r\nhas left for my place? If not, he and I may walk round together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Crisparkle, opening the entrance-door with his key,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat he left some time ago; at least I know he left, and I think he\r\nhas not come back. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll inquire. You won\xe2\x80\x99t come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy company wait,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, with a smile.\r\n\r\nThe Minor Canon disappears, and in a few moments returns. As he\r\nthought, Mr. Neville has not come back; indeed, as he remembers now,\r\nMr. Neville said he would probably go straight to the gatehouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBad manners in a host!\xe2\x80\x9d says Jasper. \xe2\x80\x9cMy company will be there before\r\nme! What will you bet that I don\xe2\x80\x99t find my company embracing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will bet\xe2\x80\x94or I would, if ever I did bet,\xe2\x80\x9d returns Mr. Crisparkle,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat your company will have a gay entertainer this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper nods, and laughs good-night!\r\n\r\nHe retraces his steps to the Cathedral door, and turns down past it to\r\nthe gatehouse. He sings, in a low voice and with delicate expression,\r\nas he walks along. It still seems as if a false note were not within\r\nhis power to-night, and as if nothing could hurry or retard him.\r\nArriving thus under the arched entrance of his dwelling, he pauses for\r\nan instant in the shelter to pull off that great black scarf, and bang\r\nit in a loop upon his arm. For that brief time, his face is knitted and\r\nstern. But it immediately clears, as he resumes his singing, and his\r\nway.\r\n\r\nAnd so _he_ goes up the postern stair.\r\n\r\nThe red light burns steadily all the evening in the lighthouse on the\r\nmargin of the tide of busy life. Softened sounds and hum of traffic\r\npass it and flow on irregularly into the lonely Precincts; but very\r\nlittle else goes by, save violent rushes of wind. It comes on to blow a\r\nboisterous gale.\r\n\r\nThe Precincts are never particularly well lighted; but the strong\r\nblasts of wind blowing out many of the lamps (in some instances\r\nshattering the frames too, and bringing the glass rattling to the\r\nground), they are unusually dark to-night. The darkness is augmented\r\nand confused, by flying dust from the earth, dry twigs from the trees,\r\nand great ragged fragments from the rooks\xe2\x80\x99 nests up in the tower. The\r\ntrees themselves so toss and creak, as this tangible part of the\r\ndarkness madly whirls about, that they seem in peril of being torn out\r\nof the earth: while ever and again a crack, and a rushing fall, denote\r\nthat some large branch has yielded to the storm.\r\n\r\nNot such power of wind has blown for many a winter night. Chimneys\r\ntopple in the streets, and people hold to posts and corners, and to one\r\nanother, to keep themselves upon their feet. The violent rushes abate\r\nnot, but increase in frequency and fury until at midnight, when the\r\nstreets are empty, the storm goes thundering along them, rattling at\r\nall the latches, and tearing at all the shutters, as if warning the\r\npeople to get up and fly with it, rather than have the roofs brought\r\ndown upon their brains.\r\n\r\nStill, the red light burns steadily. Nothing is steady but the red\r\nlight.\r\n\r\nAll through the night the wind blows, and abates not. But early in the\r\nmorning, when there is barely enough light in the east to dim the\r\nstars, it begins to lull. From that time, with occasional wild charges,\r\nlike a wounded monster dying, it drops and sinks; and at full daylight\r\nit is dead.\r\n\r\nIt is then seen that the hands of the Cathedral clock are torn off;\r\nthat lead from the roof has been stripped away, rolled up, and blown\r\ninto the Close; and that some stones have been displaced upon the\r\nsummit of the great tower. Christmas morning though it be, it is\r\nnecessary to send up workmen, to ascertain the extent of the damage\r\ndone. These, led by Durdles, go aloft; while Mr. Tope and a crowd of\r\nearly idlers gather down in Minor Canon Corner, shading their eyes and\r\nwatching for their appearance up there.\r\n\r\nThis cluster is suddenly broken and put aside by the hands of Mr.\r\nJasper; all the gazing eyes are brought down to the earth by his loudly\r\ninquiring of Mr. Crisparkle, at an open window:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my nephew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has not been here. Is he not with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He went down to the river last night, with Mr. Neville, to look at\r\nthe storm, and has not been back. Call Mr. Neville!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe left this morning, early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeft this morning early? Let me in! let me in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere is no more looking up at the tower, now. All the assembled eyes\r\nare turned on Mr. Jasper, white, half-dressed, panting, and clinging to\r\nthe rail before the Minor Canon\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\nIMPEACHED\r\n\r\n\r\nNeville Landless had started so early and walked at so good a pace,\r\nthat when the church-bells began to ring in Cloisterham for morning\r\nservice, he was eight miles away. As he wanted his breakfast by that\r\ntime, having set forth on a crust of bread, he stopped at the next\r\nroadside tavern to refresh.\r\n\r\nVisitors in want of breakfast\xe2\x80\x94unless they were horses or cattle, for\r\nwhich class of guests there was preparation enough in the way of\r\nwater-trough and hay\xe2\x80\x94were so unusual at the sign of The Tilted Wagon,\r\nthat it took a long time to get the wagon into the track of tea and\r\ntoast and bacon. Neville in the interval, sitting in a sanded parlour,\r\nwondering in how long a time after he had gone, the sneezy fire of damp\r\nfagots would begin to make somebody else warm.\r\n\r\nIndeed, The Tilted Wagon, as a cool establishment on the top of a hill,\r\nwhere the ground before the door was puddled with damp hoofs and\r\ntrodden straw; where a scolding landlady slapped a moist baby (with one\r\nred sock on and one wanting), in the bar; where the cheese was cast\r\naground upon a shelf, in company with a mouldy tablecloth and a\r\ngreen-handled knife, in a sort of cast-iron canoe; where the pale-faced\r\nbread shed tears of crumb over its shipwreck in another canoe; where\r\nthe family linen, half washed and half dried, led a public life of\r\nlying about; where everything to drink was drunk out of mugs, and\r\neverything else was suggestive of a rhyme to mugs; The Tilted Wagon,\r\nall these things considered, hardly kept its painted promise of\r\nproviding good entertainment for Man and Beast. However, Man, in the\r\npresent case, was not critical, but took what entertainment he could\r\nget, and went on again after a longer rest than he needed.\r\n\r\nHe stopped at some quarter of a mile from the house, hesitating whether\r\nto pursue the road, or to follow a cart track between two high\r\nhedgerows, which led across the slope of a breezy heath, and evidently\r\nstruck into the road again by-and-by. He decided in favour of this\r\nlatter track, and pursued it with some toil; the rise being steep, and\r\nthe way worn into deep ruts.\r\n\r\nHe was labouring along, when he became aware of some other pedestrians\r\nbehind him. As they were coming up at a faster pace than his, he stood\r\naside, against one of the high banks, to let them pass. But their\r\nmanner was very curious. Only four of them passed. Other four slackened\r\nspeed, and loitered as intending to follow him when he should go on.\r\nThe remainder of the party (half-a-dozen perhaps) turned, and went back\r\nat a great rate.\r\n\r\nHe looked at the four behind him, and he looked at the four before him.\r\nThey all returned his look. He resumed his way. The four in advance\r\nwent on, constantly looking back; the four in the rear came closing up.\r\n\r\nWhen they all ranged out from the narrow track upon the open slope of\r\nthe heath, and this order was maintained, let him diverge as he would\r\nto either side, there was no longer room to doubt that he was beset by\r\nthese fellows. He stopped, as a last test; and they all stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you attend upon me in this way?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the whole body. \xe2\x80\x9cAre\r\nyou a pack of thieves?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t answer him,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the number; he did not see which.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter be quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter be quiet?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Neville. \xe2\x80\x9cWho said so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNobody replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s good advice, whichever of you skulkers gave it,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on\r\nangrily. \xe2\x80\x9cI will not submit to be penned in between four men there, and\r\nfour men there. I wish to pass, and I mean to pass, those four in\r\nfront.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were all standing still; himself included.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf eight men, or four men, or two men, set upon one,\xe2\x80\x9d he proceeded,\r\ngrowing more enraged, \xe2\x80\x9cthe one has no chance but to set his mark upon\r\nsome of them. And, by the Lord, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it, if I am interrupted any\r\nfarther!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShouldering his heavy stick, and quickening his pace, he shot on to\r\npass the four ahead. The largest and strongest man of the number\r\nchanged swiftly to the side on which he came up, and dexterously closed\r\nwith him and went down with him; but not before the heavy stick had\r\ndescended smartly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him be!\xe2\x80\x9d said this man in a suppressed voice, as they struggled\r\ntogether on the grass. \xe2\x80\x9cFair play! His is the build of a girl to mine,\r\nand he\xe2\x80\x99s got a weight strapped to his back besides. Let him alone. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nmanage him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a little rolling about, in a close scuffle which caused the faces\r\nof both to be besmeared with blood, the man took his knee from\r\nNeville\xe2\x80\x99s chest, and rose, saying: \xe2\x80\x9cThere! Now take him arm-in-arm, any\r\ntwo of you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was immediately done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs to our being a pack of thieves, Mr. Landless,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man, as he\r\nspat out some blood, and wiped more from his face; \xe2\x80\x9cyou know better\r\nthan that at midday. We wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have touched you if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t forced\r\nus. We\xe2\x80\x99re going to take you round to the high road, anyhow, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nfind help enough against thieves there, if you want it.\xe2\x80\x94Wipe his face,\r\nsomebody; see how it\xe2\x80\x99s a-trickling down him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen his face was cleansed, Neville recognised in the speaker, Joe,\r\ndriver of the Cloisterham omnibus, whom he had seen but once, and that\r\non the day of his arrival.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what I recommend you for the present, is, don\xe2\x80\x99t talk, Mr.\r\nLandless. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find a friend waiting for you, at the high road\xe2\x80\x94gone\r\nahead by the other way when we split into two parties\xe2\x80\x94and you had much\r\nbetter say nothing till you come up with him. Bring that stick along,\r\nsomebody else, and let\xe2\x80\x99s be moving!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUtterly bewildered, Neville stared around him and said not a word.\r\nWalking between his two conductors, who held his arms in theirs, he\r\nwent on, as in a dream, until they came again into the high road, and\r\ninto the midst of a little group of people. The men who had turned back\r\nwere among the group; and its central figures were Mr. Jasper and Mr.\r\nCrisparkle. Neville\xe2\x80\x99s conductors took him up to the Minor Canon, and\r\nthere released him, as an act of deference to that gentleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is all this, sir? What is the matter? I feel as if I had lost my\r\nsenses!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Neville, the group closing in around him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my nephew?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Jasper, wildly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is your nephew?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you ask me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ask you,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause you were the last person in his\r\ncompany, and he is not to be found.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to be found!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Neville, aghast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, stay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cPermit me, Jasper. Mr. Neville, you\r\nare confounded; collect your thoughts; it is of great importance that\r\nyou should collect your thoughts; attend to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try, sir, but I seem mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou left Mr. Jasper last night with Edwin Drood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what hour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it at twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Neville, with his hand to his\r\nconfused head, and appealing to Jasper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle; \xe2\x80\x9cthe hour Mr. Jasper has already\r\nnamed to me. You went down to the river together?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. To see the action of the wind there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat followed? How long did you stay there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout ten minutes; I should say not more. We then walked together to\r\nyour house, and he took leave of me at the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he say that he was going down to the river again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He said that he was going straight back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bystanders looked at one another, and at Mr. Crisparkle. To whom\r\nMr. Jasper, who had been intensely watching Neville, said, in a low,\r\ndistinct, suspicious voice: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are those stains upon his dress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll eyes were turned towards the blood upon his clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here are the same stains upon this stick!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, taking it\r\nfrom the hand of the man who held it. \xe2\x80\x9cI know the stick to be his, and\r\nhe carried it last night. What does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of God, say what it means, Neville!\xe2\x80\x9d urged Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat man and I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville, pointing out his late adversary, \xe2\x80\x9chad a\r\nstruggle for the stick just now, and you may see the same marks on him,\r\nsir. What was I to suppose, when I found myself molested by eight\r\npeople? Could I dream of the true reason when they would give me none\r\nat all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey admitted that they had thought it discreet to be silent, and that\r\nthe struggle had taken place. And yet the very men who had seen it\r\nlooked darkly at the smears which the bright cold air had already\r\ndried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must return, Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle; \xe2\x80\x9cof course you will be\r\nglad to come back to clear yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Landless will walk at my side,\xe2\x80\x9d the Minor Canon continued, looking\r\naround him. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Neville!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey set forth on the walk back; and the others, with one exception,\r\nstraggled after them at various distances. Jasper walked on the other\r\nside of Neville, and never quitted that position. He was silent, while\r\nMr. Crisparkle more than once repeated his former questions, and while\r\nNeville repeated his former answers; also, while they both hazarded\r\nsome explanatory conjectures. He was obstinately silent, because Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s manner directly appealed to him to take some part in the\r\ndiscussion, and no appeal would move his fixed face. When they drew\r\nnear to the city, and it was suggested by the Minor Canon that they\r\nmight do well in calling on the Mayor at once, he assented with a stern\r\nnod; but he spake no word until they stood in Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s parlour.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea being informed by Mr. Crisparkle of the circumstances under\r\nwhich they desired to make a voluntary statement before him, Mr. Jasper\r\nbroke silence by declaring that he placed his whole reliance, humanly\r\nspeaking, on Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s penetration. There was no conceivable reason\r\nwhy his nephew should have suddenly absconded, unless Mr. Sapsea could\r\nsuggest one, and then he would defer. There was no intelligible\r\nlikelihood of his having returned to the river, and been accidentally\r\ndrowned in the dark, unless it should appear likely to Mr. Sapsea, and\r\nthen again he would defer. He washed his hands as clean as he could of\r\nall horrible suspicions, unless it should appear to Mr. Sapsea that\r\nsome such were inseparable from his last companion before his\r\ndisappearance (not on good terms with previously), and then, once more,\r\nhe would defer. His own state of mind, he being distracted with doubts,\r\nand labouring under dismal apprehensions, was not to be safely trusted;\r\nbut Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s was.\r\n\r\nMr. Sapsea expressed his opinion that the case had a dark look; in\r\nshort (and here his eyes rested full on Neville\xe2\x80\x99s countenance), an\r\nUn-English complexion. Having made this grand point, he wandered into a\r\ndenser haze and maze of nonsense than even a mayor might have been\r\nexpected to disport himself in, and came out of it with the brilliant\r\ndiscovery that to take the life of a fellow-creature was to take\r\nsomething that didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to you. He wavered whether or no he should\r\nat once issue his warrant for the committal of Neville Landless to\r\njail, under circumstances of grave suspicion; and he might have gone so\r\nfar as to do it but for the indignant protest of the Minor Canon: who\r\nundertook for the young man\xe2\x80\x99s remaining in his own house, and being\r\nproduced by his own hands, whenever demanded. Mr. Jasper then\r\nunderstood Mr. Sapsea to suggest that the river should be dragged, that\r\nits banks should be rigidly examined, that particulars of the\r\ndisappearance should be sent to all outlying places and to London, and\r\nthat placards and advertisements should be widely circulated imploring\r\nEdwin Drood, if for any unknown reason he had withdrawn himself from\r\nhis uncle\xe2\x80\x99s home and society, to take pity on that loving kinsman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsore bereavement and distress, and somehow inform him that he was yet\r\nalive. Mr. Sapsea was perfectly understood, for this was exactly his\r\nmeaning (though he had said nothing about it); and measures were taken\r\ntowards all these ends immediately.\r\n\r\nIt would be difficult to determine which was the more oppressed with\r\nhorror and amazement: Neville Landless, or John Jasper. But that\r\nJasper\xe2\x80\x99s position forced him to be active, while Neville\xe2\x80\x99s forced him\r\nto be passive, there would have been nothing to choose between them.\r\nEach was bowed down and broken.\r\n\r\nWith the earliest light of the next morning, men were at work upon the\r\nriver, and other men\xe2\x80\x94most of whom volunteered for the service\xe2\x80\x94were\r\nexamining the banks. All the livelong day the search went on; upon the\r\nriver, with barge and pole, and drag and net; upon the muddy and rushy\r\nshore, with jack-boots, hatchet, spade, rope, dogs, and all imaginable\r\nappliances. Even at night, the river was specked with lanterns, and\r\nlurid with fires; far-off creeks, into which the tide washed as it\r\nchanged, had their knots of watchers, listening to the lapping of the\r\nstream, and looking out for any burden it might bear; remote shingly\r\ncauseways near the sea, and lonely points off which there was a race of\r\nwater, had their unwonted flaring cressets and rough-coated figures\r\nwhen the next day dawned; but no trace of Edwin Drood revisited the\r\nlight of the sun.\r\n\r\nAll that day, again, the search went on. Now, in barge and boat; and\r\nnow ashore among the osiers, or tramping amidst mud and stakes and\r\njagged stones in low-lying places, where solitary watermarks and\r\nsignals of strange shapes showed like spectres, John Jasper worked and\r\ntoiled. But to no purpose; for still no trace of Edwin Drood revisited\r\nthe light of the sun.\r\n\r\nSetting his watches for that night again, so that vigilant eyes should\r\nbe kept on every change of tide, he went home exhausted. Unkempt and\r\ndisordered, bedaubed with mud that had dried upon him, and with much of\r\nhis clothing torn to rags, he had but just dropped into his easy-chair,\r\nwhen Mr. Grewgious stood before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is strange news,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange and fearful news.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper had merely lifted up his heavy eyes to say it, and now dropped\r\nthem again as he drooped, worn out, over one side of his easy-chair.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious smoothed his head and face, and stood looking at the\r\nfire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is your ward?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jasper, after a time, in a faint, fatigued\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor little thing! You may imagine her condition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen his sister?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Jasper, as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe curtness of the counter-question, and the cool, slow manner in\r\nwhich, as he put it, Mr. Grewgious moved his eyes from the fire to his\r\ncompanion\xe2\x80\x99s face, might at any other time have been exasperating. In\r\nhis depression and exhaustion, Jasper merely opened his eyes to say:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe suspected young man\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suspect him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to think. I cannot make up my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cBut as you spoke of him as the suspected\r\nyoung man, I thought you _had_ made up your mind.\xe2\x80\x94I have just left Miss\r\nLandless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Mr. Grewgious has his suspicions]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is her state?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDefiance of all suspicion, and unbounded faith in her brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not of her that I came to\r\nspeak. It is of my ward. I have a communication to make that will\r\nsurprise you. At least, it has surprised me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJasper, with a groaning sigh, turned wearily in his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I put it off till to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cMind, I warn\r\nyou, that I think it will surprise you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore attention and concentration came into John Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s eyes as they\r\ncaught sight of Mr. Grewgious smoothing his head again, and again\r\nlooking at the fire; but now, with a compressed and determined mouth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Jasper, becoming upright in his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, provokingly slowly and internally, as\r\nhe kept his eyes on the fire: \xe2\x80\x9cI might have known it sooner; she gave\r\nme the opening; but I am such an exceedingly Angular man, that it never\r\noccurred to me; I took all for granted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Jasper once more.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious, alternately opening and shutting the palms of his hands\r\nas he warmed them at the fire, and looking fixedly at him sideways, and\r\nnever changing either his action or his look in all that followed, went\r\non to reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis young couple, the lost youth and Miss Rosa, my ward, though so\r\nlong betrothed, and so long recognising their betrothal, and so near\r\nbeing married\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious saw a staring white face, and two quivering white lips,\r\nin the easy-chair, and saw two muddy hands gripping its sides. But for\r\nthe hands, he might have thought he had never seen the face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94This young couple came gradually to the discovery (made on both sides\r\npretty equally, I think), that they would be happier and better, both\r\nin their present and their future lives, as affectionate friends, or\r\nsay rather as brother and sister, than as husband and wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious saw a lead-coloured face in the easy-chair, and on its\r\nsurface dreadful starting drops or bubbles, as if of steel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis young couple formed at length the healthy resolution of\r\ninterchanging their discoveries, openly, sensibly, and tenderly. They\r\nmet for that purpose. After some innocent and generous talk, they\r\nagreed to dissolve their existing, and their intended, relations, for\r\never and ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious saw a ghastly figure rise, open-mouthed, from the\r\neasy-chair, and lift its outspread hands towards its head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of this young couple, and that one your nephew, fearful, however,\r\nthat in the tenderness of your affection for him you would be bitterly\r\ndisappointed by so wide a departure from his projected life, forbore to\r\ntell you the secret, for a few days, and left it to be disclosed by me,\r\nwhen I should come down to speak to you, and he would be gone. I speak\r\nto you, and he is gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious saw the ghastly figure throw back its head, clutch its\r\nhair with its hands, and turn with a writhing action from him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have now said all I have to say: except that this young couple\r\nparted, firmly, though not without tears and sorrow, on the evening\r\nwhen you last saw them together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious heard a terrible shriek, and saw no ghastly figure,\r\nsitting or standing; saw nothing but a heap of torn and miry clothes\r\nupon the floor.\r\n\r\nNot changing his action even then, he opened and shut the palms of his\r\nhands as he warmed them, and looked down at it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\nDEVOTED\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen John Jasper recovered from his fit or swoon, he found himself\r\nbeing tended by Mr. and Mrs. Tope, whom his visitor had summoned for\r\nthe purpose. His visitor, wooden of aspect, sat stiffly in a chair,\r\nwith his hands upon his knees, watching his recovery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! You\xe2\x80\x99ve come to nicely now, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the tearful Mrs. Tope;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou were thoroughly worn out, and no wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with his usual air of repeating a lesson,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccannot have his rest broken, and his mind cruelly tormented, and his\r\nbody overtaxed by fatigue, without being thoroughly worn out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear I have alarmed you?\xe2\x80\x9d Jasper apologised faintly, when he was\r\nhelped into his easy-chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, I thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too considerate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, I thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Grewgious again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must take some wine, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tope, \xe2\x80\x9cand the jelly that I\r\nhad ready for you, and that you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t put your lips to at noon,\r\nthough I warned you what would come of it, you know, and you not\r\nbreakfasted; and you must have a wing of the roast fowl that has been\r\nput back twenty times if it\xe2\x80\x99s been put back once. It shall all be on\r\ntable in five minutes, and this good gentleman belike will stop and see\r\nyou take it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis good gentleman replied with a snort, which might mean yes, or no,\r\nor anything or nothing, and which Mrs. Tope would have found highly\r\nmystifying, but that her attention was divided by the service of the\r\ntable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will take something with me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, as the cloth was laid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get a morsel down my throat, I thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr.\r\nGrewgious.\r\n\r\nJasper both ate and drank almost voraciously. Combined with the hurry\r\nin his mode of doing it, was an evident indifference to the taste of\r\nwhat he took, suggesting that he ate and drank to fortify himself\r\nagainst any other failure of the spirits, far more than to gratify his\r\npalate.
