Mr. Grewgious in the meantime sat upright, with no expression\r\nin his face, and a hard kind of imperturbably polite protest all over\r\nhim: as though he would have said, in reply to some invitation to\r\ndiscourse; \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t originate the faintest approach to an\r\nobservation on any subject whatever, I thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper, when he had pushed away his plate and\r\nglass, and had sat meditating for a few minutes: \xe2\x80\x9cdo you know that I\r\nfind some crumbs of comfort in the communication with which you have so\r\nmuch amazed me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ you?\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Grewgious, pretty plainly adding the unspoken\r\nclause: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t, I thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter recovering from the shock of a piece of news of my dear boy, so\r\nentirely unexpected, and so destructive of all the castles I had built\r\nfor him; and after having had time to think of it; yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be glad to pick up your crumbs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, dryly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there not, or is there\xe2\x80\x94if I deceive myself, tell me so, and shorten\r\nmy pain\xe2\x80\x94is there not, or is there, hope that, finding himself in this\r\nnew position, and becoming sensitively alive to the awkward burden of\r\nexplanation, in this quarter, and that, and the other, with which it\r\nwould load him, he avoided the awkwardness, and took to flight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch a thing might be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, pondering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch a thing has been. I have read of cases in which people, rather\r\nthan face a seven days\xe2\x80\x99 wonder, and have to account for themselves to\r\nthe idle and impertinent, have taken themselves away, and been long\r\nunheard of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe such things have happened,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, pondering\r\nstill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I had, and could have, no suspicion,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Jasper, eagerly\r\nfollowing the new track, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the dear lost boy had withheld anything\r\nfrom me\xe2\x80\x94most of all, such a leading matter as this\xe2\x80\x94what gleam of light\r\nwas there for me in the whole black sky? When I supposed that his\r\nintended wife was here, and his marriage close at hand, how could I\r\nentertain the possibility of his voluntarily leaving this place, in a\r\nmanner that would be so unaccountable, capricious, and cruel? But now\r\nthat I know what you have told me, is there no little chink through\r\nwhich day pierces? Supposing him to have disappeared of his own act, is\r\nnot his disappearance more accountable and less cruel? The fact of his\r\nhaving just parted from your ward, is in itself a sort of reason for\r\nhis going away. It does not make his mysterious departure the less\r\ncruel to me, it is true; but it relieves it of cruelty to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious could not but assent to this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd even as to me,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Jasper, still pursuing the new track,\r\nwith ardour, and, as he did so, brightening with hope: \xe2\x80\x9che knew that\r\nyou were coming to me; he knew that you were intrusted to tell me what\r\nyou have told me; if your doing so has awakened a new train of thought\r\nin my perplexed mind, it reasonably follows that, from the same\r\npremises, he might have foreseen the inferences that I should draw.\r\nGrant that he did foresee them; and even the cruelty to me\xe2\x80\x94and who am\r\nI!\xe2\x80\x94John Jasper, Music Master, vanishes!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nOnce more, Mr. Grewgious could not but assent to this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have had my distrusts, and terrible distrusts they have been,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJasper; \xe2\x80\x9cbut your disclosure, overpowering as it was at first\xe2\x80\x94showing\r\nme that my own dear boy had had a great disappointing reservation from\r\nme, who so fondly loved him, kindles hope within me. You do not\r\nextinguish it when I state it, but admit it to be a reasonable hope. I\r\nbegin to believe it possible:\xe2\x80\x9d here he clasped his hands: \xe2\x80\x9cthat he may\r\nhave disappeared from among us of his own accord, and that he may yet\r\nbe alive and well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle came in at the moment. To whom Mr. Jasper repeated:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI begin to believe it possible that he may have disappeared of his own\r\naccord, and may yet be alive and well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle taking a seat, and inquiring: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy so?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jasper\r\nrepeated the arguments he had just set forth. If they had been less\r\nplausible than they were, the good Minor Canon\xe2\x80\x99s mind would have been\r\nin a state of preparation to receive them, as exculpatory of his\r\nunfortunate pupil. But he, too, did really attach great importance to\r\nthe lost young man\xe2\x80\x99s having been, so immediately before his\r\ndisappearance, placed in a new and embarrassing relation towards every\r\none acquainted with his projects and affairs; and the fact seemed to\r\nhim to present the question in a new light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI stated to Mr. Sapsea, when we waited on him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jasper: as he\r\nreally had done: \xe2\x80\x9cthat there was no quarrel or difference between the\r\ntwo young men at their last meeting. We all know that their first\r\nmeeting was unfortunately very far from amicable; but all went smoothly\r\nand quietly when they were last together at my house. My dear boy was\r\nnot in his usual spirits; he was depressed\xe2\x80\x94I noticed that\xe2\x80\x94and I am\r\nbound henceforth to dwell upon the circumstance the more, now that I\r\nknow there was a special reason for his being depressed: a reason,\r\nmoreover, which may possibly have induced him to absent himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray to Heaven it may turn out so!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ pray to Heaven it may turn out so!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Jasper. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nMr. Grewgious should now know likewise\xe2\x80\x94that I took a great\r\nprepossession against Mr. Neville Landless, arising out of his furious\r\nconduct on that first occasion. You know that I came to you, extremely\r\napprehensive, on my dear boy\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, of his mad violence. You know\r\nthat I even entered in my Diary, and showed the entry to you, that I\r\nhad dark forebodings against him. Mr. Grewgious ought to be possessed\r\nof the whole case. He shall not, through any suppression of mine, be\r\ninformed of a part of it, and kept in ignorance of another part of it.\r\nI wish him to be good enough to understand that the communication he\r\nhas made to me has hopefully influenced my mind, in spite of its having\r\nbeen, before this mysterious occurrence took place, profoundly\r\nimpressed against young Landless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis fairness troubled the Minor Canon much. He felt that he was not as\r\nopen in his own dealing. He charged against himself reproachfully that\r\nhe had suppressed, so far, the two points of a second strong outbreak\r\nof temper against Edwin Drood on the part of Neville, and of the\r\npassion of jealousy having, to his own certain knowledge, flamed up in\r\nNeville\xe2\x80\x99s breast against him. He was convinced of Neville\xe2\x80\x99s innocence\r\nof any part in the ugly disappearance; and yet so many little\r\ncircumstances combined so wofully against him, that he dreaded to add\r\ntwo more to their cumulative weight. He was among the truest of men;\r\nbut he had been balancing in his mind, much to its distress, whether\r\nhis volunteering to tell these two fragments of truth, at this time,\r\nwould not be tantamount to a piecing together of falsehood in the place\r\nof truth.\r\n\r\nHowever, here was a model before him. He hesitated no longer.\r\nAddressing Mr. Grewgious, as one placed in authority by the revelation\r\nhe had brought to bear on the mystery (and surpassingly Angular Mr.\r\nGrewgious became when he found himself in that unexpected position),\r\nMr. Crisparkle bore his testimony to Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s strict sense of\r\njustice, and, expressing his absolute confidence in the complete\r\nclearance of his pupil from the least taint of suspicion, sooner or\r\nlater, avowed that his confidence in that young gentleman had been\r\nformed, in spite of his confidential knowledge that his temper was of\r\nthe hottest and fiercest, and that it was directly incensed against Mr.\r\nJasper\xe2\x80\x99s nephew, by the circumstance of his romantically supposing\r\nhimself to be enamoured of the same young lady. The sanguine reaction\r\nmanifest in Mr. Jasper was proof even against this unlooked-for\r\ndeclaration. It turned him paler; but he repeated that he would cling\r\nto the hope he had derived from Mr. Grewgious; and that if no trace of\r\nhis dear boy were found, leading to the dreadful inference that he had\r\nbeen made away with, he would cherish unto the last stretch of\r\npossibility the idea, that he might have absconded of his own wild\r\nwill.\r\n\r\nNow, it fell out that Mr. Crisparkle, going away from this conference\r\nstill very uneasy in his mind, and very much troubled on behalf of the\r\nyoung man whom he held as a kind of prisoner in his own house, took a\r\nmemorable night walk.\r\n\r\nHe walked to Cloisterham Weir.\r\n\r\nHe often did so, and consequently there was nothing remarkable in his\r\nfootsteps tending that way. But the preoccupation of his mind so\r\nhindered him from planning any walk, or taking heed of the objects he\r\npassed, that his first consciousness of being near the Weir, was\r\nderived from the sound of the falling water close at hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did I come here!\xe2\x80\x9d was his first thought, as he stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did I come here!\xe2\x80\x9d was his second.\r\n\r\nThen, he stood intently listening to the water. A familiar passage in\r\nhis reading, about airy tongues that syllable men\xe2\x80\x99s names, rose so\r\nunbidden to his ear, that he put it from him with his hand, as if it\r\nwere tangible.\r\n\r\nIt was starlight. The Weir was full two miles above the spot to which\r\nthe young men had repaired to watch the storm. No search had been made\r\nup here, for the tide had been running strongly down, at that time of\r\nthe night of Christmas Eve, and the likeliest places for the discovery\r\nof a body, if a fatal accident had happened under such circumstances,\r\nall lay\xe2\x80\x94both when the tide ebbed, and when it flowed again\xe2\x80\x94between that\r\nspot and the sea. The water came over the Weir, with its usual sound on\r\na cold starlight night, and little could be seen of it; yet Mr.\r\nCrisparkle had a strange idea that something unusual hung about the\r\nplace.\r\n\r\nHe reasoned with himself: What was it? Where was it? Put it to the\r\nproof. Which sense did it address?\r\n\r\nNo sense reported anything unusual there. He listened again, and his\r\nsense of hearing again checked the water coming over the Weir, with its\r\nusual sound on a cold starlight night.\r\n\r\nKnowing very well that the mystery with which his mind was occupied,\r\nmight of itself give the place this haunted air, he strained those\r\nhawk\xe2\x80\x99s eyes of his for the correction of his sight. He got closer to\r\nthe Weir, and peered at its well-known posts and timbers. Nothing in\r\nthe least unusual was remotely shadowed forth. But he resolved that he\r\nwould come back early in the morning.\r\n\r\nThe Weir ran through his broken sleep, all night, and he was back again\r\nat sunrise. It was a bright frosty morning. The whole composition\r\nbefore him, when he stood where he had stood last night, was clearly\r\ndiscernible in its minutest details. He had surveyed it closely for\r\nsome minutes, and was about to withdraw his eyes, when they were\r\nattracted keenly to one spot.\r\n\r\nHe turned his back upon the Weir, and looked far away at the sky, and\r\nat the earth, and then looked again at that one spot. It caught his\r\nsight again immediately, and he concentrated his vision upon it. He\r\ncould not lose it now, though it was but such a speck in the landscape.\r\nIt fascinated his sight. His hands began plucking off his coat. For it\r\nstruck him that at that spot\xe2\x80\x94a corner of the Weir\xe2\x80\x94something glistened,\r\nwhich did not move and come over with the glistening water-drops, but\r\nremained stationary.\r\n\r\nHe assured himself of this, he threw off his clothes, he plunged into\r\nthe icy water, and swam for the spot. Climbing the timbers, he took\r\nfrom them, caught among their interstices by its chain, a gold watch,\r\nbearing engraved upon its back E. D.\r\n\r\nHe brought the watch to the bank, swam to the Weir again, climbed it,\r\nand dived off. He knew every hole and corner of all the depths, and\r\ndived and dived and dived, until he could bear the cold no more. His\r\nnotion was, that he would find the body; he only found a shirt-pin\r\nsticking in some mud and ooze.\r\n\r\nWith these discoveries he returned to Cloisterham, and, taking Neville\r\nLandless with him, went straight to the Mayor. Mr. Jasper was sent for,\r\nthe watch and shirt-pin were identified, Neville was detained, and the\r\nwildest frenzy and fatuity of evil report rose against him. He was of\r\nthat vindictive and violent nature, that but for his poor sister, who\r\nalone had influence over him, and out of whose sight he was never to be\r\ntrusted, he would be in the daily commission of murder. Before coming\r\nto England he had caused to be whipped to death sundry\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNatives\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94nomadic persons, encamping now in Asia, now in Africa, now in\r\nthe West Indies, and now at the North Pole\xe2\x80\x94vaguely supposed in\r\nCloisterham to be always black, always of great virtue, always calling\r\nthemselves Me, and everybody else Massa or Missie (according to sex),\r\nand always reading tracts of the obscurest meaning, in broken English,\r\nbut always accurately understanding them in the purest mother tongue.\r\nHe had nearly brought Mrs. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s grey hairs with sorrow to the\r\ngrave. (Those original expressions were Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s.) He had\r\nrepeatedly said he would have Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s life. He had repeatedly\r\nsaid he would have everybody\xe2\x80\x99s life, and become in effect the last man.\r\nHe had been brought down to Cloisterham, from London, by an eminent\r\nPhilanthropist, and why? Because that Philanthropist had expressly\r\ndeclared: \xe2\x80\x9cI owe it to my fellow-creatures that he should be, in the\r\nwords of BENTHAM, where he is the cause of the greatest danger to the\r\nsmallest number.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese dropping shots from the blunderbusses of blunderheadedness might\r\nnot have hit him in a vital place. But he had to stand against a\r\ntrained and well-directed fire of arms of precision too. He had\r\nnotoriously threatened the lost young man, and had, according to the\r\nshowing of his own faithful friend and tutor who strove so hard for\r\nhim, a cause of bitter animosity (created by himself, and stated by\r\nhimself), against that ill-starred fellow. He had armed himself with an\r\noffensive weapon for the fatal night, and he had gone off early in the\r\nmorning, after making preparations for departure. He had been found\r\nwith traces of blood on him; truly, they might have been wholly caused\r\nas he represented, but they might not, also. On a search-warrant being\r\nissued for the examination of his room, clothes, and so forth, it was\r\ndiscovered that he had destroyed all his papers, and rearranged all his\r\npossessions, on the very afternoon of the disappearance. The watch\r\nfound at the Weir was challenged by the jeweller as one he had wound\r\nand set for Edwin Drood, at twenty minutes past two on that same\r\nafternoon; and it had run down, before being cast into the water; and\r\nit was the jeweller\xe2\x80\x99s positive opinion that it had never been re-wound.\r\nThis would justify the hypothesis that the watch was taken from him not\r\nlong after he left Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s house at midnight, in company with the\r\nlast person seen with him, and that it had been thrown away after being\r\nretained some hours. Why thrown away? If he had been murdered, and so\r\nartfully disfigured, or concealed, or both, as that the murderer hoped\r\nidentification to be impossible, except from something that he wore,\r\nassuredly the murderer would seek to remove from the body the most\r\nlasting, the best known, and the most easily recognisable, things upon\r\nit. Those things would be the watch and shirt-pin. As to his\r\nopportunities of casting them into the river; if he were the object of\r\nthese suspicions, they were easy. For, he had been seen by many\r\npersons, wandering about on that side of the city\xe2\x80\x94indeed on all sides\r\nof it\xe2\x80\x94in a miserable and seemingly half-distracted manner. As to the\r\nchoice of the spot, obviously such criminating evidence had better take\r\nits chance of being found anywhere, rather than upon himself, or in his\r\npossession. Concerning the reconciliatory nature of the appointed\r\nmeeting between the two young men, very little could be made of that in\r\nyoung Landless\xe2\x80\x99s favour; for it distinctly appeared that the meeting\r\noriginated, not with him, but with Mr. Crisparkle, and that it had been\r\nurged on by Mr. Crisparkle; and who could say how unwillingly, or in\r\nwhat ill-conditioned mood, his enforced pupil had gone to it? The more\r\nhis case was looked into, the weaker it became in every point. Even the\r\nbroad suggestion that the lost young man had absconded, was rendered\r\nadditionally improbable on the showing of the young lady from whom he\r\nhad so lately parted; for; what did she say, with great earnestness and\r\nsorrow, when interrogated? That he had, expressly and enthusiastically,\r\nplanned with her, that he would await the arrival of her guardian, Mr.\r\nGrewgious. And yet, be it observed, he disappeared before that\r\ngentleman appeared.\r\n\r\nOn the suspicions thus urged and supported, Neville was detained, and\r\nre-detained, and the search was pressed on every hand, and Jasper\r\nlaboured night and day. But nothing more was found. No discovery being\r\nmade, which proved the lost man to be dead, it at length became\r\nnecessary to release the person suspected of having made away with him.\r\nNeville was set at large. Then, a consequence ensued which Mr.\r\nCrisparkle had too well foreseen. Neville must leave the place, for the\r\nplace shunned him and cast him out. Even had it not been so, the dear\r\nold china shepherdess would have worried herself to death with fears\r\nfor her son, and with general trepidation occasioned by their having\r\nsuch an inmate. Even had that not been so, the authority to which the\r\nMinor Canon deferred officially, would have settled the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth the Dean, \xe2\x80\x9chuman justice may err, but it must\r\nact according to its lights. The days of taking sanctuary are past.\r\nThis young man must not take sanctuary with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that he must leave my house, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the prudent Dean, \xe2\x80\x9cI claim no authority in\r\nyour house. I merely confer with you, on the painful necessity you find\r\nyourself under, of depriving this young man of the great advantages of\r\nyour counsel and instruction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very lamentable, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle represented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much so,\xe2\x80\x9d the Dean assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if it be a necessity\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle faltered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you unfortunately find it to be,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Dean.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle bowed submissively: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is hard to prejudge his case,\r\nsir, but I am sensible that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so. Perfectly. As you say, Mr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed the Dean,\r\nnodding his head smoothly, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is nothing else to be done. No doubt,\r\nno doubt. There is no alternative, as your good sense has discovered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am entirely satisfied of his perfect innocence, sir, nevertheless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe-e-ell!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Dean, in a more confidential tone, and slightly\r\nglancing around him, \xe2\x80\x9cI would not say so, generally. Not generally.\r\nEnough of suspicion attaches to him to\xe2\x80\x94no, I think I would not say so,\r\ngenerally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle bowed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not become us, perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Dean, \xe2\x80\x9cto be partisans.\r\nNot partisans. We clergy keep our hearts warm and our heads cool, and\r\nwe hold a judicious middle course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you do not object, sir, to my having stated in public,\r\nemphatically, that he will reappear here, whenever any new suspicion\r\nmay be awakened, or any new circumstance may come to light in this\r\nextraordinary matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Dean. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, do you know, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwith a very nice and neat emphasis on those two words: \xe2\x80\x9cI _don\xe2\x80\x99t think_\r\nI would state it emphatically. State it? Ye-e-es! But emphatically?\r\nNo-o-o. I _think_ not. In point of fact, Mr. Crisparkle, keeping our\r\nhearts warm and our heads cool, we clergy need do nothing\r\nemphatically.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Minor Canon Row knew Neville Landless no more; and he went\r\nwhithersoever he would, or could, with a blight upon his name and fame.\r\n\r\nIt was not until then that John Jasper silently resumed his place in\r\nthe choir. Haggard and red-eyed, his hopes plainly had deserted him,\r\nhis sanguine mood was gone, and all his worst misgivings had come back.\r\nA day or two afterwards, while unrobing, he took his Diary from a\r\npocket of his coat, turned the leaves, and with an impressive look, and\r\nwithout one spoken word, handed this entry to Mr. Crisparkle to read:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear boy is murdered. The discovery of the watch and shirt-pin\r\nconvinces me that he was murdered that night, and that his jewellery\r\nwas taken from him to prevent identification by its means. All the\r\ndelusive hopes I had founded on his separation from his betrothed wife,\r\nI give to the winds. They perish before this fatal discovery. I now\r\nswear, and record the oath on this page, That I nevermore will discuss\r\nthis mystery with any human creature until I hold the clue to it in my\r\nhand. That I never will relax in my secrecy or in my search. That I\r\nwill fasten the crime of the murder of my dear dead boy upon the\r\nmurderer. And, That I devote myself to his destruction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\nPHILANTHROPY, PROFESSIONAL AND UNPROFESSIONAL\r\n\r\n\r\nFull half a year had come and gone, and Mr. Crisparkle sat in a\r\nwaiting-room in the London chief offices of the Haven of Philanthropy,\r\nuntil he could have audience of Mr. Honeythunder.\r\n\r\nIn his college days of athletic exercises, Mr. Crisparkle had known\r\nprofessors of the Noble Art of fisticuffs, and had attended two or\r\nthree of their gloved gatherings. He had now an opportunity of\r\nobserving that as to the phrenological formation of the backs of their\r\nheads, the Professing Philanthropists were uncommonly like the\r\nPugilists. In the development of all those organs which constitute, or\r\nattend, a propensity to \xe2\x80\x9cpitch into\xe2\x80\x9d your fellow-creatures, the\r\nPhilanthropists were remarkably favoured. There were several Professors\r\npassing in and out, with exactly the aggressive air upon them of being\r\nready for a turn-up with any Novice who might happen to be on hand,\r\nthat Mr. Crisparkle well remembered in the circles of the Fancy.\r\nPreparations were in progress for a moral little Mill somewhere on the\r\nrural circuit, and other Professors were backing this or that\r\nHeavy-Weight as good for such or such speech-making hits, so very much\r\nafter the manner of the sporting publicans, that the intended\r\nResolutions might have been Rounds. In an official manager of these\r\ndisplays much celebrated for his platform tactics, Mr. Crisparkle\r\nrecognised (in a suit of black) the counterpart of a deceased\r\nbenefactor of his species, an eminent public character, once known to\r\nfame as Frosty-faced Fogo, who in days of yore superintended the\r\nformation of the magic circle with the ropes and stakes. There were\r\nonly three conditions of resemblance wanting between these Professors\r\nand those. Firstly, the Philanthropists were in very bad training: much\r\ntoo fleshy, and presenting, both in face and figure, a superabundance\r\nof what is known to Pugilistic Experts as Suet Pudding. Secondly, the\r\nPhilanthropists had not the good temper of the Pugilists, and used\r\nworse language. Thirdly, their fighting code stood in great need of\r\nrevision, as empowering them not only to bore their man to the ropes,\r\nbut to bore him to the confines of distraction; also to hit him when he\r\nwas down, hit him anywhere and anyhow, kick him, stamp upon him, gouge\r\nhim, and maul him behind his back without mercy. In these last\r\nparticulars the Professors of the Noble Art were much nobler than the\r\nProfessors of Philanthropy.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle was so completely lost in musing on these similarities\r\nand dissimilarities, at the same time watching the crowd which came and\r\nwent by, always, as it seemed, on errands of antagonistically snatching\r\nsomething from somebody, and never giving anything to anybody, that his\r\nname was called before he heard it. On his at length responding, he was\r\nshown by a miserably shabby and underpaid stipendiary Philanthropist\r\n(who could hardly have done worse if he had taken service with a\r\ndeclared enemy of the human race) to Mr. Honeythunder\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Honeythunder, in his tremendous voice, like a\r\nschoolmaster issuing orders to a boy of whom he had a bad opinion, \xe2\x80\x9csit\r\ndown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle seated himself.\r\n\r\nMr. Honeythunder having signed the remaining few score of a few\r\nthousand circulars, calling upon a corresponding number of families\r\nwithout means to come forward, stump up instantly, and be\r\nPhilanthropists, or go to the Devil, another shabby stipendiary\r\nPhilanthropist (highly disinterested, if in earnest) gathered these\r\ninto a basket and walked off with them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Honeythunder, turning his chair half\r\nround towards him when they were alone, and squaring his arms with his\r\nhands on his knees, and his brows knitted, as if he added, I am going\r\nto make short work of _you_: \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Crisparkle, we entertain\r\ndifferent views, you and I, sir, of the sanctity of human life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo we?\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Minor Canon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight I ask you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon: \xe2\x80\x9cwhat are your views on that\r\nsubject?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat human life is a thing to be held sacred, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight I ask you,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Minor Canon as before: \xe2\x80\x9cwhat you suppose\r\nto be my views on that subject?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Philanthropist, squaring his arms still\r\nmore, as he frowned on Mr. Crisparkle: \xe2\x80\x9cthey are best known to\r\nyourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReadily admitted. But you began by saying that we took different\r\nviews, you know. Therefore (or you could not say so) you must have set\r\nup some views as mine. Pray, what views _have_ you set up as mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is a man\xe2\x80\x94and a young man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Honeythunder, as if that made\r\nthe matter infinitely worse, and he could have easily borne the loss of\r\nan old one, \xe2\x80\x9cswept off the face of the earth by a deed of violence.\r\nWhat do you call that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMurder,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call the doer of that deed, sir?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA murderer,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear you admit so much, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mr. Honeythunder,\r\nin his most offensive manner; \xe2\x80\x9cand I candidly tell you that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexpect it.\xe2\x80\x9d Here he lowered heavily at Mr. Crisparkle again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe so good as to explain what you mean by those very unjustifiable\r\nexpressions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t sit here, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Philanthropist, raising his voice\r\nto a roar, \xe2\x80\x9cto be browbeaten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs the only other person present, no one can possibly know that better\r\nthan I do,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Minor Canon very quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I interrupt\r\nyour explanation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMurder!\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded Mr. Honeythunder, in a kind of boisterous reverie,\r\nwith his platform folding of his arms, and his platform nod of\r\nabhorrent reflection after each short sentiment of a word. \xe2\x80\x9cBloodshed!\r\nAbel! Cain! I hold no terms with Cain. I repudiate with a shudder the\r\nred hand when it is offered me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInstead of instantly leaping into his chair and cheering himself\r\nhoarse, as the Brotherhood in public meeting assembled would infallibly\r\nhave done on this cue, Mr. Crisparkle merely reversed the quiet\r\ncrossing of his legs, and said mildly: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let me interrupt your\r\nexplanation\xe2\x80\x94when you begin it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Commandments say, no murder. NO murder, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded Mr.\r\nHoneythunder, platformally pausing as if he took Mr. Crisparkle to task\r\nfor having distinctly asserted that they said: You may do a little\r\nmurder, and then leave off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they also say, you shall bear no false witness,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr.\r\nCrisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough!\xe2\x80\x9d bellowed Mr. Honeythunder, with a solemnity and severity that\r\nwould have brought the house down at a meeting, \xe2\x80\x9cE-e-nough! My late\r\nwards being now of age, and I being released from a trust which I\r\ncannot contemplate without a thrill of horror, there are the accounts\r\nwhich you have undertaken to accept on their behalf, and there is a\r\nstatement of the balance which you have undertaken to receive, and\r\nwhich you cannot receive too soon. And let me tell you, sir, I wish\r\nthat, as a man and a Minor Canon, you were better employed,\xe2\x80\x9d with a\r\nnod. \xe2\x80\x9cBetter employed,\xe2\x80\x9d with another nod. \xe2\x80\x9cBet-ter em-ployed!\xe2\x80\x9d with\r\nanother and the three nods added up.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle rose; a little heated in the face, but with perfect\r\ncommand of himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Honeythunder,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, taking up the papers referred to: \xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\nbeing better or worse employed than I am at present is a matter of\r\ntaste and opinion. You might think me better employed in enrolling\r\nmyself a member of your Society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, indeed, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mr. Honeythunder, shaking his head in a\r\nthreatening manner. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been better for you if you had done\r\nthat long ago!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Honeythunder, shaking his head again, \xe2\x80\x9cI might think one\r\nof your profession better employed in devoting himself to the discovery\r\nand punishment of guilt than in leaving that duty to be undertaken by a\r\nlayman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may regard my profession from a point of view which teaches me that\r\nits first duty is towards those who are in necessity and tribulation,\r\nwho are desolate and oppressed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, as I\r\nhave quite clearly satisfied myself that it is no part of my profession\r\nto make professions, I say no more of that. But I owe it to Mr.\r\nNeville, and to Mr. Neville\xe2\x80\x99s sister (and in a much lower degree to\r\nmyself), to say to you that I _know_ I was in the full possession and\r\nunderstanding of Mr. Neville\xe2\x80\x99s mind and heart at the time of this\r\noccurrence; and that, without in the least colouring or concealing what\r\nwas to be deplored in him and required to be corrected, I feel certain\r\nthat his tale is true. Feeling that certainty, I befriend him. As long\r\nas that certainty shall last, I will befriend him. And if any\r\nconsideration could shake me in this resolve, I should be so ashamed of\r\nmyself for my meanness, that no man\xe2\x80\x99s good opinion\xe2\x80\x94no, nor no\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94so gained, could compensate me for the loss of my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGood fellow! manly fellow! And he was so modest, too. There was no more\r\nself-assertion in the Minor Canon than in the schoolboy who had stood\r\nin the breezy playing-fields keeping a wicket. He was simply and\r\nstaunchly true to his duty alike in the large case and in the small. So\r\nall true souls ever are. So every true soul ever was, ever is, and ever\r\nwill be. There is nothing little to the really great in spirit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen who do you make out did the deed?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Honeythunder,\r\nturning on him abruptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cthat in my desire to clear one\r\nman I should lightly criminate another! I accuse no one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTcha!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Mr. Honeythunder with great disgust; for this was by\r\nno means the principle on which the Philanthropic Brotherhood usually\r\nproceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, sir, you are not a disinterested witness, we must bear\r\nin mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow am I an interested one?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Mr. Crisparkle, smiling\r\ninnocently, at a loss to imagine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a certain stipend, sir, paid to you for your pupil, which\r\nmay have warped your judgment a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Honeythunder, coarsely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I expect to retain it still?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle returned,\r\nenlightened; \xe2\x80\x9cdo you mean that too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the professional Philanthropist, getting up and\r\nthrusting his hands down into his trousers-pockets, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t go about\r\nmeasuring people for caps. If people find I have any about me that fit\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em, they can put \xe2\x80\x99em on and wear \xe2\x80\x99em, if they like. That\xe2\x80\x99s their look\r\nout: not mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle eyed him with a just indignation, and took him to task\r\nthus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Honeythunder, I hoped when I came in here that I might be under no\r\nnecessity of commenting on the introduction of platform manners or\r\nplatform man\xc5\x93uvres among the decent forbearances of private life. But\r\nyou have given me such a specimen of both, that I should be a fit\r\nsubject for both if I remained silent respecting them. They are\r\ndetestable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t suit _you_, I dare say, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Crisparkle, without noticing the interruption,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdetestable. They violate equally the justice that should belong to\r\nChristians, and the restraints that should belong to gentlemen. You\r\nassume a great crime to have been committed by one whom I, acquainted\r\nwith the attendant circumstances, and having numerous reasons on my\r\nside, devoutly believe to be innocent of it. Because I differ from you\r\non that vital point, what is your platform resource? Instantly to turn\r\nupon me, charging that I have no sense of the enormity of the crime\r\nitself, but am its aider and abettor! So, another time\xe2\x80\x94taking me as\r\nrepresenting your opponent in other cases\xe2\x80\x94you set up a platform\r\ncredulity; a moved and seconded and carried-unanimously profession of\r\nfaith in some ridiculous delusion or mischievous imposition. I decline\r\nto believe it, and you fall back upon your platform resource of\r\nproclaiming that I believe nothing; that because I will not bow down to\r\na false God of your making, I deny the true God! Another time you make\r\nthe platform discovery that War is a calamity, and you propose to\r\nabolish it by a string of twisted resolutions tossed into the air like\r\nthe tail of a kite. I do not admit the discovery to be yours in the\r\nleast, and I have not a grain of faith in your remedy. Again, your\r\nplatform resource of representing me as revelling in the horrors of a\r\nbattle-field like a fiend incarnate! Another time, in another of your\r\nundiscriminating platform rushes, you would punish the sober for the\r\ndrunken. I claim consideration for the comfort, convenience, and\r\nrefreshment of the sober; and you presently make platform proclamation\r\nthat I have a depraved desire to turn Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s creatures into swine and\r\nwild beasts! In all such cases your movers, and your seconders, and\r\nyour supporters\xe2\x80\x94your regular Professors of all degrees, run amuck like\r\nso many mad Malays; habitually attributing the lowest and basest\r\nmotives with the utmost recklessness (let me call your attention to a\r\nrecent instance in yourself for which you should blush), and quoting\r\nfigures which you know to be as wilfully onesided as a statement of any\r\ncomplicated account that should be all Creditor side and no Debtor, or\r\nall Debtor side and no Creditor. Therefore it is, Mr. Honeythunder,\r\nthat I consider the platform a sufficiently bad example and a\r\nsufficiently bad school, even in public life; but hold that, carried\r\ninto private life, it becomes an unendurable nuisance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are strong words, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Philanthropist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle. \xe2\x80\x9cGood morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked out of the Haven at a great rate, but soon fell into his\r\nregular brisk pace, and soon had a smile upon his face as he went\r\nalong, wondering what the china shepherdess would have said if she had\r\nseen him pounding Mr. Honeythunder in the late little lively affair.\r\nFor Mr. Crisparkle had just enough of harmless vanity to hope that he\r\nhad hit hard, and to glow with the belief that he had trimmed the\r\nPhilanthropic Jacket pretty handsomely.\r\n\r\nHe took himself to Staple Inn, but not to P. J. T. and Mr. Grewgious.\r\nFull many a creaking stair he climbed before he reached some attic\r\nrooms in a corner, turned the latch of their unbolted door, and stood\r\nbeside the table of Neville Landless.\r\n\r\nAn air of retreat and solitude hung about the rooms and about their\r\ninhabitant. He was much worn, and so were they. Their sloping ceilings,\r\ncumbrous rusty locks and grates, and heavy wooden bins and beams,\r\nslowly mouldering withal, had a prisonous look, and he had the haggard\r\nface of a prisoner. Yet the sunlight shone in at the ugly\r\ngarret-window, which had a penthouse to itself thrust out among the\r\ntiles; and on the cracked and smoke-blackened parapet beyond, some of\r\nthe deluded sparrows of the place rheumatically hopped, like little\r\nfeathered cripples who had left their crutches in their nests; and\r\nthere was a play of living leaves at hand that changed the air, and\r\nmade an imperfect sort of music in it that would have been melody in\r\nthe country.\r\n\r\nThe rooms were sparely furnished, but with good store of books.\r\nEverything expressed the abode of a poor student. That Mr. Crisparkle\r\nhad been either chooser, lender, or donor of the books, or that he\r\ncombined the three characters, might have been easily seen in the\r\nfriendly beam of his eyes upon them as he entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow goes it, Neville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am in good heart, Mr. Crisparkle, and working away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish your eyes were not quite so large and not quite so bright,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Minor Canon, slowly releasing the hand he had taken in his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey brighten at the sight of you,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Neville. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you were to\r\nfall away from me, they would soon be dull enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRally, rally!\xe2\x80\x9d urged the other, in a stimulating tone. \xe2\x80\x9cFight for it,\r\nNeville!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were dying, I feel as if a word from you would rally me; if my\r\npulse had stopped, I feel as if your touch would make it beat again,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Neville. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I _have_ rallied, and am doing famously.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle turned him with his face a little more towards the\r\nlight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to see a ruddier touch here, Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, indicating his\r\nown healthy cheek by way of pattern. \xe2\x80\x9cI want more sun to shine upon\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville drooped suddenly, as he replied in a lowered voice: \xe2\x80\x9cI am not\r\nhardy enough for that, yet. I may become so, but I cannot bear it yet.\r\nIf you had gone through those Cloisterham streets as I did; if you had\r\nseen, as I did, those averted eyes, and the better sort of people\r\nsilently giving me too much room to pass, that I might not touch them\r\nor come near them, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think it quite unreasonable that I\r\ncannot go about in the daylight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon, in a tone so purely sympathetic\r\nthat the young man caught his hand, \xe2\x80\x9cI never said it was unreasonable;\r\nnever thought so. But I should like you to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that would give me the strongest motive to do it. But I cannot\r\nyet. I cannot persuade myself that the eyes of even the stream of\r\nstrangers I pass in this vast city look at me without suspicion. I feel\r\nmarked and tainted, even when I go out\xe2\x80\x94as I do only\xe2\x80\x94at night. But the\r\ndarkness covers me then, and I take courage from it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle laid a hand upon his shoulder, and stood looking down at\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I could have changed my name,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cI would have done so.\r\nBut as you wisely pointed out to me, I can\xe2\x80\x99t do that, for it would look\r\nlike guilt. If I could have gone to some distant place, I might have\r\nfound relief in that, but the thing is not to be thought of, for the\r\nsame reason. Hiding and escaping would be the construction in either\r\ncase. It seems a little hard to be so tied to a stake, and innocent;\r\nbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t complain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you must expect no miracle to help you, Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nCrisparkle, compassionately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, I know that. The ordinary fulness of time and circumstances\r\nis all I have to trust to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will right you at last, Neville.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I believe, and I hope I may live to know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut perceiving that the despondent mood into which he was falling cast\r\na shadow on the Minor Canon, and (it may be) feeling that the broad\r\nhand upon his shoulder was not then quite as steady as its own natural\r\nstrength had rendered it when it first touched him just now, he\r\nbrightened and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcellent circumstances for study, anyhow! and you know, Mr.\r\nCrisparkle, what need I have of study in all ways. Not to mention that\r\nyou have advised me to study for the difficult profession of the law,\r\nspecially, and that of course I am guiding myself by the advice of such\r\na friend and helper. Such a good friend and helper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took the fortifying hand from his shoulder, and kissed it. Mr.\r\nCrisparkle beamed at the books, but not so brightly as when he had\r\nentered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gather from your silence on the subject that my late guardian is\r\nadverse, Mr. Crisparkle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Minor Canon answered: \xe2\x80\x9cYour late guardian is a\xe2\x80\x94a most unreasonable\r\nperson, and it signifies nothing to any reasonable person whether he is\r\n_ad_verse, _per_verse, or the _re_verse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell for me that I have enough with economy to live upon,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed\r\nNeville, half wearily and half cheerily, \xe2\x80\x9cwhile I wait to be learned,\r\nand wait to be righted! Else I might have proved the proverb, that\r\nwhile the grass grows, the steed starves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe opened some books as he said it, and was soon immersed in their\r\ninterleaved and annotated passages; while Mr. Crisparkle sat beside\r\nhim, expounding, correcting, and advising. The Minor Canon\xe2\x80\x99s Cathedral\r\nduties made these visits of his difficult to accomplish, and only to be\r\ncompassed at intervals of many weeks. But they were as serviceable as\r\nthey were precious to Neville Landless.\r\n\r\nWhen they had got through such studies as they had in hand, they stood\r\nleaning on the window-sill, and looking down upon the patch of garden.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext week,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will cease to be alone, and will\r\nhave a devoted companion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cthis seems an uncongenial place to bring\r\nmy sister to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Minor Canon. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is duty to be done\r\nhere; and there are womanly feeling, sense, and courage wanted here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI meant,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the surroundings are so dull and\r\nunwomanly, and that Helena can have no suitable friend or society\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have only to remember,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are here\r\nyourself, and that she has to draw you into the sunlight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were silent for a little while, and then Mr. Crisparkle began\r\nanew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we first spoke together, Neville, you told me that your sister\r\nhad risen out of the disadvantages of your past lives as superior to\r\nyou as the tower of Cloisterham Cathedral is higher than the chimneys\r\nof Minor Canon Corner. Do you remember that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was inclined to think it at the time an enthusiastic flight. No\r\nmatter what I think it now. What I would emphasise is, that under the\r\nhead of Pride your sister is a great and opportune example to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder _all_ heads that are included in the composition of a fine\r\ncharacter, she is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay so; but take this one. Your sister has learnt how to govern what\r\nis proud in her nature. She can dominate it even when it is wounded\r\nthrough her sympathy with you. No doubt she has suffered deeply in\r\nthose same streets where you suffered deeply. No doubt her life is\r\ndarkened by the cloud that darkens yours. But bending her pride into a\r\ngrand composure that is not haughty or aggressive, but is a sustained\r\nconfidence in you and in the truth, she has won her way through those\r\nstreets until she passes along them as high in the general respect as\r\nany one who treads them. Every day and hour of her life since Edwin\r\nDrood\xe2\x80\x99s disappearance, she has faced malignity and folly\xe2\x80\x94for you\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nonly a brave nature well directed can. So it will be with her to the\r\nend. Another and weaker kind of pride might sink broken-hearted, but\r\nnever such a pride as hers: which knows no shrinking, and can get no\r\nmastery over her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pale cheek beside him flushed under the comparison, and the hint\r\nimplied in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will do all I can to imitate her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo so, and be a truly brave man, as she is a truly brave woman,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nanswered Mr. Crisparkle stoutly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is growing dark. Will you go my\r\nway with me, when it is quite dark? Mind! it is not I who wait for\r\ndarkness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville replied, that he would accompany him directly. But Mr.\r\nCrisparkle said he had a moment\xe2\x80\x99s call to make on Mr. Grewgious as an\r\nact of courtesy, and would run across to that gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s chambers, and\r\nrejoin Neville on his own doorstep, if he would come down there to meet\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious, bolt upright as usual, sat taking his wine in the dusk\r\nat his open window; his wineglass and decanter on the round table at\r\nhis elbow; himself and his legs on the window-seat; only one hinge in\r\nhis whole body, like a bootjack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do, reverend sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with abundant offers\r\nof hospitality, which were as cordially declined as made. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is\r\nyour charge getting on over the way in the set that I had the pleasure\r\nof recommending to you as vacant and eligible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle replied suitably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you approve of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I\r\nentertain a sort of fancy for having him under my eye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Mr. Grewgious had to turn his eye up considerably before he could\r\nsee the chambers, the phrase was to be taken figuratively and not\r\nliterally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how did you leave Mr. Jasper, reverend sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle had left him pretty well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where did you leave Mr. Jasper, reverend sir?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Crisparkle had\r\nleft him at Cloisterham.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when did you leave Mr. Jasper, reverend sir?\xe2\x80\x9d That morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmps!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cHe didn\xe2\x80\x99t say he was coming, perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComing where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnywhere, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause here he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, who had asked all these\r\nquestions, with his preoccupied glance directed out at window. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t look agreeable, does he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle was craning towards the window, when Mr. Grewgious\r\nadded:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you will kindly step round here behind me, in the gloom of the\r\nroom, and will cast your eye at the second-floor landing window in\r\nyonder house, I think you will hardly fail to see a slinking individual\r\nin whom I recognise our local friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmps!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. Then he added, turning his face so abruptly\r\nthat his head nearly came into collision with Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s: \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\r\nshould you say that our local friend was up to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last passage he had been shown in the Diary returned on Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s mind with the force of a strong recoil, and he asked Mr.\r\nGrewgious if he thought it possible that Neville was to be harassed by\r\nthe keeping of a watch upon him?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA watch?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Grewgious musingly. \xe2\x80\x9cAy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich would not only of itself haunt and torture his life,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nCrisparkle warmly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut would expose him to the torment of a\r\nperpetually reviving suspicion, whatever he might do, or wherever he\r\nmight go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious musingly still. \xe2\x80\x9cDo I see him waiting for\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen _would_ you have the goodness to excuse my getting up to see you\r\nout, and to go out to join him, and to go the way that you were going,\r\nand to take no notice of our local friend?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nentertain a sort of fancy for having _him_ under my eye to-night, do\r\nyou know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, with a significant nod complied; and rejoining Neville,\r\nwent away with him. They dined together, and parted at the yet\r\nunfinished and undeveloped railway station: Mr. Crisparkle to get home;\r\nNeville to walk the streets, cross the bridges, make a wide round of\r\nthe city in the friendly darkness, and tire himself out.\r\n\r\nIt was midnight when he returned from his solitary expedition and\r\nclimbed his staircase. The night was hot, and the windows of the\r\nstaircase were all wide open. Coming to the top, it gave him a passing\r\nchill of surprise (there being no rooms but his up there) to find a\r\nstranger sitting on the window-sill, more after the manner of a\r\nventuresome glazier than an amateur ordinarily careful of his neck; in\r\nfact, so much more outside the window than inside, as to suggest the\r\nthought that he must have come up by the water-spout instead of the\r\nstairs.\r\n\r\nThe stranger said nothing until Neville put his key in his door; then,\r\nseeming to make sure of his identity from the action, he spoke:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, coming from the window with a frank and\r\nsmiling air, and a prepossessing address; \xe2\x80\x9cthe beans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville was quite at a loss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRunners,\xe2\x80\x9d said the visitor. \xe2\x80\x9cScarlet. Next door at the back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Neville. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the mignonette and wall-flower?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same,\xe2\x80\x9d said the visitor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray walk in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville lighted his candles, and the visitor sat down. A handsome\r\ngentleman, with a young face, but with an older figure in its\r\nrobustness and its breadth of shoulder; say a man of eight-and-twenty,\r\nor at the utmost thirty; so extremely sunburnt that the contrast\r\nbetween his brown visage and the white forehead shaded out of doors by\r\nhis hat, and the glimpses of white throat below the neckerchief, would\r\nhave been almost ludicrous but for his broad temples, bright blue eyes,\r\nclustering brown hair, and laughing teeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have noticed,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94my name is Tartar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville inclined his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have noticed (excuse me) that you shut yourself up a good deal, and\r\nthat you seem to like my garden aloft here. If you would like a little\r\nmore of it, I could throw out a few lines and stays between my windows\r\nand yours, which the runners would take to directly. And I have some\r\nboxes, both of mignonette and wall-flower, that I could shove on along\r\nthe gutter (with a boathook I have by me) to your windows, and draw\r\nback again when they wanted watering or gardening, and shove on again\r\nwhen they were ship-shape; so that they would cause you no trouble. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take this liberty without asking your permission, so I venture\r\nto ask it. Tartar, corresponding set, next door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. I ought to apologise for looking in so late. But having\r\nnoticed (excuse me) that you generally walk out at night, I thought I\r\nshould inconvenience you least by awaiting your return. I am always\r\nafraid of inconveniencing busy men, being an idle man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not have thought so, from your appearance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo? I take it as a compliment. In fact, I was bred in the Royal Navy,\r\nand was First Lieutenant when I quitted it. But, an uncle disappointed\r\nin the service leaving me his property on condition that I left the\r\nNavy, I accepted the fortune, and resigned my commission.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLately, I presume?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I had had twelve or fifteen years of knocking about first. I\r\ncame here some nine months before you; I had had one crop before you\r\ncame. I chose this place, because, having served last in a little\r\ncorvette, I knew I should feel more at home where I had a constant\r\nopportunity of knocking my head against the ceiling. Besides, it would\r\nnever do for a man who had been aboard ship from his boyhood to turn\r\nluxurious all at once. Besides, again; having been accustomed to a very\r\nshort allowance of land all my life, I thought I\xe2\x80\x99d feel my way to the\r\ncommand of a landed estate, by beginning in boxes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhimsically as this was said, there was a touch of merry earnestness in\r\nit that made it doubly whimsical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Lieutenant, \xe2\x80\x9cI have talked quite enough about\r\nmyself. It is not my way, I hope; it has merely been to present myself\r\nto you naturally. If you will allow me to take the liberty I have\r\ndescribed, it will be a charity, for it will give me something more to\r\ndo. And you are not to suppose that it will entail any interruption or\r\nintrusion on you, for that is far from my intention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeville replied that he was greatly obliged, and that he thankfully\r\naccepted the kind proposal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to take your windows in tow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Lieutenant.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom what I have seen of you when I have been gardening at mine, and\r\nyou have been looking on, I have thought you (excuse me) rather too\r\nstudious and delicate. May I ask, is your health at all affected?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have undergone some mental distress,\xe2\x80\x9d said Neville, confused, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich\r\nhas stood me in the stead of illness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tartar.\r\n\r\nWith the greatest delicacy he shifted his ground to the windows again,\r\nand asked if he could look at one of them. On Neville\xe2\x80\x99s opening it, he\r\nimmediately sprang out, as if he were going aloft with a whole watch in\r\nan emergency, and were setting a bright example.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Neville, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t do that! Where are you going\r\nMr. Tartar? You\xe2\x80\x99ll be dashed to pieces!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll well!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Lieutenant, coolly looking about him on the\r\nhousetop. \xe2\x80\x9cAll taut and trim here. Those lines and stays shall be\r\nrigged before you turn out in the morning. May I take this short cut\r\nhome, and say good-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Tartar!\xe2\x80\x9d urged Neville. \xe2\x80\x9cPray! It makes me giddy to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Mr. Tartar, with a wave of his hand and the deftness of a cat, had\r\nalready dipped through his scuttle of scarlet runners without breaking\r\na leaf, and \xe2\x80\x9cgone below.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious, his bedroom window-blind held aside with his hand,\r\nhappened at the moment to have Neville\xe2\x80\x99s chambers under his eye for the\r\nlast time that night. Fortunately his eye was on the front of the house\r\nand not the back, or this remarkable appearance and disappearance might\r\nhave broken his rest as a phenomenon. But Mr. Grewgious seeing nothing\r\nthere, not even a light in the windows, his gaze wandered from the\r\nwindows to the stars, as if he would have read in them something that\r\nwas hidden from him. Many of us would, if we could; but none of us so\r\nmuch as know our letters in the stars yet\xe2\x80\x94or seem likely to do it, in\r\nthis state of existence\xe2\x80\x94and few languages can be read until their\r\nalphabets are mastered.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\nA SETTLER IN CLOISTERHAM\r\n\r\n\r\nAt about this time a stranger appeared in Cloisterham; a white-haired\r\npersonage, with black eyebrows. Being buttoned up in a tightish blue\r\nsurtout, with a buff waistcoat and gray trousers, he had something of a\r\nmilitary air, but he announced himself at the Crozier (the orthodox\r\nhotel, where he put up with a portmanteau) as an idle dog who lived\r\nupon his means; and he farther announced that he had a mind to take a\r\nlodging in the picturesque old city for a month or two, with a view of\r\nsettling down there altogether. Both announcements were made in the\r\ncoffee-room of the Crozier, to all whom it might or might not concern,\r\nby the stranger as he stood with his back to the empty fireplace,\r\nwaiting for his fried sole, veal cutlet, and pint of sherry. And the\r\nwaiter (business being chronically slack at the Crozier) represented\r\nall whom it might or might not concern, and absorbed the whole of the\r\ninformation.\r\n\r\nThis gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s white head was unusually large, and his shock of white\r\nhair was unusually thick and ample. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose, waiter,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nshaking his shock of hair, as a Newfoundland dog might shake his before\r\nsitting down to dinner, \xe2\x80\x9cthat a fair lodging for a single buffer might\r\nbe found in these parts, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe waiter had no doubt of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething old,\xe2\x80\x9d said the gentleman. \xe2\x80\x9cTake my hat down for a moment\r\nfrom that peg, will you? No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want it; look into it. What do you\r\nsee written there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe waiter read: \xe2\x80\x9cDatchery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you know my name,\xe2\x80\x9d said the gentleman; \xe2\x80\x9cDick Datchery. Hang it up\r\nagain. I was saying something old is what I should prefer, something\r\nodd and out of the way; something venerable, architectural, and\r\ninconvenient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have a good choice of inconvenient lodgings in the town, sir, I\r\nthink,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the waiter, with modest confidence in its resources\r\nthat way; \xe2\x80\x9cindeed, I have no doubt that we could suit you that far,\r\nhowever particular you might be. But a architectural lodging!\xe2\x80\x9d That\r\nseemed to trouble the waiter\xe2\x80\x99s head, and he shook it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything Cathedraly, now,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Datchery suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Tope,\xe2\x80\x9d said the waiter, brightening, as he rubbed his chin with\r\nhis hand, \xe2\x80\x9cwould be the likeliest party to inform in that line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Mr. Tope?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Dick Datchery.\r\n\r\nThe waiter explained that he was the Verger, and that Mrs. Tope had\r\nindeed once upon a time let lodgings herself or offered to let them;\r\nbut that as nobody had ever taken them, Mrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s window-bill, long a\r\nCloisterham Institution, had disappeared; probably had tumbled down one\r\nday, and never been put up again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll call on Mrs. Tope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9cafter dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo when he had done his dinner, he was duly directed to the spot, and\r\nsallied out for it. But the Crozier being an hotel of a most retiring\r\ndisposition, and the waiter\xe2\x80\x99s directions being fatally precise, he soon\r\nbecame bewildered, and went boggling about and about the Cathedral\r\nTower, whenever he could catch a glimpse of it, with a general\r\nimpression on his mind that Mrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s was somewhere very near it, and\r\nthat, like the children in the game of hot boiled beans and very good\r\nbutter, he was warm in his search when he saw the Tower, and cold when\r\nhe didn\xe2\x80\x99t see it.\r\n\r\nHe was getting very cold indeed when he came upon a fragment of\r\nburial-ground in which an unhappy sheep was grazing. Unhappy, because a\r\nhideous small boy was stoning it through the railings, and had already\r\nlamed it in one leg, and was much excited by the benevolent\r\nsportsmanlike purpose of breaking its other three legs, and bringing it\r\ndown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99It \xe2\x80\x99im agin!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the boy, as the poor creature leaped; \xe2\x80\x9cand made a\r\ndint in his wool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him be!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see you have lamed him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the sportsman. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99E went and lamed isself. I see \xe2\x80\x99im\r\ndo it, and I giv\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99im a shy as a Widdy-warning to \xe2\x80\x99im not to go\r\na-bruisin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99is master\xe2\x80\x99s mutton any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t; I\xe2\x80\x99ll come when yer can ketch me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay there then, and show me which is Mr. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOw can I stay here and show you which is Topeseses, when Topeseses is\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other side the Kinfreederal, and over the crossings, and round ever\r\nso many comers? Stoo-pid! Ya-a-ah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShow me where it is, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis brisk dialogue concluded, the boy led the way, and by-and-by\r\nstopped at some distance from an arched passage, pointing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookie yonder. You see that there winder and door?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Tope\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie; it ain\xe2\x80\x99t. That\xe2\x80\x99s Jarsper\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, with a second look of some interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 no nearer IM, I tell yer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cos I ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 to be lifted off my legs and \xe2\x80\x99ave my braces bust\r\nand be choked; not if I knows it, and not by \xe2\x80\x9cIm. Wait till I set a\r\njolly good flint a-flyin\xe2\x80\x99 at the back o\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99is jolly old \xe2\x80\x99ed some day!\r\nNow look t\xe2\x80\x99other side the harch; not the side where Jarsper\xe2\x80\x99s door is;\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little way in, o\xe2\x80\x99 that side, there\xe2\x80\x99s a low door, down two steps.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s Topeseses with \xe2\x80\x99is name on a hoval plate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. See here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, producing a shilling. \xe2\x80\x9cYou owe me\r\nhalf of this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie! I don\xe2\x80\x99t owe yer nothing; I never seen yer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you you owe me half of this, because I have no sixpence in my\r\npocket. So the next time you meet me you shall do something else for\r\nme, to pay me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, give us \xe2\x80\x99old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your name, and where do you live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeputy. Travellers\xe2\x80\x99 Twopenny, \xe2\x80\x99cross the green.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy instantly darted off with the shilling, lest Mr. Datchery\r\nshould repent, but stopped at a safe distance, on the happy chance of\r\nhis being uneasy in his mind about it, to goad him with a demon dance\r\nexpressive of its irrevocability.\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery, taking off his hat to give that shock of white hair of\r\nhis another shake, seemed quite resigned, and betook himself whither he\r\nhad been directed.\r\n\r\nMr. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s official dwelling, communicating by an upper stair with Mr.\r\nJasper\xe2\x80\x99s (hence Mrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s attendance on that gentleman), was of very\r\nmodest proportions, and partook of the character of a cool dungeon. Its\r\nancient walls were massive, and its rooms rather seemed to have been\r\ndug out of them, than to have been designed beforehand with any\r\nreference to them. The main door opened at once on a chamber of no\r\ndescribable shape, with a groined roof, which in its turn opened on\r\nanother chamber of no describable shape, with another groined roof:\r\ntheir windows small, and in the thickness of the walls. These two\r\nchambers, close as to their atmosphere, and swarthy as to their\r\nillumination by natural light, were the apartments which Mrs. Tope had\r\nso long offered to an unappreciative city. Mr. Datchery, however, was\r\nmore appreciative. He found that if he sat with the main door open he\r\nwould enjoy the passing society of all comers to and fro by the\r\ngateway, and would have light enough. He found that if Mr. and Mrs.\r\nTope, living overhead, used for their own egress and ingress a little\r\nside stair that came plump into the Precincts by a door opening\r\noutward, to the surprise and inconvenience of a limited public of\r\npedestrians in a narrow way, he would be alone, as in a separate\r\nresidence. He found the rent moderate, and everything as quaintly\r\ninconvenient as he could desire. He agreed, therefore, to take the\r\nlodging then and there, and money down, possession to be had next\r\nevening, on condition that reference was permitted him to Mr. Jasper as\r\noccupying the gatehouse, of which on the other side of the gateway, the\r\nVerger\xe2\x80\x99s hole-in-the-wall was an appanage or subsidiary part.\r\n\r\nThe poor dear gentleman was very solitary and very sad, Mrs. Tope said,\r\nbut she had no doubt he would \xe2\x80\x9cspeak for her.\xe2\x80\x9d Perhaps Mr. Datchery had\r\nheard something of what had occurred there last winter?\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery had as confused a knowledge of the event in question, on\r\ntrying to recall it, as he well could have. He begged Mrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npardon when she found it incumbent on her to correct him in every\r\ndetail of his summary of the facts, but pleaded that he was merely a\r\nsingle buffer getting through life upon his means as idly as he could,\r\nand that so many people were so constantly making away with so many\r\nother people, as to render it difficult for a buffer of an easy temper\r\nto preserve the circumstances of the several cases unmixed in his mind.\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper proving willing to speak for Mrs. Tope, Mr. Datchery, who\r\nhad sent up his card, was invited to ascend the postern staircase. The\r\nMayor was there, Mr. Tope said; but he was not to be regarded in the\r\nlight of company, as he and Mr. Jasper were great friends.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, making a leg with his hat under his\r\narm, as he addressed himself equally to both gentlemen; \xe2\x80\x9ca selfish\r\nprecaution on my part, and not personally interesting to anybody but\r\nmyself. But as a buffer living on his means, and having an idea of\r\ndoing it in this lovely place in peace and quiet, for remaining span of\r\nlife, I beg to ask if the Tope family are quite respectable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Jasper could answer for that without the slightest hesitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is enough, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend the Mayor,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mr. Jasper, presenting Mr. Datchery with a\r\ncourtly motion of his hand towards that potentate; \xe2\x80\x9cwhose\r\nrecommendation is actually much more important to a stranger than that\r\nof an obscure person like myself, will testify in their behalf, I am\r\nsure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Worshipful the Mayor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, with a low bow, \xe2\x80\x9cplaces\r\nme under an infinite obligation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good people, sir, Mr. and Mrs. Tope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Sapsea, with\r\ncondescension. \xe2\x80\x9cVery good opinions. Very well behaved. Very respectful.\r\nMuch approved by the Dean and Chapter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Worshipful the Mayor gives them a character,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cof which they may indeed be proud. I would ask His Honour (if I might\r\nbe permitted) whether there are not many objects of great interest in\r\nthe city which is under his beneficent sway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Sapsea, \xe2\x80\x9can ancient city, and an\r\necclesiastical city. We are a constitutional city, as it becomes such a\r\ncity to be, and we uphold and maintain our glorious privileges.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, bowing, \xe2\x80\x9cinspires me with a desire to\r\nknow more of the city, and confirms me in my inclination to end my days\r\nin the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRetired from the Army, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mr. Sapsea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour the Mayor does me too much credit,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Datchery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNavy, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mr. Sapsea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgain,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour the Mayor does me too much\r\ncredit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiplomacy is a fine profession,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Sapsea, as a general remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, I confess, His Honour the Mayor is too many for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nDatchery, with an ingenious smile and bow; \xe2\x80\x9ceven a diplomatic bird must\r\nfall to such a gun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow this was very soothing. Here was a gentleman of a great, not to say\r\na grand, address, accustomed to rank and dignity, really setting a fine\r\nexample how to behave to a Mayor. There was something in that\r\nthird-person style of being spoken to, that Mr. Sapsea found\r\nparticularly recognisant of his merits and position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I crave pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery. \xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour the Mayor will\r\nbear with me, if for a moment I have been deluded into occupying his\r\ntime, and have forgotten the humble claims upon my own, of my hotel,\r\nthe Crozier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Sapsea. \xe2\x80\x9cI am returning home, and if you\r\nwould like to take the exterior of our Cathedral in your way, I shall\r\nbe glad to point it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour the Mayor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9cis more than kind and\r\ngracious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Mr. Datchery, when he had made his acknowledgments to Mr. Jasper,\r\ncould not be induced to go out of the room before the Worshipful, the\r\nWorshipful led the way down-stairs; Mr. Datchery following with his hat\r\nunder his arm, and his shock of white hair streaming in the evening\r\nbreeze.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight I ask His Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether that gentleman we\r\nhave just left is the gentleman of whom I have heard in the\r\nneighbourhood as being much afflicted by the loss of a nephew, and\r\nconcentrating his life on avenging the loss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the gentleman. John Jasper, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould His Honour allow me to inquire whether there are strong\r\nsuspicions of any one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than suspicions, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Sapsea; \xe2\x80\x9call but\r\ncertainties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly think now!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Datchery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut proof, sir, proof must be built up stone by stone,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nMayor. \xe2\x80\x9cAs I say, the end crowns the work. It is not enough that\r\njustice should be morally certain; she must be immorally\r\ncertain\xe2\x80\x94legally, that is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9creminds me of the nature of the law.\r\nImmoral. How true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I say, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d pompously went on the Mayor, \xe2\x80\x9cthe arm of the law is a\r\nstrong arm, and a long arm. That is the way I put it. A strong arm and\r\na long arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow forcible!\xe2\x80\x94And yet, again, how true!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mr. Datchery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd without betraying, what I call the secrets of the prison-house,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Sapsea; \xe2\x80\x9cthe secrets of the prison-house is the term I used on\r\nthe bench.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what other term than His Honour\xe2\x80\x99s would express it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nDatchery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout, I say, betraying them, I predict to you, knowing the iron\r\nwill of the gentleman we have just left (I take the bold step of\r\ncalling it iron, on account of its strength), that in this case the\r\nlong arm will reach, and the strong arm will strike.\xe2\x80\x94This is our\r\nCathedral, sir. The best judges are pleased to admire it, and the best\r\namong our townsmen own to being a little vain of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this time Mr. Datchery had walked with his hat under his arm, and\r\nhis white hair streaming. He had an odd momentary appearance upon him\r\nof having forgotten his hat, when Mr. Sapsea now touched it; and he\r\nclapped his hand up to his head as if with some vague expectation of\r\nfinding another hat upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray be covered, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d entreated Mr. Sapsea; magnificently plying: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall not mind it, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour is very good, but I do it for coolness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery.\r\n\r\nThen Mr. Datchery admired the Cathedral, and Mr. Sapsea pointed it out\r\nas if he himself had invented and built it: there were a few details\r\nindeed of which he did not approve, but those he glossed over, as if\r\nthe workmen had made mistakes in his absence. The Cathedral disposed\r\nof, he led the way by the churchyard, and stopped to extol the beauty\r\nof the evening\xe2\x80\x94by chance\xe2\x80\x94in the immediate vicinity of Mrs. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nepitaph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd by the by,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Sapsea, appearing to descend from an\r\nelevation to remember it all of a sudden; like Apollo shooting down\r\nfrom Olympus to pick up his forgotten lyre; \xe2\x80\x9c_that_ is one of our small\r\nlions. The partiality of our people has made it so, and strangers have\r\nbeen seen taking a copy of it now and then. I am not a judge of it\r\nmyself, for it is a little work of my own.
