But it was troublesome to\r\nturn, sir; I may say, difficult to turn with elegance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery became so ecstatic over Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s composition, that, in\r\nspite of his intention to end his days in Cloisterham, and therefore\r\nhis probably having in reserve many opportunities of copying it, he\r\nwould have transcribed it into his pocket-book on the spot, but for the\r\nslouching towards them of its material producer and perpetuator,\r\nDurdles, whom Mr. Sapsea hailed, not sorry to show him a bright example\r\nof behaviour to superiors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Durdles! This is the mason, sir; one of our Cloisterham worthies;\r\neverybody here knows Durdles. Mr. Datchery, Durdles a gentleman who is\r\ngoing to settle here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it if I was him,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Durdles. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re a heavy lot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou surely don\xe2\x80\x99t speak for yourself, Mr. Durdles,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr.\r\nDatchery, \xe2\x80\x9cany more than for His Honour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s His Honour?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Durdles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Honour the Mayor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never was brought afore him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Durdles, with anything but the\r\nlook of a loyal subject of the mayoralty, \xe2\x80\x9cand it\xe2\x80\x99ll be time enough for\r\nme to Honour him when I am. Until which, and when, and where,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mister Sapsea is his name,\r\n    England is his nation,\r\nCloisterham\xe2\x80\x99s his dwelling-place,\r\n    Aukshneer\xe2\x80\x99s his occupation.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nHere, Deputy (preceded by a flying oyster-shell) appeared upon the\r\nscene, and requested to have the sum of threepence instantly \xe2\x80\x9cchucked\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nto him by Mr. Durdles, whom he had been vainly seeking up and down, as\r\nlawful wages overdue. While that gentleman, with his bundle under his\r\narm, slowly found and counted out the money, Mr. Sapsea informed the\r\nnew settler of Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s habits, pursuits, abode, and reputation. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose a curious stranger might come to see you, and your works, Mr.\r\nDurdles, at any odd time?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Datchery upon that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny gentleman is welcome to come and see me any evening if he brings\r\nliquor for two with him,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Durdles, with a penny between his\r\nteeth and certain halfpence in his hands; \xe2\x80\x9cor if he likes to make it\r\ntwice two, he\xe2\x80\x99ll be doubly welcome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall come. Master Deputy, what do you owe me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA job.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind you pay me honestly with the job of showing me Mr. Durdles\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse when I want to go there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDeputy, with a piercing broadside of whistle through the whole gap in\r\nhis mouth, as a receipt in full for all arrears, vanished.\r\n\r\nThe Worshipful and the Worshipper then passed on together until they\r\nparted, with many ceremonies, at the Worshipful\xe2\x80\x99s door; even then the\r\nWorshipper carried his hat under his arm, and gave his streaming white\r\nhair to the breeze.\r\n\r\nSaid Mr. Datchery to himself that night, as he looked at his white hair\r\nin the gas-lighted looking-glass over the coffee-room chimneypiece at\r\nthe Crozier, and shook it out: \xe2\x80\x9cFor a single buffer, of an easy temper,\r\nliving idly on his means, I have had a rather busy afternoon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\nSHADOW ON THE SUN-DIAL\r\n\r\n\r\nAgain Miss Twinkleton has delivered her valedictory address, with the\r\naccompaniments of white-wine and pound-cake, and again the young ladies\r\nhave departed to their several homes. Helena Landless has left the\r\nNuns\xe2\x80\x99 House to attend her brother\xe2\x80\x99s fortunes, and pretty Rosa is alone.\r\n\r\nCloisterham is so bright and sunny in these summer days, that the\r\nCathedral and the monastery-ruin show as if their strong walls were\r\ntransparent. A soft glow seems to shine from within them, rather than\r\nupon them from without, such is their mellowness as they look forth on\r\nthe hot corn-fields and the smoking roads that distantly wind among\r\nthem. The Cloisterham gardens blush with ripening fruit. Time was when\r\ntravel-stained pilgrims rode in clattering parties through the city\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwelcome shades; time is when wayfarers, leading a gipsy life between\r\nhaymaking time and harvest, and looking as if they were just made of\r\nthe dust of the earth, so very dusty are they, lounge about on cool\r\ndoor-steps, trying to mend their unmendable shoes, or giving them to\r\nthe city kennels as a hopeless job, and seeking others in the bundles\r\nthat they carry, along with their yet unused sickles swathed in bands\r\nof straw. At all the more public pumps there is much cooling of bare\r\nfeet, together with much bubbling and gurgling of drinking with hand to\r\nspout on the part of these Bedouins; the Cloisterham police meanwhile\r\nlooking askant from their beats with suspicion, and manifest impatience\r\nthat the intruders should depart from within the civic bounds, and once\r\nmore fry themselves on the simmering high-roads.\r\n\r\nOn the afternoon of such a day, when the last Cathedral service is\r\ndone, and when that side of the High Street on which the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House\r\nstands is in grateful shade, save where its quaint old garden opens to\r\nthe west between the boughs of trees, a servant informs Rosa, to her\r\nterror, that Mr. Jasper desires to see her.\r\n\r\nIf he had chosen his time for finding her at a disadvantage, he could\r\nhave done no better. Perhaps he has chosen it. Helena Landless is gone,\r\nMrs. Tisher is absent on leave, Miss Twinkleton (in her amateur state\r\nof existence) has contributed herself and a veal pie to a picnic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO why, why, why, did you say I was at home!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa, helplessly.\r\n\r\nThe maid replies, that Mr. Jasper never asked the question.\r\n\r\nThat he said he knew she was at home, and begged she might be told that\r\nhe asked to see her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I do! what shall I do!\xe2\x80\x9d thinks Rosa, clasping her hands.\r\n\r\nPossessed by a kind of desperation, she adds in the next breath, that\r\nshe will come to Mr. Jasper in the garden. She shudders at the thought\r\nof being shut up with him in the house; but many of its windows command\r\nthe garden, and she can be seen as well as heard there, and can shriek\r\nin the free air and run away. Such is the wild idea that flutters\r\nthrough her mind.\r\n\r\nShe has never seen him since the fatal night, except when she was\r\nquestioned before the Mayor, and then he was present in gloomy\r\nwatchfulness, as representing his lost nephew and burning to avenge\r\nhim. She hangs her garden-hat on her arm, and goes out. The moment she\r\nsees him from the porch, leaning on the sun-dial, the old horrible\r\nfeeling of being compelled by him, asserts its hold upon her. She feels\r\nthat she would even then go back, but that he draws her feet towards\r\nhim. She cannot resist, and sits down, with her head bent, on the\r\ngarden-seat beside the sun-dial. She cannot look up at him for\r\nabhorrence, but she has perceived that he is dressed in deep mourning.\r\nSo is she. It was not so at first; but the lost has long been given up,\r\nand mourned for, as dead.\r\n\r\nHe would begin by touching her hand. She feels the intention, and draws\r\nher hand back. His eyes are then fixed upon her, she knows, though her\r\nown see nothing but the grass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been waiting,\xe2\x80\x9d he begins, \xe2\x80\x9cfor some time, to be summoned back\r\nto my duty near you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter several times forming her lips, which she knows he is closely\r\nwatching, into the shape of some other hesitating reply, and then into\r\nnone, she answers: \xe2\x80\x9cDuty, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe duty of teaching you, serving you as your faithful music-master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have left off that study.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot left off, I think. Discontinued. I was told by your guardian that\r\nyou discontinued it under the shock that we have all felt so acutely.\r\nWhen will you resume?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever? You could have done no more if you had loved my dear boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did love him!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa, with a flash of anger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but not quite\xe2\x80\x94not quite in the right way, shall I say? Not in the\r\nintended and expected way. Much as my dear boy was, unhappily, too\r\nself-conscious and self-satisfied (I\xe2\x80\x99ll draw no parallel between him\r\nand you in that respect) to love as he should have loved, or as any one\r\nin his place would have loved\xe2\x80\x94must have loved!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sits in the same still attitude, but shrinking a little more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, to be told that you discontinued your study with me, was to be\r\npolitely told that you abandoned it altogether?\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d says Rosa, with sudden spirit, \xe2\x80\x9cThe politeness was my\r\nguardian\xe2\x80\x99s, not mine. I told him that I was resolved to leave off, and\r\nthat I was determined to stand by my resolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you still are?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI still am, sir. And I beg not to be questioned any more about it. At\r\nall events, I will not answer any more; I have that in my power.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe is so conscious of his looking at her with a gloating admiration of\r\nthe touch of anger on her, and the fire and animation it brings with\r\nit, that even as her spirit rises, it falls again, and she struggles\r\nwith a sense of shame, affront, and fear, much as she did that night at\r\nthe piano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not question you any more, since you object to it so much; I\r\nwill confess\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not wish to hear you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d cries Rosa, rising.\r\n\r\nThis time he does touch her with his outstretched hand. In shrinking\r\nfrom it, she shrinks into her seat again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must sometimes act in opposition to our wishes,\xe2\x80\x9d he tells her in a\r\nlow voice. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must do so now, or do more harm to others than you can\r\never set right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat harm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPresently, presently. You question _me_, you see, and surely that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnot fair when you forbid me to question you. Nevertheless, I will\r\nanswer the question presently. Dearest Rosa! Charming Rosa!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe starts up again.\r\n\r\nThis time he does not touch her. But his face looks so wicked and\r\nmenacing, as he stands leaning against the sun-dial-setting, as it\r\nwere, his black mark upon the very face of day\xe2\x80\x94that her flight is\r\narrested by horror as she looks at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not forget how many windows command a view of us,\xe2\x80\x9d he says,\r\nglancing towards them. \xe2\x80\x9cI will not touch you again; I will come no\r\nnearer to you than I am. Sit down, and there will be no mighty wonder\r\nin your music-master\xe2\x80\x99s leaning idly against a pedestal and speaking\r\nwith you, remembering all that has happened, and our shares in it. Sit\r\ndown, my beloved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe would have gone once more\xe2\x80\x94was all but gone\xe2\x80\x94and once more his face,\r\ndarkly threatening what would follow if she went, has stopped her.\r\nLooking at him with the expression of the instant frozen on her face,\r\nshe sits down on the seat again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRosa, even when my dear boy was affianced to you, I loved you madly;\r\neven when I thought his happiness in having you for his wife was\r\ncertain, I loved you madly; even when I strove to make him more\r\nardently devoted to you, I loved you madly; even when he gave me the\r\npicture of your lovely face so carelessly traduced by him, which I\r\nfeigned to hang always in my sight for his sake, but worshipped in\r\ntorment for years, I loved you madly; in the distasteful work of the\r\nday, in the wakeful misery of the night, girded by sordid realities, or\r\nwandering through Paradises and Hells of visions into which I rushed,\r\ncarrying your image in my arms, I loved you madly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf anything could make his words more hideous to her than they are in\r\nthemselves, it would be the contrast between the violence of his look\r\nand delivery, and the composure of his assumed attitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI endured it all in silence. So long as you were his, or so long as I\r\nsupposed you to be his, I hid my secret loyally. Did I not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis lie, so gross, while the mere words in which it is told are so\r\ntrue, is more than Rosa can endure. She answers with kindling\r\nindignation: \xe2\x80\x9cYou were as false throughout, sir, as you are now. You\r\nwere false to him, daily and hourly. You know that you made my life\r\nunhappy by your pursuit of me. You know that you made me afraid to open\r\nhis generous eyes, and that you forced me, for his own trusting, good,\r\ngood sake, to keep the truth from him, that you were a bad, bad man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis preservation of his easy attitude rendering his working features\r\nand his convulsive hands absolutely diabolical, he returns, with a\r\nfierce extreme of admiration:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow beautiful you are! You are more beautiful in anger than in repose.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you for your love; give me yourself and your hatred; give\r\nme yourself and that pretty rage; give me yourself and that enchanting\r\nscorn; it will be enough for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nImpatient tears rise to the eyes of the trembling little beauty, and\r\nher face flames; but as she again rises to leave him in indignation,\r\nand seek protection within the house, he stretches out his hand towards\r\nthe porch, as though he invited her to enter it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you, you rare charmer, you sweet witch, that you must stay and\r\nhear me, or do more harm than can ever be undone. You asked me what\r\nharm. Stay, and I will tell you. Go, and I will do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Rosa quails before his threatening face, though innocent of its\r\nmeaning, and she remains. Her panting breathing comes and goes as if it\r\nwould choke her; but with a repressive hand upon her bosom, she\r\nremains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made my confession that my love is mad. It is so mad, that had\r\nthe ties between me and my dear lost boy been one silken thread less\r\nstrong, I might have swept even him from your side, when you favoured\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA film comes over the eyes she raises for an instant, as though he had\r\nturned her faint.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven him,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeats. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, even him! Rosa, you see me and you hear\r\nme. Judge for yourself whether any other admirer shall love you and\r\nlive, whose life is in my hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean to show you how mad my love is. It was hawked through the late\r\ninquiries by Mr. Crisparkle, that young Landless had confessed to him\r\nthat he was a rival of my lost boy. That is an inexpiable offence in my\r\neyes. The same Mr. Crisparkle knows under my hand that I have devoted\r\nmyself to the murderer\xe2\x80\x99s discovery and destruction, be he whom he\r\nmight, and that I determined to discuss the mystery with no one until I\r\nshould hold the clue in which to entangle the murderer as in a net. I\r\nhave since worked patiently to wind and wind it round him; and it is\r\nslowly winding as I speak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s sacrifices]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour belief, if you believe in the criminality of Mr. Landless, is not\r\nMr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s belief, and he is a good man,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa retorts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy belief is my own; and I reserve it, worshipped of my soul!\r\nCircumstances may accumulate so strongly _even against an innocent\r\nman_, that directed, sharpened, and pointed, they may slay him. One\r\nwanting link discovered by perseverance against a guilty man, proves\r\nhis guilt, however slight its evidence before, and he dies. Young\r\nLandless stands in deadly peril either way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you really suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa pleads with him, turning paler, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\r\nfavour Mr. Landless, or that Mr. Landless has ever in any way addressed\r\nhimself to me, you are wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe puts that from him with a slighting action of his hand and a curled\r\nlip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was going to show you how madly I love you. More madly now than\r\never, for I am willing to renounce the second object that has arisen in\r\nmy life to divide it with you; and henceforth to have no object in\r\nexistence but you only. Miss Landless has become your bosom friend. You\r\ncare for her peace of mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love her dearly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou care for her good name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have said, sir, I love her dearly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am unconsciously,\xe2\x80\x9d he observes with a smile, as he folds his hands\r\nupon the sun-dial and leans his chin upon them, so that his talk would\r\nseem from the windows (faces occasionally come and go there) to be of\r\nthe airiest and playfullest\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI am unconsciously giving offence by\r\nquestioning again. I will simply make statements, therefore, and not\r\nput questions. You do care for your bosom friend\xe2\x80\x99s good name, and you\r\ndo care for her peace of mind. Then remove the shadow of the gallows\r\nfrom her, dear one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou dare propose to me to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDarling, I dare propose to you. Stop there. If it be bad to idolise\r\nyou, I am the worst of men; if it be good, I am the best. My love for\r\nyou is above all other love, and my truth to you is above all other\r\ntruth. Let me have hope and favour, and I am a forsworn man for your\r\nsake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa puts her hands to her temples, and, pushing back her hair, looks\r\nwildly and abhorrently at him, as though she were trying to piece\r\ntogether what it is his deep purpose to present to her only in\r\nfragments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReckon up nothing at this moment, angel, but the sacrifices that I lay\r\nat those dear feet, which I could fall down among the vilest ashes and\r\nkiss, and put upon my head as a poor savage might. There is my fidelity\r\nto my dear boy after death. Tread upon it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith an action of his hands, as though he cast down something precious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is the inexpiable offence against my adoration of you. Spurn\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a similar action.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are my labours in the cause of a just vengeance for six toiling\r\nmonths. Crush them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith another repetition of the action.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is my past and my present wasted life. There is the desolation\r\nof my heart and my soul. There is my peace; there is my despair. Stamp\r\nthem into the dust; so that you take me, were it even mortally hating\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe frightful vehemence of the man, now reaching its full height, so\r\nadditionally terrifies her as to break the spell that has held her to\r\nthe spot. She swiftly moves towards the porch; but in an instant he is\r\nat her side, and speaking in her ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRosa, I am self-repressed again. I am walking calmly beside you to the\r\nhouse. I shall wait for some encouragement and hope. I shall not strike\r\ntoo soon. Give me a sign that you attend to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe slightly and constrainedly moves her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word of this to any one, or it will bring down the blow, as\r\ncertainly as night follows day. Another sign that you attend to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe moves her hand once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you, love you, love you! If you were to cast me off now\xe2\x80\x94but you\r\nwill not\xe2\x80\x94you would never be rid of me. No one should come between us. I\r\nwould pursue you to the death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe handmaid coming out to open the gate for him, he quietly pulls off\r\nhis hat as a parting salute, and goes away with no greater show of\r\nagitation than is visible in the effigy of Mr. Sapsea\xe2\x80\x99s father\r\nopposite. Rosa faints in going up-stairs, and is carefully carried to\r\nher room and laid down on her bed. A thunderstorm is coming on, the\r\nmaids say, and the hot and stifling air has overset the pretty dear: no\r\nwonder; they have felt their own knees all of a tremble all day long.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\nA FLIGHT\r\n\r\n\r\nRosa no sooner came to herself than the whole of the late interview was\r\nbefore her. It even seemed as if it had pursued her into her\r\ninsensibility, and she had not had a moment\xe2\x80\x99s unconsciousness of it.\r\nWhat to do, she was at a frightened loss to know: the only one clear\r\nthought in her mind was, that she must fly from this terrible man.\r\n\r\nBut where could she take refuge, and how could she go? She had never\r\nbreathed her dread of him to any one but Helena. If she went to Helena,\r\nand told her what had passed, that very act might bring down the\r\nirreparable mischief that he threatened he had the power, and that she\r\nknew he had the will, to do. The more fearful he appeared to her\r\nexcited memory and imagination, the more alarming her responsibility\r\nappeared; seeing that a slight mistake on her part, either in action or\r\ndelay, might let his malevolence loose on Helena\xe2\x80\x99s brother.\r\n\r\nRosa\xe2\x80\x99s mind throughout the last six months had been stormily confused.\r\nA half-formed, wholly unexpressed suspicion tossed in it, now heaving\r\nitself up, and now sinking into the deep; now gaining palpability, and\r\nnow losing it. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s self-absorption in his nephew when he was\r\nalive, and his unceasing pursuit of the inquiry how he came by his\r\ndeath, if he were dead, were themes so rife in the place, that no one\r\nappeared able to suspect the possibility of foul play at his hands. She\r\nhad asked herself the question, \xe2\x80\x9cAm I so wicked in my thoughts as to\r\nconceive a wickedness that others cannot imagine?\xe2\x80\x9d Then she had\r\nconsidered, Did the suspicion come of her previous recoiling from him\r\nbefore the fact? And if so, was not that a proof of its baselessness?\r\nThen she had reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat motive could he have, according to my\r\naccusation?\xe2\x80\x9d She was ashamed to answer in her mind, \xe2\x80\x9cThe motive of\r\ngaining _me_!\xe2\x80\x9d And covered her face, as if the lightest shadow of the\r\nidea of founding murder on such an idle vanity were a crime almost as\r\ngreat.\r\n\r\nShe ran over in her mind again, all that he had said by the sun-dial in\r\nthe garden. He had persisted in treating the disappearance as murder,\r\nconsistently with his whole public course since the finding of the\r\nwatch and shirt-pin. If he were afraid of the crime being traced out,\r\nwould he not rather encourage the idea of a voluntary disappearance? He\r\nhad even declared that if the ties between him and his nephew had been\r\nless strong, he might have swept \xe2\x80\x9ceven him\xe2\x80\x9d away from her side. Was\r\nthat like his having really done so? He had spoken of laying his six\r\nmonths\xe2\x80\x99 labours in the cause of a just vengeance at her feet. Would he\r\nhave done that, with that violence of passion, if they were a pretence?\r\nWould he have ranged them with his desolate heart and soul, his wasted\r\nlife, his peace and his despair? The very first sacrifice that he\r\nrepresented himself as making for her, was his fidelity to his dear boy\r\nafter death. Surely these facts were strong against a fancy that\r\nscarcely dared to hint itself. And yet he was so terrible a man! In\r\nshort, the poor girl (for what could she know of the criminal\r\nintellect, which its own professed students perpetually misread,\r\nbecause they persist in trying to reconcile it with the average\r\nintellect of average men, instead of identifying it as a horrible\r\nwonder apart) could get by no road to any other conclusion than that he\r\n_was_ a terrible man, and must be fled from.\r\n\r\nShe had been Helena\xe2\x80\x99s stay and comfort during the whole time. She had\r\nconstantly assured her of her full belief in her brother\xe2\x80\x99s innocence,\r\nand of her sympathy with him in his misery. But she had never seen him\r\nsince the disappearance, nor had Helena ever spoken one word of his\r\navowal to Mr. Crisparkle in regard of Rosa, though as a part of the\r\ninterest of the case it was well known far and wide. He was Helena\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nunfortunate brother, to her, and nothing more. The assurance she had\r\ngiven her odious suitor was strictly true, though it would have been\r\nbetter (she considered now) if she could have restrained herself from\r\nso giving it. Afraid of him as the bright and delicate little creature\r\nwas, her spirit swelled at the thought of his knowing it from her own\r\nlips.\r\n\r\nBut where was she to go? Anywhere beyond his reach, was no reply to the\r\nquestion. Somewhere must be thought of. She determined to go to her\r\nguardian, and to go immediately. The feeling she had imparted to Helena\r\non the night of their first confidence, was so strong upon her\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nfeeling of not being safe from him, and of the solid walls of the old\r\nconvent being powerless to keep out his ghostly following of her\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nno reasoning of her own could calm her terrors. The fascination of\r\nrepulsion had been upon her so long, and now culminated so darkly, that\r\nshe felt as if he had power to bind her by a spell. Glancing out at\r\nwindow, even now, as she rose to dress, the sight of the sun-dial on\r\nwhich he had leaned when he declared himself, turned her cold, and made\r\nher shrink from it, as though he had invested it with some awful\r\nquality from his own nature.\r\n\r\nShe wrote a hurried note to Miss Twinkleton, saying that she had sudden\r\nreason for wishing to see her guardian promptly, and had gone to him;\r\nalso, entreating the good lady not to be uneasy, for all was well with\r\nher. She hurried a few quite useless articles into a very little bag,\r\nleft the note in a conspicuous place, and went out, softly closing the\r\ngate after her.\r\n\r\nIt was the first time she had ever been even in Cloisterham High Street\r\nalone. But knowing all its ways and windings very well, she hurried\r\nstraight to the corner from which the omnibus departed. It was, at that\r\nvery moment, going off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop and take me, if you please, Joe. I am obliged to go to London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn less than another minute she was on her road to the railway, under\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s protection. Joe waited on her when she got there, put her safely\r\ninto the railway carriage, and handed in the very little bag after her,\r\nas though it were some enormous trunk, hundredweights heavy, which she\r\nmust on no account endeavour to lift.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you go round when you get back, and tell Miss Twinkleton that you\r\nsaw me safely off, Joe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt shall be done, Miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith my love, please, Joe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Miss\xe2\x80\x94and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind having it myself!\xe2\x80\x9d But Joe did not\r\narticulate the last clause; only thought it.\r\n\r\nNow that she was whirling away for London in real earnest, Rosa was at\r\nleisure to resume the thoughts which her personal hurry had checked.\r\nThe indignant thought that his declaration of love soiled her; that she\r\ncould only be cleansed from the stain of its impurity by appealing to\r\nthe honest and true; supported her for a time against her fears, and\r\nconfirmed her in her hasty resolution. But as the evening grew darker\r\nand darker, and the great city impended nearer and nearer, the doubts\r\nusual in such cases began to arise. Whether this was not a wild\r\nproceeding, after all; how Mr. Grewgious might regard it; whether she\r\nshould find him at the journey\xe2\x80\x99s end; how she would act if he were\r\nabsent; what might become of her, alone, in a place so strange and\r\ncrowded; how if she had but waited and taken counsel first; whether, if\r\nshe could now go back, she would not do it thankfully; a multitude of\r\nsuch uneasy speculations disturbed her, more and more as they\r\naccumulated. At length the train came into London over the housetops;\r\nand down below lay the gritty streets with their yet un-needed lamps\r\na-glow, on a hot, light, summer night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHiram Grewgious, Esquire, Staple Inn, London.\xe2\x80\x9d This was all Rosa knew\r\nof her destination; but it was enough to send her rattling away again\r\nin a cab, through deserts of gritty streets, where many people crowded\r\nat the corner of courts and byways to get some air, and where many\r\nother people walked with a miserably monotonous noise of shuffling of\r\nfeet on hot paving-stones, and where all the people and all their\r\nsurroundings were so gritty and so shabby!\r\n\r\nThere was music playing here and there, but it did not enliven the\r\ncase. No barrel-organ mended the matter, and no big drum beat dull care\r\naway. Like the chapel bells that were also going here and there, they\r\nonly seemed to evoke echoes from brick surfaces, and dust from\r\neverything. As to the flat wind-instruments, they seemed to have\r\ncracked their hearts and souls in pining for the country.\r\n\r\nHer jingling conveyance stopped at last at a fast-closed gateway, which\r\nappeared to belong to somebody who had gone to bed very early, and was\r\nmuch afraid of housebreakers; Rosa, discharging her conveyance, timidly\r\nknocked at this gateway, and was let in, very little bag and all, by a\r\nwatchman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Mr. Grewgious live here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious lives there, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said the watchman, pointing further\r\nin.\r\n\r\nSo Rosa went further in, and, when the clocks were striking ten, stood\r\non P. J. T.\xe2\x80\x9ds doorsteps, wondering what P. J. T. had done with his\r\nstreet-door.\r\n\r\nGuided by the painted name of Mr. Grewgious, she went up-stairs and\r\nsoftly tapped and tapped several times. But no one answering, and Mr.\r\nGrewgious\xe2\x80\x99s door-handle yielding to her touch, she went in, and saw her\r\nguardian sitting on a window-seat at an open window, with a shaded lamp\r\nplaced far from him on a table in a corner.\r\n\r\nRosa drew nearer to him in the twilight of the room. He saw her, and he\r\nsaid, in an undertone: \xe2\x80\x9cGood Heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa fell upon his neck, with tears, and then he said, returning her\r\nembrace:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy child, my child! I thought you were your mother!\xe2\x80\x94But what, what,\r\nwhat,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, soothingly, \xe2\x80\x9chas happened? My dear, what has brought\r\nyou here? Who has brought you here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one. I came alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord bless me!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cCame alone! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nwrite to me to come and fetch you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no time. I took a sudden resolution. Poor, poor Eddy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, poor fellow, poor fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis uncle has made love to me. I cannot bear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, at once\r\nwith a burst of tears, and a stamp of her little foot; \xe2\x80\x9cI shudder with\r\nhorror of him, and I have come to you to protect me and all of us from\r\nhim, if you will?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious, with a sudden rush of amazing energy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn him!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Confound his politics!\r\nFrustrate his knavish tricks!\r\nOn Thee his hopes to fix?\r\n        Damn him again!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter this most extraordinary outburst, Mr. Grewgious, quite beside\r\nhimself, plunged about the room, to all appearance undecided whether he\r\nwas in a fit of loyal enthusiasm, or combative denunciation.\r\n\r\nHe stopped and said, wiping his face: \xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, my dear, but\r\nyou will be glad to know I feel better. Tell me no more just now, or I\r\nmight do it again. You must be refreshed and cheered. What did you take\r\nlast? Was it breakfast, lunch, dinner, tea, or supper? And what will\r\nyou take next? Shall it be breakfast, lunch, dinner, tea, or supper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe respectful tenderness with which, on one knee before her, he helped\r\nher to remove her hat, and disentangle her pretty hair from it, was\r\nquite a chivalrous sight. Yet who, knowing him only on the surface,\r\nwould have expected chivalry\xe2\x80\x94and of the true sort, too; not the\r\nspurious\xe2\x80\x94from Mr. Grewgious?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour rest too must be provided for,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on; \xe2\x80\x9cand you shall have\r\nthe prettiest chamber in Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s. Your toilet must be provided for,\r\nand you shall have everything that an unlimited head chambermaid\xe2\x80\x94by\r\nwhich expression I mean a head chambermaid not limited as to outlay\xe2\x80\x94can\r\nprocure. Is that a bag?\xe2\x80\x9d he looked hard at it; sooth to say, it\r\nrequired hard looking at to be seen at all in a dimly lighted room:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand is it your property, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. I brought it with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not an extensive bag,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, candidly, \xe2\x80\x9cthough\r\nadmirably calculated to contain a day\xe2\x80\x99s provision for a canary-bird.\r\nPerhaps you brought a canary-bird?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa smiled and shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you had, he should have been made welcome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand I think he would have been pleased to be hung upon a nail outside\r\nand pit himself against our Staple sparrows; whose execution must be\r\nadmitted to be not quite equal to their intention. Which is the case\r\nwith so many of us! You didn\xe2\x80\x99t say what meal, my dear. Have a nice\r\njumble of all meals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa thanked him, but said she could only take a cup of tea. Mr.\r\nGrewgious, after several times running out, and in again, to mention\r\nsuch supplementary items as marmalade, eggs, watercresses, salted fish,\r\nand frizzled ham, ran across to Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s without his hat, to give his\r\nvarious directions. And soon afterwards they were realised in practice,\r\nand the board was spread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord bless my soul,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious, putting the lamp upon it,\r\nand taking his seat opposite Rosa; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a new sensation for a poor old\r\nAngular bachelor, to be sure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Mr. Grewgious experiences a new sensation]\r\n\r\n\r\nRosa\xe2\x80\x99s expressive little eyebrows asked him what he meant?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sensation of having a sweet young presence in the place, that\r\nwhitewashes it, paints it, papers it, decorates it with gilding, and\r\nmakes it Glorious!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cAh me! Ah me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs there was something mournful in his sigh, Rosa, in touching him with\r\nher tea-cup, ventured to touch him with her small hand too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cAhem! Let\xe2\x80\x99s talk!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you always live here, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd always alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways alone; except that I have daily company in a gentleman by the\r\nname of Bazzard, my clerk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_He_ doesn\xe2\x80\x99t live here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he goes his way, after office hours. In fact, he is off duty here,\r\naltogether, just at present; and a firm down-stairs, with which I have\r\nbusiness relations, lend me a substitute. But it would be extremely\r\ndifficult to replace Mr. Bazzard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must be very fond of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe bears up against it with commendable fortitude if he is,\xe2\x80\x9d returned\r\nMr. Grewgious, after considering the matter. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I doubt if he is. Not\r\nparticularly so. You see, he is discontented, poor fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy isn\xe2\x80\x99t he contented?\xe2\x80\x9d was the natural inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMisplaced,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, with great mystery.\r\n\r\nRosa\xe2\x80\x99s eyebrows resumed their inquisitive and perplexed expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo misplaced,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Grewgious went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I feel constantly\r\napologetic towards him. And he feels (though he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mention it)\r\nthat I have reason to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious had by this time grown so very mysterious, that Rosa did\r\nnot know how to go on. While she was thinking about it Mr. Grewgious\r\nsuddenly jerked out of himself for the second time:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s talk. We were speaking of Mr. Bazzard. It\xe2\x80\x99s a secret, and\r\nmoreover it is Mr. Bazzard\xe2\x80\x99s secret; but the sweet presence at my table\r\nmakes me so unusually expansive, that I feel I must impart it in\r\ninviolable confidence. What do you think Mr. Bazzard has done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO dear!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa, drawing her chair a little nearer, and her mind\r\nreverting to Jasper, \xe2\x80\x9cnothing dreadful, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has written a play,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, in a solemn whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\ntragedy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa seemed much relieved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd nobody,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mr. Grewgious in the same tone, \xe2\x80\x9cwill hear, on\r\nany account whatever, of bringing it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa looked reflective, and nodded her head slowly; as who should say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch things are, and why are they!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ couldn\xe2\x80\x99t write a play.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bad one, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, innocently, with her eyebrows again in\r\naction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. If I was under sentence of decapitation, and was about to be\r\ninstantly decapitated, and an express arrived with a pardon for the\r\ncondemned convict Grewgious if he wrote a play, I should be under the\r\nnecessity of resuming the block, and begging the executioner to proceed\r\nto extremities,\xe2\x80\x94meaning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, passing his hand under\r\nhis chin, \xe2\x80\x9cthe singular number, and this extremity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa appeared to consider what she would do if the awkward\r\nsupposititious case were hers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsequently,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Bazzard would have a sense of\r\nmy inferiority to himself under any circumstances; but when I am his\r\nmaster, you know, the case is greatly aggravated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious shook his head seriously, as if he felt the offence to be\r\na little too much, though of his own committing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow came you to be his master, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA question that naturally follows,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s talk.\r\nMr. Bazzard\xe2\x80\x99s father, being a Norfolk farmer, would have furiously laid\r\nabout him with a flail, a pitch-fork, and every agricultural implement\r\navailable for assaulting purposes, on the slightest hint of his son\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhaving written a play. So the son, bringing to me the father\xe2\x80\x99s rent\r\n(which I receive), imparted his secret, and pointed out that he was\r\ndetermined to pursue his genius, and that it would put him in peril of\r\nstarvation, and that he was not formed for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor pursuing his genius, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cfor starvation. It was impossible\r\nto deny the position, that Mr. Bazzard was not formed to be starved,\r\nand Mr. Bazzard then pointed out that it was desirable that I should\r\nstand between him and a fate so perfectly unsuited to his formation. In\r\nthat way Mr. Bazzard became my clerk, and he feels it very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad he is grateful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite mean that, my dear. I mean, that he feels the\r\ndegradation. There are some other geniuses that Mr. Bazzard has become\r\nacquainted with, who have also written tragedies, which likewise nobody\r\nwill on any account whatever hear of bringing out, and these choice\r\nspirits dedicate their plays to one another in a highly panegyrical\r\nmanner. Mr. Bazzard has been the subject of one of these dedications.\r\nNow, you know, I never had a play dedicated to _me_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa looked at him as if she would have liked him to be the recipient\r\nof a thousand dedications.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich again, naturally, rubs against the grain of Mr. Bazzard,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is very short with me sometimes, and then I feel\r\nthat he is meditating, \xe2\x80\x98This blockhead is my master! A fellow who\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t write a tragedy on pain of death, and who will never have one\r\ndedicated to him with the most complimentary congratulations on the\r\nhigh position he has taken in the eyes of posterity!\xe2\x80\x99 Very trying, very\r\ntrying. However, in giving him directions, I reflect beforehand:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps he may not like this,\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98He might take it ill if I asked\r\nthat;\xe2\x80\x99 and so we get on very well. Indeed, better than I could have\r\nexpected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the tragedy named, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrictly between ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cit has a\r\ndreadfully appropriate name. It is called The Thorn of Anxiety. But Mr.\r\nBazzard hopes\xe2\x80\x94and I hope\xe2\x80\x94that it will come out at last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not hard to divine that Mr. Grewgious had related the Bazzard\r\nhistory thus fully, at least quite as much for the recreation of his\r\nward\xe2\x80\x99s mind from the subject that had driven her there, as for the\r\ngratification of his own tendency to be social and communicative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at this point, \xe2\x80\x9cif you are not too tired to\r\ntell me more of what passed to-day\xe2\x80\x94but only if you feel quite able\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nshould be glad to hear it. I may digest it the better, if I sleep on it\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa, composed now, gave him a faithful account of the interview. Mr.\r\nGrewgious often smoothed his head while it was in progress, and begged\r\nto be told a second time those parts which bore on Helena and Neville.\r\nWhen Rosa had finished, he sat grave, silent, and meditative for a\r\nwhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClearly narrated,\xe2\x80\x9d was his only remark at last, \xe2\x80\x9cand, I hope, clearly\r\nput away here,\xe2\x80\x9d smoothing his head again. \xe2\x80\x9cSee, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d taking her to\r\nthe open window, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere they live! The dark windows over yonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may go to Helena to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to sleep on that question to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered\r\ndoubtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cBut let me take you to your own rest, for you must need\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith that Mr. Grewgious helped her to get her hat on again, and hung\r\nupon his arm the very little bag that was of no earthly use, and led\r\nher by the hand (with a certain stately awkwardness, as if he were\r\ngoing to walk a minuet) across Holborn, and into Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s Inn. At the\r\nhotel door, he confided her to the Unlimited head chambermaid, and said\r\nthat while she went up to see her room, he would remain below, in case\r\nshe should wish it exchanged for another, or should find that there was\r\nanything she wanted.\r\n\r\nRosa\xe2\x80\x99s room was airy, clean, comfortable, almost gay. The Unlimited had\r\nlaid in everything omitted from the very little bag (that is to say,\r\neverything she could possibly need), and Rosa tripped down the great\r\nmany stairs again, to thank her guardian for his thoughtful and\r\naffectionate care of her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, infinitely gratified; \xe2\x80\x9cit is\r\nI who thank you for your charming confidence and for your charming\r\ncompany. Your breakfast will be provided for you in a neat, compact,\r\nand graceful little sitting-room (appropriate to your figure), and I\r\nwill come to you at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning. I hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t feel\r\nvery strange indeed, in this strange place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, I feel so safe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you may be sure that the stairs are fire-proof,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nGrewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cand that any outbreak of the devouring element would be\r\nperceived and suppressed by the watchmen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not mean that,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean, I feel so safe from him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a stout gate of iron bars to keep him out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nGrewgious, smiling; \xe2\x80\x9cand Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s is fire-proof, and specially\r\nwatched and lighted, and _I_ live over the way!\xe2\x80\x9d In the stoutness of\r\nhis knight-errantry, he seemed to think the last-named protection all\r\nsufficient. In the same spirit he said to the gate-porter as he went\r\nout, \xe2\x80\x9cIf some one staying in the hotel should wish to send across the\r\nroad to me in the night, a crown will be ready for the messenger.\xe2\x80\x9d In\r\nthe same spirit, he walked up and down outside the iron gate for the\r\nbest part of an hour, with some solicitude; occasionally looking in\r\nbetween the bars, as if he had laid a dove in a high roost in a cage of\r\nlions, and had it on his mind that she might tumble out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\nA RECOGNITION\r\n\r\n\r\nNothing occurred in the night to flutter the tired dove; and the dove\r\narose refreshed. With Mr. Grewgious, when the clock struck ten in the\r\nmorning, came Mr. Crisparkle, who had come at one plunge out of the\r\nriver at Cloisterham.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Twinkleton was so uneasy, Miss Rosa,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained to her, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\ncame round to Ma and me with your note, in such a state of wonder,\r\nthat, to quiet her, I volunteered on this service by the very first\r\ntrain to be caught in the morning. I wished at the time that you had\r\ncome to me; but now I think it best that you did _as_ you did, and came\r\nto your guardian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did think of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa told him; \xe2\x80\x9cbut Minor Canon Corner was so\r\nnear him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand. It was quite natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have told Mr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9call that you told me\r\nlast night, my dear. Of course I should have written it to him\r\nimmediately; but his coming was most opportune. And it was particularly\r\nkind of him to come, for he had but just gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you settled,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa, appealing to them both, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is to be\r\ndone for Helena and her brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cI am in great perplexity. If even\r\nMr. Grewgious, whose head is much longer than mine, and who is a whole\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x99s cogitation in advance of me, is undecided, what must I be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Unlimited here put her head in at the door\xe2\x80\x94after having rapped, and\r\nbeen authorised to present herself\xe2\x80\x94announcing that a gentleman wished\r\nfor a word with another gentleman named Crisparkle, if any such\r\ngentleman were there. If no such gentleman were there, he begged pardon\r\nfor being mistaken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch a gentleman is here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cbut is engaged just\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it a dark gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Rosa, retreating on her guardian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Miss, more of a brown gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure not with black hair?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa, taking courage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite sure of that, Miss. Brown hair and blue eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d hinted Mr. Grewgious, with habitual caution, \xe2\x80\x9cit might be\r\nwell to see him, reverend sir, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t object. When one is in a\r\ndifficulty or at a loss, one never knows in what direction a way out\r\nmay chance to open. It is a business principle of mine, in such a case,\r\nnot to close up any direction, but to keep an eye on every direction\r\nthat may present itself. I could relate an anecdote in point, but that\r\nit would be premature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Miss Rosa will allow me, then? Let the gentleman come in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nCrisparkle.\r\n\r\nThe gentleman came in; apologised, with a frank but modest grace, for\r\nnot finding Mr. Crisparkle alone; turned to Mr. Crisparkle, and\r\nsmilingly asked the unexpected question: \xe2\x80\x9cWho am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are the gentleman I saw smoking under the trees in Staple Inn, a\r\nfew minutes ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue. There I saw you. Who else am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle concentrated his attention on a handsome face, much\r\nsunburnt; and the ghost of some departed boy seemed to rise, gradually\r\nand dimly, in the room.\r\n\r\nThe gentleman saw a struggling recollection lighten up the Minor\r\nCanon\xe2\x80\x99s features, and smiling again, said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you have for\r\nbreakfast this morning? You are out of jam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a moment!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Crisparkle, raising his right hand. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me\r\nanother instant! Tartar!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two shook hands with the greatest heartiness, and then went the\r\nwonderful length\xe2\x80\x94for Englishmen\xe2\x80\x94of laying their hands each on the\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders, and looking joyfully each into the other\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy old fag!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy old master!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tartar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saved me from drowning!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter which you took to swimming, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tartar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless my soul!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmen!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tartar.\r\n\r\nAnd then they fell to shaking hands most heartily again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImagine,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Crisparkle, with glistening eyes: \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Rosa\r\nBud and Mr. Grewgious, imagine Mr. Tartar, when he was the smallest of\r\njuniors, diving for me, catching me, a big heavy senior, by the hair of\r\nthe head, and striking out for the shore with me like a water-giant!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImagine my not letting him sink, as I was his fag!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Tartar.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the truth being that he was my best protector and friend, and did\r\nme more good than all the masters put together, an irrational impulse\r\nseized me to pick him up, or go down with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHem! Permit me, sir, to have the honour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\nadvancing with extended hand, \xe2\x80\x9cfor an honour I truly esteem it. I am\r\nproud to make your acquaintance. I hope you didn\xe2\x80\x99t take cold. I hope\r\nyou were not inconvenienced by swallowing too much water. How have you\r\nbeen since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was by no means apparent that Mr. Grewgious knew what he said,\r\nthough it was very apparent that he meant to say something highly\r\nfriendly and appreciative.\r\n\r\nIf Heaven, Rosa thought, had but sent such courage and skill to her\r\npoor mother\xe2\x80\x99s aid! And he to have been so slight and young then!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to be complimented upon it, I thank you; but I think I\r\nhave an idea,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Grewgious announced, after taking a jog-trot or two\r\nacross the room, so unexpected and unaccountable that they all stared\r\nat him, doubtful whether he was choking or had the cramp\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI _think_ I\r\nhave an idea. I believe I have had the pleasure of seeing Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nname as tenant of the top set in the house next the top set in the\r\ncorner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Tartar. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are right so far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am right so far,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cTick that off;\xe2\x80\x9d which he did,\r\nwith his right thumb on his left. \xe2\x80\x9cMight you happen to know the name of\r\nyour neighbour in the top set on the other side of the party-wall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncoming very close to Mr. Tartar, to lose nothing of his face, in his\r\nshortness of sight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLandless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTick that off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, taking another trot, and then\r\ncoming back. \xe2\x80\x9cNo personal knowledge, I suppose, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSlight, but some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTick that off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, taking another trot, and again\r\ncoming back. \xe2\x80\x9cNature of knowledge, Mr. Tartar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought he seemed to be a young fellow in a poor way, and I asked\r\nhis leave\xe2\x80\x94only within a day or so\xe2\x80\x94to share my flowers up there with\r\nhim; that is to say, to extend my flower-garden to his windows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you have the kindness to take seats?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\n_have_ an idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey complied; Mr. Tartar none the less readily, for being all abroad;\r\nand Mr. Grewgious, seated in the centre, with his hands upon his knees,\r\nthus stated his idea, with his usual manner of having got the statement\r\nby heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot as yet make up my mind whether it is prudent to hold open\r\ncommunication under present circumstances, and on the part of the fair\r\nmember of the present company, with Mr. Neville or Miss Helena. I have\r\nreason to know that a local friend of ours (on whom I beg to bestow a\r\npassing but a hearty malediction, with the kind permission of my\r\nreverend friend) sneaks to and fro, and dodges up and down. When not\r\ndoing so himself, he may have some informant skulking about, in the\r\nperson of a watchman, porter, or such-like hanger-on of Staple. On the\r\nother hand, Miss Rosa very naturally wishes to see her friend Miss\r\nHelena, and it would seem important that at least Miss Helena (if not\r\nher brother too, through her) should privately know from Miss Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlips what has occurred, and what has been threatened. Am I agreed with\r\ngenerally in the views I take?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI entirely coincide with them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, who had been very\r\nattentive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I have no doubt I should,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mr. Tartar, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cif I\r\nunderstood them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFair and softly, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious; \xe2\x80\x9cwe shall fully confide in\r\nyou directly, if you will favour us with your permission. Now, if our\r\nlocal friend should have any informant on the spot, it is tolerably\r\nclear that such informant can only be set to watch the chambers in the\r\noccupation of Mr. Neville. He reporting, to our local friend, who comes\r\nand goes there, our local friend would supply for himself, from his own\r\nprevious knowledge, the identity of the parties. Nobody can be set to\r\nwatch all Staple, or to concern himself with comers and goers to other\r\nsets of chambers: unless, indeed, mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI begin to understand to what you tend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Crisparkle, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nhighly approve of your caution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI needn\xe2\x80\x99t repeat that I know nothing yet of the why and wherefore,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Tartar; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I also understand to what you tend, so let me say\r\nat once that my chambers are freely at your disposal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Grewgious, smoothing his head triumphantly, \xe2\x80\x9cnow we\r\nhave all got the idea. You have it, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I have,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, blushing a little as Mr. Tartar looked\r\nquickly towards her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, you go over to Staple with Mr. Crisparkle and Mr. Tartar,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mr. Grewgious; \xe2\x80\x9cI going in and out, and out and in alone, in my\r\nusual way; you go up with those gentlemen to Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s rooms; you\r\nlook into Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s flower-garden; you wait for Miss Helena\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nappearance there, or you signify to Miss Helena that you are close by;\r\nand you communicate with her freely, and no spy can be the wiser.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much afraid I shall be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe what, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Grewgious, as she hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cNot\r\nfrightened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, shyly; \xe2\x80\x9cin Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s way. We seem to be\r\nappropriating Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s residence so very coolly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI protest to you,\xe2\x80\x9d returned that gentleman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I shall think the\r\nbetter of it for evermore, if your voice sounds in it only once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa, not quite knowing what to say about that, cast down her eyes, and\r\nturning to Mr. Grewgious, dutifully asked if she should put her hat on?\r\nMr. Grewgious being of opinion that she could not do better, she\r\nwithdrew for the purpose. Mr. Crisparkle took the opportunity of giving\r\nMr. Tartar a summary of the distresses of Neville and his sister; the\r\nopportunity was quite long enough, as the hat happened to require a\r\nlittle extra fitting on.\r\n\r\nMr. Tartar gave his arm to Rosa, and Mr. Crisparkle walked, detached,\r\nin front.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor, poor Eddy!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rosa, as they went along.\r\n\r\nMr. Tartar waved his right hand as he bent his head down over Rosa,\r\ntalking in an animated way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not so powerful or so sun-browned when it saved Mr.\r\nCrisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Rosa, glancing at it; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it must have been very\r\nsteady and determined even then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Tartar told her he had been a sailor, roving everywhere for years\r\nand years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen are you going to sea again?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa wondered what the girls would say if they could see her crossing\r\nthe wide street on the sailor\xe2\x80\x99s arm. And she fancied that the\r\npassers-by must think her very little and very helpless, contrasted\r\nwith the strong figure that could have caught her up and carried her\r\nout of any danger, miles and miles without resting.\r\n\r\nShe was thinking further, that his far-seeing blue eyes looked as if\r\nthey had been used to watch danger afar off, and to watch it without\r\nflinching, drawing nearer and nearer: when, happening to raise her own\r\neyes, she found that he seemed to be thinking something about _them_.\r\n\r\nThis a little confused Rosebud, and may account for her never\r\nafterwards quite knowing how she ascended (with his help) to his garden\r\nin the air, and seemed to get into a marvellous country that came into\r\nsudden bloom like the country on the summit of the magic bean-stalk.\r\nMay it flourish for ever!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\nA GRITTY STATE OF THINGS COMES ON\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s chambers were the neatest, the cleanest, and the\r\nbest-ordered chambers ever seen under the sun, moon, and stars. The\r\nfloors were scrubbed to that extent, that you might have supposed the\r\nLondon blacks emancipated for ever, and gone out of the land for good.\r\nEvery inch of brass-work in Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s possession was polished and\r\nburnished, till it shone like a brazen mirror. No speck, nor spot, nor\r\nspatter soiled the purity of any of Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s household gods, large,\r\nsmall, or middle-sized. His sitting-room was like the admiral\xe2\x80\x99s cabin,\r\nhis bath-room was like a dairy, his sleeping-chamber, fitted all about\r\nwith lockers and drawers, was like a seedsman\xe2\x80\x99s shop; and his\r\nnicely-balanced cot just stirred in the midst, as if it breathed.\r\nEverything belonging to Mr. Tartar had quarters of its own assigned to\r\nit: his maps and charts had their quarters; his books had theirs; his\r\nbrushes had theirs; his boots had theirs; his clothes had theirs; his\r\ncase-bottles had theirs; his telescopes and other instruments had\r\ntheirs. Everything was readily accessible. Shelf, bracket, locker,\r\nhook, and drawer were equally within reach, and were equally contrived\r\nwith a view to avoiding waste of room, and providing some snug inches\r\nof stowage for something that would have exactly fitted nowhere else.\r\nHis gleaming little service of plate was so arranged upon his sideboard\r\nas that a slack salt-spoon would have instantly betrayed itself; his\r\ntoilet implements were so arranged upon his dressing-table as that a\r\ntoothpick of slovenly deportment could have been reported at a glance.\r\nSo with the curiosities he had brought home from various voyages.\r\nStuffed, dried, repolished, or otherwise preserved, according to their\r\nkind; birds, fishes, reptiles, arms, articles of dress, shells,\r\nseaweeds, grasses, or memorials of coral reef; each was displayed in\r\nits especial place, and each could have been displayed in no better\r\nplace. Paint and varnish seemed to be kept somewhere out of sight, in\r\nconstant readiness to obliterate stray finger-marks wherever any might\r\nbecome perceptible in Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s chambers. No man-of-war was ever\r\nkept more spick and span from careless touch. On this bright summer\r\nday, a neat awning was rigged over Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s flower-garden as only a\r\nsailor can rig it, and there was a sea-going air upon the whole effect,\r\nso delightfully complete, that the flower-garden might have appertained\r\nto stern-windows afloat, and the whole concern might have bowled away\r\ngallantly with all on board, if Mr. Tartar had only clapped to his lips\r\nthe speaking-trumpet that was slung in a corner, and given hoarse\r\norders to heave the anchor up, look alive there, men, and get all sail\r\nupon her!\r\n\r\nMr. Tartar doing the honours of this gallant craft was of a piece with\r\nthe rest. When a man rides an amiable hobby that shies at nothing and\r\nkicks nobody, it is only agreeable to find him riding it with a\r\nhumorous sense of the droll side of the creature. When the man is a\r\ncordial and an earnest man by nature, and withal is perfectly fresh and\r\ngenuine, it may be doubted whether he is ever seen to greater advantage\r\nthan at such a time. So Rosa would have naturally thought (even if she\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t been conducted over the ship with all the homage due to the\r\nFirst Lady of the Admiralty, or First Fairy of the Sea), that it was\r\ncharming to see and hear Mr. Tartar half laughing at, and half\r\nrejoicing in, his various contrivances. So Rosa would have naturally\r\nthought, anyhow, that the sunburnt sailor showed to great advantage\r\nwhen, the inspection finished, he delicately withdrew out of his\r\nadmiral\xe2\x80\x99s cabin, beseeching her to consider herself its Queen, and\r\nwaving her free of his flower-garden with the hand that had had Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s life in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelena! Helena Landless! Are you there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho speaks to me? Not Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d Then a second handsome face appearing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my darling!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, how did you come here, dearest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa with a blush; \xe2\x80\x9cunless I am dreaming!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhy with a blush? For their two faces were alone with the other\r\nflowers. Are blushes among the fruits of the country of the magic\r\nbean-stalk?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ am not dreaming,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI should take more for\r\ngranted if I were. How do we come together\xe2\x80\x94or so near together\xe2\x80\x94so very\r\nunexpectedly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnexpectedly indeed, among the dingy gables and chimney-pots of P. J.\r\nT.\xe2\x80\x9ds connection, and the flowers that had sprung from the salt sea. But\r\nRosa, waking, told in a hurry how they came to be together, and all the\r\nwhy and wherefore of that matter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mr. Crisparkle is here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, in rapid conclusion; \xe2\x80\x9cand,\r\ncould you believe it? long ago he saved his life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could believe any such thing of Mr. Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Helena,\r\nwith a mantling face.\r\n\r\n(More blushes in the bean-stalk country!)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Crisparkle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, quickly putting in the\r\ncorrection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand, love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was very nice of Mr. Crisparkle to be saved,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, \xe2\x80\x9cand he\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have shown his high opinion of Mr. Tartar more expressively.\r\nBut it was Mr. Tartar who saved him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelena\xe2\x80\x99s dark eyes looked very earnestly at the bright face among the\r\nleaves, and she asked, in a slower and more thoughtful tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Mr. Tartar with you now, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; because he has given up his rooms to me\xe2\x80\x94to us, I mean. It is such\r\na beautiful place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is like the inside of the most exquisite ship that ever sailed. It\r\nis like\xe2\x80\x94it is like\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike a dream?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Helena.\r\n\r\nRosa answered with a little nod, and smelled the flowers.\r\n\r\nHelena resumed, after a short pause of silence, during which she seemed\r\n(or it was Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s fancy) to compassionate somebody: \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor Neville is\r\nreading in his own room, the sun being so very bright on this side just\r\nnow. I think he had better not know that you are so near.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, I think so too!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa very readily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Helena, doubtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he must know by-and-by\r\nall you have told me; but I am not sure. Ask Mr. Crisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s advice,\r\nmy darling. Ask him whether I may tell Neville as much or as little of\r\nwhat you have told me as I think best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa subsided into her state-cabin, and propounded the question. The\r\nMinor Canon was for the free exercise of Helena\xe2\x80\x99s judgment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank him very much,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, when Rosa emerged again with her\r\nreport. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk him whether it would be best to wait until any more\r\nmaligning and pursuing of Neville on the part of this wretch shall\r\ndisclose itself, or to try to anticipate it: I mean, so far as to find\r\nout whether any such goes on darkly about us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Minor Canon found this point so difficult to give a confident\r\nopinion on, that, after two or three attempts and failures, he\r\nsuggested a reference to Mr. Grewgious. Helena acquiescing, he betook\r\nhimself (with a most unsuccessful assumption of lounging indifference)\r\nacross the quadrangle to P. J. T.\xe2\x80\x9ds, and stated it. Mr. Grewgious held\r\ndecidedly to the general principle, that if you could steal a march\r\nupon a brigand or a wild beast, you had better do it; and he also held\r\ndecidedly to the special case, that John Jasper was a brigand and a\r\nwild beast in combination.\r\n\r\nThus advised, Mr. Crisparkle came back again and reported to Rosa, who\r\nin her turn reported to Helena. She now steadily pursuing her train of\r\nthought at her window, considered thereupon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe may count on Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s readiness to help us, Rosa?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\nO yes! Rosa shyly thought so. O yes, Rosa shyly believed she could\r\nalmost answer for it. But should she ask Mr. Crisparkle? \xe2\x80\x9cI think your\r\nauthority on the point as good as his, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, sedately,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you needn\xe2\x80\x99t disappear again for that.\xe2\x80\x9d Odd of Helena!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Neville,\xe2\x80\x9d Helena pursued after more reflection, \xe2\x80\x9cknows no one\r\nelse here: he has not so much as exchanged a word with any one else\r\nhere. If Mr. Tartar would call to see him openly and often; if he would\r\nspare a minute for the purpose, frequently; if he would even do so,\r\nalmost daily; something might come of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething might come of it, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Rosa, surveying her\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s beauty with a highly perplexed face. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething might?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Neville\xe2\x80\x99s movements are really watched, and if the purpose really\r\nis to isolate him from all friends and acquaintance and wear his daily\r\nlife out grain by grain (which would seem to be the threat to you),\r\ndoes it not appear likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, \xe2\x80\x9cthat his enemy would in some\r\nway communicate with Mr. Tartar to warn him off from Neville? In which\r\ncase, we might not only know the fact, but might know from Mr. Tartar\r\nwhat the terms of the communication were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rosa. And immediately darted into her state-cabin again.\r\n\r\nPresently her pretty face reappeared, with a greatly heightened colour,\r\nand she said that she had told Mr. Crisparkle, and that Mr. Crisparkle\r\nhad fetched in Mr. Tartar, and that Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwho is waiting now, in\r\ncase you want him,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rosa, with a half look back, and in not a\r\nlittle confusion between the inside of the state-cabin and out\xe2\x80\x94had\r\ndeclared his readiness to act as she had suggested, and to enter on his\r\ntask that very day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank him from my heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena. \xe2\x80\x9cPray tell him so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain not a little confused between the Flower-garden and the Cabin,\r\nRosa dipped in with her message, and dipped out again with more\r\nassurances from Mr. Tartar, and stood wavering in a divided state\r\nbetween Helena and him, which proved that confusion is not always\r\nnecessarily awkward, but may sometimes present a very pleasant\r\nappearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, darling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Helena, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will be mindful of the caution\r\nthat has restricted us to this interview for the present, and will\r\npart. I hear Neville moving too. Are you going back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, I could never go there any more. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t indeed, after that\r\ndreadful interview!\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen where _are_ you going, pretty one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I come to think of it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa. \xe2\x80\x9cI have settled\r\nnothing at all yet, but my guardian will take care of me. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nuneasy, dear. I shall be sure to be somewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(It did seem likely.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I shall hear of my Rosebud from Mr. Tartar?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Helena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so; from\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa looked back again in a flutter, instead\r\nof supplying the name. \xe2\x80\x9cBut tell me one thing before we part, dearest\r\nHelena. Tell me\xe2\x80\x94that you are sure, sure, sure, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelp it, love?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelp making him malicious and revengeful. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hold any terms\r\nwith him, could I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know how I love you, darling,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Helena, with indignation;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I would sooner see you dead at his wicked feet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a great comfort to me! And you will tell your poor brother so,\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t you? And you will give him my remembrance and my sympathy? And\r\nyou will ask him not to hate me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a mournful shake of the head, as if that would be quite a\r\nsuperfluous entreaty, Helena lovingly kissed her two hands to her\r\nfriend, and her friend\xe2\x80\x99s two hands were kissed to her; and then she saw\r\na third hand (a brown one) appear among the flowers and leaves, and\r\nhelp her friend out of sight.\r\n\r\nThe refection that Mr. Tartar produced in the Admiral\xe2\x80\x99s Cabin by merely\r\ntouching the spring knob of a locker and the handle of a drawer, was a\r\ndazzling enchanted repast. Wonderful macaroons, glittering liqueurs,\r\nmagically-preserved tropical spices, and jellies of celestial tropical\r\nfruits, displayed themselves profusely at an instant\xe2\x80\x99s notice. But Mr.\r\nTartar could not make time stand still; and time, with his hard-hearted\r\nfleetness, strode on so fast, that Rosa was obliged to come down from\r\nthe bean-stalk country to earth and her guardian\xe2\x80\x99s chambers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is to be done next? To\r\nput the same thought in another form; what is to be done with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa could only look apologetically sensible of being very much in her\r\nown way and in everybody else\xe2\x80\x99s. Some passing idea of living,\r\nfireproof, up a good many stairs in Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s Inn for the rest of her\r\nlife, was the only thing in the nature of a plan that occurred to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has come into my thoughts,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cthat as the\r\nrespected lady, Miss Twinkleton, occasionally repairs to London in the\r\nrecess, with the view of extending her connection, and being available\r\nfor interviews with metropolitan parents, if any\xe2\x80\x94whether, until we have\r\ntime in which to turn ourselves round, we might invite Miss Twinkleton\r\nto come and stay with you for a month?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay where, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhether,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cwe might take a furnished lodging\r\nin town for a month, and invite Miss Twinkleton to assume the charge of\r\nyou in it for that period?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd afterwards?\xe2\x80\x9d hinted Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd afterwards,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cwe should be no worse off than\r\nwe are now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that might smooth the way,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen let us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, rising, \xe2\x80\x9cgo and look for a furnished\r\nlodging. Nothing could be more acceptable to me than the sweet presence\r\nof last evening, for all the remaining evenings of my existence; but\r\nthese are not fit surroundings for a young lady. Let us set out in\r\nquest of adventures, and look for a furnished lodging. In the meantime,\r\nMr. Crisparkle here, about to return home immediately, will no doubt\r\nkindly see Miss Twinkleton, and invite that lady to co-operate in our\r\nplan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Crisparkle, willingly accepting the commission, took his departure;\r\nMr. Grewgious and his ward set forth on their expedition.\r\n\r\nAs Mr. Grewgious\xe2\x80\x99s idea of looking at a furnished lodging was to get on\r\nthe opposite side of the street to a house with a suitable bill in the\r\nwindow, and stare at it; and then work his way tortuously to the back\r\nof the house, and stare at that; and then not go in, but make similar\r\ntrials of another house, with the same result; their progress was but\r\nslow. At length he bethought himself of a widowed cousin, divers times\r\nremoved, of Mr. Bazzard\xe2\x80\x99s, who had once solicited his influence in the\r\nlodger world, and who lived in Southampton Street, Bloomsbury Square.\r\nThis lady\xe2\x80\x99s name, stated in uncompromising capitals of considerable\r\nsize on a brass door-plate, and yet not lucidly as to sex or condition,\r\nwas BILLICKIN.\r\n\r\nPersonal faintness, and an overpowering personal candour, were the\r\ndistinguishing features of Mrs. Billickin\xe2\x80\x99s organisation. She came\r\nlanguishing out of her own exclusive back parlour, with the air of\r\nhaving been expressly brought-to for the purpose, from an accumulation\r\nof several swoons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I see you well, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, recognising her\r\nvisitor with a bend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, quite well. And you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Grewgious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am as well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, becoming aspirational with excess\r\nof faintness, \xe2\x80\x9cas I hever ham.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy ward and an elderly lady,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, \xe2\x80\x9cwish to find a\r\ngenteel lodging for a month or so. Have you any apartments available,\r\nma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cI will not deceive you; far\r\nfrom it.
