I _have_ apartments available.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis with the air of adding: \xe2\x80\x9cConvey me to the stake, if you will; but\r\nwhile I live, I will be candid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, what apartments, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Grewgious, cosily. To tame\r\na certain severity apparent on the part of Mrs. Billickin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is this sitting-room\xe2\x80\x94which, call it what you will, it is the\r\nfront parlour, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, impressing Rosa into the\r\nconversation: \xe2\x80\x9cthe back parlour being what I cling to and never part\r\nwith; and there is two bedrooms at the top of the \xe2\x80\x99ouse with gas laid\r\non. I do not tell you that your bedroom floors is firm, for firm they\r\nare not. The gas-fitter himself allowed, that to make a firm job, he\r\nmust go right under your jistes, and it were not worth the outlay as a\r\nyearly tenant so to do. The piping is carried above your jistes, and it\r\nis best that it should be made known to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious and Rosa exchanged looks of some dismay, though they had\r\nnot the least idea what latent horrors this carriage of the piping\r\nmight involve. Mrs. Billickin put her hand to her heart, as having\r\neased it of a load.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell! The roof is all right, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, plucking\r\nup a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cif I was to tell you, sir,\r\nthat to have nothink above you is to have a floor above you, I should\r\nput a deception upon you which I will not do. No, sir. Your slates WILL\r\nrattle loose at that elewation in windy weather, do your utmost, best\r\nor worst! I defy you, sir, be you what you may, to keep your slates\r\ntight, try how you can.\xe2\x80\x9d Here Mrs. Billickin, having been warm with Mr.\r\nGrewgious, cooled a little, not to abuse the moral power she held over\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cConsequent,\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded Mrs. Billickin, more mildly, but still\r\nfirmly in her incorruptible candour: \xe2\x80\x9cconsequent it would be worse than\r\nof no use for me to trapse and travel up to the top of the \xe2\x80\x99ouse with\r\nyou, and for you to say, \xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Billickin, what stain do I notice in the\r\nceiling, for a stain I do consider it?\xe2\x80\x99 and for me to answer, \xe2\x80\x98I do not\r\nunderstand you, sir.\xe2\x80\x99 No, sir, I will not be so underhand. I _do_\r\nunderstand you before you pint it out. It is the wet, sir. It do come\r\nin, and it do not come in. You may lay dry there half your lifetime;\r\nbut the time will come, and it is best that you should know it, when a\r\ndripping sop would be no name for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious looked much disgraced by being prefigured in this pickle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you any other apartments, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Billickin, with much solemnity, \xe2\x80\x9cI have.\r\nYou ask me have I, and my open and my honest answer air, I have. The\r\nfirst and second floors is wacant, and sweet rooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come! There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing against _them_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\ncomforting himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cpardon me, there is the\r\nstairs. Unless your mind is prepared for the stairs, it will lead to\r\ninevitable disappointment. You cannot, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin,\r\naddressing Rosa reproachfully, \xe2\x80\x9cplace a first floor, and far less a\r\nsecond, on the level footing \xe2\x80\x9cof a parlour. No, you cannot do it, Miss,\r\nit is beyond your power, and wherefore try?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Billickin put it very feelingly, as if Rosa had shown a headstrong\r\ndetermination to hold the untenable position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan we see these rooms, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired her guardian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can. I will not disguise\r\nit from you, sir; you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Billickin then sent into her back parlour for her shawl (it being\r\na state fiction, dating from immemorial antiquity, that she could never\r\ngo anywhere without being wrapped up), and having been enrolled by her\r\nattendant, led the way. She made various genteel pauses on the stairs\r\nfor breath, and clutched at her heart in the drawing-room as if it had\r\nvery nearly got loose, and she had caught it in the act of taking wing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the second floor?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious, on finding the first\r\nsatisfactory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mrs. Billickin, turning upon him with\r\nceremony, as if the time had now come when a distinct understanding on\r\na difficult point must be arrived at, and a solemn confidence\r\nestablished, \xe2\x80\x9cthe second floor is over this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan we see that too, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cit is open as the day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat also proving satisfactory, Mr. Grewgious retired into a window\r\nwith Rosa for a few words of consultation, and then asking for pen and\r\nink, sketched out a line or two of agreement. In the meantime Mrs.\r\nBillickin took a seat, and delivered a kind of Index to, or Abstract\r\nof, the general question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive-and-forty shillings per week by the month certain at the time of\r\nyear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cis only reasonable to both parties. It is\r\nnot Bond Street nor yet St. James\xe2\x80\x99s Palace; but it is not pretended\r\nthat it is. Neither is it attempted to be denied\xe2\x80\x94for why should\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x94that the Arching leads to a mews. Mewses must exist. Respecting\r\nattendance; two is kep\xe2\x80\x99, at liberal wages. Words _has_ arisen as to\r\ntradesmen, but dirty shoes on fresh hearth-stoning was attributable,\r\nand no wish for a commission on your orders. Coals is either _by_ the\r\nfire, or _per_ the scuttle.\xe2\x80\x9d She emphasised the prepositions as marking\r\na subtle but immense difference. \xe2\x80\x9cDogs is not viewed with favour.\r\nBesides litter, they gets stole, and sharing suspicions is apt to creep\r\nin, and unpleasantness takes place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time Mr. Grewgious had his agreement-lines, and his\r\nearnest-money, ready. \xe2\x80\x9cI have signed it for the ladies, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cand you\xe2\x80\x99ll have the goodness to sign it for yourself, Christian\r\nand Surname, there, if you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Grewgious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin in a new burst of candour, \xe2\x80\x9cno,\r\nsir! You must excuse the Christian name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious stared at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe door-plate is used as a protection,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nacts as such, and go from it I will not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Grewgious stared at Rosa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Mr. Grewgious, you must excuse me. So long as this \xe2\x80\x99ouse is known\r\nindefinite as Billickin\xe2\x80\x99s, and so long as it is a doubt with the\r\nriff-raff where Billickin may be hidin\xe2\x80\x99, near the street-door or down\r\nthe airy, and what his weight and size, so long I feel safe. But commit\r\nmyself to a solitary female statement, no, Miss! Nor would you for a\r\nmoment wish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Billickin, with a strong sense of injury, \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\ntake that advantage of your sex, if you were not brought to it by\r\ninconsiderate example.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosa reddening as if she had made some most disgraceful attempt to\r\noverreach the good lady, besought Mr. Grewgious to rest content with\r\nany signature. And accordingly, in a baronial way, the sign-manual\r\nBILLICKIN got appended to the document.\r\n\r\nDetails were then settled for taking possession on the next day but\r\none, when Miss Twinkleton might be reasonably expected; and Rosa went\r\nback to Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s Inn on her guardian\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\nBehold Mr. Tartar walking up and down Furnival\xe2\x80\x99s Inn, checking himself\r\nwhen he saw them coming, and advancing towards them!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt occurred to me,\xe2\x80\x9d hinted Mr. Tartar, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we might go up the river,\r\nthe weather being so delicious and the tide serving. I have a boat of\r\nmy own at the Temple Stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not been up the river for this many a day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Grewgious,\r\ntempted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was never up the river,\xe2\x80\x9d added Rosa.\r\n\r\nWithin half an hour they were setting this matter right by going up the\r\nriver. The tide was running with them, the afternoon was charming. Mr.\r\nTartar\xe2\x80\x99s boat was perfect. Mr. Tartar and Lobley (Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s man)\r\npulled a pair of oars. Mr. Tartar had a yacht, it seemed, lying\r\nsomewhere down by Greenhithe; and Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s man had charge of this\r\nyacht, and was detached upon his present service. He was a\r\njolly-favoured man, with tawny hair and whiskers, and a big red face.\r\nHe was the dead image of the sun in old woodcuts, his hair and whiskers\r\nanswering for rays all around him. Resplendent in the bow of the boat,\r\nhe was a shining sight, with a man-of-war\xe2\x80\x99s man\xe2\x80\x99s shirt on\xe2\x80\x94or off,\r\naccording to opinion\xe2\x80\x94and his arms and breast tattooed all sorts of\r\npatterns. Lobley seemed to take it easily, and so did Mr. Tartar; yet\r\ntheir oars bent as they pulled, and the boat bounded under them. Mr.\r\nTartar talked as if he were doing nothing, to Rosa who was really doing\r\nnothing, and to Mr. Grewgious who was doing this much that he steered\r\nall wrong; but what did that matter, when a turn of Mr. Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nskilful wrist, or a mere grin of Mr. Lobley\xe2\x80\x99s over the bow, put all to\r\nrights! The tide bore them on in the gayest and most sparkling manner,\r\nuntil they stopped to dine in some ever-lastingly-green garden, needing\r\nno matter-of-fact identification here; and then the tide obligingly\r\nturned\xe2\x80\x94being devoted to that party alone for that day; and as they\r\nfloated idly among some osier-beds, Rosa tried what she could do in the\r\nrowing way, and came off splendidly, being much assisted; and Mr.\r\nGrewgious tried what he could do, and came off on his back, doubled up\r\nwith an oar under his chin, being not assisted at all. Then there was\r\nan interval of rest under boughs (such rest!) what time Mr. Lobley\r\nmopped, and, arranging cushions, stretchers, and the like, danced the\r\ntight-rope the whole length of the boat like a man to whom shoes were a\r\nsuperstition and stockings slavery; and then came the sweet return\r\namong delicious odours of limes in bloom, and musical ripplings; and,\r\nall too soon, the great black city cast its shadow on the waters, and\r\nits dark bridges spanned them as death spans life, and the\r\neverlastingly-green garden seemed to be left for everlasting,\r\nunregainable and far away.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Up the river]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCannot people get through life without gritty stages, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosa\r\nthought next day, when the town was very gritty again, and everything\r\nhad a strange and an uncomfortable appearance of seeming to wait for\r\nsomething that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come. NO. She began to think, that, now the\r\nCloisterham school-days had glided past and gone, the gritty stages\r\nwould begin to set in at intervals and make themselves wearily known!\r\n\r\nYet what did Rosa expect? Did she expect Miss Twinkleton? Miss\r\nTwinkleton duly came. Forth from her back parlour issued the Billickin\r\nto receive Miss Twinkleton, and War was in the Billickin\xe2\x80\x99s eye from\r\nthat fell moment.\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton brought a quantity of luggage with her, having all\r\nRosa\xe2\x80\x99s as well as her own. The Billickin took it ill that Miss\r\nTwinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s mind, being sorely disturbed by this luggage, failed to\r\ntake in her personal identity with that clearness of perception which\r\nwas due to its demands. Stateliness mounted her gloomy throne upon the\r\nBillickin\xe2\x80\x99s brow in consequence. And when Miss Twinkleton, in agitation\r\ntaking stock of her trunks and packages, of which she had seventeen,\r\nparticularly counted in the Billickin herself as number eleven, the B.\r\nfound it necessary to repudiate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThings cannot too soon be put upon the footing,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, with a\r\ncandour so demonstrative as to be almost obtrusive, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the person of\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x99ouse is not a box nor yet a bundle, nor a carpet-bag. No, I am\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ily obleeged to you, Miss Twinkleton, nor yet a beggar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis last disclaimer had reference to Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s distractedly\r\npressing two-and-sixpence on her, instead of the cabman.\r\n\r\nThus cast off, Miss Twinkleton wildly inquired, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich gentleman\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nto be paid? There being two gentlemen in that position (Miss Twinkleton\r\nhaving arrived with two cabs), each gentleman on being paid held forth\r\nhis two-and-sixpence on the flat of his open hand, and, with a\r\nspeechless stare and a dropped jaw, displayed his wrong to heaven and\r\nearth. Terrified by this alarming spectacle, Miss Twinkleton placed\r\nanother shilling in each hand; at the same time appealing to the law in\r\nflurried accents, and recounting her luggage this time with the two\r\ngentlemen in, who caused the total to come out complicated. Meanwhile\r\nthe two gentlemen, each looking very hard at the last shilling\r\ngrumblingly, as if it might become eighteen-pence if he kept his eyes\r\non it, descended the doorsteps, ascended their carriages, and drove\r\naway, leaving Miss Twinkleton on a bonnet-box in tears.\r\n\r\nThe Billickin beheld this manifestation of weakness without sympathy,\r\nand gave directions for \xe2\x80\x9ca young man to be got in\xe2\x80\x9d to wrestle with the\r\nluggage. When that gladiator had disappeared from the arena, peace\r\nensued, and the new lodgers dined.\r\n\r\nBut the Billickin had somehow come to the knowledge that Miss\r\nTwinkleton kept a school. The leap from that knowledge to the inference\r\nthat Miss Twinkleton set herself to teach _her_ something, was easy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t do it,\xe2\x80\x9d soliloquised the Billickin; \xe2\x80\x9cI am not your\r\npupil, whatever she,\xe2\x80\x9d meaning Rosa, \xe2\x80\x9cmay be, poor thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Twinkleton, on the other hand, having changed her dress and\r\nrecovered her spirits, was animated by a bland desire to improve the\r\noccasion in all ways, and to be as serene a model as possible. In a\r\nhappy compromise between her two states of existence, she had already\r\nbecome, with her workbasket before her, the equably vivacious companion\r\nwith a slight judicious flavouring of information, when the Billickin\r\nannounced herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not hide from you, ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d said the B., enveloped in the shawl\r\nof state, \xe2\x80\x9cfor it is not my character to hide neither my motives nor my\r\nactions, that I take the liberty to look in upon you to express a \xe2\x80\x99ope\r\nthat your dinner was to your liking. Though not Professed but Plain,\r\nstill her wages should be a sufficient object to her to stimilate to\r\nsoar above mere roast and biled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe dined very well indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosa, \xe2\x80\x9cthank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccustomed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Twinkleton with a gracious air, which to the\r\njealous ears of the Billickin seemed to add \xe2\x80\x9cmy good woman\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9caccustomed\r\nto a liberal and nutritious, yet plain and salutary diet, we have found\r\nno reason to bemoan our absence from the ancient city, and the\r\nmethodical household, in which the quiet routine of our lot has been\r\nhitherto cast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did think it well to mention to my cook,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the Billickin\r\nwith a gush of candour, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich I \xe2\x80\x99ope you will agree with, Miss\r\nTwinkleton, was a right precaution, that the young lady being used to\r\nwhat we should consider here but poor diet, had better be brought\r\nforward by degrees. For, a rush from scanty feeding to generous\r\nfeeding, and from what you may call messing to what you may call\r\nmethod, do require a power of constitution which is not often found in\r\nyouth, particular when undermined by boarding-school!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt will be seen that the Billickin now openly pitted herself against\r\nMiss Twinkleton, as one whom she had fully ascertained to be her\r\nnatural enemy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour remarks,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Miss Twinkleton, from a remote moral eminence,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9care well meant, I have no doubt; but you will permit me to observe\r\nthat they develop a mistaken view of the subject, which can only be\r\nimputed to your extreme want of accurate information.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy informiation,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted the Billickin, throwing in an extra\r\nsyllable for the sake of emphasis at once polite and powerful\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmy\r\ninformiation, Miss Twinkleton, were my own experience, which I believe\r\nis usually considered to be good guidance. But whether so or not, I was\r\nput in youth to a very genteel boarding-school, the mistress being no\r\nless a lady than yourself, of about your own age or it may be some\r\nyears younger, and a poorness of blood flowed from the table which has\r\nrun through my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Twinkleton, still from her distant eminence;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand very much to be deplored.\xe2\x80\x94Rosa, my dear, how are you getting on\r\nwith your work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Twinkleton,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed the Billickin, in a courtly manner, \xe2\x80\x9cbefore\r\nretiring on the \xe2\x80\x99int, as a lady should, I wish to ask of yourself, as a\r\nlady, whether I am to consider that my words is doubted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not aware on what ground you cherish such a supposition,\xe2\x80\x9d began\r\nMiss Twinkleton, when the Billickin neatly stopped her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not, if you please, put suppositions betwixt my lips where none\r\nsuch have been imparted by myself. Your flow of words is great, Miss\r\nTwinkleton, and no doubt is expected from you by your pupils, and no\r\ndoubt is considered worth the money. _No_ doubt, I am sure. But not\r\npaying for flows of words, and not asking to be favoured with them\r\nhere, I wish to repeat my question.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you refer to the poverty of your circulation,\xe2\x80\x9d began Miss\r\nTwinkleton, when again the Billickin neatly stopped her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have used no such expressions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you refer, then, to the poorness of your blood\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrought upon me,\xe2\x80\x9d stipulated the Billickin, expressly, \xe2\x80\x9cat a\r\nboarding-school\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Miss Twinkleton, \xe2\x80\x9call I can say is, that I am bound to\r\nbelieve, on your asseveration, that it is very poor indeed. I cannot\r\nforbear adding, that if that unfortunate circumstance influences your\r\nconversation, it is much to be lamented, and it is eminently desirable\r\nthat your blood were richer.\xe2\x80\x94Rosa, my dear, how are you getting on with\r\nyour work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHem! Before retiring, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d proclaimed the Billickin to Rosa, loftily\r\ncancelling Miss Twinkleton, \xe2\x80\x9cI should wish it to be understood between\r\nyourself and me that my transactions in future is with you alone. I\r\nknow no elderly lady here, Miss, none older than yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA highly desirable arrangement, Rosa my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Miss\r\nTwinkleton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Billickin, with a sarcastic smile, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I\r\npossess the Mill I have heard of, in which old single ladies could be\r\nground up young (what a gift it would be to some of us), but that I\r\nlimit myself to you totally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have any desire to communicate a request to the person of the\r\nhouse, Rosa my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Miss Twinkleton with majestic\r\ncheerfulness, \xe2\x80\x9cI will make it known to you, and you will kindly\r\nundertake, I am sure, that it is conveyed to the proper quarter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-evening, Miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Billickin, at once affectionately and\r\ndistantly. \xe2\x80\x9cBeing alone in my eyes, I wish you good-evening with best\r\nwishes, and do not find myself drove, I am truly \xe2\x80\x99appy to say, into\r\nexpressing my contempt for an indiwidual, unfortunately for yourself,\r\nbelonging to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Billickin gracefully withdrew with this parting speech, and from\r\nthat time Rosa occupied the restless position of shuttlecock between\r\nthese two battledores. Nothing could be done without a smart match\r\nbeing played out. Thus, on the daily-arising question of dinner, Miss\r\nTwinkleton would say, the three being present together:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, my love, you will consult with the person of the house,\r\nwhether she can procure us a lamb\xe2\x80\x99s fry; or, failing that, a roast\r\nfowl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn which the Billickin would retort (Rosa not having spoken a word),\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you was better accustomed to butcher\xe2\x80\x99s meat, Miss, you would not\r\nentertain the idea of a lamb\xe2\x80\x99s fry. Firstly, because lambs has long\r\nbeen sheep, and secondly, because there is such things as killing-days,\r\nand there is not. As to roast fowls, Miss, why you must be quite\r\nsurfeited with roast fowls, letting alone your buying, when you market\r\nfor yourself, the agedest of poultry with the scaliest of legs, quite\r\nas if you was accustomed to picking \xe2\x80\x99em out for cheapness. Try a little\r\ninwention, Miss. Use yourself to \xe2\x80\x99ousekeeping a bit. Come now, think of\r\nsomethink else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this encouragement, offered with the indulgent toleration of a wise\r\nand liberal expert, Miss Twinkleton would rejoin, reddening:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, my dear, you might propose to the person of the house a duck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Miss!\xe2\x80\x9d the Billickin would exclaim (still no word being spoken\r\nby Rosa), \xe2\x80\x9cyou do surprise me when you speak of ducks! Not to mention\r\nthat they\xe2\x80\x99re getting out of season and very dear, it really strikes to\r\nmy heart to see you have a duck; for the breast, which is the only\r\ndelicate cuts in a duck, always goes in a direction which I cannot\r\nimagine where, and your own plate comes down so miserably\r\nskin-and-bony! Try again, Miss. Think more of yourself, and less of\r\nothers. A dish of sweetbreads now, or a bit of mutton. Something at\r\nwhich you can get your equal chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOccasionally the game would wax very brisk indeed, and would be kept up\r\nwith a smartness rendering such an encounter as this quite tame. But\r\nthe Billickin almost invariably made by far the higher score; and would\r\ncome in with side hits of the most unexpected and extraordinary\r\ndescription, when she seemed without a chance.\r\n\r\nAll this did not improve the gritty state of things in London, or the\r\nair that London had acquired in Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s eyes of waiting for something\r\nthat never came. Tired of working, and conversing with Miss Twinkleton,\r\nshe suggested working and reading: to which Miss Twinkleton readily\r\nassented, as an admirable reader, of tried powers. But Rosa soon made\r\nthe discovery that Miss Twinkleton didn\xe2\x80\x99t read fairly. She cut the\r\nlove-scenes, interpolated passages in praise of female celibacy, and\r\nwas guilty of other glaring pious frauds. As an instance in point, take\r\nthe glowing passage: \xe2\x80\x9cEver dearest and best adored,\xe2\x80\x94said Edward,\r\nclasping the dear head to his breast, and drawing the silken hair\r\nthrough his caressing fingers, from which he suffered it to fall like\r\ngolden rain,\xe2\x80\x94ever dearest and best adored, let us fly from the\r\nunsympathetic world and the sterile coldness of the stony-hearted, to\r\nthe rich warm Paradise of Trust and Love.\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s fraudulent\r\nversion tamely ran thus: \xe2\x80\x9cEver engaged to me with the consent of our\r\nparents on both sides, and the approbation of the silver-haired rector\r\nof the district,\xe2\x80\x94said Edward, respectfully raising to his lips the\r\ntaper fingers so skilful in embroidery, tambour, crochet, and other\r\ntruly feminine arts,\xe2\x80\x94let me call on thy papa ere to-morrow\xe2\x80\x99s dawn has\r\nsunk into the west, and propose a suburban establishment, lowly it may\r\nbe, but within our means, where he will be always welcome as an evening\r\nguest, and where every arrangement shall invest economy, and constant\r\ninterchange of scholastic acquirements with the attributes of the\r\nministering angel to domestic bliss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the days crept on and nothing happened, the neighbours began to say\r\nthat the pretty girl at Billickin\xe2\x80\x99s, who looked so wistfully and so\r\nmuch out of the gritty windows of the drawing-room, seemed to be losing\r\nher spirits. The pretty girl might have lost them but for the accident\r\nof lighting on some books of voyages and sea-adventure. As a\r\ncompensation against their romance, Miss Twinkleton, reading aloud,\r\nmade the most of all the latitudes and longitudes, bearings, winds,\r\ncurrents, offsets, and other statistics (which she felt to be none the\r\nless improving because they expressed nothing whatever to her); while\r\nRosa, listening intently, made the most of what was nearest to her\r\nheart. So they both did better than before.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\nTHE DAWN AGAIN\r\n\r\n\r\nAlthough Mr. Crisparkle and John Jasper met daily under the Cathedral\r\nroof, nothing at any time passed between them having reference to Edwin\r\nDrood, after the time, more than half a year gone by, when Jasper\r\nmutely showed the Minor Canon the conclusion and the resolution entered\r\nin his Diary. It is not likely that they ever met, though so often,\r\nwithout the thoughts of each reverting to the subject. It is not likely\r\nthat they ever met, though so often, without a sensation on the part of\r\neach that the other was a perplexing secret to him. Jasper as the\r\ndenouncer and pursuer of Neville Landless, and Mr. Crisparkle as his\r\nconsistent advocate and protector, must at least have stood\r\nsufficiently in opposition to have speculated with keen interest on the\r\nsteadiness and next direction of the other\xe2\x80\x99s designs. But neither ever\r\nbroached the theme.\r\n\r\nFalse pretence not being in the Minor Canon\xe2\x80\x99s nature, he doubtless\r\ndisplayed openly that he would at any time have revived the subject,\r\nand even desired to discuss it. The determined reticence of Jasper,\r\nhowever, was not to be so approached. Impassive, moody, solitary,\r\nresolute, so concentrated on one idea, and on its attendant fixed\r\npurpose, that he would share it with no fellow-creature, he lived apart\r\nfrom human life. Constantly exercising an Art which brought him into\r\nmechanical harmony with others, and which could not have been pursued\r\nunless he and they had been in the nicest mechanical relations and\r\nunison, it is curious to consider that the spirit of the man was in\r\nmoral accordance or interchange with nothing around him. This indeed he\r\nhad confided to his lost nephew, before the occasion for his present\r\ninflexibility arose.\r\n\r\nThat he must know of Rosa\xe2\x80\x99s abrupt departure, and that he must divine\r\nits cause, was not to be doubted. Did he suppose that he had terrified\r\nher into silence? or did he suppose that she had imparted to any one\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nMr. Crisparkle himself, for instance\xe2\x80\x94the particulars of his last\r\ninterview with her? Mr. Crisparkle could not determine this in his\r\nmind. He could not but admit, however, as a just man, that it was not,\r\nof itself, a crime to fall in love with Rosa, any more than it was a\r\ncrime to offer to set love above revenge.\r\n\r\nThe dreadful suspicion of Jasper, which Rosa was so shocked to have\r\nreceived into her imagination, appeared to have no harbour in Mr.\r\nCrisparkle\xe2\x80\x99s. If it ever haunted Helena\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts or Neville\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nneither gave it one spoken word of utterance. Mr. Grewgious took no\r\npains to conceal his implacable dislike of Jasper, yet he never\r\nreferred it, however distantly, to such a source. But he was a reticent\r\nas well as an eccentric man; and he made no mention of a certain\r\nevening when he warmed his hands at the gatehouse fire, and looked\r\nsteadily down upon a certain heap of torn and miry clothes upon the\r\nfloor.\r\n\r\nDrowsy Cloisterham, whenever it awoke to a passing reconsideration of a\r\nstory above six months old and dismissed by the bench of magistrates,\r\nwas pretty equally divided in opinion whether John Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s beloved\r\nnephew had been killed by his treacherously passionate rival, or in an\r\nopen struggle; or had, for his own purposes, spirited himself away. It\r\nthen lifted up its head, to notice that the bereaved Jasper was still\r\never devoted to discovery and revenge; and then dozed off again. This\r\nwas the condition of matters, all round, at the period to which the\r\npresent history has now attained.\r\n\r\nThe Cathedral doors have closed for the night; and the Choir-master, on\r\na short leave of absence for two or three services, sets his face\r\ntowards London. He travels thither by the means by which Rosa\r\ntravelled, and arrives, as Rosa arrived, on a hot, dusty evening.\r\n\r\nHis travelling baggage is easily carried in his hand, and he repairs\r\nwith it on foot, to a hybrid hotel in a little square behind Aldersgate\r\nStreet, near the General Post Office. It is hotel, boarding-house, or\r\nlodging-house, at its visitor\xe2\x80\x99s option. It announces itself, in the new\r\nRailway Advertisers, as a novel enterprise, timidly beginning to spring\r\nup. It bashfully, almost apologetically, gives the traveller to\r\nunderstand that it does not expect him, on the good old constitutional\r\nhotel plan, to order a pint of sweet blacking for his drinking, and\r\nthrow it away; but insinuates that he may have his boots blacked\r\ninstead of his stomach, and maybe also have bed, breakfast, attendance,\r\nand a porter up all night, for a certain fixed charge. From these and\r\nsimilar premises, many true Britons in the lowest spirits deduce that\r\nthe times are levelling times, except in the article of high roads, of\r\nwhich there will shortly be not one in England.\r\n\r\nHe eats without appetite, and soon goes forth again. Eastward and still\r\neastward through the stale streets he takes his way, until he reaches\r\nhis destination: a miserable court, specially miserable among many\r\nsuch.\r\n\r\nHe ascends a broken staircase, opens a door, looks into a dark stifling\r\nroom, and says: \xe2\x80\x9cAre you alone here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlone, deary; worse luck for me, and better for you,\xe2\x80\x9d replies a\r\ncroaking voice. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in, come in, whoever you be: I can\xe2\x80\x99t see you till\r\nI light a match, yet I seem to know the sound of your speaking. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nacquainted with you, ain\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLight your match, and try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I will, deary, so I will; but my hand that shakes, as I can\xe2\x80\x99t lay\r\nit on a match all in a moment. And I cough so, that, put my matches\r\nwhere I may, I never find \xe2\x80\x99em there. They jump and start, as I cough\r\nand cough, like live things. Are you off a voyage, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot seafaring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s land customers, and there\xe2\x80\x99s water customers. I\xe2\x80\x99m a\r\nmother to both. Different from Jack Chinaman t\xe2\x80\x99other side the court. He\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t a father to neither. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t in him. And he ain\xe2\x80\x99t got the true\r\nsecret of mixing, though he charges as much as me that has, and more if\r\nhe can get it. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a match, and now where\xe2\x80\x99s the candle? If my cough\r\ntakes me, I shall cough out twenty matches afore I gets a light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she finds the candle, and lights it, before the cough comes on. It\r\nseizes her in the moment of success, and she sits down rocking herself\r\nto and fro, and gasping at intervals: \xe2\x80\x9cO, my lungs is awful bad! my\r\nlungs is wore away to cabbage-nets!\xe2\x80\x9d until the fit is over. During its\r\ncontinuance she has had no power of sight, or any other power not\r\nabsorbed in the struggle; but as it leaves her, she begins to strain\r\nher eyes, and as soon as she is able to articulate, she cries, staring:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you so surprised to see me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I never should have seen you again, deary. I thought you was\r\ndead, and gone to Heaven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you could have kept away, alive, so long, from the\r\npoor old soul with the real receipt for mixing it. And you are in\r\nmourning too! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come and have a pipe or two of comfort?\r\nDid they leave you money, perhaps, and so you didn\xe2\x80\x99t want comfort?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was they as died, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA relative.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDied of what, lovey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProbably, Death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are short to-night!\xe2\x80\x9d cries the woman, with a propitiatory laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShort and snappish we are! But we\xe2\x80\x99re out of sorts for want of a smoke.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got the all-overs, haven\xe2\x80\x99t us, deary? But this is the place to\r\ncure \xe2\x80\x99em in; this is the place where the all-overs is smoked off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may make ready, then,\xe2\x80\x9d replies the visitor, \xe2\x80\x9cas soon as you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe divests himself of his shoes, loosens his cravat, and lies across\r\nthe foot of the squalid bed, with his head resting on his left hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you begin to look like yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d says the woman approvingly. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nI begin to know my old customer indeed! Been trying to mix for yourself\r\nthis long time, poppet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been taking it now and then in my own way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever take it your own way. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t good for trade, and it ain\xe2\x80\x99t good\r\nfor you. Where\xe2\x80\x99s my ink-bottle, and where\xe2\x80\x99s my thimble, and where\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nlittle spoon? He\xe2\x80\x99s going to take it in a artful form now, my deary\r\ndear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEntering on her process, and beginning to bubble and blow at the faint\r\nspark enclosed in the hollow of her hands, she speaks from time to\r\ntime, in a tone of snuffling satisfaction, without leaving off. When he\r\nspeaks, he does so without looking at her, and as if his thoughts were\r\nalready roaming away by anticipation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a pretty many smokes ready for you, first and last, haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nI, chuckey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good many.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you first come, you was quite new to it; warn\xe2\x80\x99t ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I was easily disposed of, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you got on in the world, and was able by-and-by to take your pipe\r\nwith the best of \xe2\x80\x99em, warn\xe2\x80\x99t ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh; and the worst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just ready for you. What a sweet singer you was when you first\r\ncome! Used to drop your head, and sing yourself off like a bird! It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nready for you now, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe takes it from her with great care, and puts the mouthpiece to his\r\nlips. She seats herself beside him, ready to refill the pipe.\r\n\r\nAfter inhaling a few whiffs in silence, he doubtingly accosts her with:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it as potent as it used to be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you speak of, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat should I speak of, but what I have in my mouth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just the same. Always the identical same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t taste so. And it\xe2\x80\x99s slower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got more used to it, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be the cause, certainly. Look here.\xe2\x80\x9d He stops, becomes\r\ndreamy, and seems to forget that he has invited her attention. She\r\nbends over him, and speaks in his ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m attending to you. Says you just now, Look here. Says I now, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nattending to ye. We was talking just before of your being used to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know all that. I was only thinking. Look here. Suppose you had\r\nsomething in your mind; something you were going to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, deary; something I was going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut had not quite determined to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight or might not do, you understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d With the point of a needle she stirs the contents of the bowl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you do it in your fancy, when you were lying here doing this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nods her head. \xe2\x80\x9cOver and over again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust like me! I did it over and over again. I have done it hundreds of\r\nthousands of times in this room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s to be hoped it was pleasant to do, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _was_ pleasant to do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe says this with a savage air, and a spring or start at her. Quite\r\nunmoved she retouches and replenishes the contents of the bowl with her\r\nlittle spatula. Seeing her intent upon the occupation, he sinks into\r\nhis former attitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a journey, a difficult and dangerous journey. That was the\r\nsubject in my mind. A hazardous and perilous journey, over abysses\r\nwhere a slip would be destruction. Look down, look down! You see what\r\nlies at the bottom there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe has darted forward to say it, and to point at the ground, as though\r\nat some imaginary object far beneath. The woman looks at him, as his\r\nspasmodic face approaches close to hers, and not at his pointing. She\r\nseems to know what the influence of her perfect quietude would be; if\r\nso, she has not miscalculated it, for he subsides again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell; I have told you I did it here hundreds of thousands of times.\r\nWhat do I say? I did it millions and billions of times. I did it so\r\noften, and through such vast expanses of time, that when it was really\r\ndone, it seemed not worth the doing, it was done so soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the journey you have been away upon,\xe2\x80\x9d she quietly remarks.\r\n\r\nHe glares at her as he smokes; and then, his eyes becoming filmy,\r\nanswers: \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the journey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSilence ensues. His eyes are sometimes closed and sometimes open. The\r\nwoman sits beside him, very attentive to the pipe, which is all the\r\nwhile at his lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant,\xe2\x80\x9d she observes, when he has been looking fixedly at her\r\nfor some consecutive moments, with a singular appearance in his eyes of\r\nseeming to see her a long way off, instead of so near him: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwarrant you made the journey in a many ways, when you made it so\r\noften?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, always in one way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways in the same way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the way in which it was really made at last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd always took the same pleasure in harping on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the time he appears unequal to any other reply than this lazy\r\nmonosyllabic assent. Probably to assure herself that it is not the\r\nassent of a mere automaton, she reverses the form of her next sentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you never get tired of it, deary, and try to call up something\r\nelse for a change?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe struggles into a sitting posture, and retorts upon her: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you\r\nmean? What did I want? What did I come for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gently lays him back again, and before returning him the instrument\r\nhe has dropped, revives the fire in it with her own breath; then says\r\nto him, coaxingly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, sure, sure! Yes, yes, yes! Now I go along with you. You was too\r\nquick for me. I see now. You come o\xe2\x80\x99 purpose to take the journey. Why,\r\nI might have known it, through its standing by you so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe answers first with a laugh, and then with a passionate setting of\r\nhis teeth: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I came on purpose. When I could not bear my life, I\r\ncame to get the relief, and I got it. It WAS one! It WAS one!\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\nrepetition with extraordinary vehemence, and the snarl of a wolf.\r\n\r\nShe observes him very cautiously, as though mentally feeling her way to\r\nher next remark. It is: \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a fellow-traveller, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa, ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d He breaks into a ringing laugh, or rather yell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo think,\xe2\x80\x9d he cries, \xe2\x80\x9chow often fellow-traveller, and yet not know it!\r\nTo think how many times he went the journey, and never saw the road!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman kneels upon the floor, with her arms crossed on the coverlet\r\nof the bed, close by him, and her chin upon them. In this crouching\r\nattitude she watches him. The pipe is falling from his mouth. She puts\r\nit back, and laying her hand upon his chest, moves him slightly from\r\nside to side. Upon that he speaks, as if she had spoken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! I always made the journey first, before the changes of colours\r\nand the great landscapes and glittering processions began. They\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t begin till it was off my mind. I had no room till then for\r\nanything else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more he lapses into silence. Once more she lays her hand upon his\r\nchest, and moves him slightly to and fro, as a cat might stimulate a\r\nhalf-slain mouse. Once more he speaks, as if she had spoken.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Sleeping it off]\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? I told you so. When it comes to be real at last, it is so short\r\nthat it seems unreal for the first time. Hark!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, deary. I\xe2\x80\x99m listening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime and place are both at hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe is on his feet, speaking in a whisper, and as if in the dark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime, place, and fellow-traveller,\xe2\x80\x9d she suggests, adopting his tone,\r\nand holding him softly by the arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could the time be at hand unless the fellow-traveller was? Hush!\r\nThe journey\xe2\x80\x99s made. It\xe2\x80\x99s over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I said to you. So soon. Wait a little. This is a vision. I\r\nshall sleep it off. It has been too short and easy. I must have a\r\nbetter vision than this; this is the poorest of all. No struggle, no\r\nconsciousness of peril, no entreaty\xe2\x80\x94and yet I never saw _that_ before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nWith a start.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaw what, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at it! Look what a poor, mean, miserable thing it is! _That_ must\r\nbe real. It\xe2\x80\x99s over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe has accompanied this incoherence with some wild unmeaning gestures;\r\nbut they trail off into the progressive inaction of stupor, and he lies\r\na log upon the bed.\r\n\r\nThe woman, however, is still inquisitive. With a repetition of her\r\ncat-like action she slightly stirs his body again, and listens; stirs\r\nagain, and listens; whispers to it, and listens. Finding it past all\r\nrousing for the time, she slowly gets upon her feet, with an air of\r\ndisappointment, and flicks the face with the back of her hand in\r\nturning from it.\r\n\r\nBut she goes no further away from it than the chair upon the hearth.\r\nShe sits in it, with an elbow on one of its arms, and her chin upon her\r\nhand, intent upon him. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard ye say once,\xe2\x80\x9d she croaks under her\r\nbreath, \xe2\x80\x9cI heard ye say once, when I was lying where you\xe2\x80\x99re lying, and\r\nyou were making your speculations upon me, \xe2\x80\x98Unintelligible!\xe2\x80\x99 I heard\r\nyou say so, of two more than me. But don\xe2\x80\x99t ye be too sure always; don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe ye too sure, beauty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnwinking, cat-like, and intent, she presently adds: \xe2\x80\x9cNot so potent as\r\nit once was? Ah! Perhaps not at first. You may be more right there.\r\nPractice makes perfect. I may have learned the secret how to make ye\r\ntalk, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe talks no more, whether or no. Twitching in an ugly way from time to\r\ntime, both as to his face and limbs, he lies heavy and silent. The\r\nwretched candle burns down; the woman takes its expiring end between\r\nher fingers, lights another at it, crams the guttering frying morsel\r\ndeep into the candlestick, and rams it home with the new candle, as if\r\nshe were loading some ill-savoured and unseemly weapon of witchcraft;\r\nthe new candle in its turn burns down; and still he lies insensible. At\r\nlength what remains of the last candle is blown out, and daylight looks\r\ninto the room.\r\n\r\nIt has not looked very long, when he sits up, chilled and shaking,\r\nslowly recovers consciousness of where he is, and makes himself ready\r\nto depart. The woman receives what he pays her with a grateful, \xe2\x80\x9cBless\r\nye, bless ye, deary!\xe2\x80\x9d and seems, tired out, to begin making herself\r\nready for sleep as he leaves the room.\r\n\r\nBut seeming may be false or true. It is false in this case; for, the\r\nmoment the stairs have ceased to creak under his tread, she glides\r\nafter him, muttering emphatically: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll not miss ye twice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere is no egress from the court but by its entrance. With a weird\r\npeep from the doorway, she watches for his looking back. He does not\r\nlook back before disappearing, with a wavering step. She follows him,\r\npeeps from the court, sees him still faltering on without looking back,\r\nand holds him in view.\r\n\r\nHe repairs to the back of Aldersgate Street, where a door immediately\r\nopens to his knocking. She crouches in another doorway, watching that\r\none, and easily comprehending that he puts up temporarily at that\r\nhouse. Her patience is unexhausted by hours. For sustenance she can,\r\nand does, buy bread within a hundred yards, and milk as it is carried\r\npast her.\r\n\r\nHe comes forth again at noon, having changed his dress, but carrying\r\nnothing in his hand, and having nothing carried for him. He is not\r\ngoing back into the country, therefore, just yet. She follows him a\r\nlittle way, hesitates, instantaneously turns confidently, and goes\r\nstraight into the house he has quitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the gentleman from Cloisterham indoors?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust gone out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnlucky. When does the gentleman return to Cloisterham?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt six this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless ye and thank ye. May the Lord prosper a business where a civil\r\nquestion, even from a poor soul, is so civilly answered!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll not miss ye twice!\xe2\x80\x9d repeats the poor soul in the street, and not\r\nso civilly. \xe2\x80\x9cI lost ye last, where that omnibus you got into nigh your\r\njourney\xe2\x80\x99s end plied betwixt the station and the place. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t so much\r\nas certain that you even went right on to the place. Now I know ye did.\r\nMy gentleman from Cloisterham, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be there before ye, and bide your\r\ncoming. I\xe2\x80\x99ve swore my oath that I\xe2\x80\x99ll not miss ye twice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly, that same evening the poor soul stands in Cloisterham High\r\nStreet, looking at the many quaint gables of the Nuns\xe2\x80\x99 House, and\r\ngetting through the time as she best can until nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock; at which\r\nhour she has reason to suppose that the arriving omnibus passengers may\r\nhave some interest for her. The friendly darkness, at that hour,\r\nrenders it easy for her to ascertain whether this be so or not; and it\r\nis so, for the passenger not to be missed twice arrives among the rest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let me see what becomes of you. Go on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn observation addressed to the air, and yet it might be addressed to\r\nthe passenger, so compliantly does he go on along the High Street until\r\nhe comes to an arched gateway, at which he unexpectedly vanishes. The\r\npoor soul quickens her pace; is swift, and close upon him entering\r\nunder the gateway; but only sees a postern staircase on one side of it,\r\nand on the other side an ancient vaulted room, in which a large-headed,\r\ngray-haired gentleman is writing, under the odd circumstances of\r\nsitting open to the thoroughfare and eyeing all who pass, as if he were\r\ntoll-taker of the gateway: though the way is free.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalloa!\xe2\x80\x9d he cries in a low voice, seeing her brought to a stand-still:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwho are you looking for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a gentleman passed in here this minute, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course there was. What do you want with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do he live, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLive? Up that staircase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless ye! Whisper. What\xe2\x80\x99s his name, deary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurname Jasper, Christian name John. Mr. John Jasper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he a calling, good gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCalling? Yes. Sings in the choir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the spire?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChoir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery rises from his papers, and comes to his doorstep. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nknow what a cathedral is?\xe2\x80\x9d he asks, jocosely.\r\n\r\nThe woman nods.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looks puzzled, casting about in her mind to find a definition, when\r\nit occurs to her that it is easier to point out the substantial object\r\nitself, massive against the dark-blue sky and the early stars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the answer. Go in there at seven to-morrow morning, and you may\r\nsee Mr. John Jasper, and hear him too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank ye! Thank ye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe burst of triumph in which she thanks him does not escape the notice\r\nof the single buffer of an easy temper living idly on his means. He\r\nglances at her; clasps his hands behind him, as the wont of such\r\nbuffers is; and lounges along the echoing Precincts at her side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggests, with a backward hitch of his head, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can go up at\r\nonce to Mr. Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s rooms there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman eyes him with a cunning smile, and shakes her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO! you don\xe2\x80\x99t want to speak to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe repeats her dumb reply, and forms with her lips a soundless \xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can admire him at a distance three times a day, whenever you like.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a long way to come for that, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman looks up quickly. If Mr. Datchery thinks she is to be so\r\ninduced to declare where she comes from, he is of a much easier temper\r\nthan she is. But she acquits him of such an artful thought, as he\r\nlounges along, like the chartered bore of the city, with his uncovered\r\ngray hair blowing about, and his purposeless hands rattling the loose\r\nmoney in the pockets of his trousers.\r\n\r\nThe chink of the money has an attraction for her greedy ears. \xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou help me to pay for my traveller\xe2\x80\x99s lodging, dear gentleman, and to\r\npay my way along? I am a poor soul, I am indeed, and troubled with a\r\ngrievous cough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know the travellers\xe2\x80\x99 lodging, I perceive, and are making directly\r\nfor it,\xe2\x80\x9d is Mr. Datchery\xe2\x80\x99s bland comment, still rattling his loose\r\nmoney. \xe2\x80\x9cBeen here often, my good woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce in all my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, ay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey have arrived at the entrance to the Monks\xe2\x80\x99 Vineyard. An\r\nappropriate remembrance, presenting an exemplary model for imitation,\r\nis revived in the woman\xe2\x80\x99s mind by the sight of the place. She stops at\r\nthe gate, and says energetically:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy this token, though you mayn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it, That a young gentleman\r\ngave me three-and-sixpence as I was coughing my breath away on this\r\nvery grass. I asked him for three-and-sixpence, and he gave it me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t it a little cool to name your sum?\xe2\x80\x9d hints Mr. Datchery, still\r\nrattling. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it customary to leave the amount open? Mightn\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nhave had the appearance, to the young gentleman\xe2\x80\x94only the\r\nappearance\xe2\x80\x94that he was rather dictated to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook\xe2\x80\x99ee here, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d she replies, in a confidential and persuasive\r\ntone, \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted the money to lay it out on a medicine as does me good,\r\nand as I deal in. I told the young gentleman so, and he gave it me, and\r\nI laid it out honest to the last brass farden. I want to lay out the\r\nsame sum in the same way now; and if you\xe2\x80\x99ll give it me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll lay it out\r\nhonest to the last brass farden again, upon my soul!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the medicine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be honest with you beforehand, as well as after. It\xe2\x80\x99s opium.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery, with a sudden change of countenance, gives her a sudden\r\nlook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s opium, deary. Neither more nor less. And it\xe2\x80\x99s like a human\r\ncreetur so far, that you always hear what can be said against it, but\r\nseldom what can be said in its praise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery begins very slowly to count out the sum demanded of him.\r\nGreedily watching his hands, she continues to hold forth on the great\r\nexample set him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was last Christmas Eve, just arter dark, the once that I was here\r\nafore, when the young gentleman gave me the three-and-six.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nDatchery stops in his counting, finds he has counted wrong, shakes his\r\nmoney together, and begins again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the young gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s name,\xe2\x80\x9d she adds, \xe2\x80\x9cwas Edwin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery drops some money, stoops to pick it up, and reddens with\r\nthe exertion as he asks:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know the young gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked him for it, and he told it me. I only asked him the two\r\nquestions, what was his Chris\xe2\x80\x99en name, and whether he\xe2\x80\x99d a sweetheart?\r\nAnd he answered, Edwin, and he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery pauses with the selected coins in his hand, rather as if\r\nhe were falling into a brown study of their value, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to\r\npart with them. The woman looks at him distrustfully, and with her\r\nanger brewing for the event of his thinking better of the gift; but he\r\nbestows it on her as if he were abstracting his mind from the\r\nsacrifice, and with many servile thanks she goes her way.\r\n\r\nJohn Jasper\xe2\x80\x99s lamp is kindled, and his lighthouse is shining when Mr.\r\nDatchery returns alone towards it. As mariners on a dangerous voyage,\r\napproaching an iron-bound coast, may look along the beams of the\r\nwarning light to the haven lying beyond it that may never be reached,\r\nso Mr. Datchery\xe2\x80\x99s wistful gaze is directed to this beacon, and beyond.\r\n\r\nHis object in now revisiting his lodging is merely to put on the hat\r\nwhich seems so superfluous an article in his wardrobe. It is half-past\r\nten by the Cathedral clock when he walks out into the Precincts again;\r\nhe lingers and looks about him, as though, the enchanted hour when Mr.\r\nDurdles may be stoned home having struck, he had some expectation of\r\nseeing the Imp who is appointed to the mission of stoning him.\r\n\r\nIn effect, that Power of Evil is abroad. Having nothing living to stone\r\nat the moment, he is discovered by Mr. Datchery in the unholy office of\r\nstoning the dead, through the railings of the churchyard. The Imp finds\r\nthis a relishing and piquing pursuit; firstly, because their\r\nresting-place is announced to be sacred; and secondly, because the tall\r\nheadstones are sufficiently like themselves, on their beat in the dark,\r\nto justify the delicious fancy that they are hurt when hit.\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery hails with him: \xe2\x80\x9cHalloa, Winks!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe acknowledges the hail with: \xe2\x80\x9cHalloa, Dick!\xe2\x80\x9d Their acquaintance\r\nseemingly having been established on a familiar footing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, I say,\xe2\x80\x9d he remonstrates, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t yer go a-making my name public. I\r\nnever means to plead to no name, mind yer. When they says to me in the\r\nLock-up, a-going to put me down in the book, \xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x99 I says\r\nto them, \xe2\x80\x98Find out.\xe2\x80\x99 Likewise when they says, \xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s your religion?\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x98Find out.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhich, it may be observed in passing, it would be immensely difficult\r\nfor the State, however statistical, to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsides which,\xe2\x80\x9d adds the boy, \xe2\x80\x9cthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no family of Winkses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think there must be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYer lie, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t. The travellers give me the name on account of my\r\ngetting no settled sleep and being knocked up all night; whereby I gets\r\none eye roused open afore I\xe2\x80\x99ve shut the other. That\xe2\x80\x99s what Winks means.\r\nDeputy\xe2\x80\x99s the nighest name to indict me by: but yer wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t catch me\r\npleading to that, neither.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeputy be it always, then. We two are good friends; eh, Deputy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJolly good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgave you the debt you owed me when we first became acquainted,\r\nand many of my sixpences have come your way since; eh, Deputy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! And what\xe2\x80\x99s more, yer ain\xe2\x80\x99t no friend o\xe2\x80\x99 Jarsper\xe2\x80\x99s. What did he go\r\na-histing me off my legs for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat indeed! But never mind him now. A shilling of mine is going your\r\nway to-night, Deputy. You have just taken in a lodger I have been\r\nspeaking to; an infirm woman with a cough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPuffer,\xe2\x80\x9d assents Deputy, with a shrewd leer of recognition, and\r\nsmoking an imaginary pipe, with his head very much on one side and his\r\neyes very much out of their places: \xe2\x80\x9cHopeum Puffer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is her name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Er Royal Highness the Princess Puffer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has some other name than that; where does she live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp in London. Among the Jacks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sailors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said so; Jacks; and Chayner men: and hother Knifers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to know, through you, exactly where she lives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Give us \xe2\x80\x99old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA shilling passes; and, in that spirit of confidence which should\r\npervade all business transactions between principals of honour, this\r\npiece of business is considered done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut here\xe2\x80\x99s a lark!\xe2\x80\x9d cries Deputy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere did yer think \xe2\x80\x9cEr Royal\r\nHighness is a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 to to-morrow morning? Blest if she ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 to\r\nthe KIN-FREE-DER-EL!\xe2\x80\x9d He greatly prolongs the word in his ecstasy, and\r\nsmites his leg, and doubles himself up in a fit of shrill laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that, Deputy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCos she told me so just now. She said she must be hup and hout o\xe2\x80\x99\r\npurpose. She ses, \xe2\x80\x98Deputy, I must \xe2\x80\x99ave a early wash, and make myself as\r\nswell as I can, for I\xe2\x80\x99m a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 to take a turn at the\r\nKIN-FREE-DER-EL!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d He separates the syllables with his former zest,\r\nand, not finding his sense of the ludicrous sufficiently relieved by\r\nstamping about on the pavement, breaks into a slow and stately dance,\r\nperhaps supposed to be performed by the Dean.\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery receives the communication with a well-satisfied though\r\npondering face, and breaks up the conference. Returning to his quaint\r\nlodging, and sitting long over the supper of bread-and-cheese and salad\r\nand ale which Mrs. Tope has left prepared for him, he still sits when\r\nhis supper is finished. At length he rises, throws open the door of a\r\ncorner cupboard, and refers to a few uncouth chalked strokes on its\r\ninner side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like,\xe2\x80\x9d says Mr. Datchery, \xe2\x80\x9cthe old tavern way of keeping scores.\r\nIllegible except to the scorer. The scorer not committed, the scored\r\ndebited with what is against him. Hum; ha! A very small score this; a\r\nvery poor score!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sighs over the contemplation of its poverty, takes a bit of chalk\r\nfrom one of the cupboard shelves, and pauses with it in his hand,\r\nuncertain what addition to make to the account.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think a moderate stroke,\xe2\x80\x9d he concludes, \xe2\x80\x9cis all I am justified in\r\nscoring up;\xe2\x80\x9d so, suits the action to the word, closes the cupboard, and\r\ngoes to bed.\r\n\r\nA brilliant morning shines on the old city. Its antiquities and ruins\r\nare surpassingly beautiful, with a lusty ivy gleaming in the sun, and\r\nthe rich trees waving in the balmy air. Changes of glorious light from\r\nmoving boughs, songs of birds, scents from gardens, woods, and\r\nfields\xe2\x80\x94or, rather, from the one great garden of the whole cultivated\r\nisland in its yielding time\xe2\x80\x94penetrate into the Cathedral, subdue its\r\nearthy odour, and preach the Resurrection and the Life. The cold stone\r\ntombs of centuries ago grow warm; and flecks of brightness dart into\r\nthe sternest marble corners of the building, fluttering there like\r\nwings.\r\n\r\nComes Mr. Tope with his large keys, and yawningly unlocks and sets\r\nopen. Come Mrs. Tope and attendant sweeping sprites. Come, in due time,\r\norganist and bellows-boy, peeping down from the red curtains in the\r\nloft, fearlessly flapping dust from books up at that remote elevation,\r\nand whisking it from stops and pedals. Come sundry rooks, from various\r\nquarters of the sky, back to the great tower; who may be presumed to\r\nenjoy vibration, and to know that bell and organ are going to give it\r\nthem. Come a very small and straggling congregation indeed: chiefly\r\nfrom Minor Canon Corner and the Precincts. Come Mr. Crisparkle, fresh\r\nand bright; and his ministering brethren, not quite so fresh and\r\nbright. Come the Choir in a hurry (always in a hurry, and struggling\r\ninto their nightgowns at the last moment, like children shirking bed),\r\nand comes John Jasper leading their line. Last of all comes Mr.\r\nDatchery into a stall, one of a choice empty collection very much at\r\nhis service, and glancing about him for Her Royal Highness the Princess\r\nPuffer.\r\n\r\nThe service is pretty well advanced before Mr. Datchery can discern Her\r\nRoyal Highness. But by that time he has made her out, in the shade. She\r\nis behind a pillar, carefully withdrawn from the Choir-master\xe2\x80\x99s view,\r\nbut regards him with the closest attention. All unconscious of her\r\npresence, he chants and sings. She grins when he is most musically\r\nfervid, and\xe2\x80\x94yes, Mr. Datchery sees her do it!\xe2\x80\x94shakes her fist at him\r\nbehind the pillar\xe2\x80\x99s friendly shelter.\r\n\r\nMr. Datchery looks again, to convince himself. Yes, again! As ugly and\r\nwithered as one of the fantastic carvings on the under brackets of the\r\nstall seats, as malignant as the Evil One, as hard as the big brass\r\neagle holding the sacred books upon his wings (and, according to the\r\nsculptor\xe2\x80\x99s representation of his ferocious attributes, not at all\r\nconverted by them), she hugs herself in her lean arms, and then shakes\r\nboth fists at the leader of the Choir.\r\n\r\nAnd at that moment, outside the grated door of the Choir, having eluded\r\nthe vigilance of Mr. Tope by shifty resources in which he is an adept,\r\nDeputy peeps, sharp-eyed, through the bars, and stares astounded from\r\nthe threatener to the threatened.\r\n\r\nThe service comes to an end, and the servitors disperse to breakfast.\r\nMr. Datchery accosts his last new acquaintance outside, when the Choir\r\n(as much in a hurry to get their bedgowns off, as they were but now to\r\nget them on) have scuffled away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, mistress. Good morning. You have seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ve_ seen him, deary; _I\xe2\x80\x99ve_ seen him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnow him! Better far than all the Reverend Parsons put together know\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Tope\xe2\x80\x99s care has spread a very neat, clean breakfast ready for her\r\nlodger. Before sitting down to it, he opens his corner-cupboard door;\r\ntakes his bit of chalk from its shelf; adds one thick line to the\r\nscore, extending from the top of the cupboard door to the bottom; and\r\nthen falls to with an appetite.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nAPPENDIX: FRAGMENT OF \xe2\x80\x98THE MYSTERY OF EDWIN DROOD\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Forster was just finishing his biography of Dickens, he found\r\namong the leaves of one of the novelist\xe2\x80\x99s other manuscripts certain\r\nloose slips in his writing, \xe2\x80\x98on paper only half the size of that used\r\nfor the tale, so cramped, interlined, and blotted as to be nearly\r\nillegible.\xe2\x80\x99 These proved, upon examination, to contain a suggested\r\nchapter for _Edwin Drood_, in which Sapsea, the auctioneer, appears as\r\nthe principal figure, surrounded by a group of characters new to the\r\nstory. That chapter, being among the last things Dickens wrote, seems\r\nto contain so much of interest that it may be well to reprint it\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x94ED.\r\n\r\nHOW MR. SAPSEA CEASED TO BE A MEMBER OF THE EIGHT CLUB\r\nTOLD BY HIMSELF\r\n\r\nWishing to take the air, I proceeded by a circuitous route to the Club,\r\nit being our weekly night of meeting. I found that we mustered our full\r\nstrength. We were enrolled under the denomination of the Eight Club. We\r\nwere eight in number; we met at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock during eight months of\r\nthe year; we played eight games of four-handed cribbage, at eightpence\r\nthe game; our frugal supper was composed of eight rolls, eight mutton\r\nchops, eight pork sausages, eight baked potatoes, eight marrow-bones,\r\nwith eight toasts, and eight bottles of ale. There may, or may not, be\r\na certain harmony of colour in the ruling idea of this (to adopt a\r\nphrase of our lively neighbours) reunion. It was a little idea of mine.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: Facsimile of a page of the manuscript of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Mystery of\r\nEdwin Drood\xe2\x80\x9d]\r\n\r\n\r\nA somewhat popular member of the Eight Club, was a member by the name\r\nof Kimber. By profession, a dancing-master. A commonplace, hopeful sort\r\nof man, wholly destitute of dignity or knowledge of the world.\r\n\r\nAs I entered the Club-room, Kimber was making the remark: \xe2\x80\x98And he still\r\nhalf-believes him to be very high in the Church.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIn the act of hanging up my hat on the eighth peg by the door, I caught\r\nKimber\xe2\x80\x99s visual ray. He lowered it, and passed a remark on the next\r\nchange of the moon. I did not take particular notice of this at the\r\nmoment, because the world was often pleased to be a little shy of\r\necclesiastical topics in my presence. For I felt that I was picked out\r\n(though perhaps only through a coincidence) to a certain extent to\r\nrepresent what I call our glorious constitution in Church and State.\r\nThe phrase may be objected to by cautious minds; but I own to it as\r\nmine. I threw it off in argument some little time back. I said: \xe2\x80\x98OUR\r\nGLORIOUS CONSTITUTION in CHURCH and STATE.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnother member of the Eight Club was Peartree; also member of the Royal\r\nCollege of Surgeons. Mr. Peartree is not accountable to me for his\r\nopinions, and I say no more of them here than that he attends the poor\r\ngratis whenever they want him, and is not the parish doctor. Mr.\r\nPeartree may justify it to the grasp of _his_ mind thus to do his\r\nrepublican utmost to bring an appointed officer into contempt. Suffice\r\nit that Mr. Peartree can never justify it to the grasp of _mine_.\r\n\r\nBetween Peartree and Kimber there was a sickly sort of feeble-minded\r\nalliance. It came under my particular notice when I sold off Kimber by\r\nauction. (Goods taken in execution.) He was a widower in a white\r\nunder-waistcoat, and slight shoes with bows, and had two daughters not\r\nill-looking. Indeed the reverse. Both daughters taught dancing in\r\nscholastic establishments for Young Ladies\xe2\x80\x94had done so at Mrs.\r\nSapsea\xe2\x80\x99s; nay, Twinkleton\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94and both, in giving lessons, presented the\r\nunwomanly spectacle of having little fiddles tucked under their chins.\r\nIn spite of which, the younger one might, if I am correctly informed\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nwill raise the veil so far as to say I KNOW she might\xe2\x80\x94have soared for\r\nlife from this degrading taint, but for having the class of mind\r\nallotted to what I call the common herd, and being so incredibly devoid\r\nof veneration as to become painfully ludicrous.\r\n\r\nWhen I sold off Kimber without reserve, Peartree (as poor as he can\r\nhold together) had several prime household lots knocked down to him. I\r\nam not to be blinded; and of course it was as plain to me what he was\r\ngoing to do with them, as it was that he was a brown hulking sort of\r\nrevolutionary subject who had been in India with the soldiers, and\r\nought (for the sake of society) to have his neck broke. I saw the lots\r\nshortly afterwards in Kimber\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings\xe2\x80\x94through the window\xe2\x80\x94and I easily\r\nmade out that there had been a sneaking pretence of lending them till\r\nbetter times. A man with a smaller knowledge of the world than myself\r\nmight have been led to suspect that Kimber had held back money from his\r\ncreditors, and fraudulently bought the goods. But, besides that I knew\r\nfor certain he had no money, I knew that this would involve a species\r\nof forethought not to be made compatible with the frivolity of a\r\ncaperer, inoculating other people with capering, for his bread.\r\n\r\nAs it was the first time I had seen either of those two since the sale,\r\nI kept myself in what I call Abeyance. When selling him up, I had\r\ndelivered a few remarks\xe2\x80\x94shall I say a little homily?\xe2\x80\x94concerning Kimber,\r\nwhich the world did regard as more than usually worth notice. I had\r\ncome up into my pulpit, it was said, uncommonly like\xe2\x80\x94and a murmur of\r\nrecognition had repeated his (I will not name whose) title, before I\r\nspoke. I had then gone on to say that all present would find, in the\r\nfirst page of the catalogue that was lying before them, in the last\r\nparagraph before the first lot, the following words: \xe2\x80\x98Sold in pursuance\r\nof a writ of execution issued by a creditor.\xe2\x80\x99 I had then proceeded to\r\nremind my friends, that however frivolous, not to say contemptible, the\r\nbusiness by which a man got his goods together, still his goods were as\r\ndear to him, and as cheap to society (if sold without reserve), as\r\nthough his pursuits had been of a character that would bear serious\r\ncontemplation. I had then divided my text (if I may be allowed so to\r\ncall it) into three heads: firstly, Sold; secondly, In pursuance of a\r\nwrit of execution; thirdly, Issued by a creditor; with a few moral\r\nreflections on each, and winding up with, \xe2\x80\x98Now to the first lot\xe2\x80\x99 in a\r\nmanner that was complimented when I afterwards mingled with my hearers.\r\n\r\nSo, not being certain on what terms I and Kimber stood, I was grave, I\r\nwas chilling. Kimber, however, moving to me, I moved to Kimber. (I was\r\nthe creditor who had issued the writ. Not that it matters.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was alluding, Mr. Sapsea,\xe2\x80\x99 said Kimber, \xe2\x80\x98to a stranger who entered\r\ninto conversation with me in the street as I came to the Club. He had\r\nbeen speaking to you just before, it seemed, by the churchyard; and\r\nthough you had told him who you were, I could hardly persuade him that\r\nyou were not high in the Church.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Idiot?\xe2\x80\x99 said Peartree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ass!\xe2\x80\x99 said Kimber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Idiot and Ass!\xe2\x80\x99 said the other five members.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Idiot and Ass, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x99 I remonstrated, looking around me, \xe2\x80\x98are\r\nstrong expressions to apply to a young man of good appearance and\r\naddress.\xe2\x80\x99 My generosity was roused; I own it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ll admit that he must be a Fool,\xe2\x80\x99 said Peartree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You can\xe2\x80\x99t deny that he must be a Blockhead,\xe2\x80\x99 said Kimber.\r\n\r\nTheir tone of disgust amounted to being offensive. Why should the young\r\nman be so calumniated? What had he done? He had only made an innocent\r\nand natural mistake. I controlled my generous indignation, and said so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Natural?\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Kimber. \xe2\x80\x98_He\xe2\x80\x99s_ a Natural!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe remaining six members of the Eight Club laughed unanimously. It\r\nstung me. It was a scornful laugh. My anger was roused in behalf of an\r\nabsent, friendless stranger. I rose (for I had been sitting down).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x99 I said with dignity, \xe2\x80\x98I will not remain one of this Club\r\nallowing opprobrium to be cast on an unoffending person in his absence.\r\nI will not so violate what I call the sacred rites of hospitality.\r\nGentlemen, until you know how to behave yourselves better, I leave you.\r\nGentlemen, until then I withdraw, from this place of meeting, whatever\r\npersonal qualifications I may have brought into it. Gentlemen, until\r\nthen you cease to be the Eight Club, and must make the best you can of\r\nbecoming the Seven.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI put on my hat and retired. As I went down stairs I distinctly heard\r\nthem give a suppressed cheer. Such is the power of demeanour and\r\nknowledge of mankind. I had forced it out of them.\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\nWhom should I meet in the street, within a few yards of the door of the\r\ninn where the Club was held, but the self-same young man whoso cause I\r\nhad felt it my duty so warmly\xe2\x80\x94and I will add so disinterestedly\xe2\x80\x94to take\r\nup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it Mr. Sapsea,\xe2\x80\x99 he said doubtfully, \xe2\x80\x98or is it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is Mr. Sapsea,\xe2\x80\x99 I replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon me, Mr. Sapsea; you appear warm, sir.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have been warm,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98and on your account.\xe2\x80\x99 Having stated the\r\ncircumstances at some length (my generosity almost overpowered him), I\r\nasked him his name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Sapsea,\xe2\x80\x99 he answered, looking down, \xe2\x80\x98your penetration is so acute,\r\nyour glance into the souls of your fellow men is so penetrating, that\r\nif I was hardly enough to deny that my name is Poker, what would it\r\navail me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I had quite exactly made out to a fraction that his\r\nname _was_ Poker, but I daresay I had been pretty near doing it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, well,\xe2\x80\x99 said I, trying to put him at his ease by nodding my head\r\nin a soothing way. \xe2\x80\x98Your name is Poker, and there is no harm in being\r\nnamed Poker.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, Mr. Sapsea!\xe2\x80\x99 cried the young man, in a very well-behaved manner.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bless you for those words!\xe2\x80\x99 He then, as if ashamed of having given way\r\nto his feelings, looked down again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come Poker,\xe2\x80\x99 said I, \xe2\x80\x98let me hear more about you. Tell me. Where are\r\nyou going to, Poker? and where do you come from?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah Mr. Sapsea!\xe2\x80\x99 exclaimed the young man. \xe2\x80\x98Disguise from you is\r\nimpossible. You know already that I come from somewhere, and am going\r\nsomewhere else. If I was to deny it, what would it avail me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then don\xe2\x80\x99t deny it,\xe2\x80\x99 was my remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Or,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Poker, in a kind of despondent rapture, \xe2\x80\x98or if I was to\r\ndeny that I came to this town to see and hear you, sir, what would it\r\navail me? Or if I was to deny\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE MYSTERY OF EDWIN DROOD ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 564-0.txt or 564-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/5/6/564/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
